# 抱朴子内篇

The *Baopuzi Inner Chapters*, by Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty
*Chapter One: Expounding the Mysterious*

The Master Baopuzi said: "The Mysterious is the primal ancestor of Nature and the great origin of the ten thousand differences. Vast and profound is its depth, thus it is called subtle. Boundless and distant is its reach, thus it is called wondrous. Lofty, it crowns the nine heavens; expansive, it envelops the eight directions. Brighter than sun and moon, swifter than lightning’s flight. Now it flickers and vanishes like a shadow, now it drifts like scattered stars; now it shimmers in the limpid abyss, now it billows like floating clouds. It takes form through signs and images, yet entrusts itself to silence and stillness as non-being. It sinks into the great darkness below, soars beyond the celestial pole above. Neither metal nor stone can match its hardness, neither dew nor mist can equal its softness. Square yet not confined by the carpenter’s square, round yet not bound by the compass. It comes—none see it; it goes—none pursue it. Qian (Heaven) is high through it, Kun (Earth) is low through it; clouds move through it, rain is bestowed through it.

It is the womb of the Primal Unity, the mold that casts the Two Modes. It exhales and inhales the Great Beginning, smelts and forges the myriad kinds. It revolves the four and seven, crafts the formless chaos, reins the numinous mechanism, breathes forth the four qi. Encompassing the profound and silent, unfolding the radiant and splendid, it suppresses the turbid and lifts the clear, measures the Yellow River and the Wei. Add to it—it does not overflow; draw from it—it does not deplete. Bestow it—it brings no glory; seize it—it causes no exhaustion. Where the Mysterious abides, joy is inexhaustible. Where the Mysterious departs, vessels decay and spirits flee.

The five tones and eight timbres, the flowing *qing* and *zhi* modes—these impair hearing. Fresh blossoms and dazzling hues, resplendent beauty—these harm vision. Feasting and ease, fine wine and fragrant spirits—these disorder the nature. Alluring charm and coquettish grace, leaden powder and white silk—these cut short life. Only the Mysterious Dao can endure forever.

Those who know not the Mysterious Dao, though they wield the divine instruments of life and death with a glance, though their lips and words are the pivot of rise and fall, though their ornate towers overlook clouds and rain, their decorated chambers gleam with variegated hues, their tasseled curtains gather like mist, their gauze canopies disperse like clouds, rare furs are arrayed in their chambers, golden goblets course back and forth, clear strings resound in unison, Zheng dances swirl in silken undulations, mournful flutes soar above the clouds, feathered canopies float on rippling waters, they pluck fragrant blossoms from orchid groves, play with red petals by pearl-studded pools, ascend heights to gaze afar and forget a hundred sorrows, peer into depths to overlook morning hunger, enter banquets amid the thousand gates’ brilliance, depart in crimson-wheeled splendor—yet when joy reaches its peak, sorrow gathers; when fullness is complete, loss follows. Thus, when music ends, sighs arise; when feasts conclude, the heart grieves. This is the inevitable summons of principle and circumstance, like echo answering sound, shadow following form. Such pleasures are borrowed, not real; thus, when things pass, they leave a sense of loss.

The Mysterious Dao is attained within, guarded without, employed as spirit, forgotten as mere vessel—this is the essential teaching of contemplating the Mysterious Dao. Those who attain it are noble without the threat of the golden axe; those who embody it are rich without rare treasures. It is too high to ascend, too deep to fathom. They ride the flowing light, spur the flying radiance, soar through the six voids, pierce the boundless expanse. They emerge from the utmost above, enter the utmost below, traverse the gate of vastness, wander the fields of the remote and obscure. Roaming carefree in the indistinct, lingering in the vague and fleeting. They swallow ninefold blossoms at the clouds’ edge, chew the six qi amid cinnabar clouds. They meander in the dim and obscure, soar in the faint and subtle, tread the winding rainbows, pace the revolving celestial sphere—these are those who have attained it.

Next are those who truly know contentment. Knowing contentment, they can withdraw and remain unused, nurturing their light in mountains and forests. They tuck the wings of phoenix and dragon among the smallest creatures, cultivate their vast qi amid thatched huts. In ragged robes and hempen belts, they do not trade for the splendor of dragon-patterned robes. Leaning on staffs, they do not exchange their steps for the relay of four-horse carriages. They hide night-shining pearls in lofty peaks, refusing the assaults of other mountains. They sink scales and armor in dark abysses, avoiding the calamity of drilling and burning. Moving and resting, they know when to stop—wherever they go, they are sufficient. They abandon the dazzling glory of courtly splendor, shun the perilous paths of overturned chariots. They chant and whistle in the leisure of green cliffs, and the myriad things dissolve into dust. They delight beneath the lush boughs, and vermilion gates become thatched huts. They grasp the plow in the fields, and insignia of office suddenly seem like holding a whip. They sip tea and rinse with springs, and the grandest feasts are no better than coarse greens. Serene, they find boundless joy in the realm of non-action, joyfully equalizing noble and base in the land of no contention. Embracing purity and guarding simplicity, free from desire and care, they preserve their true nature and empty vessels, dwelling in peace with bland flavors. Vast and unbounded, they match the uncarved block in naturalness. Vast and boundless, they harmonize with creation in accord. Now dark, now bright; now turbid, now clear. Seeming slow yet swift, seeming deficient yet full. How could they deign to occupy the seat of the ancestral shrine’s caretaker, abandon the master craftsman’s role, leap over the sacrificial vessels to replace the ignorant cook, or discard the inked line to aid the hand-wounding artisan? They do not let the stench of rats and trivial concerns become the worries and joys of mediocre men. Detached, they do not delight in the praise of the vulgar crowd; unperturbed, they do not fear the slander of the herd. They do not let external things disturb their utmost essence, nor let profit and harm stain their purity.

Thus, extreme wealth and utmost honor cannot tempt them—what else could please them? Razor-sharp blades and boiling cauldrons cannot coerce them—how could slander and abuse trouble them? Ever without intention amid the world’s troubles, they have never mingled with things.

As for those who wield Sui pearls to shoot sparrows, lick Qin’s sores to ride in carriages, climb rotten ropes to raid nests, swim the Lüliang rapids to catch fish—morning may find them guests of honor, but by evening they are the leavings of foxes and crows."

Beams bend and cauldrons overturn, capsizing and drowning without recovery—these are the very things for which the common run of men gallop and strain, yet which make the enlightened shudder with cold dread.

Thus the perfected ones silently conceal the music of Shao and Xia, sheathing their ornate rafter-tips. They spread their six pinions over the ruins of the Five Cities, yet need no reed-in-the-beak for defense. They hide their scales and horns in places of no use, yet rely not on crooked burrows for refuge. Below, no owl’s screech of arrogance; above, no regret of overreaching. No one knows them—how distant, how vast!

---

**Scroll Two · On Transcendents**

Someone asked, “That transcendents never die—can this truly be so?”
The Master Embracing Simplicity replied:
“Though vision be most keen, what has form cannot all be seen. Though hearing be most acute, what has sound cannot all be heard. Though one possess the strides of Da Zhang and Shu Hai, what is habitually trod is far less than what is untrod. Though one possess the wisdom of Yu, Yi, and Qi Xie, what is known is far less than what remains unknown.

The ten thousand things teem—what is there that does not exist? How much more must the ranks of transcendents fill the bamboo and silk records! How could the Way of no-death not be among them?”

The questioner burst into laughter and said: “What has a beginning must have an end; what exists must perish. Thus the sages of the Three and Five Dynasties and the Duke of Zhou, the wisdom of Yi Qi and Chen Ping, the eloquence of Zhan Huo, Sui Wu, and Li Yiji, the courage of Meng Ben, Xia Yu, and the Five Strongmen—all met their deaths. This is the constant principle of human existence, the great inevitability that must come to pass.

We have heard of plants withering before the frost, greenery wilting in midsummer, ears of grain failing to bloom, fruits shriveling before ripening—yet never have we heard of one who enjoys ten thousand years of life, who gazes on without end. Therefore, the ancients did not seek transcendence in their studies, nor speak of the uncanny in their words. They shut out such heterodoxies and adhered to nature’s course, classifying tortoises and cranes as distinct species, treating life and death as but morning and evening. To torment the heart and constrain the self in pursuit of futile endeavors is like carving ice or chiseling rot—ultimately, no certain success can be achieved.

Would it not be far better to unfurl grand strategies for ordering the age, summoning the blessings of the present era, so that purple reins coil thickly, black steeds and dragon-steeds stand ready, splendid carriages replace humble steps, and tripods laden with delicacies supplant the plow and hoe? Is this not splendid indeed? I often reflect on the *Poet’s* rebuke in ‘The Great Field,’ and deeply ponder Confucius’ proof that all must die. Why grasp at the formless wind, chase the elusive shadow, seek the unattainable, tread the impassable path? Why forsake glory and splendor to endure hardship and toil, abandon the effortless to strive for the most arduous? It is like a mourner pursuing a wandering maiden—surely regret for both losses will follow. To cling to a one-sided belief and partial view will only hasten calamity within and without.

Ban Di could not carve shards of tile and stone into fine needles; Ou Ye could not forge lead and tin into *Gan Jiang* swords. Thus, what cannot be done, even spirits and gods cannot accomplish; what cannot be achieved, even heaven and earth cannot bring to fruition. How then could the world possess some wondrous formula to restore the aged to youth or revive the dead to life? Yet you would prolong the mayfly’s fleeting span to endure for epochs, nurture the morning mushroom’s brief bloom to accumulate through months and years—is this not absurd? I urge you to ponder deeply and swiftly turn back from this delusion.”

Bao Puzi replied: “When hearing departs, even thunder cannot make one perceive; when sight is lost, even the Three Luminaries cannot make one see. Is it that the rumble of chariots is too faint, or the radiance of heaven too dim? Yet the deaf call it silence, the blind call it emptiness. How then could they appreciate the harmonies of pipes and strings, the resplendent brocades of mountain and dragon? If deafness and blindness lie in the bodily organs, they will not believe in the thunder god or the celestial bodies—how much less will they accept things more subtle than these? When darkness and dullness cloud the heart and spirit, they will not believe that the sages Zhou and Kong ever existed—how then could they be told of the Dao of immortals?

Life and death, beginning and end—these are indeed the great principles. Yet their variations are manifold, their outcomes uncertain; transformations are myriad, marvels boundless. Things may share the same root but diverge in their branches; they cannot be judged by a single standard. Many who speak of beginnings must also speak of ends, but to conflate them is not sound reason. One says summer must be long, yet shepherd’s purse and wheat wither; one says winter must be barren, yet bamboo and pine flourish. One says all that begins must end, yet heaven and earth are endless; one says all that lives must die, yet the tortoise and crane endure. The height of yang should bring heat, yet summer days may cool; the depth of yin should bring cold, yet winter may briefly warm. All rivers flow east, yet some surge northward; the earth’s nature is utmost stillness, yet it may quake and crumble. Water is inherently cold, yet hot springs rise in warm valleys; fire is inherently fierce, yet cold flames burn on Xiao Hill. Heavy things should sink, yet the Southern Sea has floating mountains of stone; light things should float, yet in the Yangtze’s currents, feathers sink. The myriad kinds cannot be judged by a single measure—this has long been so.

Of all living things, none is more spiritually endowed than man. As the noblest of beings, their natures should be uniform. Yet their wisdom and folly, uprightness and perversity, beauty and ugliness, length and brevity of life, purity and impurity, chastity and licentiousness, slowness and haste, preferences and aversions, desires of ear and eye—these differences are as vast as heaven and earth, as opposed as ice and fire. Why then find it strange that immortals differ from ordinary men in not sharing their mortality?

If one claims that all receive qi in a fixed measure, then how is it that pheasants become clams, sparrows become mussels, earthworms sprout wings, frogs take flight, water leeches turn to dragonflies, duckweed becomes maggots, field mice become waterfowl, rotten grass becomes fireflies, alligators become tigers, and snakes become dragons? If one claims that man is endowed with a proper nature, unlike common creatures, and that Heaven bestows destiny without distinction, then how is it that Niu Ai turned into a tiger, the woman of Chu into a soft-shelled turtle, Zhi Li into a willow, the maiden of Qin into stone, the dead revived, men and women exchanged forms, Peng Zu lived long while infants died young? What explains these things?

If there are such differences, then how can their variations be limited? As for immortals, they nurture the body with medicines and herbs, extend life with arts and calculations, preventing inner illness and warding off external harm. Though they may live long without dying, their original form does not change. If one possesses the Dao, this is not difficult.

Yet those of shallow understanding, bound by convention and custom, all say they have never seen an immortal in the world, and thus claim such things must not exist. How can what the eye has once seen be the measure of all things? Between heaven and earth, in the boundless vastness, how can its wonders be limited? One may live beneath the sky all one’s days yet not know what lies above; tread the earth a lifetime yet not fathom what lies below. The body is one’s own, yet one does not know the workings of its heart and mind. Lifespan rests with oneself, yet one does not know how to extend or shorten it. How much less can one, relying on shallow senses, judge the existence or nonexistence of the profound principles of immortals and the abstruse mysteries of the Dao? Is this not lamentable?

Suppose there were a sage of great talent, who withdrew from worldly use, concealed his brilliance, cast off falsehood and desire, held fast to unhewn jade in the midst of utter purity, and abandoned trivial affairs beyond the flow of custom—even then, few among men could discern him. Some might not aspire to his nameless virtue, nor find spirit within his humble form. How much less could they recognize an immortal, who follows a singular path, deems wealth and honor misfortune, regards glory as filth, treasures as dust, fame as morning dew, treads fiery winds unscorched, steps upon dark waves with lightness, stirs wings in the pure air, rides the wind and clouds in a chariot, ascends to the purple pole, descends to Kunlun, while the walking corpses of men—how could they see him?

If he were to wander and play, passing through the human world, concealing his true nature and hiding his differences, appearing no different from the common crowd, shoulder to shoulder, step by step—who could perceive him? If all were like the square pupils of the two boys in the wilds, the double ears of Qiong Shu, if Ma Huang rode a dragon, if Zi Jin drove a white crane, if some had scaly serpent bodies, or golden chariots and feathered robes—only then might they be known. Without such signs, how could one not possessed of penetrating sight behold their form, or one not endowed with keen hearing perceive their voice?

Since the world does not believe, and many find fault, the true person loathes this and retreats further into seclusion. Moreover, what common men love, the lofty despise; what the vulgar value, the perfected scorn. Even noble scholars and great talents, who cultivate their vast integrity, do not delight in the company of the shallow and worldly. How much less would immortals hasten to make known their existence to the likes of straw dogs? What could they seek from such as these? Yet men marvel at what they have never known.”

Seeing clearly for a hundred paces, one cannot perceive all things distinctly—yet to take what is seen as existent and what is unseen as nonexistent would mean that much of what exists in the world must be deemed absent. This is like measuring the sea with a finger, claiming that where the finger ends, the water ceases. How could the mayfly compare its lifespan to the giant turtle, or the dayfly fathom the age of the great *chun* tree? Emperor Wen of Wei, with his vast learning and exhaustive knowledge, boasted of having experienced all things under heaven. He once declared in his *Dian Lun* that there existed no blade capable of cutting jade, nor cloth that could be cleansed by fire. Yet before long, both objects appeared. The emperor sighed in remorse and swiftly retracted his words. Nothing is certain—this is precisely why.

Prince Si of Chen wrote in his *Shiyi Lun*: "At first, I believed that the arts of the Dao were mere deceptions, the empty prattle of foolish commoners, settled as fact." But after witnessing Emperor Wu test Zuo Ci and others, commanding them to abstain from grain for nearly a month while their complexion remained unchanged and their strength undiminished—claiming they could go fifty years without food—what room was left for doubt? He also recounted how Gan Shi fed fish a certain drug before boiling them in scalding oil: those without the drug cooked through and became edible, while those that had swallowed it swam about all day as if still in water. When silkworms were fed mulberry leaves dusted with another drug, they did not age until the tenth month. Chicks and newborn puppies fed an "age-halting" drug ceased to grow, while a white dog fed a "reversal" drug turned entirely black within a hundred days. From this, one realizes that the affairs of the world cannot be fully known, and to judge them by conjecture alone is unwise. His only regret was his inability to renounce sensual pleasures and devote himself wholeheartedly to the study of the Way of long life.

Both Cao Cao and Cao Pi were unparalleled in learning and talent for their generation, yet initially they dismissed such things as nonexistent. Only in their later years, after exhaustively investigating principle and fully realizing their nature, did they sigh in such remorse. Those who fall short of such men and still do not believe in immortals are hardly to be wondered at. Liu Xiang, with his profound scholarship and mastery of subtle mysteries, his deep engagement with distant classics and clear discernment of truth and falsehood, meticulously examined the existence or nonexistence of such matters. In his *Liexian Zhuan*, he recorded over seventy immortals—if these accounts were false, why would he have fabricated them? The events of remote antiquity cannot be witnessed firsthand; we rely on records and transmitted accounts. The *Liexian Zhuan* clearly demonstrates that such beings must exist. Yet because its contents did not emerge from the school of the Duke of Zhou, nor pass through the hands of Confucius,世人终究不信. If this logic were applied, then all ancient histories would be deemed nonexistent—why single out this one matter?

Common people, greedy for glory and profit, ceaselessly pursue fame and gain. Projecting their own minds onto the ancients, they refuse to believe that there were ever those who fled the abdication of emperors, scorned the noble ranks of ministers, like the likes of Chao Fu and Xu You, or Lao Lai and Zhuang Zhou. If even such men are dismissed as nonexistent, how much more so the immortals, who are even harder to know? How could one expect the world to universally believe in them? Many argue that Liu Xiang was no sage, and thus his records cannot be taken as sole evidence—this is all the more reason for sighing.

The annals of Lu did not perfectly align with the virtue of Heaven and Earth, yet Confucius used them to compile the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. Sima Qian’s brilliance did not rival the sun and moon, yet Yang Xiong praised his work as "true records." Liu Xiang was a renowned Confucian scholar and worthy of the Han dynasty—how could his writings be discarded? The reason most people do not believe that immortality can be learned or that life can be extended lies precisely in the failures of the First Emperor of Qin and Emperor Wu of Han to attain it, and in the lack of verification from figures like Luan Da and Li Shaojun. Yet one cannot cite the poverty of Qian Lou and Yuan Xian as proof that the ancients lacked the wealth of Tao Zhu or Yi Dun, nor the ugliness of the woman from Wuyan or the hunchback from Su as evidence that there were never beauties like Nan Wei or Xi Shi.

In all endeavors, there are those who do not succeed: farmers may fail to reap a harvest, merchants may not profit, generals may not achieve victory. How much more so with the pursuit of immortality, one of the most difficult of arts—why should every practitioner necessarily succeed? Those two emperors and their ministers may have sought and failed, or begun with diligence only to grow lax, or never encountered a true master—how could their failures prove the nonexistence of immortals in the world?

The pursuit of long life and the cultivation of the supreme Dao depend on resolve, not on wealth or rank. For those unsuited to the path, high position and great wealth become nothing but heavy burdens. Why? The methods of learning immortality require tranquility, simplicity, and the elimination of desires—inner contemplation, inward listening, dwelling in stillness without thought. Yet emperors bear the weighty responsibility of governing the realm, managing the affairs of state, laboring over myriad details, their spirits scattered across the cosmos. A single misstep disrupts the kingly way; the faults of the people are laid at their feet. Rich wines muddy their harmonious qi, seductive beauty severs their root vitality. The ways in which their essence is depleted, their thoughts exhausted, and their purity eroded cannot be fully enumerated.

A mosquito’s bite makes sitting unbearable; a swarm of lice makes sleep impossible. The affairs of the four seas are no less burdensome. How can one hope to shield their senses, regulate their breathing, maintain long fasts and strict purity, tend the furnace fire themselves, rise early and retire late, to refine the eight minerals? Emperor Wu of Han ruled for the longest span of any Han emperor, and even he gained only a small benefit in nurturing his nature. Yet the aid of a mere sheng or ge of elixir could not meet the demands of a zhong or dan, just as the tribute of streams and ditches cannot fill the vast drain of the Tailuo abyss.

The methods of immortality demand stillness, non-action, and forgetting the body. But the ruler of men strikes thousand-pound bells, beats drums like thunder, their clamor startling the soul and scattering the mind. A hundred arts and myriad distractions deplete essence and deafen the ears. Swift flights and rapid pursuits, angling for the deep and shooting the high—all these dissipate the spirit.

The Dao of immortality demands love extend even to creeping insects, that no breath-bearing being be harmed. Yet the ruler of men harbors wrath like scorching flames, meting out executions like reaping grass—with but a single swing of the yellow battle-axe or a momentary grasp of the executioner’s blade, corpses lie strewn for a thousand li, blood flows in torrents, and the severing of limbs never ceases in the marketplace.

The Dao of immortality seeks to end all foul odors and meat, to cease eating and purify the intestines. Yet the ruler of men boils fat and butchers beasts, slaughtering living creatures in droves; the eight delicacies and a hundred seasonings fill tables before him, while simmering and searing, blending and stewing, yield only the finest of feasts.

The Dao of immortality calls for boundless love across the eight wilds, to see others as oneself. Yet the ruler of men annexes the weak and attacks the benighted, seizing chaos and toppling the doomed; he carves out lands and expands borders, erasing the altars of other peoples, driving the living into death’s embrace, leaving orphaned souls in distant lands and exposed bones to rot in the wilds. The Five Ridges bristle with bloodied blades, the northern palace displays the heads of Da Yuan; mass graves swallow the living, executions claim tens of thousands, and burial mounds of the slain rise to scrape the clouds, their sprawling bones like tangled undergrowth, filling mountains and valleys.

The First Emperor of Qin left nine of every ten households longing for rebellion. Emperor Wu of Han left the realm in clamor, its population halved.

Should prayers bring benefit, curses too may bring harm. Those who bind grass to repay kindness will surely resent empty offerings. When the masses weary of assaulting one’s vital core, both men and ghosts share their bitter hatred.

Those two sovereigns bore only the name of loving immortality, yet lacked the substance of cultivating the Dao. Their shallow knowledge of affairs prevented full practice, while the profound and secret essentials remained beyond their hearing. Moreover, they failed to find men of the Dao to compound elixirs for them—no wonder they did not attain longevity.

As for us common folk, pressed by utter destitution, our homes endure the penury of Sima Changqing’s bare walls, our bellies suffer the hunger of Yi Sang’s famished mulberry. In winter we bear the cold of Rong Yi’s back gate, in summer the glare of Ru Zhong’s cramped hut. We long to journey far yet lack carriage or boat; we wish to undertake labor yet have no one to relieve us. Within, no silken pleasures; without, no joy of wandering sights. Sweet delicacies never pass our lips, fine fabrics never meet our eyes, fragrance never reaches our noses, the eight tones never touch our ears. A hundred sorrows assail the chambers of our hearts, countless hardships gather at our gates. Living in such a world, what is there to cling to?

Some chance upon the essentials of the Dao, some encounter extraordinary masters, yet still they fret over aging wives and weak children, remain attached to the mounds of foxes and hares, linger until death’s approach, unaware of time’s passage and their own decline. They know longevity is attainable yet fail to cultivate it, fear the stench of common rats yet cannot abandon them. Why? The bonds of habit are too hard to cast off, and the resolve to break from the world is not easily fulfilled.

How much more so for those two emperors, lords of all under heaven? Their indulgences were not confined to one path, their favored intimates were legion. Even a ten-day retreat or a few days of seclusion would have been beyond them—how then could they forsake the tender affections of the inner palace, cast off the dazzling splendor of their station, renounce rich delicacies, extinguish all desire, turn from glory and go forth alone, seeking immortals in the distant wilds? Could they have endured it?

Thus, looking back through history, those who attained the Dao of immortality were mostly men of lowly station, not the powerful and eminent. Moreover, Luan Tai’s knowledge was truly shallow and crude. He hungered and thirsted for glory and wealth, recklessly sought profit, peddled falsehoods for momentary gain, and forgot the perils of inaction. How could the deceit of such a petty man prove the nonexistence of immortality?

In ancient times, when Goujian brandished his halberd in anger, his soldiers vied to tread through fire. When King Ling of Chu favored slender waists, his people starved themselves to death. Duke Huan of Qi craved rare flavors, and Yi Ya steamed his own son. When the Lord of Song rewarded filial piety in emaciation, houses across the land were filled with the dead. Whatever a ruler desires, none dare fail to comply.

Emperor Wu of Han summoned and favored masters of esoteric arts beyond measure, emboldening such men to spin wild falsehoods. Had Luan Tai truly encountered a man of the Dao, how could he have been slain? For men of the Dao regard rank and office as boiling cauldrons, see seals of office as mourning garments, view gold and jade as dirt and dung, and behold grand halls as prisons. Would they clench their fists in empty speech, hoping to chance upon glory, dwelling in vermilion chambers, accepting boundless gifts, wearing the seal of the Five Powers, enjoying the nobility of imperial marriage, wallowing in power and profit, never knowing restraint? Clearly, they had not attained the Dao.

According to the *Household Records of Li Shaojun* compiled by Dong Zhongshu, Shaojun possessed the formula for immortality, yet his family was too poor to purchase the ingredients. Thus he entered Han service, using it as a pretext to seek funds. When his Dao was complete, he departed. Furthermore, the *Daily Records of the Han Palace* state that as Shaojun was about to leave, Emperor Wu dreamed of ascending Mount Songgao with him. Midway, a messenger riding a dragon and bearing a tally descended from the clouds, saying, "Taiyi summons Shaojun." The emperor awoke and told his attendants, "If my dream is true, Shaojun will soon leave me." Days later, Shaojun claimed illness and died. After some time, the emperor ordered his coffin opened—no corpse was found, only his clothes and cap remained.

The *Scriptures of Immortality* state: the highest adepts ascend bodily into the void, called Celestial Immortals; the middle adepts wander among famous mountains, called Earthly Immortals; the lowest adepts first die and then shed their forms, called Liberated Immortals. Shaojun must have been a Liberated Immortal. In recent times, Master Hu took Fei Zhangfang away, and the Daoist Li Yiqí took his two disciples away, all feigning death and being buried by their families. Years later, Zhangfang returned. Others saw Li Yiqí and his two disciples in Pixian County. When their families opened the coffins, each contained only a bamboo staff inscribed in cinnabar—all were Liberated Immortals.

In the past, Wang Mang cited the classics to adorn his treachery—one cannot say all Confucians are usurpers and thieves. Sima Xiangru used the zither to seduce Wenjun—one cannot say refined music leads only to licentiousness. Those who choke to death cannot blame Shennong for sowing grain; those burned cannot resent Suiren for drilling fire; those drowned cannot resent the Yellow Emperor for inventing boats; those who drown in drink cannot condemn Du Kang for making wine. How can Luan Tai’s deceit prove the absolute nonexistence of the Dao of immortality? It is like seeing Zhao Gao and Dong Zhuo and declaring there were no Yi, Zhou, Huo, or Guang in antiquity; or seeing Shang Chen and Modu and claiming there were no Boqi or Xiaoji of filial piety.

Moreover, the *Collected Writings on Immortals* contains methods for summoning spirits and subduing ghosts, as well as techniques to make people see ghosts. Common folk hear of these and dismiss them as empty words. Some say there are no ghosts or spirits in the world; others say they exist but cannot be summoned. Still others say those who see ghosts are, if male, *wu* shamans, if female, *wu* mediums, and that such abilities arise naturally, not through study. Yet the *Book of Han* and the *Records of the Grand Historian* both state that the Qi man Shao Weng was appointed by Emperor Wu as General of Literary Accomplishment.

When Emperor Wu’s beloved consort Lady Li passed away, Shao Weng was able to make the emperor see her as if she were still alive. He also enabled the emperor to see the spirit of the hearth—these are clear records in the historical annals. If methods of esoteric arts can make ghosts appear in visible form and even allow those who normally cannot see ghosts to perceive them, then by this reasoning, what else might not exist? Ghosts and spirits often manifest strange and wondrous phenomena in the human world, and many classics contain accounts of their activities. Yet common folk still refuse to believe in the existence of gods and ghosts—how much less, then, would they accept that immortals dwell in lofty, distant realms, where the pure and turbid currents diverge, ascending beyond reach and never returning to the world? Without attaining the Dao, how could one ever witness or hear of such things? The Confucians and Mohists, knowing these matters cannot be taught, ultimately avoid speaking of their existence. Is it any wonder that the common people do not believe?

Only those with true insight, who have examined and tested various methods, obtained their proofs, and verified their certainty, may come to know this for themselves—yet it cannot be forced. Thus, those who have never seen ghosts or spirits, or never seen immortals, cannot therefore claim there are no immortals in the world. People of all stations, wise or foolish, know that their own bodies possess *hun* and *po* souls. When these souls partially depart, a person falls ill; when they depart entirely, the person dies. Thus, when souls partially depart, esoteric practitioners have methods to restrain and recall them; when they depart entirely, ritual texts contain rites of summoning and invocation. These things are as close as can be—yet though they accompany a person from birth to death, no one has ever perceived them directly. How, then, can one deny their existence simply because they have not been seen or heard?

Consider the vengeful ghost of Lord Fu of Jin, the divine wrath of King Tang against Qi, Shen Sheng’s conversation with the fox spirit, Du Bo’s retribution against King Xuan of Zhou, Peng Sheng manifesting as a black boar, Ru Yi borrowing the form of a gray dog, Guan Fu guarding the field mole, Zi Yi striking the Yan tablet, Shu Su descending at Shen, Lord Luan appearing in a commoner’s home, Su Jiang’s prophecies in the *Chen Wei*, Xiao Sun’s composition of texts, the Divine Lord speaking in Shanglin Park, and Luo Yang serving in the Wu court. The deeds of ghosts and spirits are recorded in bamboo and silk, so numerous and evident that they cannot all be recounted. Yet the blind still insist they do not exist—how much less likely, then, that they would believe in the rare and extraordinary matter of attaining long life!

To demand absolute proof is like expecting a mosquito to carry a mountain or a frog in a well to discourse on the sea. Common folk have never seen dragons, unicorns, *luan* birds, or phoenixes, so they claim such creatures do not exist, assuming the ancients fabricated auspicious omens to encourage rulers to strive unceasingly in hopes of attracting such treasures. How, then, could they ever believe in the existence of immortals?

When Liu Xiang failed to transmute gold, people dismissed him as one who indulged in the obscure and strange, fond of propagating emptiness, and claimed his *Biographies of the Immortals* were all fabrications. How tragic! This is like discarding a foot-long *yeguang* pearl because of a tiny flaw, or abandoning the priceless *Chunjun* sword for a nick no larger than an ant’s nose—not the discernment of a Jing He or the connoisseurship of a Feng Hu. No wonder Zhu Gong was filled with sorrow and Xue Zhu sighed endlessly.

The methods for transmuting gold are all found in the *Collected Writings on Immortals*, which the Prince of Huainan copied out to create the *Hongbao Pillow Book*. Though the texts exist, their essential instructions are kept secret, requiring oral transmission and direct explanation before they can be practiced. Moreover, the names of the ingredients used are often altered, making it impossible to follow the texts directly. Liu Xiang’s father, Liu De, obtained these writings while overseeing the Prince of Huainan’s imprisonment, but he was never formally instructed in their use. Liu Xiang himself had no understanding of the Daoist arts; he merely happened upon these texts and assumed their meaning was fully contained on the page. This is why his attempt to transmute gold failed.

As for his *Biographies of the Immortals*, he compiled them from the writings of Ruan Cang, a grandee of Qin, and from accounts of those he had personally witnessed—his work was no fabrication. Even the ramblings of madmen and the songs of children may contain wisdom worth heeding; the words of woodcutters and grass-gatherers should not be carelessly dismissed. When gathering *feng* and *fei* greens, one does not reject the whole because of the roots—how, then, can a single error among a hundred considerations lead one to dismiss the classics as useless? Would one deny the brilliance of the sun and moon simply because they are sometimes eclipsed?

Foreigners make crystal bowls by combining five kinds of ash, and today in Jiaozhi and Guangzhou, many have mastered this method and cast them. Yet when told of this, common folk stubbornly refuse to believe it, insisting that crystal is a natural substance, akin to jade and stone. If, by chance, there exists natural gold in the world, how could they ever accept that it can be artificially created?

Fools do not believe that red lead and ceruse are made from transformed lead, nor that mules and *juyu* are born of donkeys and horses. They claim all things have their own fixed species—how much more so for matters difficult to comprehend! The less one has seen, the more one finds strange—this is the way of the world. How true these words are! Though the truth may be as bright as the heavens, if one dwells beneath an overturned steamer, how can they recognize the profoundest words?

Someone objected: "That Peng and Lao among men are like pines and cypresses among trees—endowed by nature, how can their longevity be learned?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: "Of all things that shape and transform, none is more numinous than man. Those who grasp its surface may command the myriad things; those who fathom its depths may attain long life and enduring vision. Knowing that supreme medicines extend years, they ingest those medicines to seek immortality. Knowing that tortoises and cranes enjoy remote longevity, they emulate their *daoyin* to prolong life.

Moreover, the branches and leaves of pines and cypresses differ from those of common trees, just as the forms of tortoises and cranes differ from those of other creatures. Yet Peng and Lao remain human—no different in kind—yet their lives alone are long. This is because they attained the Dao, not by nature. Common trees cannot imitate pines and cypresses, nor can other creatures learn from tortoises and cranes; thus they perish early. But man possesses wisdom: those who cultivate the Way of Peng and Lao may achieve the same merit.

If you claim there are no immortals in the world, consider that the records of former sages name nearly a thousand, each with surname and given name, each with documented deeds and origins—no empty words. If you say they were all uniquely endowed with exceptional *qi*, yet their transmissions all came from masters and the practice of ingesting medicines, not innate knowledge.

If the arts of the Dao cannot be learned, then how do men alter their forms, swallow blades and spew fire, sit yet vanish, stand yet disappear, summon clouds and raise mists, call forth insects and serpents, gather fish and turtles, instantly transform thirty-six stones into water, dissolve jade into terraces, liquefy gold into broth, plunge into abysses without getting wet, tread upon blades without injury? The arts of illusion number over nine hundred; when tested, none fail to take effect. Why then refuse to believe that immortality can be attained?

The Way of immortality is slow to mature and fraught with prohibitions. Without transcendent aspiration and resolute talent, one cannot uphold it. Some, wavering in heart and doubtful in mind, abandon it midway, then claim that the Way of immortality and long life is truly unattainable.

The Immortal Scriptures say: Ingest the elixir, guard the One, and you shall match heaven’s span; return the essence, embrace the embryo-breath, and your life shall be without limit. These are the essential words of the supreme Dao.

Even among the gentlemen of the world, none would inwardly betray their heart, outwardly shame their shadow, deceive heaven above, or break their word below. How much less would the true men of old fabricate empty texts, deluding future generations with what cannot be attained? What would they gain?

If one lacks the destiny for it, they will never believe—how can they be forced to do so?"

One might object: "The long lifespans of tortoises and cranes are but empty words of the world—who has ever accompanied these creatures from beginning to end to verify this?"

The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replies: "If one grasps the essentials, then what lies beyond the eight poles is as clear as one’s palm, and the distant past of a hundred generations seems as the present. One need not stand beside them in one’s courtyard or wait to see them with one’s own eyes before knowing. The *Records on Jade Tablets* state: The thousand-year tortoise displays all five colors; two bone-like protrusions rise from its forehead like horns. It understands human speech and floats upon lotus leaves or rests beneath clusters of milfoil, above which white clouds coil like serpents. The thousand-year crane cries in accordance with the seasons and can perch upon trees—those not yet a thousand years old never alight upon branches. Its plumage is pure white, and its brain has turned entirely crimson. Thus, upon seeing them, one may know their age.

Yet creatures that grow old are often wise, and most conceal themselves in deep, secluded places, so few people ever encounter them. According to the *Records on Jade Tablets* and the *Changyu Scripture*, not only do these two creatures possess long life. It is said that the thousand-year pine tree spreads its branches in all four directions, its uppermost tips ceasing to grow. From a distance, it appears like an overturned canopy, and within it dwells a being—sometimes resembling a green ox, sometimes a green sheep, sometimes a green dog, or sometimes a green person—all of which live ten thousand years. It is further said that serpents possess boundless longevity; the macaque lives eight hundred years before transforming into an ape, and the ape lives five hundred years before becoming a *jue*. The *jue* lives a thousand years. The toad lives three thousand years, the *qilin* two thousand. The horse *tenghuang* and the beast *jiguang* each live three thousand years. The thousand-year bird and the ten-thousand-year fowl both have human faces and avian bodies, their lifespans matching their names. Tigers, deer, and hares all live a thousand years; when they reach five hundred, their fur turns white. The bear that lives five hundred years gains the power of transformation. Foxes, raccoon dogs, jackals, and wolves all live eight hundred years; at five hundred, they become adept at assuming human form. The mouse lives three hundred years; at a hundred, its fur turns white, and it becomes skilled at divination by leaning upon humans, known as the *zhong*. It can foreknow a year’s fortunes and events a thousand *li* away. Such examples are too numerous to record in full. Only those with broad knowledge can name things upon encountering them, and only those with extensive learning remain unconfused by such principles."

Must one constantly consort with tortoises and cranes to comprehend such things? If one fails to recognize common creatures, then even the plants in one’s garden, the birds and beasts of field and pond, will remain largely unknown—how much more so the extraordinary? The *Records of the Grand Historian*’s "Treatise on Tortoises and Divination" recounts that a man from the Jiang-Huai region, in his childhood, used a tortoise to prop up his bed. When he later grew old and died, his family moved the bed, and the tortoise was still alive. This tortoise had lived no less than fifty or sixty years without drinking or eating, enduring so long without perishing—how vastly different it was from ordinary creatures! Why then doubt those said to live a thousand years? The *Scriptures of the Immortals* imitate the tortoise’s breath; is this not for good reason?

Thus, Chen Zhonggong of Yingchuan, the venerable magistrate of Taiqiu, a man of profound discourse, compiled his *Records of Strange Tales*, in which he wrote of a man from his commandery, Zhang Guangding, who during a time of chaos often took refuge elsewhere. He had a four-year-old daughter who could not walk long distances, nor could she be carried. Calculating that abandoning her would surely lead to starvation, yet unwilling to leave her bones exposed, he found at the village entrance an ancient, massive burial mound. At its summit was a preexisting hole, so he lowered his daughter into the mound in a vessel, providing her with several months’ worth of dried rice and water before departing. When the world grew peaceful again, three years had passed, and Guangding returned to his hometown. Wishing to retrieve his abandoned daughter’s bones for proper burial, he went to the mound and found her still sitting inside. Seeing her parents, she recognized them and rejoiced. Her parents, at first fearing she was a ghost, descended to her, only to discover she was alive. When asked how she had obtained food, she said that when her provisions ran out, she grew very hungry. Then she saw a creature at the corner of the mound stretching its neck and inhaling qi. She tried imitating it and no longer felt hunger. She did this day and night until the present. The clothes and bedding her parents had left remained in the mound; she had not moved about, so they had not worn out, and she had not suffered from cold. Guangding searched for the creature his daughter described and found it was merely a large tortoise. When the girl emerged and ate grain, she initially suffered abdominal pain and vomiting, but after some time, she grew accustomed to it. This further confirms that tortoises possess the method of not dying, and those who practice the Dao may emulate them, attaining the same longevity as tortoises. Sima Qian and Chen Zhonggong were not men of reckless speech.

The creatures of the world are myriad, yet the ancients singled out these two alone—clearly because they possess unique qualities absent in others. Observing one corner, one may grasp the whole.

Someone objected: “Tortoises can hibernate underground, and cranes can soar in the sky. If a person were to hibernate for even a brief moment or fly for an instant, they could not endure it—how then can their longevity be learned?”

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: “Many creatures can hibernate, and countless birds can fly, yet only the tortoise and crane are said to possess long life. Their immortality does not derive from hibernation or flight. Thus, the perfected ones teach only the methods of *daoyin* to prolong life and the techniques of ingesting qi to abstain from grain—they do not instruct in underground hibernation or celestial flight. Those who attain the Dao can ascend into the clouds or dive into the rivers and seas. Thus, Xiao Shi soared with the phoenix into the void, and Qin Gao rode a crimson carp through the deep abyss—these are the proofs. How could it be merely momentary hibernation or fleeting flight?

Dragons, serpents, jiao, and chi; macaques, gibbons, and crocodiles—all can go through winter without eating, and when they do not eat, they grow even fatter than when they do. Yet none have mastered their methods. Moreover, in matters where a single excellence is achieved, many creatures surpass humans—not only tortoises and cranes. Thus, Taihao learned to weave nets from the spider; Jintian relied on the nine pheasants to regulate the seasons; Emperor Xuan awaited the phoenix’s song to tune the pitch pipes; Tang Yao observed the *mingjia* to know the months; the tortoise knows the past, the magpie the future; the Fish Earl discerns the signs of flood and drought; the mayfly knows the locations of hidden springs; the white wolf foretold the rise of the Yin; the *yuezuo* witnessed the flourishing of the Zhou. That tortoises and cranes alone understand the arts of nourishing life is hardly surprising.

Moreover, the *Scriptures of the Immortals* contain hundreds of methods for attaining long life; they differ only in speed and complexity, not all requiring the emulation of tortoises and cranes. The superior scholar’s thoughts reach far and wide, naturally penetrating the profound—difficult for the vulgar, with their limited perspectives, to fathom the distant aims of the immortals.”

Another asked: “We do not understand the principles of long life in people today—how did the ancients come to know them?”

“This is the narrow talk of the ignorant and dull, not the reflection of the enlightened. The profound principles of celestial patterns, the waxing and waning of the seven luminaries, the retrogradations of the past, the rise and fall of the future—gazing upward to discern the omens in clouds and vapors, looking downward to determine the auspices in hexagrams; deploying the three pieces to decide the fate of armies, divining the nine tokens to know the boundaries of fortune and misfortune; calculating a single sum to fathom the conditions of spirits and gods, weaving the six relations to resolve boundless good and ill. Their roots can be examined, their patterns sought—yet even mediocre talents of limited capacity cannot unlock the mysteries of learning, let alone the unrefined, who merely sharpen their thoughts on dregs and cannot plumb the depths of the subtle.

The crude craft of the chisel and tenon, yet Wheelwright Bian possessed an untransmitted marvel; the humble art of catching cicadas, yet Master Qu Lou achieved divine skill—it lies in the person, in their utmost refinement. How much more so the Dao of immortals, whose meaning is profound and far-reaching? To seek its roots is no easy task. Even the likes of Master Red Pine and Wang Qiao, though they achieved its effects, may not have fully grasped its causes—how much less the common person? The methods can be learned, so the ancients recorded and transmitted them for those of understanding. If one’s heart comprehends and their intent aligns, they may trust and cultivate it. Those who harbor doubt in their breasts do so by their own fate; it is not for them to question why the ancients alone understood this while they themselves do not.”

Now I know that immortality can be attained, that I can cease taking grain and not eat, that I can preserve the flowing pearl and take flight, that the arts of yellow and white can be pursued. Yet if you demand of me their fundamental principles, then in truth I do not know them either.

If the world deems what can be grasped by thought as existent and what lies beyond its reach as nonexistent, then little would remain in all under heaven. Thus Laozi said: one employs the head of a wildcat to treat rat holes, the woodpecker to guard against tooth decay—these too may be sought by analogy. Yet the crab’s transformation of lacquer, the hemp’s spoiling of wine—these cannot be deduced by reason. The myriad things are manifold and chaotic; how can the mind fathom their limits?

Suppose one suffers from a grave and perilous illness, requiring a fine medicine to save them, yet refuses to take it until they know why Shen Nong and Qi Bo chose this herb to treat this ailment—would this not be folly?”

Some may say: “Life and death are fated, longevity and brevity are predetermined; neither herbs nor minerals can alter them.”

A severed finger, once rejoined, cannot be restored; spilled blood, once swallowed, brings no benefit. How much less, then, can consuming strange substances like pine and cypress extend a fleeting lifespan—this is even more absurd.”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity replied: “If your argument holds that only like can benefit like, then a severed finger and spilled blood, being of the same body and not of different kinds, should be reconnectable and drinkable—yet why can the one not be rejoined, nor the other ingested? I have seen many cases where ointment from a snake’s mouth mends a severed finger, or mulberry beans replace the feet of chickens and ducks. The benefits of dissimilar things cannot be denied. If one insists on relying solely on one’s own kind, then should one pound flesh and smelt bone to treat sword wounds, or boil skin and sear hair to cure baldness? Water and soil do not share the same body as the hundred plants, yet the plants depend on them to grow. The five grains are not of the same kind as living humans, yet humans rely on them for life. Fat is not of the fire’s kind, nor water of the fish’s, yet when fat is exhausted, fire dies; when water is gone, fish perish. Cut down a tree, and the mistletoe withers; mow the grass, and dodder wilts. When river crabs do not return, clams spoil; when a mulberry tree is felled, borers perish. Extending this by analogy, the truth becomes clear. Gold and jade placed in the nine orifices render the dead incorruptible. Brine soaking flesh and marrow preserves dried meats from decay. How much more, then, can substances that nourish the body and prolong life, when taken into oneself, fail to grant longevity?”

Some objected: “The methods of immortals in esoteric texts seem plausible yet are false—likely fabricated by meddlesome hands, not authored by the Yellow Emperor or Laozi, nor witnessed by Master Red Pine or Wang Qiao.”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity replied: “If such refined reasoning were true, these arts should yield no results. Yet even in minor trials, none fail to prove effective. I have seen many who, using a *fangzhu* vessel, draw water beneath the evening moon; with a burning mirror, kindle fire from the morning sun; conceal their form to dissolve into nothingness; alter their appearance to become another being; toss a kerchief to the ground and make a rabbit dart away; stitch a red sash with needles and make a serpent slither; cause melons and fruits to ripen in an instant; or summon dragons and fish to frolic in basins and bowls—all as described. According to the *Book of Han*, Luan Da saw Emperor Wu of Han and, when tested, made chess pieces move and clash on their own. The *Book of Later Han* records that Wei Shang could vanish while sitting and reappear while standing, and Zhang Kai could summon clouds and raise mists—all recorded by reliable historians, credible and attested. These arts belong to the domain of immortals; their authenticity is beyond doubt. If minor techniques prove true, why would the Way of longevity be any different?”

Another asked: “If immortality can indeed be attained through learning, one would soar beyond the clouds, abandon the world, and forsake ancestral rites. If the spirits of the departed are aware, will they not go hungry?”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity replied: “I have heard that preserving the body from harm is called perfect filial piety. How much greater, then, is attaining the Dao and achieving long life, outlasting heaven and earth, surpassing mere survival and return to wholeness? Would this not be far superior? If one could ascend the void and tread the light, riding cloud chariots and rainbow canopies, dining on the essence of dawn’s mist and drinking the pure quintessence of the primal chaos, sipping jade nectar and golden elixirs, feasting on jasper mushrooms and vermilion blossoms, dwelling in palaces of jade and chambers of splendor, wandering carefree through the Great Clarity—then the aware spirits of the departed would share in this glory. Some might assist the Five Emperors, others oversee the hundred spirits. Rank would come unbidden, sustenance would be savored like flowering jade, power would command the nether realms, and might would make the pillars of heaven tremble. If one truly follows the Way, who would not grasp its wonders? And who would go hungry? The highest attainment of the Dao was none other than Laozi. Laozi had a son named Zong, who served as a general in Wei and was enfeoffed at Duangan for his merits. Thus, those who study immortality today may still have descendants to continue ancestral rites—why should sacrifices ever cease?”

Another questioned: “Those who attain the Dao master the arts of breath and the essentials of ingestion, hear a thousand miles away with covered ears, and see the future with closed eyes. Some abandon their fine steeds to rein in dragons, others leave the Middle Kingdom to dwell in Penglai or Yingzhou. Some linger among the common folk, wandering freely among people, yet do not sever all ties to ascend to the mysteries. Their aspirations are the same, but their departures and stays differ—why?”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity answered: “I have heard from my teachers that immortals may ascend to heaven or remain on earth, but all achieve long life, choosing their paths according to their preferences. Those who practice the methods of returning elixirs and golden liquors, if they wish to remain in the world, need only consume half the dose and reserve the rest. If they later seek to ascend to heaven, they may take the remainder. Once immortality is assured, there is no fear of sudden demise. Even if one roams the earth or enters famous mountains, what cause for concern remains? Pengzu said that heaven is filled with exalted deities and great spirits, and newly ascended immortals hold lowly ranks, serving many superiors—often laboring arduously. Thus, it is not worth the toil to ascend to heaven, and so he remained in the world for over eight hundred years. He also said that some ancient immortals grew feathers and wings, transforming and taking flight, losing their human form to assume other shapes—like sparrows becoming clams or pheasants becoming giant clams—no longer following the human way. The human way entails eating fine foods, wearing light and warm garments, harmonizing yin and yang, holding office and rank, possessing keen senses, strong bones, and a joyful countenance, aging without decline, prolonging life, moving freely, untouched by cold, heat, wind, or damp, unharmed by ghosts, spirits, or myriad perils, immune to blades and poisons, unburdened by sorrow, joy, slander, or praise—this is true nobility.”

Should one abandon wife and children, dwell alone in mountains and marshes, cut off all human ties, and live like a lump of earth beside trees and stones—this is hardly worthy of praise. The masters of old—Anqi, Longmei Ning, Gong Xiu, and Yin Changsheng—all partook of half a dose of golden elixir. They remained in the world for nearly a thousand years before departing. To speak earnestly, those who seek long life do so precisely to preserve what they desire in this present existence, not out of eagerness to ascend the void or to take flight as superior to remaining on earth. If one could, by good fortune, stay at home and not die, why then seek swift ascent to heaven? If attaining immortality meant no longer abiding by principle, it would be but one matter more. Pengzu’s words merely accommodate human sentiment.

Someone asked, “Should those who cultivate the Dao first establish merit and virtue? Is this correct?”

Baopuzi replied, “Indeed. According to the middle chapter of the *Jade Key Classic*, establishing merit is foremost, while eliminating transgressions comes next. For those who cultivate the Dao, rescuing others from peril, sparing them from disaster, shielding them from illness, and preventing unjust death—these are the highest merits. Those who seek immortality must take loyalty, filial piety, harmony, compliance, benevolence, and trustworthiness as their foundation. If one’s virtue and conduct are not cultivated, and one merely pursues techniques and arts, none will attain long life. For grave misdeeds, the Director of Destiny seizes one’s *ji*; for minor transgressions, one’s *suan* is taken. The severity of the offense determines the extent of the loss. All receive a lifespan with an inherent allotment. Those with a greater original allotment find their *ji* and *suan* difficult to exhaust, thus delaying death. If one’s endowment is meager but one’s transgressions many, then *ji* and *suan* are swiftly spent, and death comes early.

It is further said that to become an earthly immortal, one must perform three hundred good deeds; to become a heavenly immortal, one thousand two hundred. If one has performed one thousand one hundred and ninety-nine good deeds but suddenly commits a single evil act, all prior merits are lost, and one must begin anew. Thus, good need not be great, nor evil small. Even if one commits no evil, should one boast of one’s deeds or demand recompense for almsgiving, that single act of merit is lost—though not all merits are forfeited. It is also said that if one’s accumulation of good deeds is incomplete, even consuming the elixir of immortality will be of no benefit. If one does not take the elixir but performs good deeds, though one may not immediately attain immortality, one can at least avoid the misfortune of sudden death. I further suspect that Pengzu and his ilk lacked sufficient merit, which is why they could not ascend to heaven.”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity states: I have examined and perused texts on nurturing life, gathering methods for prolonging vision, and the scrolls and volumes I have perused number in the thousands. Yet none fail to regard the elixir of return and golden liquor as the paramount essentials. Indeed, these two are the ultimate pinnacle of the Dao of immortality. If one consumes them and does not attain immortality, then immortals have never existed since ancient times.

In earlier years, the central states fell into chaos, and all fled in every direction. I wandered through the regions of Xu, Yu, Jing, Xiang, Jiang, and Guang, encountering hundreds of wandering folk and common Daoist practitioners. Some were those whose names I had long heard, yet who dwelled beyond the clouds and sun. Yet they were all alike in their uniformity; their knowledge and insights, whether deep or shallow, were insufficient to surpass one another. Though each possessed dozens of scrolls, they could not fully comprehend them, merely transcribing and preserving them.

At times, some knew the methods of circulating qi, grain avoidance, and consuming various herbs and woods, and their manuals were largely identical in content. There was not one who did not possess the *Daoji Jing*, regarding it as the utmost secret, claiming it was composed by Lord Xi. I told them: this was written by Wang Tu, a military supervisor of the Wei era—he was no ancient. Wang Tu knew nothing of the great elixir; he merely sought immortality through circulating qi and entering seclusion, composing this *Daoji* and claiming the Dao was complete therein. This is a grave misguidance of others.

When I questioned these Daoists about the affairs of divine elixir and golden liquor, or the methods of the Three Sovereigns’ inner texts for summoning celestial and terrestrial spirits, not one among them knew of them. Yet those who boasted, self-aggrandized, and deceived others, claiming to have long life or to have traveled with immortals, were more than half. Those truly refined in subtleties were exceedingly few.

Some had heard of the golden elixir but did not believe it could be attained in the present age, asserting that only the immortals of highest antiquity had mastered it. Others had obtained teachings from beyond the common realm but not the true scriptures. Some had acquired fragmentary elixir formulas and claimed the elixir method was exhausted therein.

In former times, Zuo Yuanfang of the Han meditated intently upon Mount Tianzhu, where divine beings bestowed upon him the *Scripture of the Golden Elixir of Immortality*. When the Han fell into chaos, he had no leisure to compound it and fled to the lands east of the Yangtze, intending to seek a famous mountain to cultivate this Dao. My great-uncle, the Immortal Lord, received it from Yuanfang. He was transmitted the three scrolls of the *Great Clarity Elixir Scripture*, one scroll of the *Nine Cauldrons Elixir Scripture*, and one scroll of the *Golden Liquor Elixir Scripture*.

My teacher, Master Zheng, was a disciple of my great-uncle the Immortal Lord and received these from him. Yet my family was poor and could not afford to purchase the ingredients. I personally served him, sweeping and attending for a long time, until at last, on Mount Maji, I established an altar, swore an oath, and received them, along with all the oral instructions not recorded in writing. These scriptures were previously unknown in the lands east of the Yangtze; they originated with Zuo Yuanfang, who transmitted them to my great-uncle, who transmitted them to Master Zheng, who transmitted them to me. Thus, no other Daoists knew of them.

Yet I have received these teachings for over twenty years now, and though I lack even a picul of resources, I can do nothing but sigh deeply. Those who have amassed gold filling chests and wealth piled like mountains are unaware of this method of deathlessness. Even if they were to hear of it, not one in ten thousand would believe. How could it be otherwise?

One who drinks from the Jade Terrace knows the insipidity of water-plantain broth; one who gazes upon Kunlun perceives the utmost baseness of anthills. Having beheld the Way of the golden elixir, one no longer desires to regard minor manuals. Yet the great elixir is difficult to obtain at once, and one must for now rely on lesser methods to sustain oneself. Though consuming myriad doses of other medicines may yield slight benefit, they can never grant one enduring longevity.

Thus, the oral instructions of Laozi state: “If you do not obtain the elixir of return or golden liquor, you labor in vain.” The five grains can still sustain life—one who obtains them lives, and one who is deprived of them dies. How much more so the divine medicines of the highest grade? Their benefit to humanity is ten thousand times that of the five grains.

The golden elixir, as a substance, grows more wondrous in transformation the longer it is refined. Gold enters fire and, though smelted a hundred times, does not diminish; buried, it does not decay for all eternity. Consuming these two substances refines the human body, enabling one to avoid aging and death. This is akin to borrowing from external things to strengthen oneself—like fat nourishing a flame that cannot be extinguished, or verdigris applied to the feet to prevent decay in water, which is to borrow the strength of copper to protect the flesh. When the golden elixir enters the body, it permeates and nourishes the constructive and protective qi, far surpassing the external application of verdigris.

Most in the world do not believe in the supreme Dao, and all such skeptics are the same. Yet if by chance there is one who takes interest but does not encounter this method or meet an enlightened teacher, they will never know of this wondrous affair in the world. I now briefly excerpt the essentials of the golden elixir to show those of later generations who share this aspiration. Seek it diligently, and do not cling to shallow and common methods, believing them sufficient to transcend the world. Those who ultimately do not encounter it should simply rest their hopes in boundless possibility.

When one envisions its teachings, one will surely know the difference between emerging from a stagnant puddle to float upon the vast sea, turning from a firefly’s glow to face the sun and moon, hearing thunder and recognizing the crudeness of a cloth drum, or seeing a giant whale and knowing the minuteness of a tiny shrimp. As for those who chatter aimlessly, lacking any foundation, and seek to ascend through inferior medicines—how is this different from spurring a lame donkey to chase the swift wind or paddling a small boat to cross a great river?

There are many minor elixir formulas, yet their preparation varies in depth, and thus their potency differs. Though some are superior and others inferior, they do not compare—just as a single brew of wine cannot match the richness of nine fermentations. Yet even the lesser of the minor elixirs far surpasses the finest of herbal medicines.

All common herbs and trees turn to ash when burned, yet cinnabar transforms into mercury, and through repeated refinement, reverts to cinnabar—how far removed it is from ordinary plants and trees! Thus it grants long life, and only the immortals have grasped this principle. How infinitely distant they are from the vulgar! Most people know little and find much strange. Some, unaware that mercury comes from cinnabar, refuse to believe it when told, saying, "Cinnabar is red—how could it become this white substance?" Others argue, "Cinnabar is but a stone; all stones turn to ash when burned—why should cinnabar alone behave differently?" If such simple matters remain incomprehensible, is it any wonder they laugh at the grand teachings of the Dao, dismissing them as absurd?

The ancient perfected ones, pitying those of later generations who might yet be taught, devised methods with painstaking care, hoping to deliver them from the calamity of death. Their words were truly profound. Yet the vulgar persist in disbelief, dismissing them as empty rhetoric. If these were mere empty words, how could the nine transformations and nine refinements, completed over days of labor, all proceed exactly as prescribed? The perfected ones’ understanding of this cannot be sought through common or superficial thought.

In my youth, I was fond of esoteric arts and traveled far to seek instruction, undeterred by peril or distance. Each strange revelation brought me joy. Though mocked and ridiculed, I felt no sorrow. Who can say that those who come after will not surpass the present? Thus I write this to share with those who recognize its truth—not to indulge in the bizarre or embellish with hollow words, nor to seek fame or curry favor with the masses.

The blazing sun cannot revive withered wood; the wisest cannot enlighten the foolish. Books are passed down for the enlightened; affairs are valued by the discerning. A farmer wielding a vermilion bow to scare birds, or a southern barbarian donning imperial robes to gather firewood—what can the ignorant be forced to understand?

People today eat their fill all day yet seldom devote themselves to the studies of the scholars or the cultivation of virtue. Instead, they idle away their time in leisure, their pursuits confined to fame or profit. Some chase game across the plains; others linger over wine, lost in revelry. Some drown in beauty and music; others squander their days in silks and brocades. Some strain their sinews with the bow; others waste their efforts on gambling. They hear the words of the supreme Dao as if drunk, or doze through its discourses. Neglecting the care of their bodies, they hasten toward death, unwilling to seek the methods of nurturing life. Instead, they carve away at themselves, sear and scorch, wear themselves ragged, and drain themselves dry.

Those who possess the Dao treasure and guard their knowledge, making no demands of others—why would they force such teachings upon the unwilling? The common saying goes: if long life were attainable, the wealthy and noble of ancient times would have achieved it. Since none did, such a path must not exist. Yet they fail to realize that the wealthy and noble of old were no different from those of today—all disbelieved, all failed to seek, all prioritized their immediate desires. How could they have attained it?

Even if one cannot fully commit to the belief that destiny can be extended and immortality achieved, why not at least test it? If a small trial yields results, granting even two or three hundred years of life, is that not far better than the early demise of ordinary men?

The affairs of the world are myriad, yet the arts of the Dao are harder to clarify than any other. How can one, with a mind of middling talent, assert that there is no path to long life in this world? If the mere fact that most people disbelieve is taken as proof of its nonexistence, then how can there be so many wise among them? If those who grasp the essence of the Dao and still pursue it must necessarily be the most foolish, then are they all inferior to the common man?

Others fear that if they seek long life and fail, they will be mocked as deluded fools. But if one’s judgment errs even slightly, and the path to deathlessness does indeed exist in this world, will they not then be laughed at by those who have attained it? The sun and moon cannot illuminate all things—how can the human heart claim absolute certainty?

The *Baopuzi* states: According to the *Scripture of the Yellow Emperor’s Nine Cauldrons Divine Elixir*, the Yellow Emperor consumed it and ascended as an immortal. It further says that while breath control, Daoist exercises, and herbal medicines may extend life, they cannot prevent death. Only the divine elixir grants boundless longevity, uniting one’s years with heaven and earth, riding clouds and driving dragons through the Great Clarity.

The Yellow Emperor transmitted this to Master Xuan, charging him: "This Dao is of utmost importance. It must be passed only to the worthy. If one is unfit, even if they piled jade as high as a mountain, do not reveal this Dao to them." The recipient would cast a golden figurine and golden fish into the eastern flowing waters as a pledge, swearing a blood oath. Without the bone of an immortal, one cannot even glimpse this path.

The elixir must be compounded in a renowned mountain, in a place devoid of people, with no more than three companions. First, one must fast for a hundred days, bathe in five fragrant waters, and maintain utmost purity, avoiding filth and contact with the vulgar. One must not let unbelievers know, for their slander will prevent the elixir’s completion. Once achieved, it can elevate an entire household to immortality, not just a single person.

Yet people today reject the divine elixir and trust in herbal medicines. Herbs and plants rot when buried, soften when boiled, and char when burned—they cannot even sustain themselves. How could they sustain a person? The Nine Elixirs are the essence of long life, not meant for the ears of ordinary men. The teeming masses, obsessed only with wealth and status—are they not mere walking corpses?

When compounding the elixir, one must also perform sacrifices, following a prescribed diagram in a separate scroll. The first elixir is called *Dan Hua*.

First, prepare *Xuanhuang* using realgar water, alum water, halite, brine salt, arsenolite, oyster shell, red halloysite, talc, and lead white—each in quantities of several dozen *jin*—to form the *Six-One Clay*. Fire it for thirty-six days, and upon consuming it for seven days, one ascends as an immortal. Then, knead this elixir with *Xuangao* and place it over a fierce flame; in an instant, it transforms into yellow gold. Alternatively, combine 240 *zhu* with a hundred *jin* of mercury and fire it, which also yields yellow gold. The formation of gold signifies the elixir’s completion. If the gold fails to form, reseal the elixir and fire it again for the same number of days—it will invariably succeed.

The second elixir is called *Shendan*, also known as *Divine Talisman*. Consuming it for a hundred days grants immortality. With this elixir applied to the soles of one’s feet, one can traverse water and fire. Taking three *daogui* of it dissolves the Three Corpses and Nine Worms, curing all ailments.

The third elixir is also named *Shendan*. Consuming one *daogui* grants immortality in a hundred days. If fed to livestock, they too shall never die. It also repels the Five Weapons. After a hundred days of ingestion, immortal youths and jade maidens, along with mountain and river spirits, will attend to one, appearing in human form.

The fourth elixir is called *Huandan*. Consuming one *daogui* grants immortality in a hundred days. Vermilion birds and phoenixes will hover above, and jade maidens will draw near. When one *daogui* is combined with a *jin* of mercury and fired, it instantly becomes yellow gold. Applying this elixir to money or goods ensures their return by day’s end. Inscribing it upon the brows of ordinary people causes all ghosts to flee.

The fifth elixir is named *Erdan*. Consuming it for thirty days grants immortality. Ghosts and spirits will attend, and jade maidens will appear before one.

The sixth elixir is called *Liandan*. Consuming it for ten days grants immortality. When combined with mercury and fired, it too becomes yellow gold.

The seventh elixir is named *Roudan*. Consuming one *daogui* grants immortality in a hundred days. Mixed with juice from the broken basin plant and ingested, even a ninety-year-old man may father a child. When combined with *Jingong* and fired, it becomes yellow gold.

The eighth elixir is called *Fudan*. Consuming it grants immortality on the same day. Carrying a piece the size of a jujube pit repels all ghosts. Inscribing it upon doors and gates prevents all malevolent spirits, thieves, tigers, and wolves from approaching.

The ninth elixir is named *Handan*. Consuming one *daogui* grants immortality in a hundred days. Immortal boys and girls will attend, and one may soar lightly through the air without wings.

Of these Nine Elixirs, obtaining even one is sufficient for immortality; there is no need to prepare them all. The choice depends on one’s preference. Those who consume the Nine Elixirs may ascend to heaven at will or remain in the mortal realm as they please, moving freely between both without hindrance or harm.

*Baopuzi* says: There is also the *Taiqing Divine Elixir*, whose method originates from the Primordial Lord. The Primordial Lord was the teacher of Laozi.
