The *Taiqing Observing Heaven Scripture* comprises nine scrolls, of which the upper three cannot be taught, the middle three have no worthy inheritors in the world and are thus submerged beneath the Three Springs, while the lower three constitute the *Elixir Scripture*—upper, middle, and lower volumes, totaling three scrolls. The Primordial Lord was a great immortal who could harmonize yin and yang, command ghosts and spirits, summon wind and rain, and ride a chariot drawn by nine dragons and twelve white tigers. All immortals under heaven were subject to him, yet he himself claimed that his attainments arose from studying the Dao and ingesting elixirs, not from natural endowment. How much more so for ordinary people?

The scripture states: The superior person who attains the Dao ascends as a celestial official; the middling person dwells upon Kunlun; the inferior person enjoys longevity in the mortal world. Foolish folk, refusing to believe, dismiss these as empty words. From dawn to dusk, they engage only in pursuits that hasten death, never seeking life—how could heaven force life upon them? Most people know only fine food, fine clothing, music, beauty, wealth, and status, indulging their desires to the fullest before perishing in an instant. Take care never to reveal the divine elixir to them, lest they mock the Dao and slander the truth. Transmitting the elixir scripture to the unworthy invites misfortune. If one encounters a person of sincere faith, one may share the completed elixir with them, but never lightly impart its formula.

What need has one who knows this Dao for kings and marquises? Once the divine elixir is completed, it not only grants longevity but can also produce yellow gold. Upon its formation, take a hundred *jin* of gold and first perform a grand sacrifice. The sacrificial method is detailed in a separate scroll, distinct from that of the Nine Cauldrons. The gold should be apportioned and labeled as follows: twenty *jin* for Heaven, five *jin* each for the Sun and Moon, eight *jin* for the Northern Dipper, eight *jin* for Taiyi, five *jin* for the Well, five *jin* for the Stove, twelve *jin* for the River Earl, five *jin* for the Earth Altar, and five *jin* each for the gate, door, lane spirits, and the Pure Lord—totaling eighty-eight *jin*. The remaining twelve *jin* should be placed in a fine leather pouch. On an auspicious day, during the bustling market hour, silently abandon it in a crowded place and depart without looking back. Only after using the initial hundred *jin* may one freely use the rest. Failing to first offer gold to the spirits will invite calamity.

It is further said: The Dao of longevity does not lie in sacrifices or serving ghosts and spirits, nor in breathing exercises or stretching. The essence of ascending as an immortal lies in the divine elixir. Knowing it is not easy; practicing it is truly difficult. If you can prepare it, you may endure forever.

In recent times, during the late Han, Yin Jun of Xinye compounded this *Taiqing Elixir* and attained immortality. He was originally a Confucian scholar, gifted in thought, skilled in composing poetry, and in writing odes and prefaces to elixir scriptures. He recounted his early studies of the Dao, his apprenticeship, and his personal knowledge of over forty individuals who achieved immortality—all described with great clarity. While preparing this *Taiqing Elixir* is somewhat more difficult than the Nine Cauldrons, it remains the supreme method for ascending to heaven in broad daylight.

To compound this elixir, one must first prepare the *Flower Pool*, *Red Salt*, *Gen Snow*, *Mysterious White*, and *Flying Talisman*, along with the *Three-Five Divine Water*, before kindling the fire.

The elixir refined once, when ingested, grants immortality in three years.
The elixir refined twice, when ingested, grants immortality in two years.
The elixir refined thrice, when ingested, grants immortality in one year.
The elixir refined four times, when ingested, grants immortality in half a year.
The elixir refined five times, when ingested, grants immortality in a hundred days.
The elixir refined six times, when ingested, grants immortality in forty days.
The elixir refined seven times, when ingested, grants immortality in thirty days.
The elixir refined eight times, when ingested, grants immortality in ten days.
The elixir refined nine times, when ingested, grants immortality in three days.

To refine the nine-times-cycled elixir, place it within the divine crucible. After the Summer Solstice, heat the crucible until it grows hot, then place one *jin* of cinnabar beneath its lid. Wait attentively for the essence of the sun to illuminate it. In an instant, all will rise in brilliance, radiant with divine light of five colors, transforming into the *Returning Elixir*. Ingesting a *daogui* of it will grant ascension to heaven in broad daylight.

The nine-times-cycled elixir is sealed and coated within an earthen cauldron, refined with chaff fire—first gentle, then vigorous. Each cycle, from the first to the ninth, varies in duration. The fewer the cycles, the weaker the elixir’s power, thus requiring more days of ingestion and delaying immortality. The greater the cycles, the stronger the elixir’s power, thus requiring fewer days of ingestion and hastening immortality.

There is also the *Nine Radiance Elixir*, distinct in method from the nine-times-cycled elixir, though largely similar. Its preparation involves combining various ingredients with fire to refine the *Five Stones*: cinnabar, realgar, arsenolite, azurite, and magnetite. Each stone is cycled five times, yielding five colors per stone—twenty-five colors in total, each weighing one *liang* and stored in separate vessels.

To revive the dead who have not been deceased for three days, take a *daogui* of the green elixir, mix it with water, bathe the corpse, and place another *daogui* into its mouth—the dead will immediately revive.

To summon provisions, take the black elixir, mix it with water, and anoint your left hand. Whatever you desire, as you speak it, will manifest, granting access to all things under heaven.

To become invisible, foreknow future events, or attain ageless longevity, ingest a *daogui* of the yellow elixir—you will immediately achieve eternal life. You may also sit and perceive events a thousand *li* away, knowing fortune and misfortune as if they were before your eyes. You will comprehend the destined prosperity, decline, longevity, or early death of all, as well as their wealth or poverty—all methods are recorded in the middle scroll of the *Taiqing Scripture*.

The *Baopuzi* states: There is also the *Five Spirits Elixir Scripture* in one scroll, containing five methods. It employs cinnabar, realgar, orpiment, sulfur, azurite, alum, magnetite, halite, *Taiyi’s Remnant Grain*, and *Six-One Mud*, along with a divine chamber and sacrificial rites, to compound the elixir over thirty-six days. It also uses the *Five Emperors’ Talismans*, inscribed in five colors, which can grant immortality, though not as effectively as the *Taiqing* or *Nine Cauldrons* elixirs.

There is also the *Min Mountain Elixir* method, discovered by the Daoist Zhang Gai, who refined his thoughts in a stone chamber on Min Mountain. Its method involves smelting bronze to craft a *fangzhu* vessel, which collects the water of the moon. Mercury is then used to cover it, drawing the essence of the sun’s fire within. Long-term ingestion grants immortality. Placing this elixir in a realgar and copper crucible, covering it with mercury and exposing it to the sun for twenty days, then refining it and ingesting a dose the size of a small bean with well-water at dawn, will restore sight to the blind, cure all ailments, reverse white hair to black, and regrow fallen teeth.

There is also the *Master Wucheng’s Elixir* method, which uses *Ba sand* mercury placed in an eight-inch copper tray. A clay furnace holds charcoal, with the tray supported by three earthen mounds. Sulfur water is poured over it, keeping it mud-like. Ingesting it for a hundred days grants immortality.

There is also the *Master Xianmen’s Elixir* method: mix one *jin* of elixir with three *sheng* of wine, expose it to the sun for forty days, and ingest it. After one day, the three worms and all diseases will be expelled. After three years of ingestion, the Dao of immortality is perfected, and two jade maidens will attend you, capable of summoning provisions. This elixir can also suppress all ghosts, as well as the malevolent influences of the dead from the four directions or construction disturbances—hang it facing the source of harm to avert misfortune.

There is also the *Instant Elixir*, which has nine variants, resembling the *Nine Cauldrons* but inferior in efficacy. Its essential method states: take orpiment and realgar, burn them to extract their essence, and cast them into a vessel. Cover it with aged bitter wine for three years, and after a hundred days, the vessel will sprout red nodules, several *fen* long, or five-colored stalactites. Refine and ingest them to attain longevity. They may also be mixed with dodder—the root of its initial growth, resembling a rabbit. Extract its blood, blend it with this elixir, and ingest it to achieve instantaneous transformation, enabling any desired feat.

It may also be mixed with *crimson grass*: ingesting a single dose allows you to ride the void and travel upon clouds. *Crimson grass* resembles a small jujube, growing three to four *chi* tall, with red branches and leaves. Its stem resembles coral, thriving beneath cliffs in famous mountains. When cut, its sap flows like blood. Casting jade, the eight stones, gold, or silver into it will immediately soften them like mud, eventually dissolving into liquid. Gold cast into it becomes *Golden Nectar*; jade cast into it becomes *Jade Wine*. Ingesting either grants longevity.

There is also a method for obtaining the *Hidden Elixir*: among the world’s waters, some are named after cinnabar, such as the *Cinnabar Water* of Nanyang. Within them dwell cinnabar fish. Ten days before the Summer Solstice, observe them at night—the cinnabar fish will float near the water’s edge, their red glow illuminating upward like fire. Cast a net to catch them, but do not take all. Slice their blood and anoint your soles to walk upon water and dwell indefinitely in the depths.

The *Red Pine Master’s Cinnabar Method*: Take the juice of thousand-year-old *lü* vines and the juice of *fan* peaches, steep cinnabar in them, and place the mixture in a non-leaching vessel. Seal the mouth with refined honey, bury it three feet underground, and after a hundred days, press the red fruit of the *ning* tree to extract its juice. Mix and ingest it, and one’s face, eyes, temples, and hair will all turn red, granting longevity. Of old, was not the *Red-Bearded Master* among the *Central Yellow Immortals* one who had consumed this?

The *Stone Master’s Cinnabar Method*: Take fledgling crows before their feathers grow, feed them with true cinnabar mixed into beef, and when fully grown, their feathers will all turn red. Then slaughter them, dry them in the shade for a hundred days, and grind them—feathers and all—into powder. Ingest one *daogui* measure, and after a hundred days, one gains a lifespan of five hundred years.

The *Kang Fengzi Cinnabar Method*: Use the blood of sheep, crows, cranes, and sparrows, combine it with the juice of *Shaoshi* aconite, and mix it with cinnabar inside a pigeon egg. Seal it with lacquer, place it in mica-infused water, and after a hundred days, it transforms into *Red Water*. Ingesting one *ge* measure extends life by a hundred years; ingesting one *sheng* grants a thousand years.

The *Cui Wenzi Cinnabar Method*: Place cinnabar inside a duck’s belly and steam it. Ingesting it extends years; long-term consumption grants immortality.

The *Liu Yuan Cinnabar Method*: Place cinnabar inside *Mysterious Water* liquid for a hundred days until it turns purple. When grasped, it does not stain the hands. Mix it with mica-infused water, seal it in a bamboo tube, and cast it into a well. After a hundred days, it transforms into *Red Water*. Ingesting one *ge* measure grants a hundred years; long-term consumption grants longevity.

The *Le Zichang Cinnabar Method*: Combine malachite, lead cinnabar, mercury, and cinnabar, place them in a bronze cylinder, and seal it with dry clay and talc. Steam it in white sand for eighty days. Ingest a dose the size of a small bean, and after three years, one becomes an immortal.

The *Li Wen Cinnabar Method*: Wrap cinnabar in white silk and boil it in bamboo juice, calling it *Red Spring*. Then steam it over boiling water, mix it with *Mysterious Water*, and ingest one *ge* measure. After a year, one becomes an immortal.

The *Yinzi Cinnabar Method*: Mix cinnabar with mica-infused water and seal it tightly. Place it in the *Golden Flower Pool* for a year, then retrieve it. Ingest one *daogui* measure; consuming a full *jin* grants five hundred years.

The *Great Unity’s Summoning Soul Cinnabar Method*: The five minerals used, sealed with *Six-One Mud*, resemble the *Nine Elixirs*. It excels at reviving those who have died suddenly within three days. Break their teeth, insert one pill along with a sulfur pill, and send both down with water. Once it reaches the throat, the person revives, all claiming to have seen messengers bearing tallies summoning them.

The *Cai Nü Cinnabar Method*: Mix rabbit blood with cinnabar and honey, steam it for a hundred days, then ingest a pill the size of a *wutong* seed, three times daily. After a hundred days, two divine maidens will attend you, ready to be commanded.

The *Ji Qiuzi Cinnabar Method*: Combine pure wine, sesame oil, *Hundred-Flower Wine*, *Dragon Ointment*, and seal it with *Six-One Mud*. Smolder it with chaff fire for ten days until complete. Ingest one pill the size of a small bean; consuming the entire dose grants a lifespan of five hundred years.

The *Mozi Cinnabar Method*: Use mercury and the liquid of the five minerals in a bronze vessel, heat it with fire, and stir with an iron spoon. After ten days, it reverts to cinnabar. Ingest one *daogui* measure, and all diseases leave the body; long-term consumption grants immortality.

The *Zhang Zihe Cinnabar Method*: Combine lead, mercury, malachite, and water, seal it, and steam it in red millet for eighty days until complete. Mix with jujube paste to form pills. Ingest a dose the size of a soybean; after a hundred days, one gains a lifespan of five hundred years.

The *Qili Cinnabar Method*: First refine and extract the *Jade Dust* of the five minerals, combine it with cinnabar and mercury, and boil it in a large bronze vessel for a hundred days until it turns five colors. Ingesting it grants immortality. Take a hundred *jin* of lead and a hundred *daogui* of the elixir, combine them with fire to produce white silver. Mix with realgar-infused water and heat it for a hundred days to produce yellow gold. If the gold is too hard, boil it in lard; if too soft, boil it in white plum juice.

The *Jade Pillar Cinnabar Method*: Mix cinnabar with *Flower Pool* liquid, cover it with powdered malachite and sulfur, place it in a cylinder, and steam it in sand for fifty days. Ingest it for a hundred days, and the *Jade Maidens*—the *Six Jia* and *Six Ding* divine women—will attend you, ready to be commanded, and you will know all affairs under heaven.

The *Elbow-Behind Cinnabar Method*: Mix cinnabar with *Golden Flower* liquid, seal it in dry clay, and steam it for eighty days. Take a dose the size of a small bean, place it on a plate, and face the sun. Its light will rise to meet the sun. Ingest a dose the size of a small bean to attain longevity. Cast it into *Danyang* copper and refine it with fire to produce gold.

The *Li Gong Cinnabar Method*: Take one *sheng* each of true cinnabar and the water of the five minerals, mix them into a paste, and heat them in a cauldron for thirty-six days. Remove it, mix with liquid sulfur, and ingest it. After ten years, one’s lifespan aligns with heaven and earth.

The *Liu Sheng Cinnabar Method*: Mix the juice of white chrysanthemums, *dichu*, and *chu* with cinnabar, steam it for thirty days, then grind and ingest it. After a year, one gains five hundred years. An old man who ingests it becomes youthful beyond recognition; a young man who ingests it never ages.

The *Wang Jun Cinnabar Method*: Place *Ba* sand and mercury inside a chicken egg, seal it with lacquer, and have a hen brood over three eggs. Ingest it on a *Wangxiang* day to remain ageless. Children must not ingest it, or they will never grow. If fed to newborn chicks or puppies, they will never grow larger; the same holds true for birds and beasts.

The *Chen Sheng Cinnabar Method*: Mix white honey with cinnabar, seal it in a bronze vessel, and submerge it in a well for a full year. Ingest it for a year to be free from hunger; consuming a full *jin* grants a lifespan of a hundred years.

The *Han Zhong Cinnabar Method*: Boil cinnabar with lacquer and honey. Ingesting it extends years and prolongs vision; one will cast no shadow at midday.

Beyond these, there are still dozens of methods, which cannot all be discussed.

*Baopuzi* says: The *Golden Nectar* is what the *Great Unity* consumed to attain immortality, and it is no less potent than the *Nine Elixirs*. To compound it, use an ancient scale to weigh one *jin* of yellow gold, along with *Mysterious Bright Dragon Ointment*, *Great Unity’s First-Stone-of-the-Decade*, *Ice Stone*, *Purple Wandering Maiden*, *Mysterious Water* liquid, *Gold-Transforming Stone*, and cinnabar. Seal them to form a liquid. The classic states: "When *Golden Nectar* enters the mouth, one’s body becomes entirely golden."

Laozi received this from the Primordial Lord, who declared: "This Way is of utmost gravity—emerging but once in a hundred generations. It is concealed within a stone chamber. To compound it, one must fast and purify for a hundred days, avoiding all contact with the profane. Build a secluded retreat beside a mountain stream flowing east; after a hundred days, the elixir will be complete. Ingesting a single *liang* grants immortality. Should one not yet wish to depart the world but remain as an earthbound or aquatic immortal, a hundred days of fasting and purification suffice. To ascend to heaven, one must first abstain from grains for a year before ingesting the elixir. Consuming half a *liang* ensures longevity and deathlessness—no harm or poison can assail one, and one may keep a wife and children, hold office, and pursue desires without restraint. To ascend further, fast and purify again, then ingest another *liang* to become a flying immortal.

The *Golden Nectar* is the supreme method of *Wei Xi* and *Ju Sheng*. Combine *Golden Nectar* with mercury and boil them together for thirty days. Remove the mixture, store it in a yellow clay jar, seal it with *Six-One Mud*, and heat it over a fierce fire for sixty hours until it transforms into cinnabar. Ingesting a dose the size of a small bean grants immortality. A *dao* of this cinnabar powder mixed with a *jin* of mercury instantly becomes silver. Taking a *jin* of this cinnabar and fanning it over fire melts it into flowing crimson gold, called *Dan Gold*. Coating blades with this gold repels weapons for ten thousand *li*. Crafting plates and bowls from *Dan Gold* and eating from them bestows longevity. Collecting the essence of the sun and moon with it—like gathering water with a *fang zhu*—and drinking it ensures deathlessness. Blending *Golden Nectar* with yellow earth and placing it in a *Six-One Mud* jar, then heating it over a fierce fire, transforms it entirely into gold, which may be used. Reheating this gold over fire reduces it back to cinnabar; ingesting a dose the size of a small bean allows one to wander immortal through famous mountains and great rivers. A *dao* of this cinnabar powder mixed with mercury instantly becomes silver; combining an *ounce* of this silver with a *jin* of lead yields pure silver.

The *Golden Nectar Classic* states: Cast eight *liang* of gold into an eastward-flowing stream, swear an oath in blood, and recite the oral formula. Those who steal the method and attempt it without following the original procedure will never succeed. Only those of perfect sincerity may be given the elixir; the text must never be lightly transmitted, for both giver and receiver will suffer calamity. The celestial spirits observe mortals closely—though mortals remain unaware.

*Baopuzi* says: The *Nine Elixirs* are indeed the highest method of immortality, yet their compounding requires a multitude of rare ingredients. If the realm is at peace and trade flows freely, these may be procured. But if the nine domains are divided, the materials will be unattainable.

Moreover, one must tend the fire day and night for several dozen days, carefully regulating its intensity to avoid any lapse in precision. The labor is arduous and exacting—far more difficult than compounding the *Golden Nectar*. The sole challenge in preparing the *Golden Nectar* lies in obtaining gold. By the ancient measure, one *jin* of gold would now weigh two *jin*, costing roughly thirty thousand cash. The other ingredients are comparatively easy to procure. No fire-tending is required; one simply immerses the gold in the *Flower Pool*, and after the prescribed number of days, it transforms. The total cost for one dose amounts to forty thousand cash, sufficient to confer immortality upon eight individuals. Yet if the compound is even slightly deficient, its *qi* will lack the power to effect transmutation—akin to brewing wine from a mere few *sheng* of rice, which will never ferment.

*Baopuzi* states: Next in efficacy is the method of ingesting yellow gold, though it falls short of the *Golden Nectar*, it far surpasses all other elixirs. Some refine it with pig’s back fat and wine, others treat it with *chun* bark, or dissolve it in magnet-infused *jing* wine. Some can be drawn into a cloth, while others are rendered into liquid for immediate consumption. Each variant carries its own prohibitions, none equaling the *Golden Nectar*. Some combine it with realgar or orpiment, stretching it like hide—these are but methods for earthbound immortality. Silver and large pearls from mussels may also be transmuted into liquid for ingestion. However, these require prolonged, uninterrupted consumption, and thus remain inferior to the *Golden Nectar*.

*Baopuzi* says: Compounding the *Golden Nectar* and *Nine Elixirs* demands wealth, seclusion in sacred mountains, and the renunciation of worldly affairs. Few can undertake it, and among millions, only one may chance upon the scripture. Thus, most Daoist texts omit the subject entirely. The foremost prohibition: never allow skeptics who scorn the Dao to slander or disparage the work, for it will surely fail. Master Zheng explained that preparing these great elixirs requires ritual offerings, through which the Supreme Unity Primordial Sovereign, Lord Lao, and the Dark Lady all descend to witness. If the alchemist does not retreat to a secluded, remote place, allowing vulgar fools to observe or hear of the process, the deities will rebuke the practitioner for failing to uphold the scriptural precepts. Such transgressions invite the scorn of the profane, causing the gods to withdraw their protection, allowing malevolent *qi* to intrude and the elixir to fail. One must enter the depths of a sacred mountain, observe a hundred days of purification, abstain from the five pungent vegetables and raw fish, and avoid all contact with the profane. Only then may the great elixir be prepared. The fast must be maintained until the work is complete—not merely at its inception.

Master Zheng relayed the words of Lord Zuo: No minor mountain is suitable for compounding the *Golden Nectar* or divine elixirs. Lesser peaks lack a true presiding deity and are instead inhabited by the spirits of trees and stones, millennia-old entities, and blood-consuming ghosts. These beings are all of perverse *qi*, indifferent to bestowing blessings and eager only to wreak havoc. They delight in testing Daoists, who must shield themselves with arts and bring disciples for protection—yet even then, the elixir may be ruined. Even physicians today, when preparing fine medicines or salves, take care to keep them hidden from chickens, dogs, children, and women. If such things defile the work, its efficacy is lost.

Moreover, dyers and dyed fabrics lose their vibrant hues when seen by those with malevolent eyes—how much more so for the great medicines of divine transcendence? Thus, the Daoists of antiquity, when compounding sacred elixirs, invariably entered renowned mountains, never settling for mere ordinary peaks, precisely for this reason.

According to the immortal scriptures, the mountains where one may concentrate the mind to refine transcendent medicines include: Mount Hua, Mount Tai, Mount Huo, Mount Heng, Mount Song, Mount Shaoshi, Mount Chang, Mount Taibai, Mount Zhongnan, Mount Nüji, Mount Difei, Mount Wangwu, Mount Baodu, Mount Anqiu, Mount Qian, Mount Qingcheng, Mount Emei, Mount Rui, Mount Yuntai, Mount Luofu, Mount Yangjia, Mount Huangjin, Mount Biezu, the greater and lesser Mounts Tiantai, Mount Siwang, Mount Gaizhu, and Mount Kuocang. These are all mountains where true presiding deities reside, and where earthbound immortals may dwell. Upon their summits grow *zhi* herbs, which can avert great wars and calamities—not merely for compounding medicines. If one of the Dao ascends such a mountain, the mountain’s spirit will surely aid in bestowing fortune, and the elixir will surely succeed. If one cannot ascend these mountains, the great islands of the sea may also serve for compounding medicines. These include Dongwengzhou and Danzhou, Zhuyu in Kuaiji, as well as Xinguanzhou, Taiguangzhou, and Yuzhou off Xu Province—though these are of secondary merit.

Now, as the renowned mountains of the Central Plains are inaccessible, among the notable mountains of Jiangdong where one may reside are Mount Huo in Jin’an; Mount Chang and Mount Taibai in Dongyang; and Mount Siwang, the greater and lesser Mounts Tiantai, Mount Gaizhu, and Mount Kuocang, all in Kuaiji.

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: Though I am the descendant of great ministers, my talents are insufficient to govern the state or order the world. Yet among those of my generation whom I have known, many have soared to the clouds or shone like stars in the heavens. The reason I have severed all mourning and felicitations among my kinsmen and abandoned the glory of the present age is that I must ascend the famed mountains to complete the books I have written, and next, to compound the divine elixirs and pursue the Dao of longevity. The common folk all find it strange that I have forsaken my homeland, turned my back on the clear path of office, and instead till the soil in the forests and marshes, my hands and feet calloused—calling me afflicted with madness. Yet the Dao and worldly affairs cannot flourish together. If I did not set aside the concerns of the human realm, how could I cultivate such aspirations? When one’s vision is truly clear and one’s resolve truly firm, what fear is there of slander or praise? How could one be swayed by encouragement or discouragement? I write these words to reveal my heart to those of like mind who may come after.

Those who later share this unbreakable bond will abandon the same things I have.

**Minor Divine Elixir Formula:**
Take three *jin* of true cinnabar and six *jin* of white honey, mix them together, and dry them in the sun daily until they can be formed into pills. Take ten pills the size of hemp seeds each morning. Within a year, white hair will turn black, fallen teeth will regrow, and the body will become moist and lustrous. With prolonged use, an old man will become a youth, and one will attain longevity without death.

**Minor Cinnabar Method:**
Take one *jin* of cinnabar, grind and sift it, then add three *sheng* of pure bitter wine and two *sheng* of lacquer. Combine the three ingredients until well blended, then gently heat until they can be formed into pills. Take three pills the size of hemp seeds twice daily. After thirty days, the hundred diseases of the abdomen will be cured, and the Three Corpses will depart. After a hundred days, the flesh and bones will become strong and firm. After a thousand days, the Director of Destinies will erase one’s name from the register of death, and one will endure as long as heaven and earth, gazing upon the sun and moon. One’s form and countenance will transform, and one’s changes will be boundless. At midday, one will cast no shadow, for one will possess a light of one’s own.

**Method for Ingesting Gold:**
Refine gold by repeatedly immersing it in clear wine, approximately two hundred times, until it bubbles upon entry and exit. Knead it until it flows between the fingers like mud. If it does not bubble or cannot be kneaded thus, cut it and return it to the clear wine, repeating the process without count. Once complete, take a piece the size of a pellet. Alternatively, divide one pellet into smaller pills. After thirty days of ingestion, one will be free from cold and heat, and divine beings and jade maidens will attend to one. Silver may also be ingested in the same manner as gold. Those who take these two substances and dwell in the stone chambers of renowned mountains will ascend lightly within a year. Even if one remains among people, one will become an earthbound immortal. Do not transmit this recklessly.

**Two Modes Master’s Method for Ingesting Gold:**
Take three *jin* of pig’s caul fat and one *sheng* of pure bitter wine. Place five *liang* of gold in a vessel and heat it in an earthen furnace. Immerse the gold in the fat, withdrawing and reinserting it a hundred times; do the same with the bitter wine. Ingesting one *jin* will grant a lifespan as vast as heaven and earth; ingesting half a *jin* will grant a lifespan of two thousand years.

Five *liang* grants a lifespan of one thousand two hundred years. There is no fixed amount—one may ingest it as desired. Choose a day when the celestial stems and earthly branches are in their ascendant phase to prepare and consume it, and the spiritual efficacy will be excellent. Do not transmit this to the unworthy; if shown to those who are not fit, the elixir will fail to form and lose its numinous power.

To ingest medicines that expel the corpse, one should take cinnabar.

**On the Highest Principle**
*Baopuzi* states: The subtle and wondrous are difficult to recognize, and those who doubt are many. How could my intelligence surpass others? I merely happen to have partial understanding, like the crane that knows midnight or the swallow that knows the *wu-si* day, yet may not comprehend other matters. Still, there is evidence to confirm that long life can be attained and that immortals are not born of a special lineage.

The profound mysteries of the Dao cannot all be committed to writing, while its more accessible aspects are not worth expounding. In ancient times, Gengsang Chu and Wenzi Li Yan toiled arduously for years before receiving the great esoteric instructions—surely there was reason for this. Of all those with rounded heads who breathe, who does not delight in life and fear death? Yet glory, splendor, power, and profit seduce the mind; unadorned faces and jade-like skin dazzle the eyes; clear *shang* and flowing *zheng* notes confuse the ears; love and hate, benefit and harm disturb the spirit; and fame and reputation bind the body. These all arrive unbidden, mastered without study. Unless one is destined to become an immortal, comprehends the principle in solitude, perceives transformations beyond ordinary affairs, and wields clear discernment in the realm of the obscure, who could abandon close relationships to pursue distant cultivation, suppress desires and habits, sever immediate cravings, and undertake the difficult work of remote attainment?

That which exists arises from non-existence; form is established through spirit. The existent is the palace of the non-existent; form is the dwelling of spirit. Thus, it is like a dike: when the dike breaks, water does not remain. It is like a candle: when the candle melts away, the flame does not abide. When the body labors, the spirit scatters; when qi is exhausted, life ends. If the root is depleted while the branches flourish, the verdant leaves will fall from the tree. When qi is fatigued and desire prevails, the essence and spirit depart the body. The departed have no appointed time of return; once decayed, there is no principle of rebirth. Those who grasp the Dao are truly to be pitied! How could they not value the hidden over the manifest?

Thus, those who nurture their nature in mountain forests and those who find fulfillment by transcending the mundane regard lofty honors as mere excrescences and weigh the myriad things as lightly as a cicada’s wing. Are they merely boasting in grand words or forcibly belittling worldly affairs? Truly, what they see is clear, so they abandon such things as if forgetting them. Therefore, they retreat to secluded places, conceal their brilliance, restrain the eyes from desiring sights, avoid dazzling colors, close the ears to distracting sounds, distance themselves from chaotic noises, purify the dark mirror of perception, embrace the feminine and hold to the One, concentrate qi to achieve suppleness, stabilize with tranquility and simplicity, dispel the perverse emotions of joy and sorrow, disregard the glory and shame of gain and loss, sever the poison of excessive indulgence, silence loquacity at the pivot of discourse, reverse hearing to achieve thorough perception, and turn inward to see without trace. They nurture the spiritual root in the hidden balance, eliminate enticements from external encounters, reject shallow affairs, govern with joy and sincerity, practice non-action to preserve the heavenly principle.

Then, like a father, they inhale precious blossoms, bathe the spirit in the Great Clarity, externally expel the five luminaries, internally guard the nine essences, fortify the jade lock at the Gate of Life, bind the Northern Culmen to the Yellow Court, draw the Three Radiances into the Hall of Light, soar with the Primordial Beginning to refine the form, gather numinous fluid from the Golden Beam, drive the white away and retain the green, congeal the limpid spring in the Cinnabar Field, guide the sunken pearl from the Five Cities, with the jade tripod below and simmering flames, the aquatic birds above and singing, rare blossoms sprouting, celestial deer disgorging jade, embracing the heavy compass in the Crimson Palace, concealing the nine lights in the cavernous darkness, clouds dark and dense connecting to the heavens, long valleys deep and intersecting, treading upon *qian* and *dui*, summoning the Six Dings, sitting and lying in the Purple Chamber, chewing and absorbing golden flowers, autumnal mushrooms gleaming, vermilion blossoms with emerald stems, luminous precious ointments overflowing like celestial dew, curing hunger and thirst, preventing the hundred ailments, roaming freely on the *wu-si* day, harmonious and balanced in drink, restraining the hun and po souls, filling the bones and lightening the body. Thus, they can ride wind and clouds to soar through the void and merge with the primordial chaos to achieve eternal life.

Yet the dust that settles an inch deep cannot be sought in a fleeting moment, and the mountain stream that flows through caves cannot be attained in haste.

The trouble lies in those who hear of it not believing, those who believe not practicing, and those who practice not persisting. Those who attain it are exceedingly rare and hidden, while those who fail are vastly numerous and conspicuous. The world cannot perceive the hidden ones and sees only the conspicuous, thus declaring that immortality and the Dao truly do not exist.

The Master Who Embraces Simplicity says: Seal cracks tightly, and water incurs no wasteful leakage; apply ample grease, and fire suffers no dimming of its flame. The Dragon Spring sword, unused for cutting, remains ever-sharp, while axes and hatchets, employed daily, swiftly wear out. Concealed snow, shunning warmth, endures through summer; stored ice, kept in the depths, outlasts the heat. A single layer of silk shields a mirror from scorching, and mere thatch, when properly covered, survives the winter. Mud and earth are easily dissolved, yet when fired into tiles, they endure as long as heaven and earth. Oak and elm rot swiftly, yet when burned into charcoal, they may last for countless ages without decay. Pigs, pampered and fattened, meet early slaughter, while fine horses, climbing steep paths, perish young. Cold-loving insects, adapting to their nature, live twice as long, and southern trees, dwelling in warmth, flourish evergreen. Exposed to killing frost, plants wither and waste away; bathed in spring’s harmony, they luxuriate and put forth new shoots. Though of the same kind, their flourishing or withering yields different outcomes—how can there be fixed limits to autumn’s harvest or immutable rules for winter’s storage?

Yet human lifespans, the appointed times of death and life, are less predictable than the fate of plants in the cold of winter. The principles of nurturing life and the methods of remedying harm offer benefits far beyond mere warmth. How could the effects of prolonged vision not hold true? Yet worldly people cling to the familiar and the narrow, dismissing the Dao of immortality as empty fabrication and the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi as wild falsehoods—is this not lamentable?

Even fools refuse to trust in decoctions, acupuncture, and moxibustion—how much less will they believe in matters deeper than these? They say, "If healers like Yu Fu, Bian Que, He and Huan, and Cang Gong could cure illness, why did they not avoid death?" They also say, "The wealthy and noble lack no medical skill, yet they too do not live long—this proves that lifespan is naturally determined." To demand that such people believe in immortals is like making an ox climb a tree or a horse chase a bird.

The Master Who Embraces Simplicity says: The Minor Elixir for Summoning the Soul, the Three Messengers Pill, and the lesser medicines of Five Flowers and Eight Minerals—some instantly dissolve solid ice, others float upon water of their own accord. They can sever ties with ghosts and spirits, ward off tigers and leopards, break up accumulations in the viscera, expel the Two Malevolents from the inner chambers, revive the suddenly dead from their abandoned corpses, and restore wandering souls that have already departed. These are all common medicines, yet they can bring the dead back to life—how then could the supreme medicines fail to grant the living immortality?

The physician from Yue revived the Crown Prince of Guo after his apparent death; the Hu doctor restored Su Wu from his severed breath. Chunyu Yi could split the skull to treat the brain; Hua Tuo could open the abdomen to wash the stomach. Wen Zhi delayed his arrival to cure a dire illness; Zhang Zhongjing pierced the chest to insert the Red Cake. These are but the humble skills of physicians, yet they achieved such feats—how much more can the Dao of immortality accomplish?

The reasons people die are these: the harm of myriad desires, the ravages of old age, the assault of the hundred diseases, the strike of poisons and malign influences, the injury of evil *qi*, and the encroachment of wind and cold. Now, through guiding and pulling *qi*, returning essence to replenish the brain, regulating food and drink, moderating activity and rest, ingesting medicinal substances, focusing the mind and guarding unity, upholding celestial prohibitions, and wearing talismans and seals—while shunning all that harms life—one may thus avoid these six perils.

Today, physicians prescribe the Pill for Unblocking Kidney *Qi*, the Powder for Internal Supplementation of the Five Meridians, the Decoction of Lycium and Cistanche for Filling the Bones, and the Decoction of Astragalus for Fortifying the Center. Those who take them all become robust and flourishing. Lacquer leaves and *Qing Zhen*—mere withered grasses—when taken by Fan A, granted him a lifespan of two hundred years, his ears and eyes remaining keen, still able to wield the needle and treat illness. This is a verified event of recent times, recorded by faithful historians. It is also said that Wu Pu, who learned the Five Animal Frolics from Hua Tuo to replace *daoyin* exercises, lived over a hundred years. These are but the most rudimentary of medicinal arts, yet they achieved such results—how much more can be accomplished by mastering their subtleties?

Yet when one tells the common people that the Four Harmonies Decoction can save those with cholera, that *Kuandong* and *Ziwan* can cure cough and counterflow, that *Lilu* and *Guan Zhong* can slay the Nine Worms, that *Danggui* and *Shaoyao* can stop colic, that *Qinjiao* and *Duhuo* can dispel the Eight Winds, that *Changpu* and dried ginger can halt numbness and dampness, that *Tusi* and *Congrong* can supplement deficiency, that *Gansui* and *Tingli* can expel phlegm and accumulations, that *Gualou* and *Huanglian* can heal wasting-thirst, that *Jini* and licorice can resolve the hundred poisons, that *Luru* and *Yire* can protect all wounds, and that *Mahuang* and *Daqing* can master cold damage—they still refuse to believe. They would rather slaughter living beings to pray for blessings or divine fortunes with milfoil and consult omens, unwilling to trust skilled physicians in curing illness, instead relying on the chaotic arts of shamans and scribes. How much less would they believe that golden elixirs can transcend the world or that *Zhi* mushrooms can prolong life?

In former times, Zhang Liang, the Marquis of Liu, spewed forth extraordinary strategies, unmatched in his generation. His wisdom and foresight were far from shallow, yet he still declared that immortality could be attained. His intelligence and discernment were not inferior to others, yet he said, "I shall abandon worldly affairs to wander with Red Pine." He then practiced *daoyin* and abstained from grains for a year, seeking the Way of light ascent. Pressured by Empress Lü, he was compelled to devise the plan to secure the Crown Prince, drawing in the Four Whiteheads. As he predicted, Empress Lü was grateful and forced him to eat heartily, thus preventing the completion of his Way.

According to the *Secret Records of Kong Anguo*, Zhang Liang obtained from the Yellow Stone Elder not only the arts of war but also the method of immortality.

The text further states that Zhang Liang’s original teachers were the Four Whiteheads—Master Luli, Qi Liji, and their companions—all of whom were immortals. Liang received from them their divine methods in full. Though later compelled by Empress Lü to eat and drink, he soon resumed his cultivation of the immortal Dao, secretly transcending the world. Yet the world remained unaware, and so it was said that he had died. According to Kong Anguo’s account, then, Liang had indeed attained immortality.

Moreover, the Han Chancellor Zhang Cang happened upon a minor technique: by suckling a woman’s milk, he lived to one hundred and eighty years—three times the ordinary lifespan. This was but a shallow practice of the Way, yet even so, it granted him such longevity. How much more, then, might one achieve through the full mastery of secret and wondrous arts? How could one not attain immortality? This account appears in the *Book of Han* and is no empty claim.

The *Baopuzi* states: "Ingesting elixirs is the foundation of longevity, but if one can also practice *qi*-circulation, the benefits come far more swiftly. Even without elixirs, if one cultivates *qi* to its fullest principle, one may yet live for several centuries. However, one must also master the arts of the bedchamber. Why? Without knowledge of yin-yang techniques, repeated exhaustion will cause harm, and *qi*-circulation will prove difficult to sustain. Man dwells within *qi*, and *qi* dwells within man. From heaven and earth to the myriad things, nothing exists without *qi* to give it life. Those skilled in *qi*-circulation nurture the body within and repel evil without, though the common people use it daily without awareness.

In Wu and Yue, there exists a method of incantatory prayer with proven efficacy, relying on the abundance of *qi*. Those who master it may enter the heart of great plagues, sharing a bed with the sick without falling ill. They may lead dozens of followers, all rendered fearless. This is *qi* warding off heavenly disasters. When malevolent spirits or mountain demons invade a household—hurling tiles and stones, setting fires to homes, appearing in visible forms, or merely speaking in disembodied voices—adepts of *qi*-incantation can suppress them instantly. This is *qi* restraining ghosts and gods.

In mountain forests thick with poisonous vipers and venomous creatures, ordinary travelers cannot pass without harm. Yet those skilled in *qi*-incantation can shield an area spanning dozens of *li*, ensuring their companions remain unharmed. They can also subdue tigers, leopards, snakes, and wasps, rendering them docile and unable to rise. With *qi*, they can staunch bleeding from wounds, mend broken bones, and rejoin severed sinews. They can render blades harmless, walking upon them unscathed or making them impervious to thrusts. If one is bitten by a venomous serpent, *qi*-incantation brings instant healing.

In recent times, Zuo Ci, Zhao Ming, and others have used *qi* to make water flow backward for one or two *zhang*. They have kindled fires atop thatched roofs to cook meals without burning the thatch. They have driven large nails seven or eight *cun* into pillars, then blown upon them with *qi* to make the nails spring out. They have subdued boiling water, tossing in over a hundred coins and allowing a man to scoop them out barehanded without scalding. They have exposed water to the open air in freezing weather without it turning to ice. They have prevented all cooking fires within a *li* from properly steaming food. They have silenced dogs, rendering them unable to bark.

Once, when the state of Wu sent General He to suppress mountain bandits, the rebels included a skilled *qi*-incantator. Whenever battle was joined, the government troops found their blades and swords impossible to draw, and their arrows turned back upon them, bringing constant defeat. General He, a man of keen intelligence, devised a plan: "I have heard that edged weapons and venomous creatures can be restrained by *qi*, but objects without edges or venom cannot. If their adept can bind our weapons, he surely cannot bind edgeless things." He then had many sturdy wooden clubs made and selected five thousand elite soldiers of exceptional strength to lead the assault. When they charged the bandits, the rebels relied on their adept’s powers and were wholly unprepared. The government troops struck with their clubs, crushing the enemy. The adept’s *qi* proved ineffective, and the slaughter numbered in the tens of thousands.

*Qi* issues from the body, and its effects are as wondrous as these. Why doubt that it can enable grain-abstention, cure illness, prolong life, and nurture one’s nature?

Zhongchang Gongli, a man of penetrating insight, wrote in his *Changyan*: "Circulating *qi* can eliminate hunger and illness. At first, I did not believe it, but upon witnessing its practice, I saw that it was indeed so. The methods of nurturing one’s nature are this simple, yet I have not mastered them. Is it not because my mind is distracted by worldly affairs and my thoughts consumed by mundane concerns? Others who fail to achieve this must share the same affliction.

Long ago, a master who knew the Way of immortality was sought by the Lord of Yan. The envoy failed to learn it before the master died. Enraged, the lord prepared to execute the envoy. A counselor advised: 'There is no greater sorrow than death, no greater urgency than life. If he himself lost his life, how could he have secured immortality for you?' The lord relented. The counselor’s words were well-reasoned. Had the master possessed the Way of immortality—such as the *qi*-circulation methods I have heard of—then his explanation of the master’s death would not necessarily have been ignorant. He simply could not abandon worldly affairs to practice it, and so, though he knew the method, it brought him no benefit. It was not that the Way of immortality did not exist."

The text further relates: "In Mixian County of Henan, there was a diviner named Bu Cheng who had studied the Dao for many years. One day, he bid farewell to his family, took a step, and rose slightly higher with each stride until he vanished into the clouds. This is what is meant by 'lightening the body to soar' and 'ascending to heaven in broad daylight'—the highest attainment of immortality."

Chen Yuanfang and Han Yuanchang, both eminent scholars of Yingchuan, lived near Mixian. Their belief in the existence of immortals stemmed from their fathers and grandfathers having personally witnessed Bu Cheng’s ascension. This, then, is yet another proof of immortality.

**Scroll Six: Subtle Principles**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: "I have heard that those who share the same bond and purpose require no words to establish trust, while those who follow divergent paths and pursuits, though offered loyal counsel, will still harbor doubt. Even in matters of ordinary understanding—trivial affairs of human choice, where gain and loss outweigh gold and feathers, and black and white are as distinct as ink and powder—those burdened by confusion often fail to discern them. How much more so when one speaks of what lies beyond the world’s ways, revealing the most subtle principles? To mock such truths is an ancient habit—how could it be unique to our time?

What the eye of understanding can perceive, even in the darkest recesses of midnight or the most minute objects, presents no difficulty in seeing. Yet where understanding does not reach, even the radiance of the sun and moon illuminating the heavens or the towering heights of Mount Song and Mount Tai remain beyond its grasp. The profound sages of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, with their deep insight and unique vision, unlocked secret texts in the sacred mountains and received the immortal scriptures from divine beings. They cast off the dust of the world to shed its burdens, soaring to distant heights and ascending beyond the ordinary. Neither metal nor stone can match their firmness, nor tortoises nor cranes rival their longevity. Moved by compassion for those of future generations who aspire to the Way yet lack guidance, they left behind methods, clearly and brilliantly set forth: small efforts yield small results, great endeavors bring great verification.

Yet those of shallow perception cling stubbornly to their limited views, content with bitterness like wormwood and smartweed, unaware of the sweetness of honey; drunk on thin wine, they fail to appreciate fine liquor. They know the value of life but not the Way of nurturing it; they fear death but do not believe in the methods of transcending it. They recognize that excess in food and drink breeds illness yet cannot restrain their indulgence in rich flavors. They understand that unrestrained desires lead to depletion and harm yet cannot sever their attachments. Though I speak of the attainability of immortality, how can I make them believe?"

Someone objected: "Your body lacks the extraordinary marks of wisdom—no intersecting veins or penetrating bones. Your years do not match the longevity of Anqi Sheng or Peng Zu. Your eyes have not beheld immortals, nor have your ears alone heard their teachings. How then do you know that long life can be attained or that the cultivation of nature bears proof? If you claim to perceive the profound through inner realization or to wield discernment through solitary insight, I cannot accept it. One who lacks even the means to cover his skin or secure his next meal yet boasts of the strategies of Tao Zhu or compares himself to Yi Dun invites ridicule—this is only natural. To speak of Bian Que’s healing arts while afflicted by chronic illness, or to claim mastery of Sun and Wu’s tactics after repeated defeats, is to be met with disbelief, for there is no evidence to support it."

I replied: "A minnow drifting in a puddle would claim there are no vast seas. An ant writhing within a fruit pit would assert that the boundaries of the world end there. Though told of boundless oceans or the expanse of the cosmos, it would dismiss such words as empty talk and refuse to believe. If my eyes had square pupils and my ears extended above my head, I would ride flying dragons and mount auspicious clouds, outpace lightning and ascend to the inverted heavens—how then could you question me? Even if you saw me, you would call me a celestial spirit or a divine being, not one who had attained such heights through study. I merely seek to guide those of like mind with the insights of an early awakening—how could I force all of your kind to believe? If every household had an immortal, visible at every turn, even you, though blind, would not doubt. But when such individuals achieve the Dao, they tread the azure heavens and wander the purple pole. Unless one possesses spiritual communion, they remain unseen and unheard—you would surely claim they do not exist.

People trust their own conjectures and rely on their narrow vision, believing their judgments infallible. What they are accustomed to, they accept; what they rarely encounter, they find strange. Though you pull their ears and point to their palms, they remain unmoved. This has been the case for ages—how could it be unique to our time?"

Another asked: "Having heard your excellent discourse, I no longer doubt the existence of immortals. My only regret is that I cannot become one myself. Might there be an essential method, something that can be practiced alone?"

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: "In learning the Dao, one must progress from the shallow to the deep, from the easy to the difficult. With unwavering resolve and determination, nothing is unattainable. Doubt yields no success—this is no trivial matter. Just as roots that do not penetrate the earth cannot produce towering branches, or shallow springs cannot give rise to rivers flowing a thousand miles, so too is it impossible to achieve greatness without foundation.

Thus, without accumulating hidden virtues and good deeds, one cannot move the divine; without sincere devotion and mutual affinity, one cannot form bonds with teachers and friends; without meritorious deeds, one cannot undertake great trials. Moreover, without encountering an enlightened teacher, the essential Way cannot be obtained. The Nine Elixirs and Golden Liquor are the foremost methods of immortality, yet their preparation requires great expense and cannot be swiftly accomplished. Preserving essence and cherishing qi are the most urgent tasks. One should also ingest minor medicines to prolong life and study lesser arts to ward off evil and misfortune—only then can one gradually ascend to the subtlest principles."

Another asked: "The methods are numerous and difficult to master in full. Setting aside the Golden Elixir, what other practices are worth pursuing? Which are the best?"

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: "If one has not yet grasped the greatest essentials, the lesser methods cannot be neglected. For it is through the convergence of many arts that long life is achieved. To illustrate with a grand analogy: it is like a ruler governing a state—civil and martial arts, rites and laws, none can be dispensed with. To illustrate with a smaller analogy: it is like a craftsman building a chariot—shafts, wheels, axles, and linchpins, none can be lacking.

The methods serve to cultivate both body and spirit internally, prolonging life and curing illness, and to repel evil and misfortune externally, preventing calamity from encroaching. Compared to a zither, one cannot produce the five notes with a single string; compared to armor, one cannot withstand blades with a single plate."

What is more, the five notes must be used in concert and none may be omitted, just as no part may be lacking where blades converge. In all matters of nurturing life, one must seek extensive knowledge while grasping the essentials, observe broadly and choose wisely—relying solely on one method is never sufficient. Yet there are those who, fond of novelty, cling to their own strengths: those versed in the arts of *xuan* and *su* claim only the bedroom arts can transcend the world; those who understand the Dao of exhalation and inhalation assert that only qi-cultivation can prolong life; those who know the methods of flexion and extension declare that only *daoyin* can forestall old age; those who know the remedies of herbs and trees insist that only medicinal tonics can ensure boundless vitality. The failure of Daoist cultivation stems precisely from such one-sidedness.

Those of shallow vision, having stumbled upon a single method, claim it is enough, yet lacking true discernment, they may chance upon an excellent technique but still seek endlessly, wasting time and effort, their practice ever shifting without constancy—both approaches are equally misguided. Some, by nature dull and limited in understanding, rashly enter the famous mountains, braving venomous stings and bites, repeatedly harmed yet too ashamed to return. Some are devoured by tigers and wolves, some slain by mountain spirits, some starve for lack of grain-cessation methods, some freeze for want of self-warming techniques, dying in ravines and gorges—is this not sheer folly?

In the pursuit of learning, choosing the right teacher is more crucial than mere diligence. If the teacher’s knowledge is narrow and they withhold full instruction, they may claim, "The Dao does not lie in quantity." Indeed, if one possesses the supreme essential of the Golden Elixir, other methods may be unnecessary. Yet such knowledge is exceedingly rare—how can one idly await an uncertain great attainment while neglecting the small arts that bring mutual benefit? It is like a household that scorns all other goods, claiming it needs nothing else—if it truly holds gold, silver, pearls, and jade in its grasp, enough to sustain generations, then perhaps. But lacking such wealth, how can one not cultivate a hundred grains, store abundant fruits and vegetables?

Thus, grain-cessation, evading weapons, suppressing and expelling ghosts and demons, warding off poisons, curing illnesses, entering mountains without fear of beasts, crossing waters unharmed by dragons, surviving plagues unscathed, concealing oneself in peril—these are minor arts, yet indispensable. How much more should one seek what lies beyond them?

Someone asked, "May I inquire about the prohibitions and taboos for those who cultivate the Dao of long life?"

Holding Simplicity replied: "The most urgent prohibitions lie in avoiding harm and injury. According to the *Yi’s* inner admonitions, the *Scripture of Red Pine*, and the *River Chart’s* records of fate and talismans, heaven and earth have spirits that oversee transgressions, deducting from one’s allotted lifespan according to the severity of the offense. When lifespan is diminished, one suffers poverty, illness, and repeated misfortunes; when it is exhausted, one dies. There are hundreds of actions that incur such deductions—it would be impossible to enumerate them all.

It is also said that within the body dwell the Three Corpses, formless yet akin to spirits, ghosts, and numinous beings. They desire a person’s early death so they may become ghosts themselves, roaming freely to enjoy sacrificial offerings. Thus, on every *gengshen* day, they ascend to heaven to report one’s transgressions to the Controller of Fate. On the last night of each lunar month, the Stove God likewise ascends to report one’s sins. Grave offenses deduct a *ji* (three hundred days), while lesser ones deduct a *suan* (three days). I cannot vouch for the truth of these matters, for the Dao of heaven is distant, and ghosts and spirits are hard to fathom.

Yet Zhao Jianzi and Duke Mu of Qin both received golden decrees from the Supreme Emperor, with clear evidence in their lands. Even mountains, rivers, grasses, trees, wells, stoves, ditches, and ponds possess vital spirits—how much more so the human body, which contains hun and po? Heaven and earth, being the greatest of all things, must logically possess spirit; if they possess spirit, they should reward good and punish evil. However, their vastness makes their net loose, so their responses need not be immediate.

Nevertheless, all Daoist admonitions agree: those who seek long life must accumulate goodness, establish merit, be compassionate toward all things, extend empathy to others, show kindness even to insects, rejoice in others’ good fortune, pity others’ suffering, aid those in distress, rescue those in dire straits, refrain from harming living beings, avoid inciting misfortune, regard others’ gains as one’s own, view others’ losses as one’s own, avoid self-aggrandizement or self-praise, neither envy nor flatter, nor harbor malice—only then does one possess virtue and receive heaven’s blessings. One’s endeavors will succeed, and the pursuit of immortality may be hoped for.

But those who despise goodness and delight in killing, whose words belie their hearts, who speak differently behind others’ backs, who oppose the upright, oppress their subordinates, deceive their superiors, betray their patrons, show no gratitude for kindness, manipulate laws for bribes, pervert justice, serve private interests, punish the innocent, destroy families, seize treasures, harm others’ bodies, usurp positions, exploit the worthy, execute the submissive, slander immortals and sages, injure Daoist practitioners, shoot birds in flight, disembowel fetuses and crush eggs, hunt in spring and summer, curse spirits, teach others to do evil, conceal others’ virtues, endanger others for self-preservation, claim others’ achievements as their own, sabotage others’ good deeds, seize what others love, sever others’ bonds of kinship, humiliate others to gain advantage, lend at high interest, repay debts with short measures, unleash floods and fires to harm others, coerce the weak, exchange good for bad, take by force, plunder for wealth, act unjustly, indulge in debauchery and perversion, bully orphans and widows, pocket lost items, deceive and swindle, gossip about others’ secrets, exploit others’ weaknesses, invoke heaven and earth to curse, refuse to repay loans, default on debts, crave endlessly, reject loyalty and honesty, disobey superiors, disrespect teachers, mock those who do good, ruin others’ crops, damage others’ belongings, exploit others’ hardship, feed others unclean food, use false weights and measures, shortchange cloth, mix counterfeits with genuine goods, seek illicit gains, entice others to steal, step over wells or stoves, sing on inauspicious days or wail at the new moon—such actions invite the deduction of lifespan."

For every single transgression, there is a corresponding sin; according to the gravity of the offense, the Controller of Fate deducts from one’s allotted lifespan or destiny. When the lifespan is exhausted, death follows. Even those who harbor evil intentions without acting upon them suffer deductions to their lifespan; those whose evil deeds harm others lose their destiny. If one dies before their lifespan or destiny is fully spent, the misfortune extends to their descendants.

Those who forcibly seize others’ property may have their wives, children, or household members calculated as compensation, leading to their deaths—though not immediately. If their evil deeds are insufficient to claim the lives of their family, they will eventually encounter disasters such as floods, fires, banditry, or the loss of possessions. They may also face official punishments, illness, or incur expenses for medical treatment, sacrificial offerings, or animal slaughter, all of which will inevitably exhaust the value of what they unjustly took.

Thus, Daoist teachings say that those who wrongfully kill others will in turn be slain by blades. Taking wealth through unrighteous means, heedless of resentment, is like using spoiled meat to sate hunger or poisoned wine to quench thirst—though it may temporarily satisfy, death will soon follow.

For those who have committed such evils but later repent, if they have wrongfully killed, they must seek to save those facing death to atone. If they have unjustly taken others’ property, they must give alms to the poor to resolve it. If they have falsely accused others, they must promote and elevate the worthy to undo the harm. In all cases, they must repay double what they took, and only then can they receive blessings, turning misfortune into fortune.

Those who refrain entirely from such transgressions will surely prolong their years, extend their lifespan, and swiftly achieve the Dao. Though heaven is high, it hears the lowly; nothing escapes its scrutiny. Persisting in good deeds ensures auspicious reward. Lord Yang accumulated virtue and practiced generosity until his hair turned white, and was rewarded with gold from heaven. Cai Shun’s utmost filial piety moved the spirits to respond. Guo Ju, who killed his son to care for his mother, received a heavy iron charter of reward.

Yet good deeds are difficult to perform, while evil deeds come easily. Fools, like those who cite Bo Niu’s misfortune, claim that heaven and earth cannot distinguish right from wrong. They do not realize that those with external reputations may lack inner virtue, and public praise cannot absolve hidden sins. To doubt the great qi of yin and yang based on the fleeting life and death of shepherd’s purse and wheat is to fall short of true understanding. Thus, the superior person acts with utmost care and barely escapes harm, while the mediocre fail to attain their desires.”

Someone asked, “Before achieving the Dao or retreating to the mountains, and given that the world today differs from antiquity with its abundance of thieves, how can one ward off daily troubles and prevent unforeseen disasters?”

The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replied, “Regularly on *zhiri* days, take soil from above the sixth *gui* stem, mix it with hundred-leafed mugwort, and plaster a one-foot square on your door—thieves will not come. Alternatively, take soil from the south gate of the market, or from the earth of the year’s broken sector or the month’s dominant sector, shape it into a figure, and place it in the Vermilion Bird direction to suppress thieves. In emergencies, enter a *shengdi* (life-preserving ground) to remain safe. There are *shengdi* in the world, in every province, commandery, county, village, hamlet, residence, and even within a single room.”

Someone asked, “A *shengdi* in a single room—is that not too confined?”

The Master replied, “The scriptures say, ‘In dire straits, hide beneath the carriage crossbar.’ If even a carriage has a *shengdi*, how much more a room?”

Someone asked, “I have heard that to seek longevity, one must know of two mountains. May I inquire where these mountains are located? I humbly seek your enlightenment to dispel my confusion.”

The Master replied, “They exist, but they are neither Mount Hua nor Mount Tai. The Mountain of the Great Origin is difficult to know yet easy to seek—neither celestial nor terrestrial, neither submerged nor floating. Its perilous paths stretch endlessly, rugged and winding. Harmonious qi intermingles, and divine intent roams freely. Jade wells run deep, their waters ceaselessly irrigating. One hundred twenty offices and bureaus align in succession. The positions of *li* and *kan* are arrayed, with ten thousand stalks of dark mushrooms. Crimson trees grow uniquely, their treasures unparalleled—gold and jade towering. Sweet springs emerge from the corners, and those who drink their pure flow become ageless. If you cultivate this, you may join the ranks of the pine and cypress. This is the first mountain.

The Mountain of the Long Valley stands vast and towering, its dark qi drifting, its jade liquid misting. Golden pools and purple chambers lie hidden in its recesses. Fools who venture there blindly meet only death, but those who possess the Dao ascend without decline. They gather and consume yellow essence, attaining celestial flight. This is the second mountain. Both have been kept secret by the sages of old; ponder them deeply.”

Someone asked, “I wish to hear of the true person’s art of guarding the body and refining the form.”

The Master replied, “A profound question indeed. Beneath the First Green, the moon and sun each contribute half to form a single whole. It emerges from the jade pool and enters the golden chamber, as large as a pellet and as yellow as a tangerine. Within lies a fine flavor, sweet as honey—if you obtain it, guard it well. Once it departs, it cannot be reclaimed, and the body will perish. The pure white qi is most subtle and dense, ascending through the secluded pass in three winding turns. The central cinnabar shines brilliantly, unmatched. Placed at the Gate of Life, the form does not decay. Profound and wondrous, it defies inquiry. This is the oral transmission of my former teacher. Those who know it fear neither the ten thousand ghosts nor the five weapons.”

One asked, “I have heard that those who fully master the arts of the bedchamber may, through this practice alone, attain transcendence and become immortals. They may also avert calamities, absolve transgressions, transform misfortune into fortune, secure promotions in office, and double the profits of merchants. Is this true?”

The Master replied, “Such claims are but the extravagant and deceptive words found in the books of shamans—distortions born of meddlers who embellish and exaggerate until truth is lost. Some are even the fabrications of deceivers, spun to delude the world, conceal their true methods, and solicit offerings, gathering disciples to pursue worldly gain.

The arts of yin and yang may, at best, cure minor ailments or, at least, prevent depletion and exhaustion. Their principles have their limits—how could they lead to transcendence or ward off misfortune and bring fortune? Yet one cannot remain without the union of yin and yang, for to do so invites illness.

If one indulges in unrestrained desire without moderation, it will cut short one’s years. But those skilled in these arts can halt the galloping steed to replenish the brain, return the cinnabar of yin to redden the intestines, gather the jade nectar from the golden pool, and draw the Three and Five to the flowering beam. This allows one to retain a youthful countenance in old age and fulfill the full span of one’s allotted years.

Yet common folk, hearing that the Yellow Emperor ascended to heaven with one thousand two hundred women, assume he attained longevity through this practice alone. They do not know that the Yellow Emperor, beneath Mount Jing and above Lake Ding, refined the Nine Elixirs to completion before riding the dragon to heaven. He may well have had one thousand two hundred women, but this was not the sole path to his transcendence.

Those who consume a thousand medicines and partake of the three sacrificial animals yet remain ignorant of the arts of the bedchamber will gain no benefit. Thus, the ancients, fearing that people would indulge their passions recklessly, spoke of these arts in glowing terms—but one must not believe them entirely.

The Plain Girl and the Dark Maiden likened it to water and fire: both can kill, yet both can also give life, depending on whether one knows how to use them. In general, knowing the essential methods, the more women one engages with, the better. But without understanding the Way, even one or two will hasten death.

The method of Peng Zu contains the most essential teachings. Other texts are overly cumbersome and difficult to practice, yet their benefits do not necessarily match their claims. Few can truly master them. The oral instructions amount to several thousand words. Without this knowledge, even consuming a hundred medicines will not grant longevity.”

Someone asked: "The August Heaven is most divine, and its allotment of fate should be impartial—why then does it grant the longevity of immortality to commoners like Qiao and Song, while the great sages Zhou and Kong are denied even prolonged life?"

Master Embracing Simplicity replied: "The length of a life is truly determined by what one encounters. When one receives *qi* and forms in the womb, each is tied to a particular star. The Dao of Heaven is non-action, letting things follow their nature—without partiality, without favor, without distinction between this and that. If one’s fate is bound to the star of life, that person will necessarily delight in the Dao of immortality. Those who delight in the Dao of immortality will surely attain it if they seek it. If one’s fate is bound to the star of death, that person will not believe in the Dao of immortality. Not believing in it, they will not cultivate its practices. Whether one delights in goodness or not is determined by what one is endowed with; alteration, bestowal, or deprivation are not within Heaven’s power.

"It is like gold and stone melting in the furnace, or earthenware being shaped in the kiln: though their forms are completed through these means, the sharpness or dullness of bronze and iron, the crookedness or straightness of jars and urns, depend on what they encounter—this is no longer the work of the furnace or kiln."

Another objected: "A skilled craftsman’s works all come from his own hands. The divine clarity of Heaven—what is there it cannot do? Yet you claim that human lives each encounter their own fate, not shaped by the August Sky. I am deeply perplexed and cannot accept this."

Master Embracing Simplicity answered: "When the primordial chaos split and the clear and turbid separated, some rose and moved, others descended and stilled—even Heaven and Earth do not know why this is so. The myriad things respond to *qi* and likewise follow their nature. Together with Heaven and Earth, each is a single thing, differing only in whether they form earlier or later, whether their bodies are vast or small. Because Heaven and Earth are great, the myriad things seem small. Because the myriad things are small, Heaven and Earth seem great.

"Moreover, though the belly and back enclose the five viscera, the five viscera are not made by the belly and back. Though skin and flesh wrap the blood and *qi*, the blood and *qi* are not created by the skin and flesh. Though Heaven and Earth contain the myriad things, the myriad things are not made by Heaven and Earth. It is like grasses and trees relying on mountains and forests to flourish, yet mountains and forests do not act upon them; or fish and turtles depending on lakes and marshes to multiply, yet lakes and marshes do not intervene.

"Vulgar people, seeing the vastness of Heaven and Earth and the smallness of the myriad things, say that Heaven and Earth are the parents of the myriad things, and the myriad things are their descendants. But lice are born from me—did I make them? Thus, though lice do not arise without me, I am not their parent, nor are they my descendants. Gnats breed in vinegar, *zhimao* mushrooms grow on wood and stone, leeches thrive in filth, and emerald vines flourish on pine branches—none of these four are crafted by those things. How is the teeming of the myriad things within Heaven and Earth any different?

"Heaven has its sun and moon, cold and heat; humans have their seeing and breathing. Using the distant to analogize the near, and this to infer that, if humans cannot know why their own bodies age, suffer pain, or itch, then Heaven likewise cannot know why its own body waxes or wanes, or why disasters or blessings arise. If humans cannot make their ears and eyes always sharp, or their *ying* and *wei* never obstructed, then Heaven cannot make the sun and moon never eclipse, or the four seasons never lose their order. From this reasoning, the matter of great longevity is truly not in the hands of Heaven and Earth, and whether one becomes immortal or not is decided not by what one encounters.

"Those who give me life are my father, and those who bear me are my mother—yet even they cannot ensure my form is perfectly suited, my appearance lovely, my nature balanced, my wisdom profound, my strength abundant, or my years prolonged. Some are stunted and frail, some dark and ugly, some deaf, blind, stubborn, or crippled. What they receive is not what they desire; what they desire is not what they receive. How much less can the vastness of Heaven and Earth do so? Even parents are distant in comparison.

"Once I have a body, I cannot make it forever strong and unaging, always healthy and free of illness, its joy and anger always fitting, its plans and thoughts never regrettable. Those who bestow *qi* and shape the form are my parents; those who receive and possess it are my own body. Beyond these, nothing is more intimate, nothing has greater control. If even these two cannot benefit or harm me, how can Heaven and Earth have any part in it?

"If all people and things were made by Heaven and Earth, they should all be good and without evil, all complete and without flaw. All living things would thrive, and the osmanthus and poplar would know no autumn’s withering sorrow. You claim that Heaven cannot grant Zhou and Kong the blessing of transcending the world—this only proves that what one is endowed with is natural, not apportioned by Heaven and Earth.

"The virtue of the sage is the highest virtue. If Heaven could bestow such perfect virtue, yet made their knowledge incomplete, their achievements unbuilt, their position not hegemonic, their lifespan not a full century—this would not be evidence of Heaven’s intentional action. The death of the sage is not caused by Heaven, so the birth of the sage is not raised by Heaven either.

"The worthy need not live long, the foolish need not die young. Goodness does not bring near blessings, nor evil near misfortune. Life has no fixed term, death no constant measure. The most virtuous and wise may blossom but bear no fruit, while the mediocre man Dou may live nearly two hundred years. Boniu was stricken with illness, Zixia lost his sight. Robber Zhi was utterly wicked yet lived to white hair, while Zhuang Qiao was extremely evil yet reached yellowed locks. Heaven’s non-action is clear in this."

Someone said: "Confucius said, 'From ancient times, all have died.' Laozi said, 'The immortality of the *xian* can be learned.' The words of the sage are trustworthy and verifiable, while the Daoists’ claims are extravagant and difficult to apply."

Master Embracing Simplicity replied: "Confucius is the sage of the Confucians..."

Laozi is the sage of those who attain the Dao. The Confucian teachings are near and easily seen, so those who follow them are many.
