The Dao’s meaning is distant and difficult to recognize, so those who grasp it are few. The Dao is the source of the ten thousand differences; Confucianism is a branch of the great purity. Before the Three Sovereigns, the Dao governed. From the rise of emperors and kings, Confucian teachings prevailed. Those who discuss these matters all know the lofty antiquity’s honest simplicity and despise the decadence of later ages—why then do they honor Confucius alone and slight Laozi? This is like admiring the blossoms at the tips of branches while failing to recognize the root from which they grow. How is it different from prizing bright pearls while despising the deep abyss, loving the *he* jade while hating Mount Jing—unaware that the abyss is where bright pearls arise, and Mount Jing is where the *he* jade is born?

Moreover, nurturing one’s nature is but a remnant of the Dao; rites and music are but the fringe of Confucianism. What is valued in Confucianism is its power to transform customs and alter mores—not merely bowing and yielding or pacing in circles. What is revered in the Dao is its silent transformation—not merely the single matter of nurturing life. If Confucianism and the Dao truly had an order of precedence, then Confucius could not be exclusively trusted, nor Laozi employed in isolation.

Confucius himself paid respectful homage to Boyang and wished to be compared to Lao Peng. He also admitted that though he understood fish and birds, he did not know the dragon—and likened Laozi to the dragon. These were words of heartfelt admiration, not empty praise. They differ not at all from what he told Yan Hui: *"Gazing, it is before me; suddenly, it is behind. Boring into it, it grows ever harder; looking up to it, it grows ever higher."*

Someone asked: "Confucius personally met Laozi yet did not study the Dao—why?"

Master Embracing Simplicity replied: "From this, we see all the more clearly that what one is endowed with is a natural mandate, and what one esteems is an unchanging nature. Confucius recognized Laozi’s profound subtlety and exalted strangeness, yet could not draw from the pure and empty, the great origin and fundamental source—emerging beyond formlessness, entering into the supreme Dao. What he sought to learn was limited to matters among the people; how could he have requested the methods of immortality?

I surmise that his heart was wholly absorbed in teaching and transformation, with no thought for esoteric arts. Though Confucius was a sage in worldly affairs, he was not one who could abide in stillness and silence, guarding non-action. Thus Laozi warned him: *'A good merchant stores deeply as if empty; a noble man of great virtue appears foolish. Rid yourself of your prideful air and excessive desires, your affected manner and extravagant ambitions—these bring no benefit to your person.'* This is enough to know that Confucius was not free from worldly passions and was no student of immortality.

Ever restless and hurried, his efforts were devoted to correcting the age. Gazing up, he mourned the phoenix’s cry; looking down, he sighed for the gourd. Fearing his teachings would not sell, he declared with indignation his willingness to hold the whip—how then could he abandon the work of ordering the world to pursue the impractical study of nurturing life?"

Someone asked: "Between the Confucian and Daoist paths, which is more difficult?"

Master Embracing Simplicity answered: "Confucianism is difficulty within ease; the Dao is ease within difficulty. To abandon socializing, forsake wife and children, renounce fame and glory, forgo profit and emolument, turn away from what dazzles the eyes and stifle what delights the ears, to be serene, joyful, and retiring, guarding one’s integrity alone—unmoved by slander, unelated by praise, unmoved by wealth, unashamed by lowliness: this is the difficulty of the Dao. To go out without expectation of condolences or congratulations, to return without obligation to visit or be seen, to spare the mind the labor of the Seven Classics, to free thought from the calculations of calendars and music, to avoid the toil of astronomical reckoning and the servitude of literary arts, to shed the burdens of the many, allowing harmonious qi to flourish naturally—this is the ease of the Dao, what I call ease within difficulty.

As for the Confucian, what he cultivates are all established precedents: conduct has its rules, speech and silence follow the moment. Teachers can be found in every household; books come with commentaries to resolve doubts—this is the ease of Confucianism. To probe the profound and reach the distant, to interweave the classics and ancient texts, to master the clamorous records of the Yellow River and Luo River, to encompass the vast sayings of the hundred schools, to accumulate virtue in humble lanes, to exhaust loyalty and integrity in serving one’s lord, to drive the spirit upward toward celestial signs, to direct thought downward toward wind and clouds—if one matter is unknown, all one does is obstructed; if a single word is amiss, praise and blame are confused. Every step becomes a model for the world; every utterance is transmitted across the land—this is the difficulty of Confucianism, what I call difficulty within ease.

Weighing the two, the Confucian path is mostly difficult, the Daoist path concise and easy. Fearing its difficulty, I will abandon it and follow what is easy. Those who mock me for this are as numerous as the crowd, but those who might share my understanding—I have yet to meet such a person. If like-minded people must await the future, then I do not consider them rare."

Someone said: "I have observed many eminent scholars, widely learned and renowned, who have indeed delved deeply into principle and nature, rigorously examining the boundaries of existence and non-existence—yet none have spoken of extending years or attaining immortality. Though your brilliance does not rival the sun and moon, and your thoughts do not surpass the multitude, I still cannot bring myself to believe in the way of long life."

Master Embracing Simplicity replied: "I am a common man of modest talent, shallow in insight and narrow in learning—how dare I claim to surpass the crowd with unique understanding? Yet I have sought the hidden through what is manifest, obtained the difficult through what is easy. Comparing small proofs, I know the great effects; seeing what has already come to pass, I understand what has yet to be tested.

Moreover, those who do not believe in the immortals of heaven and earth have never even attempted to investigate. Possessing only the talents of the common and the skills of the well-trodden path, they skim through books that aid teaching to assess the near and easy in human affairs, to distinguish what perplexes the vulgar. Then they declare that what the crowd doubts, they alone can decide; that what shows no sign of coming, they alone can foresee. They claim to have exhausted the feelings of all things, to have grasped all that is dark and obscure."

"I say there are no immortals, and immortals must indeed be nonexistent—such has been the stubbornness from time immemorial. I often observe mediocre scholars, clinging to hollow doctrines, refusing to believe in the most profound truths. Their affliction lies in possessing some cleverness, yet being crippled by bias and constraint, burdening themselves with petty cunning rather than remaining purely in utter darkness, no better than those who cannot tell beans from wheat. With the narrow vision of peering through a tube, they block off all that lies beyond their limited understanding—how is this different from drawing water from a hundred-fathom well with a rope merely a fathom long, unaware of their tool’s inadequacy, and then declaring the well dry? The vulgar hear the roar of fierce winds and raging fires and call it winter thunder; they see drifting clouds moving west and claim the moon races east. Should someone correct them, they refuse to believe or awaken—such is their blind faith in themselves. Those who listen trust their ears without question; those who see trust their eyes without doubt. Yet what they hear and see may well be false. If so, can our ears and eyes truly be trusted? How much more difficult, then, is it to discern the heart’s judgments, which have no form or sound, far surpassing the clarity of sight and hearing? To rely solely on one’s own mind and then dismiss the most distant truths of the world, declaring the Way of immortals mere fiction—is this not the height of delusion?"

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: "Beauty and ugliness are fixed, yet love and hate differ in nature, so the two eyes do not see alike. Refined and vulgar music are distinct, yet likes and dislikes vary, so the two ears do not hear alike. Truth and falsehood have their essence, yet choices and rejections clash, so the two hearts do not deliberate alike. Some mistake ugliness for beauty, muddy for clear, loss for gain—these three are starkly opposed, plainly evident, so easily discerned, yet they remain irreconcilable. How much less, then, can the subtlest matters of immortality be made universally believable? There is no reason why all should accept them. If everyone were made to understand, what value would there be in the enlightened? To wait for the vulgar to cease their delusions would be like awaiting the Yellow River’s waters to run clear—it would not be long, but it would never come. I cannot remain silent because I hope to guide those who may yet be swayed. As for the unyielding, even the ancients could do nothing with them."

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: "The difficulty of clarifying the highest truths and the disbelief in immortals are long-standing, not unique to our time. The most enlightened grasp them naturally; the next rank awakens only after being told. As for those who hear and laugh in derision—such are the many. In speaking of this, I may well invite repeated failures and the blame of careless words. For when nothing aligns with one’s view, harm is sure to follow."

For even the fullness of yang cannot revive a withered and rotted tree, nor can divine illumination alter a nature sunk in deep delusion. Zigong could not please the rustic who herded horses, and Gu Gong could not free the Rong and Di barbarians from their covetous lands—truth has its limits of reach, and good words their bounds of efficacy. The ceremonial cap finds no buyer among the Man and Yue tribes, just as red shoes are of no use to the barefoot Yi. How can one force such things?

To point at jade and call it stone is not because the jade is false, but because only a He Shi can recognize it. To see a dragon and name it a snake is not because the dragon lacks numinosity, but because only a Cai Mo can discern it. The Way is prized because nothing can be added to augment it, nor taken away to diminish it. Virtue is prized because one hears slander without distress and receives praise without delight.

If they truly believe the world holds no immortals, while I alone contend that they exist in truth, the longer the dispute drags on, the more stubbornly they cling to their position. This only prolongs the futile entanglement without resolving the impasse—should I then follow the principle of the linked rings?

Someone asked: "The human Dao has many paths, and seeking immortality is most difficult. If one does not abandon other affairs, then nothing can be accomplished fully. The pursuit of arts and letters, the burdens of sorrow and joy, the Way of ruler and minister—how can these be set aside?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: "The essential Dao is not burdensome; what matters is that few practice it. The only concern is whether one’s resolve is firm and one’s faith unwavering—why fear the neglect of human affairs? Those of great talent cultivate both simultaneously; what difficulty is there? Within, they treasure the Dao of nurturing life; without, they harmonize with the world. Cultivating the self, the self endures; governing the state, the state attains great peace. They instruct the common scholar with the Six Classics and transmit esoteric arts to those of kindred spirit. If they wish to linger awhile, they remain to aid their age; if they wish to ascend, they soar beyond the clouds—such are the superior scholars. Those who, relying on their own talent and strength, cannot achieve both, abandon worldly affairs to focus solely on the cultivation of virtue—these are of the next rank.

Of old, the Yellow Emperor bore the weight of the Four Seas yet did not hinder his ascent at Mount Dinghu. Peng Zu served as a grandee for eight hundred years before journeying west to the Flowing Sands. Laozi was the Keeper of the Archives, Ning Feng the Pottery Master, Fang Hui the Village Scholar, Lü Wang the Grand Preceptor, Qiu Sheng served in Yin, Ma Dan held office in Jin, Lord Fan subdued Yue and set sail upon the sea, Qin Gao bore the tablet at Song Kang’s court, Chang Sheng humbled himself to hold the whip, and Duke Zhuang concealed his power in a minor post. Many ancients attained the Dao while aiding the world, cultivating it in the midst of courtly reclusion—surely they had strength to spare. Why must one retreat to the mountains and forests, wholly abandoning the affairs of the living, before attaining perfection?

There are also those whose hearts are tranquil and silent, whose natures abhor clamor, who find joy in freedom and sorrow in honor and duty. They gird themselves in hempen robes, eat wild herbs, grasp the plow, delight in their three joys, abide in constancy, await their end, neither scheming for a fleeting life nor fearing a swift death. They decline appointments worth a thousand gold and scorn the rank of minister. Even without cultivating anything, they remain thus—how much more so if they add to this the knowledge of the immortal’s Dao? They would surely refuse to serve the world. Each follows their own aspiration; one cannot speak of them as a single type."

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: "The world values a single good word more than a thousand gold pieces, yet this refers only to the gain or loss of armies and states, or the rightness or wrongness of one’s conduct. As for teaching others the secret of long life or transmitting the method of not dying—this is not merely the good word of an ordinary person. How could it be worth only a thousand gold? If one were gravely ill and on the verge of death, and another saved them, restoring them to health, would this not be called a great kindness and weighty favor? Now, if one follows the immortal classics, flies with the Nine Elixirs, and ingests gold and jade, the whole world could be made deathless—such beneficence is not merely the merit of reviving one person. The virtue of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi is truly boundless, yet none recognize it, dismissing it as wild and absurd talk. How lamentable!"

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: "To seek immortality, one must grasp its essentials. The essentials lie in treasuring essence, circulating qi, and ingesting a single great elixir—no more is needed. Yet these three matters have depths and shallows. Without a enlightened teacher or enduring hardship, one cannot master them hastily.

Though it is said to circulate qi, there are several methods for doing so. Though it is called the bedchamber arts, the techniques therein number over a hundred. Though it is said to ingest elixirs, the formulas for doing so number roughly a thousand. When first teaching others, one begins with the shallow. Only those of steadfast intent and tireless diligence, whose efforts are evident, are then told the essentials.

Thus, circulating qi can cure a hundred illnesses, ward off pestilence, subdue tigers and snakes, stanch wounds and bleeding, allow one to dwell underwater or walk upon it, stave off hunger and thirst, or extend lifespan. Its great essential is embryonic breathing. Those who attain embryonic breathing can refrain from inhaling and exhaling through nose and mouth, as if within the womb—then the Dao is achieved.

When first learning to circulate qi, draw it in through the nose and hold it, silently counting to one hundred twenty in the mind, then exhale gently through the mouth. Both inhalation and exhalation should be so subtle that one’s own ears cannot hear the sound of the breath. Always let the intake be greater than the outflow. Place a goose feather before the nose and mouth; when exhaling, the feather should remain motionless. Gradually increase the count over time, and eventually one may reach a thousand. At a thousand, the old become young again, regaining a day with each passing day.

One should circulate qi only during the time of living qi, not during the time of dead qi. Thus it is said: 'The immortal ingests the six qi.' In a day and night, there are twelve hours. From midnight to noon are the six hours of living qi; from noon to midnight are the six hours of dead qi. Circulating qi during dead qi is of no benefit.

Those skilled in using qi can exhale upon water, causing it to flow backward for several paces; exhale upon fire, and it is extinguished; exhale upon tigers and wolves, and they crouch, unable to move; exhale upon snakes and vipers, and they coil, unable to flee. If another is wounded by blade or weapon, exhaling upon them stanches the blood. If one hears of another bitten by a poisonous creature, though they may not see the person, they can exhale upon their own hand from afar—men exhale upon the left, women upon the right—and though the person be a hundred leagues away, they will immediately recover. For sudden afflictions and acute illnesses, merely swallowing the qi of three nines brings instant relief.

Yet most people are hasty by nature and few can remain tranquil to cultivate the Dao. Another great essential of circulating qi is to avoid overeating, as well as raw vegetables, fatty meats, and rich foods, which make the qi strong and difficult to contain."

Moreover, one must abstain from wrath and anger, for excessive anger disorders the qi, preventing it from overflowing; it may even provoke coughing, so few can master this practice. My ancestor, the Immortal Master, would plunge into the depths of deep pools after heavy drinking or in the scorching heat of summer, remaining submerged for a full day before emerging—this was precisely because he had mastered the art of closing the qi and embryonic breathing.

There are over ten schools of the bedchamber arts: some serve to mend injuries, others to treat various illnesses, some to absorb yin to replenish yang, and others to extend life and prolong years. Yet their essential principle lies solely in the practice of returning essence to replenish the brain. This method is passed down orally from one true adept to another and was never committed to writing. Even those who consume renowned elixirs but remain ignorant of this essential technique will not attain longevity.

One must not entirely sever yin and yang, for without their union, one incurs blockages and obstructions, leading to illness. Thus, those who are secluded, resentful, or deprived of union often fall ill and die young. Yet indulging without restraint also shortens one’s lifespan. Only by attaining harmony through regulated release can one avoid harm. Without the oral transmission of its techniques, not a single practitioner among ten thousand can engage in this without self-injury or ruin. Masters like Xuansuzi, Du Rongcheng, and Pengzu recorded only its superficial aspects, never committing its most crucial elements to paper. Those who seek immortality should diligently pursue this path.

I record these words of my teacher, Master Zheng, to guide future believers in the Dao—this is no baseless conjecture. Yet I myself have not fully mastered its secrets.

Some Daoist practitioners, fixated solely on the arts of union, seek immortality through them while neglecting the great elixir of golden cinnabar—this is utter folly.

*Baopuzi* states: "Of the Daoist texts attributed to the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, only a small portion are genuine; most were composed by later enthusiasts who expanded upon their own limited knowledge, resulting in a mountain of volumes. The ancients were simple and often lacked talent; their discussions of natural principles were incomplete, their evidence unclear, and their expositions of essentials obscure and difficult to grasp. Even when explained, their insights were shallow and insufficient to expound profound teachings, enlighten the perplexed, encourage the aspiring, instruct beginners, reveal the paths of the mysterious and subtle, or trace the origins of fortune and misfortune. Reciting such texts ten thousand times yields no benefit. While one may wish to study broadly, one should carefully select the finest works and focus on them, disregarding those that are irrelevant.

Yet some later learners fail to distinguish the depth of their authors. Upon encountering texts labeled as Daoist, they amass them by the boxful, exhausting their minds in pursuit of their meaning. This is like searching a swallow’s nest for phoenix eggs or dredging a well for eels—no matter how diligent, they seek what is not there, gaining nothing of practical use. They waste their days in vain, enduring fatigue without the slightest benefit. In public life, they neglect their duties; in private, they fail to achieve longevity. Thus, others inevitably point and say, 'They practiced the Dao so diligently yet did not transcend the world—surely there is no method for avoiding death!' Unaware that their pursuit of immortality was like gazing at a river, longing for fish without a net—it is not that the river lacks fish.

The *Five Thousand Characters*, though attributed to Laozi, offers only broad and cursory discussions. It never fully presents its subject from beginning to end, nor provides verifiable methods. Merely reciting this scripture without grasping its essentials is labor in vain—how much more so for lesser texts?

As for works by Wenzi, Zhuangzi, Guan Lingyin, and their ilk, though their writings trace their lineage to the Yellow Emperor and Laozi and uphold the mysterious and subtle, they merely elaborate on general principles without conveying the ultimate teachings. Some even equate life and death, treating existence as forced labor and death as rest—such views are already a thousand *li* removed from the path of immortality. How can they be worthy of study? Their allegories and parables may offer some utility, serving as makeshift remedies in times of need. Yet it is lamentable that later generations of glib, unscrupulous charlatans and dissolute scoundrels have taken Laozi and Zhuangzi as their refuge."

Someone may ask: "In an age governed by sagely wisdom, only the worthy are treasured. If those who study immortality refuse to serve in office, and all pursue the Dao, who will then assist in governance?"

Bao Puzi replied: "Those who abandon the sagely ruler to dwell in the mountains—this is why Chao Fu and Xu You were esteemed as lofty. Those who withdraw from an age of the Dao—this is why Zhuang Bo was held in honor. When the Yellow Emperor ruled all under heaven, his governance could be called perfect, yet Guangcheng shunned him. When Tang Yao possessed the Four Seas, his reign could be called supremely peaceful, yet Wo Quan refused to serve him. Yet the transformative power of virtue was not diminished by this, nor were worthy men lacking.

When Tian Yi overthrew the Xia, Wu Guang bore a stone and cast himself into the river. When King Wu of Zhou destroyed the Shang, Boyi and Shuqi starved on Western Mountain. When Duke Huan of Qi rose to power, Shao Ji reclined in a humble alley. When the Wei dynasty flourished under King Wen, Gan Mu let his hair loose by the Western River. The Four Elders concealed themselves like phoenixes in Shangluo, yet this did not hinder the Han from teeming with scholars. Zhou Dang stood aloof as a unicorn in the forests and marshes, yet this did no harm to Emperor Guangwu’s just rule.

Those whose hearts cannot be swayed by favor or rank, whose aspirations cannot be altered by utmost wealth—who wash their tassels in the Canglang’s waters, neither submitting nor suffering disgrace—who take fragrant groves as their pavilions, towering peaks as their mansions, kingfisher-green orchids as their couches, verdant leaves as their curtains, who wear coarse cloth in place of embroidered robes, and take ferns and beans as fine fare; who will not eat unless they till the soil themselves, nor clothe themselves unless their wives weave; such men appear but rarely in a thousand years. How much rarer still are those who further abandon their kin to the state, forsake their households without a glance, spurn glory and splendor as though discarding footprints, extinguish desire from their hearts, scale soaring peaks alone, and take echoes and shadows as companions in the famous mountains? Inwardly, they gaze upon the realm of formlessness; outwardly, they listen to the depths of perfect stillness. Within the Eight Directions, how many such men could there be? Yet you fear the ruler will lack ministers—is this not an excess of worry?"

Some may say: "Those who study the Dao of immortality purify only themselves while neglecting the great ethical bonds, turning their backs on the sovereign with disloyal arrogance. I fear they will fail to achieve longevity and instead bring down the net of punishment upon themselves."

The Master Embracing Simplicity replies: "Consider the northerners Shi Hu, Shan Juan, and Zi Zhou—all men of great talent—who lived in seclusion and freedom, nurturing their vast and unbounded qi. Their rise and fall did not diminish them, nor did the great transformations of the world leave them lacking. How much more so for those who study the Dao of immortality, who may not possess the talent to govern the state or serve in court? Gaining them brings no more benefit than a speck of dust or a drop of dew, while losing them causes no more loss than a hair’s breadth. Today, the Nine Provinces are united under one roof, yet those from distant lands come to serve; men of exceptional ability lie idle in abundance, with no use for them. Scholars wait endlessly for appointments, while offices remain briefly vacant. Those who have labored long sigh at delayed promotions, and those of high merit suffer under rigid seniority. In this flourishing age, so rich in talent, even the humblest are not lacking.

In ancient times, Prince Jin abandoned his duty to attend the royal meals and relinquished his position as heir apparent, yet King Ling did not accuse him of disloyalty. Yin Sheng cast aside his official sash and departed from his post, yet the Zhou dynasty did not condemn him for disloyalty. Why? Because they truly understood that such men were not slighting the world or their rulers, but simply followed their own inclinations—a resolve no ordinary man could sway. A ruler who embodies the Dao harbors tolerance and forgiveness, recognizing that human hearts cannot be the same and that each follows their own nature. Without coercion or prohibition, he fosters greatness, free from personal bias, allowing his subjects the utmost joy in fulfilling their aspirations. Thus, his glory and renown spread boundlessly, and even the greedy, upon hearing of his virtue, feel shame. I have heard that when the balmy winds rise, fur garments and stoves are put away; when the world is at peace, extraordinary men retreat. Now that the chaos of war has settled, oxen are left to rest and horses released, beacon fires vanish like shadows, and weapons are sheathed. The mighty bows are stored away, and even the finest hounds face the pot. Zhang Liang emerged from the imperial tent to return to the streets, while Han Xin and Peng Yue laid down their armor to take up fishing. How much more so for those who study the Dao of immortality, of whom not even one in ten thousand exists? What would the state lose by sparing them?

Moreover, their path lies in reducing thoughts and desires, their practice in preserving the body and prolonging life. They do not engage in vulgar competition, nor do they harm the customs of the world. What crime have they committed? The peaks of Hua and Heng are vast, the blue sea boundless—neither their height awaits the dust of soaring birds, nor their depth relies on the trickle of rainwater. A handful of earth cannot diminish their towering majesty, nor a ladle of water lessen their expanse. Even if there were but a few immortals in an age, how could they disrupt the world’s affairs?"

Some may ask: "If the Dao of immortality can indeed be attained, why do the Five Classics not record it? Why do Zhou and Kong not speak of it? Why did the sages not transcend the world, and why do the wisest not live forever? If Zhou and Kong did not know of it, they cannot be called sages. If they knew but did not pursue it, then the Dao of immortality does not exist."

The Master Embracing Simplicity replies: "The stars under which men are born each have their destined influence, as I have explained in detail elsewhere. You are like one who wears a basin on his head and looks up, unable to see the brilliance of the seven luminaries; or one who briefly cranes his neck toward a great river, unaware of the wonders hidden in its depths. There are countless things the Five Classics do not record, and many truths Zhou and Kong did not speak of. Let me briefly mention but a fraction for you. Though you may laugh uncontrollably and your narrow mind may struggle to open, at least you will hear a rough outline.

Heaven and earth are the greatest of all things. The nine sages together composed the *Book of Changes*, which fully encompasses the workings of yin and yang—nothing more can be added. Yet ask even the most skilled interpreters of the *Changes*: How many *li* span the circumference of the heavens? How wide are the Four Seas? How far apart are the cosmos? Where does the sky end above, and what supports it below? What force sets it in motion? Why do the sun and moon vary in speed, following the nine paths? Why do dawn and dusk differ in length? How do the seven stars take turns governing the seasons? Why do the five planets wax and wane, form halos, or suffer eclipses? What causes the conjunctions of the four and seven stars, the appearance of comets, the omens of celestial arrows, the portents of solar halos? Why does the North Star remain fixed while Saturn moves eastward? Why does the sun’s outer light bring heat, while the moon’s inner reflection brings cold? Why does the Milky Way, though visible above, embody the nature of moisture below? Why do the tides ebb and flow with great and small variations? How do the five notes and six tones reveal the emotions of joy and anger? How do clouds and winds carry signs of fortune and misfortune? What of the celestial spears, banners, and arrows, the red and crimson vapors, the Four Pillars and Five Remnants, the Heavenly Dogs and Returning Demons—some foretelling success, others ruin? Even the *Book of Changes* cannot explain these. If you ask scholars of the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, the *Four Books*, the *Book of Songs*, or the *Three Ritual Classics*, they too will have no answers. They will say these matters are not recorded in the classics, that only Wu Xian, Gan Gong, Shi Shen, Hai Zhong, and Xie Meng’s records of the seven luminaries contain them.

I would then ask: Are these six scholars’ works part of the canonical teachings? They would say no. I would further ask: Are Gan, Shi, and the others sages? They would also say no. Yet men are born beneath the heavens and walk upon the earth, yet seek these truths in the Five Classics in vain, and find nothing in the writings of Zhou and Kong. Should we then dismiss them all as falsehoods? Heaven and earth are vast—even what lies before our eyes remains incomprehensible. How much more so the mysteries beyond mysteries, the subtleties beyond subtleties?"

He further asked the skeptics: "What of the lands where men ride clouds and women give birth to cocoons, where people’s livers and hearts do not decay? What of those who dwell in nests or caves, with single eyes or three heads, horses with human feet or dogs with cloven hooves, arms so long they cross, or legs so entwined they cannot walk? What of the Yellow Pool without men, or those with chests pierced and mouths on their sides? What of Lin Jun, who rose from stone and sailed in a boat of earth, or Sha Yi, who touched wood and gave birth to dragons? What of Nü Wa, who emerged from the earth, or Du Yu, who fell from the heavens? What of the flying tiles, the talking dogs, the shifting mountains, the moving altars? What of the three armies that transformed in a single morning—gentlemen into cranes, commoners into sand? What of Nü Chou, who leaned upon a withered tree, or Er Fu, who bore his shackles? What of the hermit crabs that shed their shells and walked in flesh, or the two-headed snakes that became bowstrings? What of the unburning wood, the unheated fire, the birds of Changshu with no eyes, the beasts with no bodies, the heads without bodies, the bodies without heads? What of Jing Wei, who filled the sea, or the trees that yielded their places in turn? What of the fire-washed cloth, the jade-cutting knives, the flames that spew from darkness, the water filtered from mud? What of the withered trees that revived and took form, or the mountain *kui* that walked ahead? What of the nine-headed *Shixiu*, the human-faced *Bifang*, the *Shaoqian* who subdued the Earl of Wind, the *Shengqing* who commanded the frost? What of the Western Qiang who rose with the tiger’s shadow, or the Xianbei who grew strong by riding turtles? What of Lin Yi, ruled by divine records, or Yong and Shu, whose emperors were floating corpses? What of the Salt God, who summoned insects to fly, or the slant-eyed people who transformed in the Jing Mountains? What of the five Ding who lured the serpent to topple the peaks, or the flesh that sprouted wings over the three seas?"

"Golden tablets and jade-script characters emerged beside the Well of Yu. The true mechanism balanced evenly, carved within the veins of patterned stone. All these marvels number in the thousands—unrecorded in the Five Classics, unspoken by Zhou or Kong. Can one still claim such things do not exist?

As for the southerners who enter pillars and exit through ears, the archer who stilled water in his elbow to draw the bow, Bohun who trod cliffs of ten thousand *ren* yet stood on tiptoe, Lü Liang who walked singing over the abyss, Duke Song who imitated leaves to deceive the eye, Gongshu whose wooden birds soared with dark wings, Li Zhu who discerned the tip of a hair at a hundred paces, Ben and Huo who displayed their strength of ten thousand *jun*, the Yue man who revived the dead with a needle’s touch, Shu Hai who leapt over distances of a thousand *li*, the Ying craftsman who swung his axe at the whitewash on a nose, Zhong Du who bared his body in freezing weather—these are all feats beyond the reach of Zhou or Kong. Can one still deny their existence?

If the sage truly has limits, then it is no wonder he does not attain immortality. Yet not attaining immortality does not diminish his sageliness. That the sage happens to be unversed in certain arts—how does this serve as grounds for criticism? The sage may choose to align with the ebb and flow, yielding to nature, possessing a body without selfishness, having life without scheming, entrusting survival or death to heaven, and length of days to fate. Thus, his not pursuing immortality is nothing strange." *Scroll Nine: The Meaning of the Dao*

The *Baopuzi* states: "The Dao encompasses heaven and earth, yet its root is nameless. If we speak of its non-being, even echoes and shadows are still something; if we speak of its being, even the myriad things are as nothing. Li Shou could not calculate its quantity, Li Zhu could not perceive its faintest trace, Wu Zhai and Jin Ye exhausted their hearing yet could not follow its sounds into the depths of darkness, while swift-footed beasts could not track its signs beyond the cosmos. Speaking of what is near, it circulates through the tip of an autumn hair and still has surplus; speaking of what is distant, it pervades the great void and yet is insufficient.

It is the sound of sounds, the echo of echoes, the form of forms, the shadow of shadows. The square attains stillness through it, the round attains movement; the descending attains humility, the ascending attains reverence. To forcibly name it 'Dao' is already to lose its truth—how much more so when it is sliced into a thousand parts, divided into ten thousand fragments, and given endless names? The farther one strays from the Dao, the more distant it becomes.

Vulgar people cannot recognize its primordial root but instead cultivate its decadent branches. If one can be tranquil and serene, unstained and unshaken, nurturing the heart with desirelessness and cultivating the spirit with purity, sweeping away allurements and returning to rectitude, casting aside thoughts of the unattainable and dispelling burdens that harm the true, moderating the excesses of joy and anger, and extinguishing the roots of love and aversion—then blessings will come unbidden, and misfortunes will depart without exorcism. Why? Because destiny lies within, not without; the Dao resides here, with no need to seek it elsewhere.

The trouble with ordinary people is their inability to preserve the true. Lacking restraint, they are swayed by desires and attachments, racing and fleeing without return. Emotions arise externally in response to things, while intellect spills over in engagement with affairs. Seduced by what is desirable, their heavenly principle is extinguished; deluded by what they see and hear, their purity is lost. The heart is enslaved by extravagance, the emotions muddied by turbulence—thus disasters of ruin and misfortunes of collapse arise. Yet they merely slaughter fattened beasts, pour libations of fine wine, strike gongs and beat drums, sing and dance, prostrate themselves in worship, and sit in vain supplication, begging for blessings in the hope of certain fulfillment. Even unto death, they do not awaken—is this not lamentable?

When the spirit is exhausted by disturbance and the *ying* and *wei* are depleted by exertion, when form and *qi* are scorched and the harmony of heaven is carved away, when labor and rest exceed their bounds—then, though one smashes one’s head in prayer, illness arises in the vital organs, and though one offers sacrifices to seek healing, the body is but a fragile vessel that brings these afflictions upon itself. How can heaven, earth, and the gods provide relief? What use are the slaughter of herds and the exhaustion of flocks?

Blessings are not obtained through obsequiousness, nor are misfortunes averted by sacrifices. If destiny could be prolonged by fervent prayer or illness dispelled by lavish offerings, then the wealthy would surely live forever, and the noble would never fall ill. Yet the gods do not accept sacrifices from those outside their lineage, nor do ghosts partake of improper rites. The alleys of servants cannot accommodate the golden chariots of emperors, nor can the gates of commoners summon the six-horse carriages of the noble. Though all are human, the gap between high and low is absolute—how much more so for the gods, who are distant, pure, and exalted, belonging to a different order entirely? Their nobility is supreme. Clearly, the wine and food of rats and the bowing of the vulgar cannot move them.

Disloyalty and unfiliality are the greatest of sins. Even with bribes of a thousand gold and feasts of the finest cattle, one cannot obtain a good name from a wise ruler or absolve guilt before the state. If men cannot absolve men, how can one expect to gain longevity, which is harder to attain than a good name, or cure grave illness, which is harder to remove than guilt? The gods are of a different kind, favoring only the upright—hoping for their partiality is futile.

Even a ruler ashamed of his virtue or a minister who endures disgrace can reward goodness without relying on wealth and punish evil without bias, adhering strictly to the rule of law without favoritism. How much more so for the gods, who are far beyond such concerns? They cannot be moved by clever words or swayed by adorned bribes—this much is clear.

King Ling of Chu personally performed as a shaman, sparing no beasts in sacrifice, yet could not repel the Wu army’s invasion. The Prince of Guangling revered Li Xu, exhausting his treasury, yet could not escape the punishment for rebellion. Emperor Wu of Han was especially devoted to the gods, offering sacrifices to all without exception, yet could not avoid his death at Five Oaks. The Lord of Sun honored Hua Xiang with a royal title, yet could not extend his allotted lifespan. It was not that the sacrifices were insufficient or the jades and silks inadequate; their faith was not insincere, nor their reverence lacking. They suffered great losses but gained not the slightest benefit—was this not because they neglected what was near and pursued what was distant?

When Gong Di exterminated the wayward Daoists, he lived long and prospered; when the Prefect of Lujiang abolished mountain sacrifices, his blessings and fortune endured; when Wen Weng destroyed the temple of the water spirit, he and the people thrived; when Cao Cao banned excessive sacrifices, great fortune followed. These past events serve as a mirror for the future.

Virtue alone is fragrant; those without worries live long. Those who treasure their essence do not die young, while those who indulge in excess age prematurely—this is the natural principle. What can external things do? If one’s cultivation is imbalanced and one’s depletion unrelieved, a hundred ailments take root, the *ying* and *wei* are exhausted, and one remains unaware—what use are the finest cattle and three sacrificial beasts?

The practices of the vulgar are mostly deceptive, deceiving one another and growing ever more deluded over time. Unable to cure their illnesses or awaken from their great delusion, they neglect the remedies of medicine and stone, instead clinging to the folly of prayers and sacrifices. They beseech endlessly, consult oracles tirelessly, while shamans and petty sorcerers fabricate tales of misfortune and calamity. In times of grave illness, they hear every kind of absurdity and act upon it, spending without restraint. The rich exhaust their wealth, the poor borrow at exorbitant interest, their fields and homes are divided and lost, their chests and cabinets emptied—yet to what avail?"

At times, when illness happens to subside, they claim it a boon from the gods; when death comes, they say the spirits have shown no mercy. If by chance one survives, their wealth is utterly depleted, and they soon perish from hunger and cold. Some turn to robbery and plunder, others to burglary and excess, losing their lives to the blade’s edge or falling into the disgrace of punishment—all arising from these very causes.

Or their possessions are exhausted by the costs of sacrifices, their silks and fabrics squandered on greedy, corrupt shamans. When they die, there is no money left for funeral implements or proper shrouds, leaving their corpses to rot and be devoured by worms—how truly lamentable!

The blindness of the foolish people has reached such extremes! Lewd sacrifices and heretical practices are forbidden by ritual law and statute. Yet the common folk remain forever unenlightened. Only a ruler should strengthen the legal system, imposing the death penalty without regard for severity, and offering rewards for the capture of shamans and sorcerers who refuse to cease. Execute them publicly in the streets, and in no time, such practices will surely be eradicated. This would cut off the source of hunger and cold for the people, stifle the rise of theft and banditry—no small benefit.

In the past, there were men like Zhang Jiao, Liu Gen, Wang Xin, and Li Shen, who claimed millennial longevity, feigned minor arts, and boasted of instant vanishing or shape-shifting to deceive the masses. They gathered the foolish, yet neither sought to prolong life nor to cure illness. Instead, they assembled wicked factions, plotted rebellion, and brought ruin upon themselves. Some even slaughtered the innocent or swindled the people for profit

When local officials harbor doubts about the existence of spirits, fearing that suppression might invite misfortune or curses, even if some privately hold such suspicions, they fail to act decisively. Moreover, this is not a pressing duty of their office, nor an urgent matter of performance evaluation. Yet their foolish wives and stubborn children cling to such beliefs, while petty subordinates all echo that it must not be done. With many voices of obstruction, and no sincere conviction behind them, those who counsel caution speak in unison. Thus, hesitation prevails, and none dare act—this is truly a cause for clenched fists and seething frustration.

I have personally known several men who paid no reverence to the gods, never once offering prayers or sacrifices in their lives, yet they lived to a ripe old age, attained lofty rank and renown, and saw their descendants flourish in both wealth and nobility. As for myself, I have no business with such matters, observing only the seasonal rites for my ancestors.

I have traveled by land and water for tens of thousands of *li*, passing no fewer than a hundred shrines and temples along the roadsides. Yet on every journey, I passed them by without a glance, and my carriages and horses never met with overturning, nor did I encounter storms or waves when crossing rivers. Though I have repeatedly faced epidemics, I have always had the benefit of medicine; though I have frequently braved arrows and blades, I have fortunately suffered no wounds or injuries. This only deepens my conviction that ghosts and spirits are powerless.

Among the over a hundred kinds of heterodox cults, all engage in blood sacrifices and slaughter, with only the Way of the Li family being slightly less extreme. Yet though they do not kill animals themselves, they still prepare offerings for blessings, with no limit to the quantities. Whatever is purchased from the market must be abundant and lavish, and fine delicacies cannot be omitted. Sometimes their kitchens employ dozens of people, and the expenses are considerable. They are hardly models of frugality and restraint, and thus should also be banned.

Someone asked when the Way of the Li family originated. I replied: During the reign of Sun Quan, the Great Emperor of Wu, there was a man in Shu named Li A who lived in a cave and ate nothing. He was said to have been seen across generations, earning the title "Lord of Eight Hundred Years." People often sought his counsel, but Li A never spoke—he only divined by reading their expressions. If his countenance was cheerful, all matters would be auspicious.

If his expression was somber and sorrowful, all matters would be inauspicious; if Li A smiled, there would be great fortune; if he sighed faintly, there would be deep sorrow. Such omens never once failed. Later, one day, he suddenly vanished, and no one knew his whereabouts.

Afterward, a man named Li Kuan from Shu arrived in Wu, speaking the Shu dialect. He could heal illnesses by chanting over water, and many recovered. Thus, people far and near unanimously declared Kuan to be Li A, calling him "Li of Eight Hundred Years," though in truth he was not. From high officials to commoners, none failed to flock to his door. Later, he grew arrogant and haughty, no longer granting regular audiences. Visitors could only bow at his outer gate before departing—such was his strange and extraordinary behavior. Those seeking to evade corvée labor and taxes often became his disciples, numbering nearly a thousand. Yet those who advanced to higher teachings and entered his inner circle learned nothing more than chanting over water, the Three Passes talismans, and guiding the circulation of solar and lunar *qi*—no essential methods for cultivating the body, no divine elixirs for ingestion, no techniques for prolonging life or halting death. Swallowing *qi* and abstaining from grains could sustain one for a hundred days at most, but not for long—such was the shallowness of his arts.

Many of my acquaintances who had met Kuan attested that he grew frail and emaciated with age, coughing and sighing as he moved, his eyes dim, his ears deaf, his teeth fallen, his hair white. Gradually, his mind weakened, and he would forget his own children and grandchildren, no different from an ordinary person. Yet people still claimed Kuan feigned infirmity to deceive them—could this truly be so?

Once, Wu suffered a great epidemic, and over half the population perished. Kuan’s place of Daoist practice was called a "hut," and he too contracted a fever. Claiming he was entering the hut to fast and purify himself, he died within it. Yet his followers still insisted he had transformed his form and achieved *shijie* transcendence, not truly died. The essence of the divine immortals’ arts lies precisely in their distinction from ordinary people—namely, their agelessness and deathlessness. Now, Kuan aged and died like any other—his failure to attain the Dao was plainly evident. What doubt could remain?

If he were truly a *shijie* immortal, why did he not remain in the world for a century or two, preserving his youth before departing? The Dao of immortality is not nonexistent; Kuan simply was not its rightful practitioner. I have discussed this in such detail because Kuan’s disciples, passing down his teachings, have spread across the lands south of the Yangtze, numbering in the thousands. Unaware of the shallowness of his methods, they cling to them in hopes of transcending the world. My aim is to awaken them from their delusion.

The world abounds with things that seem true but are false. Let me briefly recount a few tales to enlighten those who fail to discern the truth.

Long ago in Runan, a man set a snare in his field to catch a roebuck. When he returned, he found one trapped, though he had not noticed. A passerby saw it, stole the roebuck, and left, but feeling guilty, he placed a salted fish in the snare before departing. The field’s owner returned, found the fish, and marveled, deeming it a divine omen. Afraid to take it home, he left it there. Word spread through the village, and soon they built a shrine, naming it the "Shrine of the Salted Fish Lord." Devotees multiplied, adorning the shrine with red pillars and painted beams, its bells and drums never ceasing. When the occasional illness was cured, they attributed it to the deity’s power. Travelers passing by never failed to make offerings. For seven or eight years, this continued until the fishmonger happened to pass the shrine. When told the story, he laughed and said, "That is my salted fish—what divinity is there?" And so the worship ceased.

Another tale: In Nandun, a man named Zhang Zhu was plowing a white field when he found a plum sapling. Feeling pity for it, he dug it up, intending to take it home, but left in haste before securing it. He wrapped its roots in damp soil and placed it in the hollow of a mulberry tree, then forgot about it. Later, Zhang took a distant post and was away for some time. When villagers saw a plum tree suddenly growing in the mulberry, they called it a miracle. A man with an eye ailment rested in its shade and prayed, "If the Plum Lord can heal my eyes, I will offer a piglet in thanks." His eyes happened to improve, so he slaughtered a piglet and made the offering. The story grew exaggerated, and soon people claimed the tree could restore sight to the blind. From far and near, they came seeking blessings, their carriages and horses filling the roads, their wine and meat flowing like a river. This continued for years until Zhang returned from his post. Seeing the commotion, he said, "This is the plum sapling I once planted—what divinity is there?" He cut it down, and the worship ceased.

In Runan, the Peng family tomb stood near a main road. At its entrance was a stone statue. One day, an old peasant woman returning from the market with several cakes sought shade under the tomb’s trees to escape the heat. She placed her cakes on the statue’s head and left, forgetting to retrieve them. A passerby saw the cakes atop the statue’s head and asked about them. Someone claimed the statue was a deity who could cure illnesses, and those healed would offer cakes in thanks. The story spread, and soon people said rubbing the statue’s head cured headaches, rubbing its belly cured stomachaches, and rubbing oneself after touching it cured all ailments. From a thousand *li* away, they came to seek healing. At first, they offered chickens and pigs; later, they sacrificed oxen and sheep, erecting tents and playing music without end. This continued for years until the old woman who had forgotten the cakes heard of it. She explained the truth, and the crowds ceased coming.

West of Luoyang stood an ancient tomb, its walls crumbling and filled with water. The tomb contained much lime, and its water was known to heal sores. In summer, travelers with festering wounds, overcome by heat, would bathe in the tomb’s clear water, and their sores would miraculously heal.

Hearing of this, all those afflicted with ailments went to bathe themselves, and some even drank the water to cure internal illnesses. The residents living near the tomb then built a shrine at the site and sold this water. Those who came to buy it also constantly offered sacrifices at the shrine, with wine and meat never ceasing. As the number of buyers grew, the water was exhausted, so the sellers would secretly fetch water from elsewhere at night to replenish it. Travelers from afar who could not make the journey would entrust messengers to purchase it on their behalf, carrying vessels along their way. Thus, the water-sellers grew immensely wealthy. Some later claimed there was no divine power at work, and officials issued prohibitions, filling and sealing the tomb, putting an end to it.

In Xinggu, a prefectural governor of the Ma clan had a relative or old friend who sought his aid. Ma ordered this person to stay outside the city, falsely claiming he was a divine immortal or Daoist adept whose treatments cured all illnesses instantly. He then sent eloquent persuaders to travel about, spreading exaggerated rumors that he could restore sight to the blind and enable the lame to walk. From all directions, people flocked to him like a marketplace, and his wealth in money and silk soon piled up like a mountain. He further instructed those seeking treatment that even if they were not immediately cured, they must tell others they had been healed; only then would they truly recover. If they admitted to others they were not yet cured, they would never recover—such was the principle of the Daoist method, and it could not be doubted. Thus, when later arrivals asked those who had come before, the earlier visitors always claimed to have been cured, and none dared say otherwise. Within ten days, he had amassed great wealth.

Most people, though cunning in small matters, are foolish in great ones. They dismiss methods for extending life and achieving longevity as empty falsehoods, yet eagerly believe in malevolent spirits and ghosts, dancing and praying to them. The so-called divine beings are all of the same kind as Ma’s deception. I record these few cases as a warning to those who have not yet awakened.

Someone asked: “In the world, there are those who possess no knowledge of the Dao, arts, or techniques, yet live in peace and enjoy long life. Why is this?”

Gebuzi replied: "Those of this kind may possess hidden virtue and good deeds that bring them blessings; or their allotted lifespan is inherently long, thus delaying aging and death; or they may simply be fortunate enough to avoid misfortune by chance. This is like the birds and beasts that escape the hunter’s path, or the grass and trees left unburnt after a great fire has passed. To guard against harm and protect oneself, one must cultivate the defenses of preserving form, wear the talismans of celestial patterns, and bear the sword of the heavens. Sacrificial prayers are of no benefit—one should rely on one’s own inviolability, not on the forbearance of ghosts and spirits.

Yet contemplating the mysterious and holding to unity, embracing the luminous and encircling the body, can ward off evil and dispel misfortune, though it cannot extend life or cure bodily illness. Those who entrust themselves to nature without any arts may indeed live out their allotted years, but when confronted by the sudden violence of malevolent spirits or the ravages of great epidemics, they will have no means to resist.

Storing armor and helmets, hoarding raincoats and straw hats—these are prepared for war and rain. If, by good fortune, there is no conflict and the skies remain clear, then having them or not makes little difference. But when arrows and stones fill the air like mist, and flying blades clash in the smoke, the plight of the unarmored becomes plain. When torrential rains swell the rivers and endless snow blankets the sky, the suffering of those standing exposed is evident.

One cannot dismiss the great forces of yin and yang on the grounds that shepherd’s purse and wheat are small and fragile, nor mislead latecomers to the Way by claiming that the arts are of no benefit." *Scroll Ten: Clarifying the Root*

One inquired about the precedence of Confucianism and Daoism. *Baopuzi* replied: "The Dao is the root of Confucianism; Confucianism is the branch of the Dao. The arts of yin and yang, with their multitude of taboos, make men timid and constrained. Confucianism is broad but lacks essentials, laborious yet yields little fruit. Mohism is frugal but hard to follow, impossible to practice universally. Legalism is severe and unkind, damaging to benevolence and righteousness. Only the teachings of the Daoist school enable one to unify the spirit, act in accord with the formless, embrace the virtues of Confucianism and Mohism, and distill the essentials of fame and law. Adapting to the times, responding to change, its principles are concise and clear, its affairs few yet its achievements many. Its aim lies in preserving the great simplicity of the primordial root and guarding the true source of correctness.

Yet Ban Gu, following Sima Qian’s placement of the *Huang-Lao* before the Six Classics, called Qian’s arrangement mistaken. Qian’s learning was vast, his synthesis of the obscure and hidden thorough; he sifted the good and bad of events, verified the uprightness or depravity of the ancients. His critiques were grounded in nature, his praise and blame aligned with the highest principle. He neither flattered nor concealed, nor echoed convention for its own sake. Liu Xiang, a man of extraordinary learning for his age, called it a truthful record. Ban Gu’s critique, then, cannot be taken as authoritative. Gu was indeed a pure Confucian, unversed in the meaning of the Dao, and thus, clinging to his own biases, could not judge fairly.

What is called the Dao—is it merely the art of nourishing life? The *Book of Changes* says: 'The Dao of Heaven is established as yin and yang; the Dao of Earth is established as softness and hardness; the Dao of Man is established as benevolence and righteousness.' It also says: 'The *Changes* contains the four Daoist paths of the sage; if the man is not fit, the Dao will not manifest.' Moreover, in an age of flourishing peace, it is called 'having the Dao'; in a state of peril and a disordered ruler, it is called 'lacking the Dao.' To sit and discourse on the Dao is the role of the Three Dukes; when the state possesses the Dao, the poor and lowly feel shame. In all cases, the term 'Dao'—from the Two Modes above to the myriad things below—none do not proceed from it. Only the *Huang-Lao* school grasps its root, while Confucianism and Mohism manage its branches.

Now, when the world speaks of those who possess the Dao, it refers to those broadly learned in past and present, able to look up and observe, look down and examine, traverse change and penetrate the subtle, comprehend the workings of rise and fall, master the principles of order and chaos, whose minds hold no confusion, whose answers leave no question unmet. Why must they necessarily cultivate the arts of longevity or admire the martial prowess of Master Red Pine and Wang Qiao? Yet scholars with narrow vision, making blind judgments and speaking in ignorance, when they hear of those dwelling in the mountains and forests, following the teachings of Bo Yang, they slander and ridicule them, saying, 'That is but a minor path, not worth reckoning.' Alas! They are like those who hold a firefly’s light within four walls, never beholding the brilliance of the heavens."

Those who keep company with gudgeons and shrimp in the shallows of a mere stream know not the vastness of the four seas; those who prize the depth of rivers and streams yet do not recognize that their waters issue from Kunlun; those who treasure the harvest of millet and grain yet remain unaware that their ripening comes from the fertile earth.

Now if one knows only to exalt the Confucian arts yet does not understand that their completion arises from the Dao—this Dao is that by which the hundred schools are cultivated, the Two Modes are cast, the ten thousand kinds are conceived, and the constant norms are fermented. In the world, the shallow and near are many, while the deep and far are few; the few cannot overcome the many—this has been so since ancient times. Thus, though Sima Qian was great, he was not praised; though Ban Gu was lesser, he was not censured.

Yet things are valued for their rarity and disdained for their abundance—how could human affairs alone be otherwise? Thus, coarse herbs blanket the plains while the sacred fungus appears but once in an age; thorns and brambles cover the wilds while towering trees stand solitary among them; sand and gravel are measureless while pearls and jade are exceedingly rare; geese and falcons swarm in flight while phoenixes seldom appear; vipers and lizards fill the marshes while dragons are seldom seen.

Ban’s generation was partisan—this was only fitting.

The Dao, internally, governs the self; externally, it governs the state. It enables the seven luminaries to follow their courses, the two qi to declare harmony, the four seasons to keep their cycles of cold and warmth, wind and rain to refrain from violent calamity. The jade candle manifests the signs of ascendant peace; the limpid wine reveals the tokens of virtue’s fullness. The burning wheel and rainbow banners restrain their ominousness; the crumbling clouds and Shangyang birds fold their wings. Radiance shines on high, fine grain ripens in full, pestilence does not spread, misfortune and disorder do not arise, ramparts are not raised, shields and spears are not wielded. Rightness is achieved without deliberation, trust without covenant, solidity without binding, success without planning, encouragement without reward, solemnity without punishment, attainment without seeking, cessation without prohibition. One dwells above yet the people do not find it burdensome; one stands before yet the people do not find it troublesome. The proclamation has not been issued yet customs shift; the command has not been given yet mores change—this is the Dao’s governance of the world.

Thus, when the Dao flourishes, the Three August Ones and Five Emperors ruled with folded arms and had abundance to spare. When the Dao declines, later generations gallop in haste yet remain insufficient. Only when there is abundance can transformation be beautiful without action. Only when there is insufficiency do punishments grow severe and treachery multiply. The common folk grumble below; the august spirits rage above. Sometimes great waves surge across the land; sometimes scorching droughts parch the earth; sometimes mountains and valleys shift their forms; sometimes winter thunders and summer snows; sometimes blood flows and floats shields, corpses pile high as hills; sometimes tens of thousands surrender and are buried alive, bones are split and children exchanged. The higher the walls, the more ingenious the siege engines; the deeper the moats, the more cunning the ladders. Laws and decrees grow clearer yet bandits and thieves multiply; oaths and pacts grow more frequent yet rebellions and disorders worsen. It is like waves churning and fish and turtles stirring in the depths, fine nets tightening and winged birds growing restless in the marshes, jackals and wolves multiplying and beasts fleeing in terror through the forests, or a fierce fire blazing and small fish scorching in the cauldron.

There have been cases where ruler and minister exchanged places, where father and son turned blades upon each other. Only then is loyalty and righteousness given name in a imperiled state; only then does the filial son earn praise in a ruined household. Plagues arise and shamans and physicians grow esteemed; the Dao and virtue are lost and the Confucians and Mohists gain weight. From this perspective, the order of precedence between the Confucians and the Daoists can be settled.

Someone asked: “In ancient times, Red Pine, Wang Qiao, Qin Gao, Laozi, Peng Zu, and Wu Cheng Yu Hua—all true persons—served in the world and did not withdraw into seclusion. Yet from the middle ages onward, those who pursue the Dao invariably drift away, vanishing into hidden retreat. Why is this?”

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: “In the distant past, purity and simplicity prevailed, and craft and deceit had not yet sprouted. Those who believed in the Dao diligently studied it; those who did not believe remained silent. Slanderous words were not spoken, and malicious intent did not lodge in the breast. Thus, true persons moved leisurely among the people and were not hasty in ascending to transcendence.

In these latter days, customs are shallow and thin, and artifice and falsity run deep. The transformation of the mysterious and tranquil has been abandoned, while the factions of perverse customs have multiplied. Since people do not believe in the Dao, they delight in slander and defamation, calling the true and upright ‘heretical and deceptive,’ and labeling the divine immortals ‘absurd and delusional.’ Some say they ‘bewilder the masses,’ others that they ‘disrupt the community.’ Thus, superior persons feel shame in dwelling among them.

The perspicacious of old guarded against the gradual and subtle, departing at the first sign of trouble, not waiting for dawn if they saw it at night, rising at the first hint, not lingering till day’s end. Thus, when Zhao harmed Ming Du, Confucius turned his carriage; when sweet wine was not set out, Mu Sheng departed at once. When the many outnumbered the few, Hua Yuan left. How much more so for the wise and discerning, whose calling fundamentally differs—what attachment could keep them in such a place?

When the deep pools dry up, the jiao-dragons do not swim; when nests topple and eggs are gathered, the phoenixes do not alight. When reckless words fill the chamber, the soaring gulls do not descend; when common plants are cut in spring, the sacred fungi do not bloom. When the vulgar despise the upright and insults and humiliations draw near, how can those of the Dao fail to spread their wings beyond the winds and clouds and swiftly conceal their tracks in the mysterious wilderness?

The mountains and forests do not inherently possess the Dao, yet those who pursue the Dao invariably enter the mountains and forests. Truly, they seek to distance themselves from the rank and foul and draw near to this purity and stillness. Those who enter the nine chambers to refine their thoughts and preserve the True Unity to summon the spirits do not delight in clamor or mix with filth. Those who compound the great elixir of golden cinnabar and refine the flying essences of the eight minerals particularly shun the foolish chatter of glib tongues and the perceptions of the vulgar. The enlightened spirits do not descend; the immortal medicines do not form—this is no minor prohibition. If one remains among people, shallow-minded officials may slander and punish them, or relatives and old friends may visit, entangling them in celebrations and condolences. It is far better to retreat into seclusion and escape such ‘stinking rats.’

Those who wander alone in the distant reaches, finding joy in the lofty peaks—do they not have their reasons?

Some say: ‘Superior persons attain the Dao amidst the three armies; middling persons attain the Dao in the cities; inferior persons attain the Dao in the mountains and forests.’ This refers to those whose immortal medicines have already formed but who do not yet wish to ascend to heaven. Though they may be among the three armies, blades cannot harm them; though they may be in the cities, human calamities cannot befall them. Inferior persons have not yet reached this stage, so they remain in the mountains and forests. It does not mean that those of the highest rank, when first learning the Dao, should remain among the three armies or in the cities to attain it. If that were so, the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi would not have departed to this day.”

Someone asked: “The Dao is the source and root; Confucianism is the branch and stream—this I have heard. But what of the minor differences between them today?”

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: “The teachings of rising and bowing, the rituals of the three thousand refinements, the arts of attack and defense, the techniques of advancing and retreating, the ethos of lightening the body and valuing righteousness, the affairs of joy and sorrow in rites and music, the strategies of ordering the world and aiding the customs—these are the concerns of the Confucians.”

The Daoist discards external things and abandons cunning, washes away scheming and deception, forgets wealth and disdains nobility, blocks all inducements and dissuasions, feels no distress in poverty nor glory in success, no sorrow in slander nor joy in praise—these are the pursuits of the Daoists. The Confucians perform sacrifices to pray for blessings, while the Daoists tread the upright path to ward off evil. What the Confucians cherish is power and profit; what the Daoists treasure is desirelessness. The Confucians toil ceaselessly for fame and gain, while the Daoists embrace the One to cultivate themselves in solitude. What the Confucians expound are the minutiae of administrative records; what the Daoists practice are the teachings and admonitions of dispelling the passions.

The Daoist, in action, excels at self-cultivation to accomplish tasks; in repose, excels at claiming what others do not contend for; in governance, excels at cutting off misfortune before it arises; in bestowal, excels at aiding others without claiming virtue; in movement, excels at observing the people to employ the mind; in stillness, excels at dwelling in caution without vexation. This is why it is the sovereign of the hundred schools and the ancestor of benevolence and righteousness. The minor differences between them are thus compared, and the rise and fall of their fortunes have never altered.”

Someone said: “The Confucians follow Zhou and Kong; their texts are the Six Classics. They are the very means by which the world is ordered and uprightness preserved, the standard by which one conducts oneself and acts. Their use is far-reaching and their enterprise noble, their affairs grand and their words refined. They are the unchanging institutions for states and families. As for those who practice the Dao, they neglect ritual and teaching, disregard the great ethical bonds, consort with foxes and badgers in the marshes, pair with apes and gibbons in the forests, cast out and forsaken, dwelling beside trees and stones—this is like fleeing eastward in confusion or forgetting the sweetness of sunflowers.”

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: “To flaunt ornament and gallop in splendor is not what the plain and honest esteem. To dispel ignorance and resolve confusion is what I have long been sated with. Truly, I do not wish to contend with you over the goodness of principles or debate gains and losses at the tip of the tongue. Yet to see a child fall into a well and not feel the impulse of benevolence, to watch a blind man strike a pillar and not call it an act of universal love—is this not so? Let me but sketch an outline and roughly defend one corner.

Those who embody the Dao to shape things and treasure virtue to attain longevity follow the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. The Yellow Emperor was able to govern the world and bring about great peace, and he also ascended as an immortal—how can he be deemed inferior to Yao and Shun? Laozi both synthesized ritual and teaching and attained long life—how can he be said to fall short of Zhou and Kong? Thus Confucius sighed in emulation, yet never uttered a word of disparagement. But later generations of mediocre men, unable to enter its gate, practice Confucianism and Mohism while slandering Daoism—how is this different from descendants cursing their ancestors? They do not recognize their own origins; this is truly excessive.

The hand of a dwarf cannot topple Mount Song or Mount Hua; the shin of a pygmy cannot fathom the depths of the sea. Yet I often see vulgar Confucians clinging to their stumps, busily practicing what they do not broadly understand, instructing the stubborn and commanding the perverse, adorning their vile words, slandering Daoism. When they speak of the dregs of糟粕, they do so as if watching a swift horse pass through a crack; when they wade into the abyss of the spirit, they drown and lose themselves. Like a sparrow flapping its short wings to ride the waves of the sunlit sea, or a fly straining its feeble strength to scale the peaks of the gibbon, they undertake what they cannot bear and only hasten their own exhaustion. Yet they chatter on within their narrow confines, their hearing unable to reach the vast, their sight unable to penetrate the distant. They would stretch on tiptoe to encompass the Three Luminaries, puff out their bellies to stir the thunder spirits—are they not deluded?

Only by ascending the distant turning sphere of the celestial mechanism does one know the depths of the well and valley; only by beholding the great brightness adorning the heavens does one recognize the meanness of the wren’s nest. I was not born with this knowledge, nor did I believe it from youth. At first, I too was benighted, just like you. But after witnessing the vast cultivation of its profound mysteries, I regretted not escaping my confinement sooner.

The affairs of the Five Classics are illuminated by commentaries and expositions; even beginners need not be perplexed. How much more so with the golden tablets and jade slips of the immortals’ scriptures, the essential words of which are often not written down? Only after ascending the altar and swearing blood oaths are the oral instructions transmitted. If one is not the right person, even if the land were split and cities joined, halls filled with gold and jade, they would not be casually shown.

To point to the profound and return to the distant, even if one obtains the texts but does not receive instruction from a master, it is like looking up and not seeing the head, looking down and not knowing the feet—how could you, sir, be thoroughly versed in this? Those who attain immortality may ascend to the Great Clarity, soar through the Purple Empyrean, journey to the Dark Continent, or dwell on Fusang. They listen to the music of the Celestial Hook, feast on the ninefold fungus, go forth bearing pine and cassia beyond the inverted shadows, enter to banquet in the Jasper Chambers within the Eternal Sun. Why should they consort with foxes and badgers or pair with apes and gibbons?

This is what is called acting without knowledge. As for the Dao, it roams freely through the rainbow’s arc, soars through the cinnabar clouds, traverses the six voids—wherever the mind wills. To be cast out and forsaken is not a cause for sorrow. Though sacrificial beasts gather in herds, adorned in embroidered finery, in terms of joy, how does this compare to the solitary departure of the carefree unicorn or the felicitous splendor of the auspicious light breaking from its mate?

*Scroll Eleven: Immortal Medicines*

Ge Hong said: The *Divine Farmer’s Four Classics* states that superior medicines bring bodily peace and extend life, enabling one to ascend as a celestial immortal, roam above and below, command the myriad spirits, grow feathers and fur, and summon traveling kitchens at will. It further says that the Five Fungi, as well as cinnabar, jade slips, malachite, realgar, orpiment, mica, and Great Unity’s Yu Remnant Grain, may each be taken alone, all granting the power of flight and long life. It also states that middle-grade medicines nourish the nature, while lower-grade medicines dispel illness, ensuring that venomous creatures do not strike, fierce beasts do not attack, malign qi does not spread, and all malevolent spirits are repelled.

The *Filial Classic’s Divine Concordance* says: Sichuan pepper and ginger ward off dampness, sweet flag sharpens hearing, sesame prolongs years, and *weixi* repels weapons. These are the ultimate words of the supreme sages and the true records of esoteric arts, their luminous texts resplendent—yet the world’s people ultimately do not believe them. How lamentable!

Among immortal medicines, cinnabar ranks first, followed by gold, then silver, then the various fungi, then the Five Jades, then mica, then luminous pearls, then realgar, then Great Unity’s Yu Remnant Grain, then stone’s yellow essence, then stone cassia, then quartz, then stone brain, then sulfur, then stone terrace, then malachite, then pine and cypress resin, *fuling*, rehmannia, ophiopogon, sesame, *chonglou*, coptis, pyrrosia, paper mulberry fruit, and *xiangchai*—also called *tuolu*. Some call it the Immortal’s Staff, others the Queen Mother of the West’s Staff, or Heavenly Essence, or Anti-Aging, or Earth Bone, or *gouqi*.

Asparagus root may be called Earth Asparagus, or *yan* Asparagus, or Thorny Bramble, or Goat’s Delight, or Pipe Pine. That which grows in high places has short roots with a sweet taste and fragrant qi and is superior. That which grows by water or in low ground has slender leaves resembling chives but slightly yellow, long roots with a bitter taste, and foul qi and is inferior. It also tends to cause qi to descend, and its benefits manifest more slowly. After a hundred days of consumption, its effects surpass those of *zhu* and Solomon’s seal. When entering the mountains, it may be steamed or boiled and eaten to sate hunger, allowing one to cease grain consumption. If one has the means, it may also be taken as a powder or its juice extracted to make wine, with the powder being especially effective.

The people of Chu call asparagus root *baibu*, though there is also a *baibu* herb whose roots number in the hundreds, all resembling one another, though their sprouts differ slightly.
