True *baibu* resembles *baxie* and is used only to treat coughs and kill lice—it is not suitable for consumption and must not be mistaken for asparagus. Similarly, Solomon’s seal is also called *bai ji*, but it is not the *bai ji* used to make paste. According to the *Materia Medica*, many herbs share names with others, and only those with deep knowledge can distinguish them—one cannot afford to be careless.

Solomon’s seal is also called Rabbit Bamboo, Salvation from Poverty, or Hanging Pearl. Its flowers are more potent than its fruit, and its fruit more potent than its roots, though flowers are difficult to obtain in quantity. Gathering ten *hu* of fresh flowers yields only five or six *dou* when dried, and the daily dose is three *ge*—only those with great resources can manage this. Consuming Solomon’s seal for a full decade is required to reap its full benefits. Though it is inferior to *zhu* for ceasing grain consumption, *zhu* makes one robust and capable of bearing heavy loads and traversing perilous terrain. Yet Solomon’s seal is sweeter and easier to eat, and in times of famine, it can sustain the old and young, though people cannot distinguish it and mistake it for dried rice.

The Five Fungi comprise stone fungi, wood fungi, herb fungi, flesh fungi, and mushroom fungi, each with a hundred or so varieties. Stone fungi include the stone-elephant fungus, which grows on coastal mountains and islands with rocky shores. Resembling a living creature with a head, tail, and four limbs, it clings to large stones, favoring lofty peaks and perilous cliffs, sometimes hanging upside down. Red ones resemble coral, white ones like suet, black ones like lacquer, green ones like kingfisher feathers, and yellow ones like purple gold—all are lustrous and translucent like solid ice. On dark nights, their glow can be seen from three hundred paces away. The largest weigh over ten *jin*, the smallest three or four *jin*. Without prolonged fasting, utmost purity, and the wearing of Laozi’s talismans or the Five Numinous Treasures of the Mountain-Entering Scripture, one cannot encounter these beings.

Upon seeing any fungus, first place the Mountain-Opening and Harm-Repelling Talisman upon it to prevent it from vanishing or transforming. Then, on an auspicious day, perform a sacrificial rite with wine and dried meat, pray, and gather it, approaching from the sun’s direction with Yu’s Pace while holding the breath. If one obtains the stone-elephant fungus, pound it thirty-six thousand times, take a square-inch spoonful thrice daily, and consume a full *jin* to attain a thousand years of life; ten *jin* grants ten thousand years. It may also be shared with others.

The jade-fat fungus grows on jade-bearing mountains, often in precarious places where jade sap flows. After ten thousand years, it congeals into a fungus resembling birds or beasts, its color ever-changing, though often like dark mountain jade or deep water jade. It is also crystal-clear like rock crystal. Ground into powder and mixed with heartless-grass juice, it turns to water. Drinking one *sheng* grants a thousand years of life.

The Seven Luminaries and Nine Radiance fungi are both stones, growing on high cliffs by water. Resembling plates or bowls no larger than a *chi* in diameter, they have stems and bases rising three or four *cun*, with seven holes called Seven Luminaries and nine holes called Nine Radiance. Their lights resemble stars, visible at night from over a hundred paces away, each distinct and separable but not combinable.

At the autumn equinox, one must await the moment to gather it. Pound it into powder and take a square-inch spoonful; upon entering the mouth, it swiftly warms the body, its five flavors sweet and fine. Consuming a full *jin* grants a thousand years of life, rendering the body luminous—where one dwells, even in darkness, it is as bright as moonlight, and one may see in the night.

The Stone Honey Fungus grows within the Stone Gate of Mount Shaoshi. Beyond the gate lies a deep ravine, impassable; when a stone is cast into it, its echo lingers for half a day. Over ten *zhang* from the gate stands a stone pillar, atop which rests an inverted stone canopy, roughly a *zhang* in height and diameter. One may observe the Stone Honey Fungus falling from the gate into the canopy, drop by drop, like the lingering drip from a roof after rain, each falling in its own time. Yet though the fungus falls without cease, the canopy never overflows. Above the gate, characters in tadpole script are carved, declaring: *"He who consumes a *dou* of Stone Honey Fungus shall live ten thousand years."* Daoist adepts have pondered the place, yet none can reach it—only by placing a bowl at the end of a sturdy bamboo or wooden pole might one catch it, though none have yet succeeded. Given the inscription, it is certain that in former ages, some must have obtained it.

The Stone Cassia Fungus grows in the stone caves of renowned mountains, resembling a cassia tree yet made of stone. Roughly a *chi* in height and a *chi* in diameter, it is luminous and pungent in taste, with branches and twigs. Pounded and consumed, a *jin* grants a thousand years of life.

The Stone-Yellow Essence is found everywhere, though Mount Qin in Qinshui abounds with it. When embedded in large stones, the stone remains perpetually moist. Striking it reveals layers—dozens deep—before reaching the essence. Within the stone, it appears red-yellow and fluid, like an egg yolk within its shell. It must be drunk immediately; if left, it hardens into stone and becomes unfit for consumption. The proper method is to drink it before it solidifies; if already hardened, it should be ground into powder. Breaking open a stone may yield as much as a *sheng* or as little as a few *ge*. It may be consumed all at once. Though one may not obtain much at a time, continuous consumption—totaling three *sheng*—grants a thousand years of life. The only hindrance is its scarcity.

The Stone-Brain Fungus grows within talc, resembling the Stone-Yellow Essence, though not all talc contains it. Breaking open a thousand or so large talc stones may yield but a single piece. When first split, it glows with five colors within the stone and moves of its own accord. Consuming a *sheng* grants a thousand years of life.

The Stone Sulfur Fungus is found on the Five Peaks, though Mount Ji has the most. Local lore holds that Xu You consumed it here to attain longevity, thus freeing his heart from the burdens of wealth and honor, and refusing Yao’s abdication. The Cinnabar of Stone is the red essence of stone, akin to sulfur. All seep from cliffs and banks—those still moist may be rolled into pills, while hardened ones may be taken as powder. There are one hundred and twenty such stone fungi, as recorded in the *Taiyi Jade Strategy* and *Changyu Inner Records*, too numerous to list in full.

As for Wood Fungi: when pine or cypress resin sinks into the earth for a thousand years, it transforms into *fuling*; after ten thousand years, a small plant grows atop it, resembling a lotus flower, called the Wood Might-and-Joy Fungus. It glows at night, is slippery to the touch, and does not burn when set aflame. Carried on one’s person, it repels weapons. If tied to a chicken among twelve others in a coop, and one shoots twelve arrows from twelve paces away, all other chickens will be struck, but the one bearing the Might-and-Joy Fungus remains unharmed. It must be gathered from the Gate of Life, dried in the shade during the six *jia* days, then ground into powder. Taking a square-inch spoonful thrice daily, consuming one whole fungus grants three thousand years of life.

The thousand-year-old *guamu* tree has roots resembling a seated person, seven *cun* long. When carved, it bleeds. Applying its sap to the soles of the feet allows one to walk upon water without sinking; applying it to the nose enables one to enter water, which parts to allow dwelling at the riverbed. Applied to the body, it renders one invisible; to reappear, one need only wipe it away. It may also cure illness: for internal ailments, scrape and consume a *daogui*; for external swelling and pain, scrape a *daogui* and apply it to the affected area, and the ailment vanishes at once. If the left foot is afflicted, scrape and apply the sap to another’s left foot. When scraped and mixed with *jusheng* to make a torch, it illuminates the earth at night; where gold, jade, or treasures lie buried, the light turns green and dips downward—digging with a spade reveals them. Consuming ten *jin* of its powder grants a thousand years of life.

The branches of a three-thousand-year-old pine tree contain concentrated resin, shaped like a dragon, called the Flying Node Fungus. The largest weigh ten *jin*; consuming ten *jin* of its powder grants five hundred years of life.

There is also the *fantao* fungus, its wood resembling an ascending dragon, its flowers and leaves like red silk, its fruit like a kingfisher. No taller than five *chi*, it grows on the shady side of renowned mountains, in soil where east-flowing springs emerge. It must be gathered at the time of the Beginning of Summer. Pounded and consumed, a whole plant grants five thousand years of life.

The Threefold Fungus is red and luminous; when struck, its branches and leaves ring like metal or stone. Broken, it may be rejoined, returning to its original state. The Wood Channel Fungus grows as a parasite on large trees, resembling a lotus flower, nine stems in a cluster, its taste sweet and pungent. The fruit of the *jianmu* fungus grows in Duguang, its bark like a *yingshe* snake, its fruit like a *luan* bird. Consuming any of these three fungi allows one to ascend to heaven in broad daylight.

The Yellow Reed Seed, the *xunmu* Blossom, and the Dark Liquid Blossom—these three fungi grow in Yaoxiang and Fenghao on Mount Tai. Consuming them grants a thousand years of life.

The Yellow Sandalwood Fungus grows for a thousand years, its yellow roots extending beneath the tree like a three-*hu* vessel, connected to the main trunk one or two *zhang* away by fine roots resembling threads. Pounded and consumed, a single specimen grants earthly immortality and deathlessness.

This group comprises one hundred and twenty varieties, each depicted in illustrations.

Among the herbaceous fungi is the Solitary Swaying Fungus, which stirs without wind. Its stem is as thick as a finger, crimson as cinnabar, with plain leaves resembling amaranth. Its root bears a large bulb as large as a *dou*, and smaller ones like chicken eggs—twelve in number—encircling the main root on all four sides like the twelve earthly branches, each a *zhang* apart, all linked by fine roots like white hair. It grows upon high mountains and in deep valleys, where no other grasses grow nearby. Pounded and consumed, the large bulb grants a thousand years of life; a single small bulb grants a hundred years and may be shared with others. Carrying the large root renders one invisible; to reappear, turn left and emerge. The Ox-Horn Fungus grows on Tiger Longevity Mountain and Wu Slope, resembling scallions but singular like an ox horn, three or four *chi* in length, blue-green in color. Consuming a square *cun* spoonful thrice daily for a hundred days grants a thousand years of life. The Dragon Ascension Fungus resembles ascending dragons bearing one another, its leaves forming scales, its roots coiled like a dragon. Consuming one specimen grants a thousand years. The Hemp Mother Fungus resembles hemp but with a red stem and purple flowers. The Purple Pearl Fungus has yellow flowers, red leaves, and fruit like plums but purple, with twenty-four branches linked and hanging like strung pearls. The White Talisman Fungus stands four or five *chi* tall, resembling a plum tree, blooming in heavy snow and bearing fruit in late winter. The Vermilion Grass Fungus has nine bends, each bend bearing three leaves, each leaf three fruits. The Five Virtues Fungus resembles a towered palace, its stem square, its leaves of five colors distinct yet unmingled, its top like an inverted canopy, where sweet dew often gathers and purple qi rises several *chi*. The Dragon-Held Fungus always grows in pairs during mid-spring, with three nodes and twelve branches, its lower root resembling a seated person.

All these herbaceous fungi number one hundred and twenty varieties. Dried in the shade and consumed, they allow one to endure with heaven and earth, granting either a thousand or two thousand years of life.

The Flesh Fungi include the Ten-Thousand-Year Toad, which bears horns on its head and double-layered red characters in eight strokes beneath its chin. Captured at noon on the fifth day of the fifth month, dried in the shade for a hundred days, its left foot can trace the ground to summon flowing water, and carrying its left foreleg repels all weapons—if an enemy shoots at one, bows, crossbows, and arrows reverse to strike back. The Thousand-Year Bat is white as snow, roosting upside down due to the weight of its brain. Dried in the shade and consumed, these two creatures grant forty thousand years of life. The Thousand-Year Spirit Turtle displays all five colors; the male’s forehead bears two bony ridges like horns. Bathed in sheep’s blood, its shell is removed, roasted, pounded, and consumed in a square *cun* spoonful thrice daily. Consuming an entire shell grants a thousand years of life. Encountering in the mountains small figures seven or eight *cun* tall riding carriages or horses—these are Flesh Fungi. Capturing and consuming them grants immortality. The Wind-Born Beast resembles a marten, blue-green in color, and as large as a wildcat. Found in the great forests of the Southern Sea, it is caught in nets and burned with piles of firewood. Though the wood is consumed, the beast remains unscathed in the ashes, its fur uncharred, blades unable to pierce it, and blows rebounding like striking a leather sack. Only after hammering its head dozens of times does it die; yet if its mouth is opened to the wind, it revives and flees. Plugging its nostrils with stone parsley kills it. Its brain, mixed with chrysanthemum and consumed—ten *jin* in all—grants five hundred years of life. The Thousand-Year Swallow nests facing north, its color mostly white with a forked tail. Dried in the shade and consumed, one head grants five hundred years.

These, too, number one hundred and twenty varieties, all Flesh Fungi.

The Mushroom Fungi grow in deep mountains, beneath great trees, or beside springs. Their forms vary—some like palaces, others like carriages or horses, dragons or tigers, human figures, or flying birds—ever-changing in five colors. These, too, number one hundred and twenty varieties, each depicted in illustrations. To gather them, perform the Yu Step, cut with a bone knife, dry in the shade, and consume a square *cun* spoonful to ascend as an immortal. The middling attain several thousand years, the least a thousand.

To seek fungal herbs, enter famous mountains in the third or ninth month, when the mountains open to yield divine medicines. Avoid inauspicious days; the hour of Heaven’s Aid is best, especially when the Three Auspicious signs converge. Departing through an auspicious gate of the Three Auspicious, arrive at the mountain on a day of six yin forces, during the Bright Hall hour. Carry the Lingbao talisman, lead a white dog, hold a white chicken, and place a *dou* of white salt and the Mountain-Opening talisman and proclamation upon a great stone. Enter the mountain grasping a handful of Wu Tang grass, and the mountain spirit will rejoice, ensuring the discovery of fungi.

To gather and consume fungi, choose days when the Celestial Stems and Earthly Branches are in mutual generation, with the kingly and ministerial phases in harmony. Though these fungi abound in famous mountains, ordinary Daoists—lacking single-minded devotion, pure conduct, and profound virtue, and unversed in mountain-entry techniques—may possess the illustrations yet fail to recognize the fungi, thus never obtaining them.

Every mountain, great or small, harbors spirits. These spirits do not bestow fungi upon humans; even if one treads upon them, they remain unseen.

Mica has five varieties, though most cannot distinguish them. The method: hold it to the sun, observe its color, and examine it closely to discern its nature.

When viewed in shaded areas, their mixed colors remain indiscernible. Those with all five colors but predominantly green are called *yunying*; they should be consumed in spring. Those with all five colors but predominantly red are called *yunzhu*; they should be consumed in summer. Those with all five colors but predominantly white are called *yunye*; they should be consumed in autumn. Those with all five colors but predominantly black are called *yunmu*; they should be consumed in winter. Those with only green and yellow hues are called *yunsha*; they should be consumed in late summer. Pure, radiant white ones are called *linshi*; they may be consumed year-round.

The method for consuming the five *yun* varieties: some are transformed into liquid by steeping in cassia, scallion, or jade-infused water; some are exposed to dew in iron vessels and simmered with dark water; some are buried in bamboo tubes with saltpeter to dissolve into liquid; some are kneaded with honey into a paste; some are steeped in autumn dew for a hundred days, then pounded in leather pouches into powder; some are mixed with *wudian* grass and lacquer tree sap for consumption. After one year of ingestion, all illnesses vanish; after three years, an old man reverts to youth; after five years of uninterrupted use, one may command ghosts and spirits, walk through fire unburned, enter water undrenched, and tread upon thorns without injury—even meeting immortals. While other substances rot when buried or char when exposed to fire, the five *yun* varieties remain unburned in fierce flames and never decay when buried, thus granting longevity. It is also said that after ten years of consumption, clouds perpetually hover above the practitioner. Consuming the mother substance begets the child—this is the natural principle.

When held to the light, if a murky, pure black hue appears, it is unfit for consumption, causing urinary ailments and sores. Even when prepared as a liquid or pill, they must first be steeped for a hundred days in water from thatched-roof drippings, east-flowing streams, or dew, then rinsed to remove sediment before use. Master Wei Shuqing of Zhongshan consumed them over time and eventually rode clouds to travel. After sealing his formula in a jade casket and ascending as an immortal, his son Shi and the Han emissary Liang Bo obtained it, followed the method, and likewise attained immortality.

Cinnabar must come from Wudu Mountain, pure and unadulterated, its red hue like a rooster’s comb, gleaming brilliantly—only then is it suitable. If it is merely pure yellow like cinnabar but lacks the red luster, it cannot be used for immortality elixirs but may serve in medicinal remedies. Methods of ingestion include steaming, decocting, or consuming with wine; some first dissolve it with saltpeter into liquid before solidifying; some wrap it in dark animal intestines and steam it beneath red clay; some blend it with pine resin; some refine it with three substances, drawing it out like cloth, white as ice. All methods confer longevity, cure ailments, expel the Three Corpses, erase scars, restore black hair, regrow fallen teeth. After a thousand days, jade maidens attend, ready to serve and summon celestial kitchens. True jade maidens bear a yellow jade mark on their noses, the size of a millet grain—without this sign, they are mere spirits testing mortals.

Jade is also an immortal’s medicine, though difficult to obtain. The *Classic of Jade* states: *"Consuming gold grants a lifespan like gold; consuming jade grants a lifespan like jade."* It also says: *"Ingesting Dark Perfection defies mortal limits."* Dark Perfection is another name for jade, enabling flight and transcendence—not merely earthbound immortality. Yet its path is slow; one must consume one or two hundred pounds before effects manifest. Jade may be dissolved in black rice wine or burnet wine, or softened with scallion broth into a paste; it may be ingested as pills or burned into powder. After a year of consumption, one enters water unstained, fire unscathed, blades uncut, and poisons unharmed. Do not use finished jade objects, as they harm without benefit—only raw jade is suitable. White jade from Khotan is finest; next are jade from Xu Shanting’s domain in Nanyang and jade from the waters of Lurong in Rinan. Red Pine Master steeped jade in dark worm blood to create a liquid elixir, enabling him to ride smoke and ascend. Consuming jade shards or jade-infused water both grant immortality. Its only drawback compared to gold is that it induces frequent heat sensations akin to cold-food powder. Those ingesting jade shards should take a knife-tip’s measure of realgar and cinnabar every ten days, wash their hair, bathe in cold water, and walk in the wind to prevent overheating.

Dong Junyi once gave jade nectar to a blind man, restoring his sight in ten days. Wu Yanzhi, intending to consume jade, obtained an incomplete jade formula and, ignorant of its protocols and taboos, gathered ritual tablets, rings, and sword fittings to ingest. Later, I explained their unsuitability, and he sighed: *"Matters cannot be imprecise—not only is it futile, but nearly disastrous."* Silver, though inferior to gold and jade, can still grant earthly immortality. Methods of ingestion include dissolving it in wheat broth, consuming it with vermilion grass wine, or refining it with dragon fat. However, each dose must be as large as a pellet—beyond the means of impoverished Daoists.

Pearls over an inch in diameter may be consumed for longevity. Steeped in yogurt or broth, they dissolve like mercury; they may also be dissolved with pumice water or wasp nest liquid, then mixed with red snake gall and drawn into strands three or four feet long. Consumed as pills while fasting, they grant deathlessness and immortality. Pure lacquer that does not stick may be ingested to commune with spirits and attain longevity. Methods include mixing it with ten large crabs or combining it with mica water or jade water. The Nine Worms are expelled, and foul blood exits through the nose; after a year, the Six Jia spirits will deliver celestial feasts.

Cinnamon may be steamed with scallion mucus to produce a liquid, or taken with bamboo sap; it may also be blended with the brain of the *Xianzhi Jun*—or tortoise—and consumed. After seven years, one can walk upon water and attain deathless longevity.

*Huma* (sesame), also called *jusheng*, when ingested prevents aging, resists wind-dampness, and restores vitality in the elderly. Peach resin, steeped in mulberry ash infusion, cures all illnesses when consumed; long-term use lightens the body and grants radiance, so that in dark places one shines like the moon. Excessive consumption enables fasting. The red fruit of the *ning* tree, when eaten for a year, restores youth to the aged and sharpens vision to see spirits. The Daoist Liang Xu, at seventy, began consuming it and grew younger; by one hundred and forty, he could write at night and run as swiftly as a galloping horse. Later, he entered Green Dragon Mountain.

Sophora seeds are sealed in a new jar with mud for over twenty days until their outer skin rots; then washed, they resemble large beans. Daily consumption strengthens the brain, and long-term use prevents graying hair and grants longevity. The *Xuan Zhong Man Fang* lists over three hundred herbs—such as *Chu Feilian*, *ze xie*, *di huang*, and *huang lian*—all of which extend life and may be taken individually. *Lingfei Powder*, *Weiguai Pills*, *Zhiming Pills*, and *Yangxue Pills* all preserve youth and reverse aging.

In the mountains of Li County, Nanyang, there is a Sweet Valley whose water is sweet because sweet chrysanthemums grow on both sides of the valley; their flowers fall into the water over generations, altering its taste. The residents near this valley do not dig wells but drink only Sweet Valley water. None fail to live long: the oldest reach one hundred forty or fifty, the youngest no less than eighty or ninety. No one dies young, for they benefit from the chrysanthemums’ power. Thus, when the Minister of Works Wang Chang, Grand Commandant Liu Kuan, and Grand Tutor Yuan Wei served as governors of Nanyang, they each ordered Li County to deliver forty *hu* of Sweet Valley water monthly for drinking and cooking. Though these officials suffered from wind impediment and dizziness, their conditions improved. Yet they did not gain the full benefit as did the valley’s residents, who drank the water from birth.

Chrysanthemum flowers resemble *yiyi* flowers, distinguished only by taste: chrysanthemum is sweet, *yiyi* bitter—hence the saying, "bitter as *yiyi*." True chrysanthemums exist everywhere but are rare; they mostly grow near water, with Mount Goushi and Li County having the most. The immortals’ formulas speak of the *Sun Essence Renewing Elixir*, all referring to the same chrysanthemum, though its roots, stems, flowers, and seeds bear different names. The teachings are profound, yet those who consume it nowadays see little effect, for they do not obtain true chrysanthemums. If even Sweet Valley water, infused with chrysanthemum essence, can extend life, how much more so with superior herbs?

My late grandfather, the Vice Minister of Rites, once served as magistrate of Linyuan. He said that in that county lived the Liao family, who for generations enjoyed long life—some over a hundred, others eighty or ninety. After they moved away, their descendants increasingly died young. Others who later occupied their old residence again lived long, generation after generation. This revealed the house’s influence, though its cause remained unknown. Suspecting the well water was unusually red, they dug around it and found several dozen *hu* of cinnabar buried by ancients a few feet away. The cinnabar’s essence had gradually seeped into the well, so drinking its water conferred longevity—how much more so if one refined and ingested the cinnabar itself?

I have also heard of Zhao Qu from Shangdang, who suffered from leprosy for years. All treatments failed, and he was near death. Some said he could not be saved, so his family abandoned him. Later, his descendants took turns caring for him, but his condition worsened. Finally, they carried him with provisions and left him in a mountain cave. Qu, lamenting his misfortune, wept day and night for months. A transcendent passed by the cave, saw him, and took pity. After inquiring, Qu, recognizing the stranger’s extraordinary nature, kowtowed and pleaded for mercy. The transcendent gave him a pouch of medicine and instructed him on its use. After consuming it for a hundred days, Qu’s sores healed, his complexion became radiant, and his skin smooth as jade. The transcendent visited again, and Qu thanked him for the gift of life, begging to know the formula. The transcendent replied, "It is merely pine resin. This mountain has much of it; refine and consume it, and you may attain deathless longevity."

Qu returned home, but his family, thinking him a ghost, was terrified. He then consumed pine resin continuously. His body grew lighter, his strength multiplied a hundredfold, and he could climb perilous heights and traverse dangers all day without fatigue. At one hundred seventy, his teeth remained intact, his hair unwhite. One night, he suddenly saw a light in his room as bright as a mirror. When he asked those around him, they saw nothing. Over time, the light grew larger, illuminating the entire room like daylight. Another night, he saw two maidens of two or three inches, fully formed but tiny, playing around his mouth and nose. This continued for a year, the maidens gradually growing larger and appearing beside him. He often heard the sounds of zithers and lutes, laughing joyfully alone. After living among mortals for over three hundred years, his appearance like a child’s, he entered Ox-Keeping Mountain—surely an earthbound immortal.

When word spread of Qu’s experience with pine resin, many rushed to consume it. Those who labored heavily carted and carried it by donkey, filling their rooms. Yet those who took it for a month or more, seeing no great benefit, soon stopped. Few possess such perseverance.

During the reign of Emperor Cheng of Han, hunters in the Zhongnan Mountains saw a man without clothes, his body covered in black hair. When they tried to capture him, he leapt over gullies and valleys as if flying, impossible to catch.

Then they secretly observed her whereabouts, surrounded and captured her—surely a woman. Questioned, she said: “I was originally a palace lady of Qin. When I heard the eastern rebels had arrived and the King of Qin surrendered, the palaces were set ablaze. Terrified, I fled into the mountains. Starving and with nothing to eat, I was near death when an old man taught me to consume pine needles and pine nuts. At first, they were bitter and harsh, but gradually I grew accustomed. Thereafter, I neither hungered nor thirsted, felt no cold in winter nor heat in summer. This woman must have been a palace attendant of King Ziying of Qin. By the reign of Emperor Cheng, she had lived over two hundred years. When they took her back and fed her grain, at first she retched at its smell, but after several days she recovered. After about two years, her body hair fell away, and she grew old and died. Had she not been captured by men, she would have become an immortal.”

The Wen clan of Nanyang recounted that during the great chaos of the late Han, their ancestor fled into the mountains, starving and near death. A man taught him to eat *zhu* (Atractylodes), and he no longer suffered hunger. After several decades, he returned to his hometown, his complexion youthful and his strength greater than before. He said that in the mountains, his body felt light enough to leap, scaling heights and traversing perilous paths for days without exhaustion. Walking on ice and snow, he felt no cold. Often, he saw several men on a high cliff—some playing board games, others reading—and they looked down at him. He overheard them ask, “Should we call this one up?” One replied, “Not yet.” *Zhu* is also called *shanlin* or *shanjing*. Thus, the *Divine Medicines Classic* states: “If you wish for long life, always consume mountain essence.”

Formerly, the Eight Immortals each consumed a single substance, attaining earthly immortality for hundreds of years before compounding the divine elixir of golden fluid and ascending to the Great Clarity. If one combines all eight substances, refines, and consumes them, they yield no power—this is because the medicinal forces mutually overcome and restrain one another.

Han Zhong consumed calamus for thirteen years. His body grew hair, and he could read ten thousand words in a day, reciting them all. In winter, he bared his chest without feeling cold. The best calamus grows on rocks, with nine or more nodes per inch and purple flowers.

Zhao Tazi consumed cinnamon for twenty years. Hair grew on the soles of his feet, he could walk five hundred *li* in a day, and lift a thousand *jin*.

Yimenzi consumed schisandra for sixteen years. His complexion became like that of a jade maiden; he entered water without getting wet and fire without being scorched.

Chu Wenzi consumed rehmannia for eight years. At night, his vision emitted light, and he could draw a crossbow with his hands.

Lin Ziming consumed *zhu* for eleven years. His ears grew five inches long, his body light as flight, and he could leap over ravines and valleys over two *zhang* wide.

Du Ziwei consumed asparagus for years. He kept eighty concubines, fathered one hundred and thirty children, and could walk three hundred *li* in a day.

Ren Ziji consumed poria for eighteen years. Immortals and jade maidens came to follow him; he could conceal or reveal himself at will, no longer ate grain, and his scars from moxibustion vanished, his face and body glowing like jade.

Lingyang Zizhong consumed *yuanzhi* (polygala) for twenty years. He fathered thirty-seven children, remembered everything he read, and could vanish while seated or standing.

The *Scripture of Immortals* states: “Though one may consume the leaves of plants and trees and live for hundreds of years, if they neglect the divine elixir, they will never attain immortality.” From this, it is clear that plants and trees merely extend life—they are not the medicine of eternal life. Before one can compound the elixir, they may consume these to sustain themselves.

Someone asked: “In consuming medicinal substances, is there a proper order of precedence?”

Master Baopu replied: “According to the *Regimen of Consuming Medicines* by Master Zhonghuang, medicines for treating illness should be taken before meals, while those for nourishing life should be taken after. I consulted Master Zheng about this, and he said: ‘This is easily understood. If you wish to attack an illness with medicine, it is best to take it on an empty stomach, so the medicinal force may act unimpeded. If taken after eating, the medicine would merely contend with the grain and exhaust its power. If one seeks to nourish life but takes medicine before meals, its force will not yet have acted when it is driven downward by the grain, rendering it useless.’”

Another asked: “When people consume medicines to nourish life, is it true that there are suitable and unsuitable times?”

Master Baopu replied: “According to the *Jade Strategy Records* and the *Scripture of Kai Ming*, the five tones and six correspondences reveal the location of a person’s year and fate. *Zi* and *wu* belong to *geng*; *mao* and *you* belong to *ji*; *yin* and *shen* belong to *wu*; *chou* and *wei* belong to *xin*; *chen* and *xu* belong to *bing*; *si* and *hai* belong to *ding*. If one word matches, it is *gong* and Earth. If three words match, it is *zhi* and Fire. If five words match, it is *yu* and Water. If seven words match, it is *shang* and Metal. If nine words match, it is *jiao* and Wood. If one’s fate belongs to Earth, one should not take green medicines; if to Metal, one should not take red medicines; if to Wood, one should not take white medicines; if to Water, one should not take yellow medicines; if to Fire, one should not take black medicines. This follows the principle of the Five Phases: Wood overcomes Earth, Earth overcomes Water, Water overcomes Fire, Fire overcomes Metal, and Metal overcomes Wood. However, for the great medicine of the Golden Elixir, these considerations of suitability no longer apply.”

One word matches *gong*: *geng-zi*, *geng-wu*; *xin-wei*, *xin-chou*; *bing-chen*, *bing-xu*; *ding-hai*, *ding-si*; *wu-yin*, *wu-shen*; *ji-mao*, *ji-you*.

Three words match *zhi*: *jia-chen*, *jia-xu*; *yi-hai*, *yi-si*; *bing-yin*, *bing-shen*; *ding-you*, *ding-mao*; *wu-wu*, *wu-zi*; *ji-wei*, *ji-chou*.

Five words match *yu*: *jia-yin*, *jia-shen*; *yi-mao*, *yi-you*; *bing-zi*, *bing-wu*; *ding-wei*, *ding-chou*; *ren-chen*, *ren-xu*; *gui-si*, *gui-hai*.

Seven words match *shang*: *jia-zi*, *jia-wu*; *yi-chou*, *yi-wei*; *geng-chen*, *geng-xu*; *xin-si*, *xin-hai*; *ren-shen*, *ren-yin*; *gui-mao*, *gui-you*.

Nine words match *jiao*: *wu-chen*, *wu-xu*; *ji-si*, *ji-hai*; *geng-yin*, *geng-shen*; *xin-mao*, *xin-you*; *ren-wu*, *ren-zi*; *gui-chou*, *gui-wei*.

Yu’s Stepping Method:
First, lift the left foot, cross the right past the left, then bring the left to meet the right.
Next, lift the right foot, cross the left past the right, then bring the right to meet the left.
Then, lift the right foot again, cross the right past the left, and bring the left to meet the right.
After three such steps, one should cover a distance of twenty-one feet, leaving nine footprints behind.

Minor Divine Formula:
Use three *jin* of true cinnabar and one *jin* of white honey. Combine and sun-dry, then simmer until it can be formed into pills. Take ten pills the size of sesame seeds at dawn. Within a year, white hair will turn black, fallen teeth will regrow, and the body will become lustrous. With continued use, an old man will revert to youth, and long-term consumption grants immortality.

Minor Gold Ingestion Formula:
Refine gold in fire and immerse it in clear wine, repeating two hundred immersions and extractions until it boils. Press it between the fingers until it becomes like mud; if it does not boil or fails to extrude between the fingers, return it to the wine and refine it repeatedly. Once prepared, take a pill the size of a bullet, or divide one pill into smaller doses. After thirty days of consumption, one will be free from cold and heat, and divine beings and jade maidens will descend. Silver may also be ingested in the same manner. Consuming these two substances allows one to dwell in a mountain stone chamber; after a year, one will ascend lightly. Those who consume it in the mortal realm become earthbound immortals—do not transmit this recklessly.

Twofold Primordial Child’s Gold-Refining Ingestion Method:
Take three *jin* of pig’s back fat and one *dou* of pure bitter wine. Place five *liang* of yellow gold in a vessel and refine it. Remove it from the furnace and immerse the gold in the fat, repeating one hundred immersions and extractions; do the same with the bitter wine. Consuming one *jin* of gold grants a lifespan coeval with heaven and earth; half a *jin* grants two thousand years; five *liang*, one thousand two hundred years. There is no fixed limit—ingestion is always beneficial. Perform this on a day of flourishing vitality, and the divine efficacy will be optimal. Do not transmit this to others; if shared, the medicine will fail to achieve its potency.

To consume the corpse-expelling medicine, one should ingest cinnabar.
Cinnabar Ingestion Method:
Take one *jin* of cinnabar, grind and sift it, then mix with three *sheng* of pure bitter wine and two *sheng* of refined lacquer. Combine the three ingredients thoroughly and simmer over a low flame until it can be formed into pills. Take three pills the size of sesame seeds, twice daily. After forty days, all ailments in the abdomen will be cured, and the Three Corpses will depart. After one hundred days, the muscles and bones will become firm and strong.

After one thousand days of ingestion, the Registrar of Life will erase one’s name from the ledger of death, ensuring preservation alongside Heaven and Earth, with the sun and moon as one’s peers. One’s form and countenance will transform, shifting without constancy; at midday, no shadow will be cast, for one will possess a radiance apart.

**Book Twelve: Discerning Questions**

Someone asked: “If immortality were truly attainable, the sages would have cultivated it. Yet the Zhou and Confucius did not pursue it—this proves that no such Way exists.”

The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replied: “The sage need not be an immortal, nor the immortal a sage. The sage receives his mandate and, not encountering the Way of long life, devotes himself instead to eradicating cruelty, eliminating bandits, leveling peril and pacifying violence, establishing rites and composing music, inscribing laws and bequeathing teachings, reforming corrupt customs, and transforming degenerate mores. He rectifies faltering rulers, rescues failing states, edits the *Book of Songs* and *Book of Documents*, compiles the *River Chart* and *Luo Writing*, composes classics and proclamations, harmonizes the *Ya* and *Song* odes, instructs the young, and answers summons to serve in various states. Through smoke and flame, his mat never grows warm. His affairs are endless, his labors unceasing—how then could he close his ears and cover his eyes, turn inward to listen, practice breath cultivation and guiding exercises, maintain long fasts and strict purity, seclude himself to refine his form, ascend mountains to gather herbs, regulate his breathing to commune with spirits, or abstain from grains? As for immortals, they need only single-minded devotion, unwavering faith, and diligent perseverance. With tranquility and stillness, attainment is possible—no extraordinary talent is required. Those who enter the world as lofty transcendents become the greatest burdens to Daoists. By obtaining a single great elixir and mastering the essentials of guarding the One and nurturing the spirit, one achieves long life and enduring vision—how could this compare to the endless labors of the sages?

Moreover, the so-called sages of the world are sages of governance, not sages of the Dao. The sages of the Dao are the Yellow Emperor and Laozi; the sages of governance are the Zhou and Confucius. The Yellow Emperor first governed the world and later ascended as an immortal—this was due to his rare ability to excel in both. Among the ancient emperors and kings who inscribed their deeds on Mount Tai, seventy-two records remain legible, while countless others have faded into oblivion. Yet only the Yellow Emperor is remembered as an immortal—this is undeniable proof.

The world calls those who excel in what others cannot master ‘sages.’ Thus, one unmatched in *weiqi* is called a *qi* sage—hence Yan Ziqing and Ma Suiming are still known as such today. One peerless in calligraphy is called a *writing* sage—hence Huang Xiang and Hu Zhao still bear that title. One surpassing others in painting is called a *painting* sage—hence Wei Xie and Zhang Mo are still revered as such. One exceptionally skilled in carving is called a *wood* sage—hence Zhang Heng and Ma Jun are still known as such.

Mencius said: Boyi was the sage of purity; Liuxia Hui, the sage of harmony; Yi Yin, the sage of responsibility. I would expand this: sageship is not confined to one domain. Luban and Chui were sages of machinery; Bian Que and Hua Tuo, sages of healing; Ziwei and Ganjun, sages of astrology; Shisu and Xin Liao, sages of divination; Xiayu and Du Hui, sages of strength; Jing Ke and Nie Zheng, sages of courage; Feilian and Kuafu, sages of speed; Ziye and Yanzhou, sages of music; Sun Wu, Han Xin, and Bai Qi, sages of warfare. Sageship is the highest title for human achievement—it is not limited to literature alone.

Zhuangzi said: Thieves possess five qualities of the sage. Guessing where others hide their treasures is wisdom; entering first without hesitation is courage; exiting last without fear is righteousness; knowing what is permissible is discernment; and distributing spoils equally is benevolence. Without these, no one could become a great thief in the world.”

Another asked: “The Way of the sage cannot be divided or scattered—it must be comprehensive to be called sagely.”

I replied: “Among Confucius’ disciples, seventy-two attained mastery, each embodying one aspect of the sage—this shows that sagely affairs can be partitioned. It is also said: Yan Yuan possessed all the qualities of a sage, though in lesser measure—this shows that sagely affairs admit degrees. The *Book of Changes* states: There are four aspects to the Way of the sage: in speech, one values eloquence; in action, one values adaptability; in crafting tools, one values symbolism; in divination, one values interpretation. This is clear evidence that the sagely Way can be divided. Why then should those who excel in the Dao and attain immortality not be called sages of the Dao? If there are no sages of the Dao, would the Zhou and Confucius not be sages of governance? Since sageship is not singular, why demand that one person embody both?”

According to the immortals’ scriptures, those who attain immortality are those whose destinies align with the qi of transcendence, naturally endowed with it. From the womb, they already harbor the nature to trust the Dao. Upon gaining awareness, their hearts incline toward it, and they must encounter an enlightened master to receive the method—otherwise, they will neither believe nor seek, and even if they seek, they will not obtain.

The *Jade Key Scripture: Origin of Life’s Mandate* states: A person’s fortune or misfortune is determined at the moment of conception, when they receive the essence of the constellations. Those who encounter the constellation of sages become sages; those who encounter the constellation of worthies become worthies; those who encounter the constellation of literature become learned; those who encounter the constellation of martiality become martial; those who encounter the constellation of nobility become noble; those who encounter the constellation of wealth become wealthy; those who encounter the constellation of lowliness become lowly; those who encounter the constellation of poverty become poor; those who encounter the constellation of longevity become long-lived; those who encounter the constellation of immortality become immortals.

There are constellations of immortals and sages, constellations of governance sages, and constellations that combine both. There are constellations of nobility without wealth, wealth without nobility, and those that combine both. There are constellations of early wealth followed by poverty, early nobility followed by lowliness, and those that combine poverty and lowliness. There are constellations of wealth and nobility that do not endure, constellations of loyalty and filial piety, and constellations of malevolence and wickedness.”

Such cases cannot be exhaustively recorded, but their general outlines are as described. A person’s life is fundamentally determined by fate—this is the teaching of Zhang Chezi. If one is not destined to receive the mandate of immortality, one will surely lack the heart that yearns for the Dao; never has there been one who, without such a heart, sought its practice, nor one who, without seeking, attained it.

From antiquity to the present, there have been those of towering talent and keen insight who did not believe in immortals, and those of modest ability who studied and achieved transcendence. Some, though broadly discerning, were blind to the Way of immortality; others, though largely unlearned, uniquely grasped its principles. Could this not be the work of Heaven’s mandate?

The Daoist tradition treasures and guards its esoteric arts of transcendence. Even among disciples, it exercises the utmost selectivity, imparting essential secrets only after prolonged refinement. How much less would it force such teachings upon the worldly, who neither believe nor seek them? To attempt to convert the unbelieving only invites mockery and slander.

Thus, those who attain the Dao walk a path apart from the world, dwell in places apart, and speak in ways that avoid entanglement. Though separated by a thousand miles, they still fear the harassment of the clamorous; though they erase their tracks, they still dread the shame of defamation. Neither rank nor wealth can sway them—why then would they parade their arts before the vulgar, declaring, “I possess the methods of transcendence”? This is why even the sages Zhou and Kong had no means to know the Way of immortality.

Moreover, Zhou and Kong were but men of exceptional talent and profound learning. Even in minor skills, they were often unversed. Had they been asked to juggle balls and swords, leap over blades, tread upon poles, scale tables, cling to sheer cliffs, swim the perilous currents of Lüliang, lift a thousand jun with their hands, outrun the wind with their feet, grapple with tigers and leopards, or catch arrows in flight—ordinary men might perform such feats, yet Zhou and Kong could not. How much less could they surpass these?

The concerns of others—whether the orientation of lice, the hues of pigments behind walls, the grasses beneath trees, the texts in chests, the treasures buried in earth, the birds and beasts of dense forests, or the fish and turtles of deep abysses—if Zhou and Kong were tasked with describing their colors, distinguishing their names, enumerating their quantities, or verifying their existence, they could not exhaust such knowledge. How then could they fathom what lies beyond?

Sages, like all men, hunger without food, thirst without drink, burn when scorched, shiver when frozen, feel pain when struck, bleed when cut, age with time, sicken when injured, and die when their qi is spent. In these ways, they differ little from common folk; their distinctions are few. What sets them apart lies only in their superior talent, far-reaching thought, eloquence, literary skill, moral integrity, and vast learning. How could they be omniscient?

Having already composed the *Canon* and the *Counsels*, governing the realm and guiding the people, why demand that they also master the Way of transcendence and attain deathless longevity? Such expectations are excessive.

I have heard that profound words offend the ears of the vulgar, and truthful speech contradicts the crowd. When Confucian scholars hastily peruse this text, they will surely accuse me of slandering the sages. But do I? I merely seek to exhaust the principles of things. When principles are fully explored and matters thoroughly examined, it may *seem* as though I revile Zhou and Kong.

The world claims that sages descend from Heaven, beings of divine spirit, omniscient and omnipotent. So revered are their names that none dare scrutinize their deeds, declaring: “What sages do not do, no man can do; what sages do not know, no man can know.” Is this not laughable?

Let us compare this with recent events to provoke reflection. The birds of Mount Wan sang their dirges of mourning and parting—Confucius did not understand their cries, yet could one then claim that Yan Hui alone could partially comprehend them? When he heard the wailing of a woman on Mount Tai, he inquired and learned that tigers had devoured three of her kin, yet he did not grasp why she had not fled. Only upon questioning did he realize. Seeing a bird-catcher capture only fledglings, he failed to discern the reason until asked. When he wished to bury his mother, he did not know his father’s grave—only after being told did he proceed. When the tomb collapsed, he remained unaware until his disciples informed him, whereupon he wept. He suspected Yan Yuan of stealing food, resorting to the pretense of a sacrifice to test the dust’s deception. When the stable burned, he did not know whether men or horses were harmed. Upon Yan Yuan’s death, he declared him already gone. Though he wandered through seventy states, he could not foresee that none would employ him, yet he remained restless, his seat never warm. He did not anticipate the people of Kuang would besiege him, yet he took that road. He asked Laozi about ancient rites and found some beyond his understanding. He inquired of Tanzi about the avian bureaucracy and encountered offices he did not recognize. Lost on the road, he sent others to ask directions, unaware that those he questioned would mock him and refuse to guide him—clearly, he should have known not to ask. He alighted from his carriage to chase a phoenix-singer, not realizing the bird would not stay. He met Nanzi without recognizing the futility. Such instances are too numerous to list—why then is it strange he did not know the methods of transcendence?

Moreover, the vulgar Confucians claim: “What sages cannot do, no other can.” Yet the diver of Dang dwells in water, Liang Mu transforms in fire, Bozi endures scorching heat, Zhongdu withstands bitter cold, Zuo Ci survives dismemberment by soldiers, Gan Shi fasts for years, Fan Ye remains unharmed when struck, the Magistrate of Bie revives after his corpse floats away, Shaoqian commands a hundred ghosts, Changfang contracts the earth’s veins, Zhongfu assumes the form of a morning duck, and Zhang Kai summons clouds and mist with his breath—never have we heard that Zhou or Kong could perform such feats.

The common folk may say: “Zhou and Kong could have done all this, but simply chose not to.” To this I reply: “If one does not seek evidence in clear records but instead points to empty words, then I could just as well claim that Zhou and Kong could spread their wings and soar to the eight directions, summon clouds and bring forth rain, move mountains and uproot wells—but simply chose not to. If no reliance is placed on documented accounts as proof, then what bounds remain? If we follow such reasoning, I could likewise assert that Zhou and Kong both ascended as immortals, yet kept this method hidden lest the world learn that deathlessness is attainable. For if all knew this, they would surely abandon their duties—forsaking official careers to scale perilous heights and plumb unfathomed depths in pursuit of the Dao. Thus, families would be left without descendants, states without ministers or officials; loyalty and filial piety would perish together, and the great moral order would collapse into chaos. Therefore, Zhou and Kong practiced this in secret, revealing only the guise of mortality while inwardly achieving celestial transcendence. If this were so, how then could you refute me? Such a claim might well be true.”

The *Lingbao Jing* contains three scrolls—*The Balanced Mechanism*, *The Soaring Tortoise*, and *The Granted Mat*—all of which are arts of immortality. When the King of Wu quarried stone to build his palace, he discovered within the joined slabs a book of purple script on golden tablets. Unable to decipher it, he dispatched an envoy to ask Confucius, deceiving him by saying: “The King of Wu was at leisure when a red sparrow delivered a book to his hall. We do not understand its meaning, so we have come from afar to seek your counsel.” Confucius examined it and said: “This is the method of the *Lingbao*, the Way of long life, which Yu once practiced. Hidden in the watery realm, his years equaled those of Heaven and Earth, and he presented himself at the Purple Court. When Yu was about to ascend as an immortal, he sealed this text within a stone casket in the famous mountains. Now the red sparrow has borne it forth—surely this is a bestowal from Heaven.” By this account, Yu of Xia did not die, and Confucius knew of it. How then can we be certain that Confucius did not secretly cultivate this Way himself? Even if the sage did not pursue such matters, we cannot deny their efficacy.

Human preferences and aversions vary endlessly. If one explains something face-to-face, how could it not be believed? When it aligns with their inclination, even the smallest thing will be done; when it clashes with their spirit, even the greatest will not be learned. Some love bitterness and despise sweetness, while others crave profit and abandon righteousness—such cases are countless. As it is said: “The greatest treasure of the sage is rank; what gathers people is wealth.” And again: “Wealth and honor are what all desire.” Yet in ancient times, there were those who declined the abdication of imperial rule, spurned the riches of the Four Seas, scorned the offices of the Three Dukes and Nine Ministers, turned their backs on jade and silk, retreated to the lofty purity of the mountains and forests, and contented themselves with the humble life of fishing—such examples are too many to count. It is also said: “The desires for food and intimacy are fundamental to human nature.” Thus, passion for beauty cannot be admonished, and fine flavors can dispel sorrow. There were those who abandoned grain and forsook delicacies, took no wives or concubines, wandered alone in transcendence, and found joy in solitude—gazing at their shadows in delight, rinsing their mouths with streams and forgetting all taste—such cases are too numerous to record.

Human nature cherishes rosy cheeks and graceful forms, yet the Yellow Emperor wed the homely Momu, and the Lord of Chen favored the repulsive Dunqia. No nose dislikes fragrance, so orpiment, turmeric, orchid, storax, black bile, white glue, *jiangli*, *jieche*, spring cress, and autumn orchids are prized like jade and jasper. Yet the women of the Eastern Sea pursue men reeking of rancid stench, following them without cease. King Wen of Zhou relished unpalatable pickles and would not trade them for the finest sacrificial meats. Emperor Ming of Wei preferred the sound of hammer and chisel to the harmony of strings and bamboo.

Each person harbors their own inclinations—how can one demand this to align with that? The Zhou and Kong [Confucius] kept to their own company and disbelieved in the Dao of immortals. Yet the sun and moon do not illuminate all beneath them, and even sages have what they do not know. How can it be said that because sages do not pursue it, the world holds no immortals? This is to fault the Three Luminaries for not shining within an overturned basin.

**Scroll Thirteen: Utmost Words**

Someone asked: "Were the immortals of old all achieved through learning, or were they simply endowed with exceptional qi?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: "What sort of question is this? None of them did not shoulder their satchels to follow teachers, accumulating merit and diligence, braving frost and peril, enduring wind and rain, personally sweeping and cleaning, enduring hardship and toil. Only after being tested through trust and conduct, and finally proven through trials and tribulations—steadfast in nature, unwavering in resolve, free from resentment or doubt—did they ascend the hall and enter the inner chamber.

Some grew weary and halted midway; some bore grudges and retreated; some were lured by glory and profit, returning to the pursuits of the common world; some were swayed by heretical teachings, losing their tranquil resolve. Some sought instant success by evening for what they began at dawn; some expected immediate results from seated practice.

Those who behold wealth and beauty yet remain unmoved, who hear worldly words yet stand unshaken—among ten thousand, one such person is already many. Thus, those who strive are as numerous as ox hairs, while those who attain are as rare as unicorn horns.

The archer who draws a powerful crossbow demonstrates strength in loosing the arrow; one who crosses a great river preserves their life only upon reaching the far shore. A well that does not reach the spring is as good as undug; a single step not taken is as good as never having set forth. The burdens of the long road are not overcome in the space of a watch; the heights of soaring clouds are not built by a single basket of earth. Yet those who climb perilous heights falter when nearly at the summit, their strength spent; those who cultivate the Way falter when on the verge of completion, their resolve unfulfilled.

A thousand granaries and ten thousand chests are not filled by a single planting; the towering trees of heaven do not grow in ten days. The fathomless abyss begins as a trickle; the wealth of Tao Zhu was amassed over hundreds and thousands. Thus, when others retreat, one advances—this is how Yinzi exhausted the utmost Dao. Reverence at the end as at the beginning—this is how Xianmen summoned the cloud-dragon. If my resolve is truly steadfast, what matter who they are?"

Ge Hong said: "The common folk, unable to preserve life, devote themselves instead to what destroys it. Finite things cannot supply infinite depletion; the flow of rivers cannot fill a bottomless vessel. Even those whose income is small and expenses great cannot keep pace—how much less those with not a single coin of income yet a thousand in outlay?

From the youngest to the oldest, none are without illness; it is only a matter of severity. Each receives a greater or lesser measure of qi: those with more exhaust it slowly, while those with less deplete it swiftly. Those who know the Way replenish and rescue it, first restoring what was lost before seeking further gains. If one were to consume elixirs all day, flesh would fly and bones soar; if one were to practice Daoist arts with each new moon, feathers would sprout in disarray—then the world would have no one who did not believe in the Way.

The trouble lies in the fact that the benefits of a single spoonful are not yet secure, yet the costs of bushels and tons follow one after another; the roots are not yet firmly planted, yet the poison of frost and ice assails them. Unaware that the fault lies within themselves, they instead claim the Way is of no benefit, and so discard their pills and powders, abandoning breathwork and swallowing techniques.

Thus it is said: it is not attaining long life that is difficult, but hearing the Way; it is not hearing the Way that is difficult, but practicing it; it is not practicing it that is difficult, but persisting to the end. A skilled craftsman can give others the compass and square, but cannot make them truly skillful. A wise teacher can impart the methods, but cannot make them act upon them.

Cultivating the Way is like sowing grain; completing it is like reaping the harvest. Though the fields be fertile and the waters abundant, if one misses the proper season, fails in plowing and weeding, then the sprouts will wither in the furrows—unharvested, uncut. Though the acres be vast, there will be no yield.

The common folk not only fail to recognize what truly benefits them, but also fail to recognize what harms them. Harm is easily known and swift, while benefit is difficult to know and slow. If people do not grasp what is easy, how can they comprehend what is difficult?

Harm is like the consumption of lamp oil—unseen, yet suddenly exhausted. Benefit is like the planting of seedlings—unnoticed, yet suddenly lush. Therefore, in cultivating the body and nurturing nature, one must attend carefully to the smallest details. One cannot dismiss minor benefits as insignificant and neglect them, nor treat minor harms as harmless and fail to guard against them.

All great things arise from the accumulation of small things; all billions begin with a single unit. If one cherishes the minute and brings it to fruition in the manifest, then one nearly understands the Way."

Someone asked: "In ancient times, were there those who did nothing at all yet accidentally attained long life?"

Ge Hong replied: "No. Some followed enlightened teachers, accumulating merit and diligent effort, and were thus granted completed elixirs. Others received secret formulas and prepared them themselves, their affairs untouched by the world, their words unburdened by the common—yet records preserve only their names, not how they attained immortality, and so the details are lost.

In the past, the Yellow Emperor could speak at birth and command a hundred spirits—one might call him naturally endowed by heaven—yet even he could not attain the Way by sitting idly. Thus he ascended Wangwu Mountain to receive the alchemical scriptures, went to Dinghu to receive the flying pearl, climbed Kongtong to consult Guangcheng, journeyed to Juci to serve Taiwei, traveled to Mount Tai to honor Zhonghuang, entered the Golden Valley to consult Juanzi, relied on the Dark and Plain Maidens for nourishment, sought out Shan, Ji, Li, and Mu for astrological calculations, consulted Fenghou on divination, received diagnoses from Leixi, adopted the Five Tones’ strategies for warfare, recorded the *Baize*’s words on spirits and demons, studied the *Qingwu*’s teachings on geography, and compiled metallurgical techniques for healing wounds. Thus he mastered all secret essentials, exhausted the Way and fulfilled the True, and so ascended on a dragon to lofty heights, becoming boundless as heaven and earth.

Yet according to the scriptures of the immortals, both the Yellow Emperor and Laozi served the Supreme Unity, the Primordial Sovereign, to receive the essential instructions. How much less could those who do not match these two lords attain transcendence and escape the world on their own? I have never heard of such a thing."

Some may ask: "If the Yellow Emperor indeed attained transcendence, why then does his tomb exist at Mount Qiao?" Master Baopu replied: "According to the *Classic of Mount Jing* and the *Record of Dragon’s Head*, after the Yellow Emperor ingested the divine elixir, a dragon came to welcome him. His ministers, overwhelmed with longing, knew not what to do. Some took his mat and staff, erecting temples to offer sacrifices; others took his robes and crown, burying them and keeping vigil. The *Biographies of the Immortals* states: The Yellow Emperor chose his own day of departure, leaving for seventy days and returning after seventy days. He was buried at Mount Qiao, but suddenly the mountain collapsed, and the tomb was found empty, save for his sword and sandals. Though these accounts differ, they all affirm his transcendence. Those who speak of the Yellow Emperor’s ascension appear frequently in Daoist scriptures and the writings of the Hundred Schools, yet the Confucians, unwilling to indulge in marvels or open paths of strangeness, focus solely on ritual and teaching. Since matters of transcendence cannot instruct the common folk, they claim he died, merely to pacify the people’s hearts. When officials like Zhu Yi, Luan Ba, or Yu Gong rendered great kindness to the people, the people built temples and shrines for them while they still lived. Moreover, in ancient times, when men of surpassing virtue passed away, their ministers and descendants inscribed their merits on enduring vessels. Even today, when local officials are transferred, the people, cherishing their memory, erect steles to praise their virtue—this is akin to the Yellow Emperor’s temples and tombs. How can such things prove he must have died?"

Another asked: "Peng Zu lived eight hundred years, and Anqi three thousand—such longevity surpasses ordinary men. If there truly exists a Way of immortality, why did they not ultimately transcend? Is it not that their allotted lifespan and received qi naturally determined their span, and they merely happened to live longer, yet could not extend it further, thus still succumbing to decay and death?"

Master Baopu replied: "According to the *Scripture of Peng Zu*, he served Emperor Ku and assisted Yao, then lived through the Xia and into the Yin as a grandee. The Yin king sent maidens to learn the arts of the bedchamber from him, and when they proved effective, sought to kill Peng Zu to suppress his teachings. He sensed this and fled, already over seven or eight hundred years old—far from dead. The *Records of the Yellow Stone Master* state that more than seventy years after Peng Zu’s departure, his disciples saw him west of the Flowing Sands. Clearly, he did not die. Moreover, Peng Zu’s disciples—Green Robe Lord Wu, Black Cave Lord, Fine Brow Lord, White Rabbit Gentleman, Li Lou Lord, Great Foot Noble, High Mound Master, and the one called ‘Unwilling to Come’—all lived for centuries during the Yin dynasty before each ascended as immortals. How could Peng Zu himself have died?

The *Biographies of the Immortals* recorded by Liu Xiang also affirms that Peng Zu was a transcendent. As for Master Anqi, he sold medicines by the sea, and the people of Langye passed down accounts of seeing him across generations, reckoning he had lived a thousand years. The First Emperor of Qin summoned him for conversation, which lasted three days and nights. His words were lofty, his meaning profound, his knowledge vast and well-evidenced. The First Emperor, struck with awe, bestowed upon him gold and jade worth thousands of *yi*. Anqi accepted them but left them at Fu Xiang Pavilion, offering a single pair of red jade sandals in return. He left a letter saying: ‘Seek me again after several thousand years on Mount Penglai.’

Thus, he was already a thousand years old when he met the First Emperor—far from dead. Moreover, the First Emperor was harsh and arrogant, the least likely in the world to believe in transcendence, nor would he tolerate those who spoke falsely to him. Yet when he asked Anqi about the Way of longevity, Anqi answered fittingly. The First Emperor, awakened, came to believe that transcendence must exist in the world. He bestowed generous gifts and earnestly wished to learn the art of immortality, but lacking a true master, he was deceived by the likes of Lu Ao and Xu Fu, and so failed to attain it. Had Anqi’s words lacked proof, the First Emperor would have exhausted his arguments within three days and nights, and Anqi would have faced the cauldron and the knife, unable to escape the disaster of the sacrificial tripod. How then could he have received such lavish gifts?"

Another asked: "There are those who ingest medicines, practice qi-cultivation, and perform daoyin exercises, yet still cannot avoid death. Why is this?"

Ge Hong replied: "Those who do not obtain the Golden Elixir but merely ingest herbal medicines and practice minor arts may prolong their years and delay death, yet they cannot attain transcendence. Some know only to take herbs but not the essential techniques for rejuvenation—such persons will never achieve long life. Others, unaware of how to wear spirit talismans, observe prohibitions, visualize the body’s gods, or guard the True Unity, may only prevent internal illnesses and ward off wind-dampness. Should fierce ghosts or malevolent spirits, mountain demons or water toxins assail them, they will perish at once. Some fail to master the method of entering mountains, provoking mountain spirits to bring calamity—thus demons test them, savage beasts maim them, mountain streams poison them, serpents and vipers sting them, leading to many deaths, each by a different cause.

Others begin their practice late, having already inflicted deep harm upon themselves, making restoration difficult. Before the benefits of restoration take root, illness returns, so how can such harsh and depleting efforts ever yield long life? Some who pursue the Dao in old age attain transcendence, while others who begin young fail—why? Those who are old yet succeed were born with abundant qi; abundant qi means their injuries are slight, slight injuries are easily nourished, and easy nourishment leads to transcendence. These, though young, were born with scant qi; scant qi means deep injury, deep injury is hard to remedy, and hard remedy means they cannot attain transcendence.

The rose of Sharon and willow, when cut and replanted, revive; even if uprooted, they live; laid horizontally, they still live. No plant revives more readily than these. Yet if buried too shallowly, not given time to take root, now carved, now stripped, now shaken, now pulled—even if enriched with fertile soil and drenched with spring rains—they will still wither and perish, for their roots are not firm, they cannot sprout new buds, and their vital fluids fail to coalesce into life-sustaining qi.

The human body is easily harmed and hard to nourish, far more so than these two plants. Yet what attacks and destroys it exceeds even the carving and stripping, the shaking and uprooting. Few aid it, many ruin it—death is only fitting. Those who exhale the old and inhale the new rely on qi to augment qi, but when qi is greatly depleted, augmentation is difficult. Those who ingest medicines rely on blood to replenish blood, but when blood is nearly exhausted, replenishment is hard.

Panting after running, coughing or bloating, straining the body with labor, constantly weary and short of breath—these are signs of qi depletion. A dull complexion, withered skin, parched lips, pale veins, shriveled pores—these are symptoms of blood loss. When these two signs manifest externally, the numinous root within has already withered. In such cases, even superior medicines cannot save them.

All who pursue the Dao yet fail, who manage their lives yet meet death—though they possess qi and blood, what sustains their qi and blood has already perished, leaving only residual traces. Like embers cast into water, the fire is gone yet smoke lingers; like a severed tree, branches and leaves may yet sprout. Neither lacks smoke nor leaves, yet what makes them smoke or leaves has already vanished.

People mistake the day they notice illness for the day it began, just as they take the day of last breath for the moment of death. They blame wind and cold, heat and dampness, unaware that these cannot harm the robust and vigorous—only the frail and qi-deficient cannot endure them, thus succumbing. How to illustrate this? Suppose several people, equal in age and strength, equally nourished, together traverse a desert, braving a bitterly cold night. Snow falls upon them, black ice forms beneath, the night wind howls through the branches, coughs and spittle freeze upon their lips—yet only some fall ill, not all. Not that the cold favors some, but that some bodies cannot endure it.

Likewise, when all eat the same food, why do some alone fall ill? Not that the food is selectively poisonous. When all drink from the same vessel, why do some remain sober while others grow drunk? Not that the wine’s potency varies. When all endure scorching heat, why do some alone die of heatstroke? Not that the sun plays favorites. When all take the same medicine, why do some alone grow dizzy and agitated? Not that the drug’s toxicity discriminates.

Thus, when a gale strikes the forest, the withered boughs break first; when torrents batter the cliffs, the cracked fissures crumble first."

When wildfire sweeps the plain, the parched grasses burn first; when a dragon bowl shatters upon the ground, the brittle ones alone break. From this we see: those whose bodies lack the Way and are already chronically weak—it is the wind, cold, heat, and damp that merely trigger their afflictions. If one can ensure that the upright qi does not decline and that form and spirit guard each other, nothing can harm them.

For all who pursue the Dao, the danger lies in beginning too late, not too early. Those who rely on their youthful vigor and robust strength overtax themselves, accumulating a hundred ailments, their lives hanging by a thread like morning dew. Without the great elixir, mere herbs and plants may restore them to the level of ordinary men, but cannot extend their allotted span. Thus the immortal scriptures say: *"In nurturing life, not causing harm is the foundation."* This is the essential teaching.

Shennong declared: *"If the hundred diseases are not cured, how can one attain long life?"* Truly, these words ring true.

Someone asked: *"Is it not excessive indulgence that causes harm?"*

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: *"Why single out that alone? Yet the key to long life lies in the Way of Returning to Youth. Those of the highest rank who understand it may prolong their years and banish illness; the next rank are those who do not harm themselves. If one is still young and strong yet knows the Way of Returning to Youth, supplements the brain with yin elixirs, and gathers jade nectar from the long valleys, even without consuming medicinal substances, they will not fall short of three hundred years—though they may not attain immortality. Those who fail to grasp the method are like ancient men who filled ice cups with boiling water or wrapped fire in feather bundles. Moreover, straining beyond one’s capacity in thought injures; forcing what one’s strength cannot bear injures; sorrow and emaciation injure; excessive joy injures; relentless pursuit of desire injures; prolonged talking and laughter injure; untimely rest injures; drawing bows and bending crossbows injure; deep intoxication and vomiting injure; lying down immediately after a full meal injures; running until breathless injures; exuberant shouts and bitter weeping injure; lack of union between yin and yang injures. When accumulated injuries reach their limit, early death follows—and early death is not the Way.

Thus, the methods of nurturing life prescribe: do not spit far, do not walk briskly, do not strain the ears to listen, do not fix the gaze for long, do not sit too long, do not lie down to exhaustion, dress before the cold arrives, remove clothing before the heat sets in. Do not eat only when ravenous, and never to excess; do not drink only when parched, and never too much. Overeating leads to accumulations and masses; overdrinking breeds phlegm and stagnation. Avoid extreme labor and extreme idleness; do not rise late, do not sweat profusely, do not sleep excessively, do not gallop on horseback or race in carriages, do not strain the eyes gazing afar, do not consume too much raw or cold food, do not drink wine in the wind, do not bathe too frequently, do not harbor grand ambitions or distant aspirations, do not devise strange or intricate crafts. In winter, avoid extreme warmth; in summer, avoid extreme coolness. Do not sleep exposed beneath the stars, do not bare the shoulders while sleeping. Do not brave bitter cold, scorching heat, fierce winds, or thick fog.

When the five flavors enter the mouth, do not favor any to excess. For sourness in excess harms the spleen; bitterness in excess harms the lungs; pungency in excess harms the liver; saltiness in excess harms the heart; sweetness in excess harms the kidneys. Such is the natural principle of the five phases. What is called harm does not manifest immediately—it is only that over time, longevity is diminished.

Therefore, those skilled in nurturing life rise and retire according to the seasons’ rhythms, conduct themselves with perfect harmony in daily life, regulate sinews and bones through proper stretching and bending, ward off illness and repel evil with breath-control techniques, circulate and harmonize the constructive and defensive qi with methods of supplementation and drainage, and balance activity and rest with the essentials of giving and taking. They restrain anger to preserve yin qi and temper joy to nourish yang qi. Only then do they first take herbs and plants to remedy deficiencies, and later ingest the golden elixir to secure boundless life. The principle of long life is fully contained herein.

If one is determined to follow their inclinations, self-assured in their broad understanding and acceptance of fate, dismissive of heterodox teachings, indulging their passions and exhausting their strength, with no care for longevity—then to such a person, these words will pass like the wind through the ears or lightning across the eyes, utterly incomprehensible. Though their bodies wither in dissipation and their breath expires amid silk and brocade, they remain content. How could one even speak to them of nurturing life? Not only will they not accept it, but they will call it heresy. To expect them to believe would be like offering a bright mirror to the blind or playing strings and bamboo to the deaf."

Bao Puzi said: "The greatest virtue of Heaven and Earth is life, for they cherish all living things. Thus, the most secret and weighty teaching of the Daoist tradition is none other than the method of attaining long life. For this reason, transmission is sealed by blood oaths, and to pass it to the unworthy is forbidden under pain of heavenly punishment. Even my late master dared not lightly bestow it upon others; only when a seeker pursued it with utmost diligence would he carefully select the most refined among them to instruct—how much less, then, could one peddle it to those who neither love nor seek it, or who seek without sincerity? Those whose destiny does not align with immortality, even if they saw immortals thronging the world each day, would still insist they were a different breed of men, that such beings exist apart in the world, or dismiss them as transformations of ghosts and spirits, or mere chance encounters with the natural.
