How could they ever accept that such attainments come from cultivation? If their hearts do not believe, even if Red Pine or Wang Qiao took them by the ear to instruct them, they would still call it heresy.

Yet while there are some in this age who possess discernment and faith, they suffer from their inability to diligently seek a true master. Those who grasp the essentials and obtain the authentic Dao are indeed exceedingly rare, and cannot be found in haste. Still, those who know of it are few but not entirely absent from the world. It is only that seekers are neither broad nor earnest in their pursuit; those destined for immortality will naturally encounter the Way when the time is right. Thus, there are those who seek and do not find, but never has one who did not seek found it.

In the world, there are countless deceitful charlatans who usurp the title of Daoist to swindle money. Yet these frauds are not entirely ignorant—they may have glimpsed the rudiments or falsely cultivated a reputation, embellishing their lies with perverse arts. The credulous, unable to distinguish truth from falsehood, flock to them with questions, only to be deceived and constrained, prevented from seeking further. These impostors then declare to earnest seekers, 'The Dao is exhausted here.' How lamentable and infuriating that so many of the well-intentioned are thus misled!

When they hear of those who understand the dissolution of the Five Clouds, the transmutation of the Eight Stones, the refinement of the Nine Elixirs, the alchemy of yellow and white, the crystallization of jade-like waters, the transformation of cinnabar and azure, the congealing of frost and snow in the divine crucible, or the gathering of magic mushrooms on Mount Song, they slander them, saying, 'Only Red Pine and Wang Qiao knew such arts; those who claim to know them today are all liars.' The shallow-minded, failing to recognize the deceit in these words, abandon their distant quests. Alas! How grievous and worthy of sighs!"

Swift as the sun’s shadow racing, fleeting as the whirlwind’s flight—scarcely has youth waned before old age arrives. Nothing in this world moves faster. A lifespan of a hundred years amounts to no more than thirty thousand days. In infancy and weakness, one knows nothing; in decline and dotage, all joy and pleasure fade. Between the dullness of childhood and the confusion of senility, only a few decades remain. Yet peril, distress, sorrow, and sickness follow one upon another, consuming half one’s years in the world. Even those who reach a full century find that moments of laughter, peace, and ease scarcely exceed fifty or sixty years. In a breath, all is extinguished—grief, worry, and senility claim six or seven thousand days. In the blink of an eye, life is spent. How much rarer still are those who attain the full measure of a hundred years? Reflecting deeply, one can hardly mock the mayfly or the morning mushroom. Truly, those who do not know the Dao reach the depths of sorrow.

A village saying goes: "A man in the world at leisure loses a day with each passing day, like an ox or sheep led to the slaughter—with every step forward, death draws nearer." Though the comparison is crude, it holds truth. The enlightened do not grieve over death, not because they do not seek to avoid it, but because they know no way to escape it. To fret in vain brings no benefit. Thus, they embrace fate and understand destiny, and so feel no sorrow—not because they do not desire long life. The Duke of Zhou offered himself in place of King Wu; Confucius dragged his staff and mourned his time. Even sages do not delight in early death.

Yet common folk, seeing Zhuang Zhou’s parable of the great dream, rush to espouse the doctrine that life and death are the same. These are but cunning sophisms, forced displays of detachment, utterances of defiance and restraint—all of which Confucius would have refuted and condemned. Observe those who speak thus: when afflicted by illness, they hasten to seek acupuncture and moxibustion; when facing peril, they fear death intensely. Yet the prevailing folly of the age scorns true faith, turning from the classics to the sayings of the philosophers. If one does not spout clever, paradoxical arguments, they are dismissed as rustic and unlearned, no follower of Laozi or Zhuangzi.

Thus, those without conviction drift into baseless doctrines and cannot turn back. Laozi made long life and enduring vision his pursuit, while Zhuang Zhou prized wagging his tail in the mud, refusing to be a netted tortoise or an embroidered ox, starving yet begging grain from the Marquis of the River. From this, we know he could not truly equate life and death. Later scholars, unable to examine truth from falsehood, cling to a single phrase—how mistaken they are!

Moreover, to descend into the Nine Springs below, where endless night reigns, first to become food for ants and worms, then to merge with dust and earth—this thought sears the heart, drawing involuntary gasps. If one harbors the will to seek life, how can one not cast aside all non-essential affairs and cultivate the profound and wondrous practice? Those who do not believe may do as they please. But those who do believe yet remain entangled in worldly attachments, unable to devote themselves wholly to nurturing life, practicing only in the spare moments between worldly pursuits—such efforts often begin too late and rarely succeed.

What men pursue most fervently are power, profit, and desire. If one’s body is not whole, then high office, great wealth, mountains of gold and jade, and countless beauties are not truly one’s own. Therefore, the superior person first attends to the work of long life, for once long life is secured, all things may be pursued at will. If one has not yet ascended to the immortal realm, one may at least become an earthbound immortal, at ease. Even if one lives for centuries like Peng Zu or Laozi, enjoying the pleasures of mortal life, and only then gradually ascends, it is still a glorious achievement. Yet one must find a worthy teacher; without a teacher to honor, there is no way to succeed.

In former times, the Han Empress Dowager studied the *Book of Documents* under Xiahou Sheng, gifting him a hundred catties of gold and countless other treasures. When Sheng died, she bestowed two million coins upon his family and wore mourning for him for a hundred days. When Emperor Cheng was heir apparent, he studied the *Analects* under Zhang Yu. Upon ascending the throne, he enfeoffed Yu as a Marquis within the Pass, granting him a thousand households, and appointed him Grand Master of Splendid Happiness, bestowing a hundred catties of gold. Later, he promoted Yu to Chancellor and advanced his rank to Marquis of Anchang. When Yu grew old and sought to retire, the emperor granted him a carriage drawn by four horses and a hundred catties of gold, along with tens of thousands of coins. When Yu fell ill, the Son of Heaven personally visited to inquire after him, kneeling at Yu’s bedside.

When Emperor Zhang was heir apparent, he studied the *Classic of Filial Piety* under Huan Rong. Upon ascending the throne, he appointed Rong as Grand Master over the Masses. The emperor visited Rong’s residence, allowing him to sit facing east with a desk and staff. When officials and Rong’s disciples and students—numbering in the hundreds—gathered, the emperor personally held the text and lectured. He enfeoffed Rong as a Marquis within the Pass, granting him five thousand households. When Rong fell ill, the emperor visited his home, dismounting at the entrance to the lane and hurrying forward with the scrolls in his arms, like a disciple. When Rong passed away, the emperor wore mourning for him.

None of these men conquered cities or battled in the wilds, broke enemy lines or expanded borders, displayed banners or performed meritorious service, campaigned in the Qilian Mountains, or achieved great deeds in distant lands. They merely transmitted the teachings of a single classic, expounding its passages, yet were revered to such heights. This was but the recitation of the dead’s leftover words. Even the noble emperor humbled himself to honor them thus. Yet in the world today, some wish to practice the Dao of long life but refuse to humble themselves before a worthy teacher, instead treating them with disdain and demanding the essentials. How can they succeed?

What benefit does the student’s reverence and service bring to the teacher’s slightest measure? Yet without such devotion, the student’s heart is not fully given; if the heart is not fully given, the teacher’s instructions will not be earnest; if the instructions are not earnest, how can the secret methods be fully obtained? At best, the teacher may offer superficial teachings—how can this achieve the work of immortality?

There are also those whose appearance is pleasing and who feign devotion to the Dao, yet their faith does not root in heart and spirit. When they seek something, they feign humility, but within days, their negligence and disrespect become apparent.

If one encounters a wise and discerning teacher who wishes to carefully observe the transformations of the seeker’s disposition, they may deliberately withhold instruction for a prolonged period to test their resolve. In such cases, those whose true nature and deceit are exposed will ultimately not be taught, and even if taught, the teacher will not fully disclose the truth. If the words are not complete, the instruction will be of no benefit.

Chen Anshi was but thirteen years old, a mere retainer’s son under Guan Shuben, yet he had already attained the immortal Dao. Shuben, at seventy with a head of white hair, bowed morning and night to Anshi, saying, “The Dao is noble, and virtue is precious—those who attain the Way first are to be revered as teachers. I dare not grow weary in upholding the rites of a disciple.” Thereupon, Anshi revealed to him the essential methods, and Shuben too ascended as an immortal.

Man receives his spirit first from Heaven and Earth, then his qi and blood from his parents. Yet without a enlightened teacher to impart the Way of transcending the world, there is no means to escape death. The flame may still flicker in the carved stone, but one’s allotted years will have already withered. From this, we may conclude: the grace of an enlightened teacher truly surpasses that of Heaven and Earth, and is weightier by far than that of one’s parents. How can one fail to revere them? How can one fail to seek them?

Bao Puzi said: “The ancients valued substance over form, prizing conduct and disdaining mere words. Thus, those who governed did not exalt rhetorical flourish, and those who cultivated the Dao did not esteem eloquent discourse. As customs declined and grew shallow, external adornments multiplied endlessly. The writings of the Confucians piled high as mountains, while the esoteric texts of the arts likewise proliferated in the hands of practitioners. Yet beginners could not be entrusted with the great essentials at once. Moreover, human nature delights in abundance—whether in fundamentals or trifles. Thus, later adepts of the Dao, such as Gan Ji, Rong Song, and the various masters of talismanic silk, each composed thousands of chapters. But these were mostly admonitions and exhortations, unwilling to clearly reveal the ultimate direction of the great Way. The truest and most profound secrets were either transmitted orally or inscribed on no more than a foot of silk, concealed within the folds of a sash. Without long and devoted study under a master, without enduring trials and repeated efforts, one could not obtain them.

“Disciples of varying quality, each according to the depth of their dedication, the length of their austerities, the reach of their intelligence, and the strength of their resolve, received instruction suited to their capacity. Over hundreds and thousands of years, only occasionally would one exhaust the hidden teachings concealed in their master’s pillowcase or at their elbow. Some were merely instructed in compounding medicines; once the medicine was made, it was divided among them—enough to grant them immortality, yet they were never given the written formula. Thus, among the Daoists of the world, scarcely one in ten thousand knows the art of the Golden Elixir. Yet the shallow-minded assume that the methods of immortality lie fully recorded in voluminous texts or in the rituals of sacrifice and prostration. But long life is governed by the Great Medicine alone—it cannot be attained through offerings and invocations.

“In the Qin and Han dynasties, grand supplications were raised, sacrifices made to the Great Unity and the Five Spirits, to Chenbao and the Eight Deities, expending cattle, sheep, grain, silk, and countless sums of money—all to no avail. How much less could a common man, lacking in virtue and neglecting his own body, hope to extend his years by offering the three sacrificial beasts, wine, and delicacies to beseech the spirits? Such delusion is profound indeed.

“Some well-meaning seekers, though earnest in their pursuit of the Dao, fail to diligently seek enlightened teachers or compound the rare medicines. Instead, they spend their days reciting and expounding useless texts by the thousands, gaining nothing even in old age, and then conclude that immortality is but a myth. Others prostrate themselves before empty seats, slaughtering animals and burning incense to pray for blessings, yet their illnesses remain uncured, deaths follow one after another, their wealth is exhausted, and no miracles occur. Still, they do not repent, insisting only that their devotion was insufficient. Had they applied such diligence to seeking a master of true methods, had they spent such wealth on purchasing medicines, they would surely have attained transcendence and long life. What difference is there between this and plowing a field of stone, hoping for a thousand granaries’ harvest? Though their labor is spent in full, they gain nothing, for they have not found the proper path. It is like setting out for Chu but taking the road to Yan—though the horse be swift, it will never arrive, not for lack of speed, but for having lost the way.

“Some are by nature credulous and quick to trust others, yet their intelligence is insufficient to discern truth from falsehood or gauge the depth of teachings. Their learning is narrow, and they lack the discernment to judge things rightly. Later generations, being coarse and shallow, upon meeting a single person who boasts of his own attainments—claiming to possess secret texts—will cling to him in devotion. Yet many petty men and mere youths falsely assume the name of ‘adept,’ their reputations exceeding their true worth through boasting and deceit. Inwardly greedy and impure, they seek only profit. When asked for instruction, they feign reluctance, nodding and sighing, as if the treasures they guard are too profound to impart. If a request is made, they comply with a smile, promising fulfillment ‘in due time,’ ensnaring the unwary so they cannot withdraw, convincing them that their efforts have been insufficient and their offerings too meager. Thus, the disciple’s faith deepens, their reverence grows, and they present rare treasures, performing menial tasks like a servant—carrying heavy burdens over long distances, braving peril and hardship, hoping to prove themselves through toil and suffering, to gain some rare teaching. Yet years pass in vain, their parents’ support squandered, their wives and children neglected. They endure frost and ice, following their master year after year, wasting their resources and strength, achieving nothing in the end.

“At first, the master deceives them; later, he may feel shame, yet remains bewildered, his own abilities exhausted and lacking. What method can he use to teach others? How can he lead anyone to attainment? I have seen no small number of such cases—perhaps more than ten. Some style themselves with lofty names, lingering in the world for ages, claiming to be three or four hundred years old, merely changing their names and falsely calling themselves sages, relying on others to spread their fame. Many serve them, yet I refrain from recording their names. Moving among the common folk, where people cannot distinguish truth from falsehood, they are praised and exalted, their deceptions amplified. The artful lies of these frauds generate empty renown, and their influence spreads unchecked, for none can discern the truth. Thus, even the wise may occasionally overlook careful scrutiny, relying instead on hearsay, leading students astray. Such cases are the cause of constant sighs.

“Whenever I see these charlatans deceiving the world to gain power and profit, I know that sooner or later they will suffer calamity. Though the net of Heaven is wide, none escape its mesh. Yet it is the misguided seekers who deserve pity. Most people chase empty reputations, few examine the facts. Hearing that so-and-so has many disciples—perhaps a hundred—they assume he must be extraordinary, rushing to join the throng, becoming his devoted followers, wasting their time in reverence for such a dullard. They do not seek the essence, and he profits from their numbers. Some grow wealthy, yet though they pursue him for years, he imparts no true method. Foolish men do not realize this person is unworthy of service—how can they remain so utterly blind? It is truly lamentable!”

How can one hope to find a towering tree fit to reach the heavens by scouring a mound no taller than a fathom? How can one expect to catch a fish large enough to swallow a boat by straining the water in an ox’s hoofprint? No matter how long one searches, how could such a thing ever be found?

Alas! Future seekers, though they must make finding a teacher their duty, must also treat careful selection as their most urgent task. Men of narrow minds, shallow conduct, meager virtue, and scant karmic conditions lack the Way to cultivate others, nor can they repay the weight of another’s kindness with any true practice. Ponder this deeply—do not let your efforts be in vain.

*Baopuzi* says: “Those Daoist masters who trade in empty reputations excel at deception to mislead their students. Moreover, they conceal their shortcomings and hide their ignorance, ashamed to admit what they do not know. Outwardly, they pretend to have studied broadly enough, yet they will never deign to seek instruction from those superior to themselves. Like fools, they cling to their poverty of knowledge, standing blind before a wall. And they do not merely remain silent—they even resent and slander those who truly possess the Way, fearing that another’s reputation might surpass their own.

Do such men truly seek the method of long life? Their only aim is to attract disciples and exploit their wealth to satisfy their desires. Yet they do not realize that Heaven hears from on high, and in the end, they will surely suffer for this. The poor cannot falsely claim to be rich, nor the lowly pretend to be noble—how much less can those who possess no true virtue or power sustain a following of disciples?

Even ordinary people should not harbor envy toward the good, let alone Daoists, who ought to live by loyalty, trust, and joyful sincerity. How can they allow such corruption to fester in their hearts? Men cannot perceive the divine, yet the divine perceives them with ease. This is no different from standing outside a gauze curtain, unable to see into the inner chamber, yet behaving arrogantly as if unseen. It is like a thief who steals a bell, fearing others will hear its sound, and so covers his own ears. Those who are spiritually deaf and blind care only for monopolizing fame, gathering disciples, seeking external reputation, and scheming for wealth. Their jealousy of those superior to them exceeds even the worldly struggle for power.

They wield their lips and tongues like blades, forming factions through slander and praise. Outwardly, they feign closeness, but their hearts are distant; their words profess shared purpose, yet their actions conceal the venom of wasps and scorpions. Such men are despised by both Heaven and humanity—they summon their own misfortune.

Even in studying the Five Classics, one should not hesitate to ask those beneath them to cultivate virtue and refine their work, daily advancing in brightness. For coarse skills like archery and charioteering, minor arts like calligraphy and arithmetic, the humble labor of farming and sericulture, or the small techniques of carpentry—all require a teacher to master their principles. How much more so for the method of long life, which seeks to extend one’s years and preserve one’s existence? This is no different from rescuing someone from certain death.

How can one cling to the shame of seeking instruction, forever trapped in ignorance, never changing even in old age, never repenting even at death? Such men are the most benighted of mortals. Those who witness this feel shame on their behalf, yet the doers themselves remain oblivious, heedless of their own reflection.

Even Confucian scholars should steadfastly uphold simplicity, presenting themselves with humility—knowing as if ignorant, possessing as if lacking—so that mediocre men cannot fully praise them. If praised, they do not respond; if questioned, they reply only after deferring. How is it that some Daoists, instead, insist on claiming knowledge where there is none, pretending to possess what they lack, brazenly flaunting themselves to pursue illicit gains? Those who stray and do not turn back only sink deeper. If any act in this way, I trust they will feel no shame in correcting themselves.

I do not speak idly—these words arise from genuine concern, for I cannot remain silent in the face of such affliction. I merely pity the foolish and cannot bear to watch infants fall into a well. If any reader awakens to this, it will be like a single herb in the elixir of immortality—what more need I say? To refuse bitter medicine when danger is near is to invite disaster so obvious it requires no pulse-taking to foresee.”

*Baopuzi* says: “If a man were sentenced to death, and another could save him, would he hesitate to endure hardship or humble himself with entreaties? Surely he would gain the merit of preserving life. Yet these mediocre Daoists, lacking the great method of the Golden Elixir, will certainly not attain long life—this is already clear. Even if they can cure illnesses with life-restoring effects, fast for years without hunger, command ghosts and spirits, appear and vanish at will, see a thousand miles, discern others’ fortunes, dispel hidden curses, and foresee misfortune before it arises—none of this will benefit their lifespan. Still, they feel shame in seeking instruction and disdain serving those more advanced, sparing themselves a moment’s humility while embracing endless suffering. Such men fail to see the obvious.

An ancient saying goes: *Life is of the greatest benefit to me.* In terms of value, even the throne of an emperor cannot compare to this method. In terms of weight, even ruling the world cannot equal this art. Hence the parable of the dead king who would gladly be a living rat.

Governing a state to bring peace, cultivating the body to preserve life—these do not come by chance but are achieved through effort. To cling to the hollow name of brevity and shun the temporary labor of seeking a teacher—though one may claim not to be foolish, I do not believe it.

If a man were spared certain death only to face execution, he would still rejoice in exchanging a heavy punishment for a lighter one, trading agony for mere survival, finding its pain sweeter than rebirth. People only do not worry about death because they do not know its appointed day. If they truly knew, and could delay mutilation by even a little, they would surely do so. How much less should they shrink from personally sweeping floors, holding towels, and serving those greater than themselves to learn the deathless Way? What hardship is there in this that the deluded fear?”

Suppose a man, ashamed to run swiftly, awaited the scorching of wildfire, or shamed to flee the wind, sank into the depths of a deep abyss—surely the world would call him senseless. All would know to laugh at his failure to avoid disaster, yet none would fault him for not fearing true calamity. Why is this?

The Master of Embracing Simplicity says: “Of old, those who composed Daoist texts were many, yet none failed to devote themselves to expansive, florid words to exalt abstruse principles. None thoroughly discussed the steps and paths to long life, nor cauterized the afflictions and pains of the Way, as I have earnestly done. My true aim is to guide the deluded back, to gather at dusk what was lost at dawn, to draw up the well-rope for one fallen—better this than to let them drown. Yet those who cling to fair-seeming sickness and reject the bitter stone cannot be helped.

Who among men is without fault? But to err and then reform is like the eclipse of sun and moon—even the disciple of Yan would approve. I further wish those of later times who cherish the Way of life to examine carefully where they place their trust. Thus I exhaust my loyal counsel and good plans, without adorning them with extravagant or ornate words. My speech, once uttered, strikes to the quick; my brush, once set to paper, spares no pain. My only concern is long life, and to curb what strays—what else do I seek?”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity says: “I deeply reflect that those who study the arts of the Dao and cultivate life often follow teachers unworthy of the name, and so achieve nothing. This leads later aspirants, seeing their failure to attain long life, to claim that immortality’s methods do not exist. Those who judge themselves incapable of disciplining body and restraining self to cultivate the wondrous and subtle will merely advance without the work of seeking office, and retreat without the merit of defying age. They mislead themselves within and discourage those to come without.

That immortality can be learned and attained is as clear as millet and grain can be sown and reaped. Yet there has never been one who reaped fine grain without plowing, nor one who attained long life and transcended the world without diligence.”

One may ask: “Dare I inquire whether those who abstain from grains may attain long life? How many methods are there, and which is the most excellent?”

The Master Embracing Simplicity replies: “Those who abstain from grains may only cease the expense of meat and grain; this alone does not confer long life. I have questioned those who have abstained from grains for many years, and they say they suffer fewer illnesses and pains, surpassing their condition when they ate grain. Those who ingest *Atractylodes* or consume *Polygonatum*, or take the *Yu Yu Liang* pills twice daily, after three days feel abundant in strength, able to bear burdens and travel far, their bodies light and untiring. Those who take various mineral elixirs—those who, with a single dose, preserve their center for ten or five years—and those who swallow *qi*, consume talismans, or drink divine water, merely avoid hunger; their bodies cannot endure labor.

Though Daoist texts say, ‘To attain long life, the intestines must be pure; to attain deathlessness, the intestines must be without dregs,’ and further, ‘Those who eat grass are swift but foolish; those who eat meat are strong but fierce; those who eat grain are wise but short-lived; those who eat *qi* are spiritually luminous and deathless’—these are but the one-sided claims of *qi*-practitioners and cannot be solely relied upon.

If one wishes to ingest the great elixir of golden cinnabar, it is best to abstain from food for about a hundred days beforehand. If one cannot, taking it directly will only delay one’s transcendence slightly, without great harm. Should one encounter a time of famine and hide in the mountains and forests, knowing this method allows one to avoid starvation. Otherwise, there is no urgent need to abstain, for haste brings no great benefit.

Moreover, those who abstain from meat while still among people cannot help but desire the scent of rich and fresh foods. If one has not yet abandoned the world and retired to mountain caves, one cannot entirely forsake the five flavors. There is no need to impose suffering upon oneself; it is better not to abstain from grains but to moderate hunger and satiety.

There are about a hundred methods: some involve taking several dozen pills of center-preserving mineral elixirs, allowing one to avoid hunger for forty or fifty days. Refining pine, cypress, or *Atractylodes* can also preserve the center, though not as effectively as the great elixirs, and their effects do not last beyond ten years. Some methods allow one to abstain for a hundred or two hundred days, while others require daily ingestion to avoid hunger.

Another method involves first eating a sumptuous meal to fullness, then taking medicine to nourish the ingested food, preventing its digestion and allowing one to abstain for three years. To return to eating grain, one should take *mallow seeds* and lard to purge it, so the undigested food descends unchanged.

In Luoyang, the Daoist Dong Weinian resided in the White Society, consuming nothing. Chen Zixu attended him and studied the Dao for many years before obtaining his method. It involves pounding over ten ingredients—such as licorice, *Saposhnikovia*, and *Amaranthus* seeds—into a powder. One first takes a square-inch spoonful, then swallows twelve stone pellets the size of sparrow eggs, which suffice for a hundred days. One then takes more powder, and one’s strength and complexion remain as before. To return to eating grain, one must drink *mallow seed* decoction to expel the stones before eating.

There is also the method of *Red Dragon’s Blood* and *Blue Dragon’s Ointment*: using cinnabar, malachite, and water, one places stones within and waits a moment until the stones soften and become edible. If not consumed immediately, they dissolve entirely. Eating these stones to satiety makes one robust.

Another method is the *Stone-Drawing Powder*: adding a square-inch spoonful to a *dou* of white pebbles and boiling them with water renders them edible like taro, serving as grain. Zhang Taiyuan and his family, along with dozens of disciples, lived in the Linlü Mountains, eating stones by this method for over ten years, all growing plump and healthy. However, one must obtain white stones, unlike the *Red Dragon’s Blood* and *Blue Dragon’s Ointment*, which can be used immediately upon obtaining stones, though they require boiling, which is troublesome.

Some methods use talismans, some use water, and some combine talisman and water. Others involve eating nine dried jujubes daily with one or two *sheng* of wine, or consuming the *qi* of the twelve hours, beginning at midnight and counting down from nine nines to five fives.

In spring, one faces east to consume the green *qi* of Jupiter, allowing it to enter the liver; in summer, one ingests the red *qi* of Mars, allowing it to enter the heart. In the months between seasons, one consumes the yellow *qi* of Saturn, allowing it to enter the spleen; in autumn, the white *qi* of Venus, allowing it to enter the lungs; in winter, the black *qi* of Mercury, allowing it to enter the kidneys.

The Zhongyue Daoist Xi Yuanjie consumed the essence of the six *wu* days, which was highly effective. For instance, during the ten-day period of *jiazi*, if the essence of *wuchen* is present, one faces the direction of *chen* and swallows *qi* for the entire ten days. When the next *jia* day arrives, one faces the *wu* of that period.

Gan Shi’s method involves summoning the Jade Maidens of the six *jia* and six *ding* days, each with specific names, and drinking water upon which one has recited incantations. This can also prevent oxen and horses from growing hungry.

Another method involves visualizing the spirit within the spleen, named Yellow Skirt Child, and inhaling internal *qi* with the mouth closed. All these methods have genuine efficacy.

I have seen many who have abstained from grains for two or three years; they are all light of body, fair of complexion, and resistant to wind, cold, heat, and dampness, though none are particularly plump. While I have not seen those who have abstained for decades, people who completely abstain from grain die within ten days, yet these practitioners remain unaffected after years. Why doubt that they cannot endure much longer?

If those who abstain from grains were all emaciated and constantly anxious, I would fear they could not last long. Yet when I ask them, they all say that initially, they felt weak, but gradually grew stronger, improving month by month and year by year. Thus, they can endure long without concern.

Those who attain long life and the Dao invariably do so through ingesting medicines and swallowing *qi*, and none achieve it without effort. Those who take medicines and abstain from grains almost always first endure hardship. Those who use talismanic water or solely practice *qi*-ingestion all become fatigued and emaciated within forty days, but after this period, they grow healthy.”

Zheng Jun said: "By nature, I do not drink much wine. Formerly, when I resided in Mount Tong, I abstained from grains for about two years and could drink several *dou* of wine without becoming drunk. From this, we may infer that fasting renders one more resistant to toxins, and resistance to toxins is a sign of immunity to illness."

I then asked how he obtained wine in the mountains. Zheng Jun replied: "First, brew fine cloud-liquid wine without pressing or straining it. Then, mix powdered cinnamon, *fuzi*, licorice, and five or six other ingredients into pills, dry them in the sun, and drop one pill—about the size of a chicken’s egg—into a *dou* of water. Immediately, it becomes excellent wine. There is also the Yellow Emperor’s method for cloud-liquid springs: combine sprouted rice with seven or eight medicinal ingredients, take one *sheng*, and add it to a *sheng* of water. It resembles the water added to thousand-year-old bitter wine, yet it never depletes, and its flavor remains consistently fine, greatly benefiting those who drink it."

As for talismanic water for grain abstention, though it initially emaciates the body, it is advisable to master it, for in times of sudden famine, when there is no opportunity to prepare medicines, talismanic water is the best recourse. There was a certain Master Feng who solely ingested *qi* and had abstained from grains for three years. Observing him, I saw that he could climb mountains carrying a load of about one *hu*, walking all day without fatigue. He would also draw his bow from time to time but spoke very little, and when he did, he refused to raise his voice. When asked why, he said: "Abstaining from grains depletes essence and expends *qi*—these are the greatest taboos."

I have also repeatedly observed shallow Daoist practitioners who, seeking to appear ethereal and extraordinary, court fame as non-eaters, yet in truth, they know nothing of the Way. They merely feign abstention from congee and rice. As for drinking wine, they consume over a *dou* daily, along with dried meats, preserved fruits, dates, chestnuts, eggs, and the like, never ceasing to eat. Some devour large quantities of meat, swallowing only the juices and spitting out the dregs, consuming dozens of *jin* in a single day—this is nothing but indulging in fine food. Ordinary wine-drinkers who consume wine and dried meats without eating grains can all endure for half a year or a full year without distress, yet this cannot be called grain abstention.

In Wu, there was a Daoist named Shi Chun who, whenever he practiced *qi*-ingestion to treat others’ illnesses, would abstain from food until the patient recovered—sometimes for a hundred days, sometimes for a month. When Emperor Jing of Wu heard of this, he said: 'This cannot last long; he will surely starve to death.' He then summoned Chun, locked him up, and had him closely guarded. Chun only requested three or two *sheng* of water, and after more than a year, his complexion grew even more radiant, and his strength remained as before. The emperor asked how much longer he could endure. Chun replied: 'There is no limit; I could go for decades. I only fear dying of old age, not hunger.' The emperor then released him. Judging by Chun’s words, it is clear that grain abstention cannot extend lifespan.

Nowadays, there are also those who have mastered Chun’s method."

Someone asked about the Way of not feeling cold.

Bao Puzi said: "Some, on the day of *Lidong* (the beginning of winter), ingest the talisman of the six *bing* and six *ding* celestial stems, or perform the *qi* of the Five Fires one thousand two hundred times with sealed lips—thus they feel no cold for the entire twelfth month. Others drink *Taiyang* wine, or take the *Zishi ying zhuqi* powder, or first ingest one *yang* pellet followed by two *yin* pellets, which may also suffice to endure a day and night without cold. The *yin* pellets are made of orpiment, azurite, alum, and magnetite. The *yang* pellets are made of realgar, cinnabar, and chalcanthite. Yet none of these contribute to the matter of extending lifespan."

Someone asked about the Way of not feeling heat.
Bao Puzi said: "Some, on the day of *Lixia* (the beginning of summer), ingest the talisman of the six *ren* and six *gui* celestial stems, or circulate the *qi* of the six *gui*, or take the *Xuanbing* pellet, or consume the *Feishuang* powder. However, these require the bark of trees from Xiaoqiu and the blood of a black snake traveling north at noon on the fifth day of the fifth month—thus few succeed in compounding them. Only You Bozi and Wang Zhongdu, these two men, could don heavy furs, expose themselves to the summer sun, and surround themselves with ten braziers of fire without ever complaining of heat or breaking a sweat. They must have employed this method."

Someone asked about the Way of warding off the five weapons.

Bao Puzi replied: "I have heard that the late Emperor of Wu once received essential methods from Master Jie, who taught that merely writing the characters of the Northern Dipper and the sun and moon would render one unafraid of blades. The Emperor tested this on dozens of his attendants, who would always charge first into battle and breach enemy lines, yet none suffered injury throughout their lives. Master Zheng said that simply reciting the names of the five weapons also proved effective. The blade is called *Great Chamber*, governed by the *Xu* star; the spear is called *Crooked Bow*, governed by the *Di* star; the arrow is called *Wandering*, governed by the *Yinghuo* star; the sword is called *Lost Wound*, governed by the *Jiao* star; the crossbow is called *Far Gaze*, governed by the *Zhang* star; the halberd is called *Great General*, governed by the *Shen* star. Before battle, one should quietly invoke them. Alternatively, on the fifth day of the fifth month, one may craft the *Crimson Numinous Talisman* and wear it over the heart. Or at noon on a *bingwu* day, one may make the *Talisman of the Three Pouches of the Swallow Lord, Dragon, and Tiger*. Yearly talismans are renewed each year, monthly talismans each month, and daily talismans each day. One may also wear the *Talisman of the Pledge of the Queen Mother of the West*, the *Talisman of Yinghuo and Vermilion Bird*, the *Talisman of the Southern Culmen and Molten Metal*, the *Talisman of Repelling Blades*, or the *Talisman of Zhurong*. Or one may apply the *Jade Tablet Powder*, bathe in *forbidden onion broth*, or take the *male chaste tree* to craft the *Talisman of the Six Yin Spirit Generals*, pointing it toward the enemy. Or during a lunar eclipse, one may use the blood of a three-year-old toad with eight characters beneath its throat to inscribe the blade one wields. Or one may carry the *Martial Might Talisman* and the *Firefly Fire Pellet*. Or in the midst of clashing blades, one may tread the *Kui* star’s path, invoke the lords of the four directions, and witness clear efficacy. Even in this age, there are those who have mastered the arts of warding off the five weapons, and such cases are not uncommon."

Someone asked about the Way of vanishing and concealment.
Bao Puzi said: "The divine arts number five, and the methods of *sitting in presence yet vanishing* are among them. Yet they offer no benefit to the matters of lifespan and destiny; if one practices them idly without cause, they invite rumors of the uncanny and are not to be recklessly employed. They may be prepared for times of war and peril, and when used in extremity, can avert disaster. Master Zheng said that after ingesting the *Great Concealment Talisman* for ten days, one may turn left to vanish and turn right to reappear. Alternatively, one may apply the *Jade Terrace Pellet* to the body; or take the *Snake’s Foot Powder*; or carry the *Mother-Separating Herb*; or break the *Green Dragon Herb* to submit beneath the *Six Ding* spirits; or enter a bamboo field while grasping the *Celestial Pivot’s soil*; or construct a *Stone Chamber of the River Dragon* to hide in the shade of the *Cloud Canopy*; or crouch in the *Clear and Cold Abyss* to traverse the path of the *Dark Gate*; or ride the *Celestial One Steed* to roam the *Purple Chamber*; or ascend the *Hall of the Celestial One*; or enter the *Golden Coffer of the Jade Maiden*; or turn one’s back to the *Auxiliary* and face the *Palace*, standing beneath the *Three Canopies*."

"Alternatively, one may cast the *Kerchief and Untied Sandals*, the *Gallbladder Decoction*, or the *Child’s Clothing Talisman*; dwell as a child among the ignorant; employ the *Green Sap of Cinnamon Twigs*; invoke the *Six Jia Parents*; use the *Oblique Glue*; apply the *Dappled Horse’s Mud Pill*; summon the *Wood Spirit’s Offspring*; or burn the *Metal Shang Mugwort*. Thus, one may become a small child, an old man, a bird, a beast, grass, a tree, or one of the six livestock. Or, by relying on wood, become wood; by relying on stone, become stone; by relying on water, become water; by relying on fire, become fire—this is what is called shifting form and altering appearance, for those who cannot wholly conceal themselves."

Someone asked, "Cao Cao of Wei once captured Zuo Yuanfang and bound him in shackles, yet he achieved spontaneous liberation—by what method?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied, "I cannot precisely know what arts Lord Zuo employed. Yet, having perused various esoteric manuals, I find methods such as taking *coix seeds* thrice monthly, combined with *Three-Five Yin Elixir*; or using *paired yang teeth and placenta*; or leaping to catch the *Jumping Insect* on the seventh day of the seventh month while moving east; or wearing the *Dragon’s Offspring Single Garment* on a stone on the fifth day of the fifth month; or the *Thunderbolt Wedge* on the Summer Solstice; or the *Twenty-One-Character Celestial Talisman*; or using one’s own blood from a severed umbilical cord; or *jade and limonite*; or combining the *Mountain Lord’s Eye*, the *River Earl’s Limonite*, and *floating cloud dregs* as an unguent—all of which may effect self-liberation. Yet Lord Zuo’s transformations knew no bounds; he likely did not rely on these alone. When employing the *Six Jia* for transformation, his true form could not be seized."

Someone further asked, "Can one who cultivates the Dao be free from illness?"

Bao Puzi replied: "Those who fully grasp the principles of nurturing life both ingest divine medicines and practice qi-cultivation without ceasing. Morning and evening, they guide and stretch to circulate the constructive and defensive energies, ensuring no blockages arise. They further employ the arts of the bedchamber, regulate their diet, avoid wind and dampness, and refrain from what they cannot endure—thus may they remain free from illness. Yet those who dwell among common folk often find their resolve divided, their practice inconsistent, and their efforts slack and inattentive; hence they cannot avoid ailments. If one merely harbors faith in the Dao but undertakes no beneficial practice, their years and fate governed by the *gu* and *xu* stars, their body at risk of harm, then the Three Corpses, seizing their moments of weakness and perilous days, will enter the domain of fatal illness, summoning malevolent qi and recklessly inviting ghosts and demons to bring calamity. When the Six Adversities converge and the Three Punishments align, disaster will be great. In those still robust, various illnesses arise; in those already afflicted, their conditions flare anew.

Thus, the ancients who first pursued the Dao invariably studied medicine as well, to remedy immediate misfortunes. Yet ordinary Daoists, ignorant of this principle, rely on what they have heard, which often excludes methods for treating illness. Unable to withdraw from the world and dwell in seclusion, they fail to focus on internal practices to ward off sickness. When illness strikes, they lack means to cure it, and so fare worse than common folk who rely solely on decoctions and medicines. This is what is meant by 'advancing, they cannot master the steps of Handan; retreating, they lose the essence of Shouling.'

I have seen the *Golden Casket* and *Green Satchel* compiled by Dai Ba and Hua Ta, the *Yellow Silk Prescriptions* of Cui Zhongshu, and over five hundred scrolls of miscellaneous formulas from various schools. Gan, Hu, Lü, Fu, Zhou, Shi, Gan, Tang, Tong, Ruan, Nanhe, and others have each compiled *Emergency Remedies for Sudden Illness*—some one hundred and ten, others ninety-four, eighty-five, or forty-six—all deemed by the world to be thorough and beyond improvement. Yet upon close examination, I find them woefully incomplete, with many urgent conditions still unaddressed. Moreover, their contents are jumbled and disordered, lacking systematic arrangement, making it difficult to locate remedies when needed. Treatments for acute and sudden ailments often require rare and costly ingredients—dozens of varieties—which, unless one is wealthy and resides in the capital, cannot be readily obtained. Many prescribe acupuncture, yet their moxibustion methods fail to specify precise locations and measurements, merely listing the names of meridian points and *shu*-transport points. Without prior study of the *Mingtang* charts, the flow of *liuzhu*, and diagrams of the body’s postures, how can one comprehend them?

The hundred-scroll work I have compiled, titled *Jade Case Formulary*, categorizes illnesses by name and arranges them by type, avoiding disorder. The volumes on emergency remedies employ simple, direct, and effective methods, easily accessible even in rural lanes, where every glance reveals a cure. All urgent conditions are fully addressed; with this formulary at home, one need not rely on physicians. Many doctors inherit their profession in name only, cultivating empty reputations to pursue profit. The poor and humble cannot afford them, and those who do often suffer misdiagnoses, faring worse than if they had mastered the essentials themselves. Physicians are not always available when needed, and even when found, may refuse to attend promptly. Thus, minor ailments of the pores and interstices may fester into deep-seated, incurable maladies. In sudden emergencies, seeking help from afar often leads to untimely death."

Someone asked: "Can one foresee future fortune or misfortune, safety or peril, and thus preserve oneself? Is this part of the Dao?"

Bao Puzi replied: "Gazing at celestial patterns, examining earthly veins, divining wind and qi, casting calculations, advancing the Three Chessmen, pacing the Nine Palaces, consulting the Eight Trigrams, tracking the convergence of *fei* and *fu*, diagnosing omens in creatures, and reading fortunes through tortoise shells and milfoil—these are all inferior techniques, laborious and unreliable. To perceive all under heaven without stepping beyond one’s curtains—that would be truly divine.

Alternatively, one may invoke the celestial texts of the Three Sovereigns to summon the Lords of Life and Peril, the Sovereigns of the Five Peaks, the spirits of crossroads and village chiefs, and the numina of the Six Ding. These beings may appear and answer questions on any matter, revealing fortune and misfortune as clearly as if held in the palm, regardless of distance or obscurity.

Or one may summon the Six Yin Jade Maidens; the method requires sixty days to complete, after which they may be commanded for life. Or one may sacrifice to the Eight Scribes, the essences of the Eight Trigrams, which also enable foreknowledge of the unmanifest.

Or one may ingest a *fangcun* spoonful of *ge* flower, autumn *mang* hemp pollen, or a *daogui* of powdered medicine. Suddenly overcome by drowsiness, one may hear voices resolve undecided matters, instantly determining fortune or misfortune.

Or one may use a bright mirror nine *cun* or larger to gaze upon oneself while contemplating a question. After seven days and seven nights, immortals will appear—some male, some female, some old, some young. After a single revelation, one’s heart will intuit events a thousand *li* away. The mirror may be used singly or in pairs, called the Sun and Moon Mirrors. Or four may be used, called the Four Compass Mirrors, placed front, back, left, and right during observation. With four mirrors, the spirits seen are numerous—some flying freely, some riding dragons or tigers, all adorned in raiment and colors unlike those of the mortal world. Their appearances are recorded in manuals.

To practice this method, one must first memorize the names, titles, and ranks of the spirits to be summoned, as well as their attire. Without this, one may forget their identities upon sudden appearance or become startled, risking harm. The practice requires stillness, solitude, and seclusion in forests or groves, where external forms do not meet the eye and external sounds do not enter the ear—only then will the method succeed.

The Longevity Lord of the Three Lads and Nine Maidens, the hundred-and-twenty officials with nine-headed serpentine bodies—though they appear, one must not gaze upon them too intently."

Should any question or rebuke arise, do not respond. Should attendants blaze with radiance, or armored warriors and soldiers appear—riding dragons or tigers, with flutes and drums clamoring—do not raise your eyes nor speak. Instead, concentrate solely upon the true form of the Elder Lord. When the true form of the Elder Lord manifests, rise and bow twice.

The true form of the Elder Lord is thus envisioned: his surname is Li, his given name Dan, his courtesy name Boyang. He stands nine feet tall, with a yellow complexion, a beak like a bird’s, a prominent nose, and elegant brows five inches long. His ears measure seven inches, and three lines run vertically across his forehead. Upon his feet appear the Eight Trigrams. He rests upon a divine tortoise as his couch, within a hall of gold and jade, its steps of white silver. His robes are woven of five-colored clouds, his crown layered and towering, and at his side hangs the sharp-edged sword. He is attended by one hundred and twenty yellow youths. To his left stand twelve azure dragons, to his right twenty-six white tigers. Before him are twenty-four vermilion phoenixes, behind him seventy-two dark warriors. Preceding him march twelve *qiongqi*, and following him thirty-six *pixie*. Above, thunder and lightning gleam and flash resplendently. This account is drawn from the immortal scriptures.

Upon beholding the Elder Lord, one’s years and life shall extend, and the heart shall shine like the sun and moon, knowing all things without exception.

One inquired about the Way of strengthening the teeth. Baopuzi replied: "Those who can nourish them with the Flowery Pool, steep them in sweet wine, and strike the teeth three hundred times at dawn shall never know loosening. Next best is to hold rehmannia decoction in the mouth, or hold the Dark Gallbladder Decoction, or take the Snake Fat Pill, Alum Stone Pill, or Nine Thorns Powder. Thus, those already loose shall be firmed, and those with worms shall be cured. Moreover, taking the Numinous Flight Powder can even cause those already fallen to regrow."

One inquired about the Way of sharpening the ears. Baopuzi replied: "Those who can practice Dragon Leading Tiger Drawing, Bear Hanging Turtle Swallowing, Swallow Flying Snake Bending Bird Stretching, Heaven Bending Earth Raising—so that the red and yellow radiance never leaves the Cavern Chamber—and Monkey Crouching Hare Startling, reaching one thousand two hundred repetitions, shall not suffer hearing loss. For those already deaf, fumigate with the Dark Turtle, or stuff the ears with a pouch of thorn tips, sheep dung, cassia hairs, and sparrow cinnamon; or use wolfsbane and *yege*, or aconite with scallion mucus, compounded and inserted into the ears; or pour steamed carp brain into them—all shall cure deafness."

One inquired about the Way of brightening the eyes.

Baopuzi replied: "Those who can draw forth the ascending radiance of the Triple Burner and summon the Great Fire from the southern *Li*, washing it with luminous stone and soothing it with yang light, or burn the *Bing-Ding Cavern-Viewing Talisman* and rinse the eyes with wine, may write even in the night—ancients once did so. Or boil turnip seeds in bitter wine until done, dry them in the sun, grind them into powder, and take a square-inch spoonful thrice daily; after consuming a *dou*, one shall see clearly in the dark. Or decoct dog gallbladder with green sheep, turtledove, abalone shell, and the hundred flowers of *chongwei*; or instill a mixture of clove, coptis, and milk. All hundred ailments of the eyes shall be cured, and vision shall grow twice as keen as before."

One inquired about the Way of ascending perilous heights, traversing treacherous paths, and journeying afar without exhaustion. Baopuzi replied: "Only by ingesting the great elixir can the body grow light and strength endure, laboring without fatigue. For those newly entering mountains and forests whose bodies are not yet fully robust, it is fitting to wash the feet with *yunzhu* powder, *Baihua* nectar, or *Xuanzitang*; or take Tiger Gallbladder Pellets, Vermilion Bright Wine, *Tianxiong* Crane-Fat Pellets, *Feilian* decoction with autumn *mang*, plantain, or *zexie* powder. After ten days of use, not only shall one traverse great distances without weariness, but one’s pace shall quicken to thrice the usual speed.

Those who master *qiao* may roam the world, unbound by mountains or rivers. The Way of *qiao* has three methods: first, Dragon *Qiao*; second, Tiger *Qiao*; third, Deer-Reel *Qiao*. Some ingest talismans and concentrate their intent—if one wishes to travel a thousand *li*, one need only focus for a single hour. If one concentrates through all twelve hours of day and night, one may traverse twelve thousand *li* in a single day and night, though no farther; to exceed this, one must renew the focus as before. Others craft flying chariots from jujube wood, binding oxhide rings to swords to draw the mechanism; or visualize five snakes, six dragons, and three oxen interlocking their *gang* to ride upon, ascending forty *li* to the Great Clarity. Within the Great Clarity, the qi is fiercely cutting, able to sustain a person. The master says that when kites soar ever higher, they merely stretch their wings straight without flapping, yet advance of their own accord—gradually borne by the cutting qi. When dragons first ascend through layered clouds, by the time they reach forty *li*, they move on their own. Such words come from immortals and are passed down among the common folk, yet they are truly beyond the ken of ordinary men.

Moreover, to ride *qiao*, one must maintain a long fast, abstain from meat and greens, and forgo bloody foods; only after a full year may one mount these three *qiao*. Even with talismans, visualizing the Five-Dragon *Qiao* allows the farthest travel, while the others do not exceed a thousand *li*. Their ascent, descent, departure, and pause all follow their own methods—one must not act at will. If one fails to observe the prohibitions, one must not recklessly ride *qiao*, lest one meet with the calamity of a fall."

Some have asked: "The texts of Laozi and the *Tortoise-Scripture Classics* both speak of great plagues arising in the Metal and Wood years after the era of *medicinal soldiers*, where but one in ten thousand survives. May we inquire into the way of averting such calamity?"

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* replied: "The immortals possess secret methods to enter plague-stricken regions. One visualizes one’s body as composed of the Five Jades. These Five Jades correspond to the colors of the four seasons: green in spring, red in summer, yellow in the intercalary months, white in autumn, and black in winter. One further visualizes donning a golden headscarf, and one’s heart as a raging flame, vast as a *dou*, so that nothing may inspire fear.

Another method involves imagining one’s hair spreading to envelop the body, with a great star affixed to the tip of each strand. Alternatively, one visualizes the Seven Stars of the Northern Dipper, with the *Kui* constellation covering the head and the *Gang* star pointing forward.

One may also envision the qi of the five viscera issuing from the eyes and suffusing the body like clouds and mist: the liver’s green qi, the lungs’ white qi, the spleen’s yellow qi, the kidneys’ black qi, and the heart’s red qi. When these five colors intermingle in profusion, one may even share a bed with the plague-stricken.

Some perform the *Yu Step* while invoking the Jade Maiden of the Direct Sun. Others hold their breath and visualize a forceful warrior wielding a thousand-*jin* golden hammer, attended by one hundred and twenty men for protection.

Alternatively, one may employ the *Shooting-Ghost Pellets*, the *Red Chariot Envoy Pellets*, the *Commander-in-Chief Pellets*, the *Xu Changqing Powder*, the *Jade Casket Essence Powder*, the *Youthful Daoist Fumigation Pellets*, the *Cui Wen Yellow Powder*, the *Grass-Jade Wine*, the *Yellow Court Pellets*, the *August Talismans*, the *Laozi Neck-Talismans*, or the *Red-Bearded Master’s Peach Blossom Talismans*—all of which have proven highly effective."

Ge Hong said: The *Divine Immortals’ Canon* contains twenty-five scrolls on the methods of the Yellow and White, comprising over a thousand formulas. The Yellow refers to gold; the White, to silver. Ancient masters guarded this Way in secrecy, unwilling to name it directly, hence the veiled reference. Some texts title their chapters "Geng and Xin"—for Geng and Xin are also gold. Yet most of these methods are profound and abstruse, with only a few that can be clearly understood.

The world often doubts the veracity of such arts, no different from those who disbelieve in immortals. In my youth, I received from Master Zheng the *Nine Elixirs* and the *Canon of Gold and Silver Liquids*, and further sought from him five scrolls on the Yellow and White. Master Zheng said that he and Master Zuo had once tested these methods in the copper mountains of Lujiang, and all succeeded. Yet the rigor of purification rites and taboos was no less demanding than that required for the elixirs of immortality.

Vulgar men often mock my pursuit of such arcane arts, claiming I strive to force comprehension of what is inherently incomprehensible. Why should I do so? If my aim were merely to display literary skill for posterity, my *Outer Chapters* and miscellaneous writings—over two hundred scrolls in all—would suffice to convey my thoughts to future generations, with no need for these. Moreover, these *Inner Chapters* are written in plain speech, devoid of embellishment.

I am also aware that discussing such matters, the world will inevitably dismiss them as impractical and irrelevant, far less compelling than discourses on mundane, immediate truths that align with popular sentiment. Yet I cannot cease from this pursuit. Though I know my words will not enter the ears of the worldly, I persist in recording them because I have witnessed their efficacy, and the masters from whom I received transmission did not speak falsely.

But I am poor and lack resources, beset by misfortune and relentless hardship. Roads are blocked, ingredients unobtainable, and I have never had the leisure to attempt these arts. Now, when I tell others I know how to make gold and silver, yet suffer hunger and cold myself, how is this different from a lame man selling remedies for paralysis, seeking others’ trust—trust that cannot be won? Yet one cannot dismiss all possibilities because of a single setback. My diligent recording of these methods is for the sake of future scholars—those who cherish the strange and prize truth—so that they may read my words and grasp the full intent of the Way.

The arts of transformation know no bounds. The human body is visible, yet there are methods to conceal it; ghosts and spirits are hidden, yet there are ways to reveal them. Many have mastered such arts. Water and fire reside in the heavens, yet we draw them forth with fire-drills and dew-catchers. Lead is inherently white, yet it can be reddened into cinnabar; cinnabar is inherently red, yet it can be whitened back into lead. Clouds, rain, frost, and snow are all qi of Heaven and Earth, yet they can be replicated with medicines, indistinguishable from the real.

Even creatures that fly and crawl, those that slither and creep—once their forms are fixed by creation—can suddenly shed their old bodies and become something else. The myriad transformations, countless in kind, defy enumeration. Humans, the most noble of beings, can change their sex, become cranes or stones, tigers or apes, sand or soft-shelled turtles—such cases are not rare. Mountains can become abysses, and deep valleys can rise into hills—these, too, are transformations of great things.

Transformation is the natural way of Heaven and Earth. Why then deny that gold and silver can be made from other substances? Is the fire obtained from a burning-mirror or the water gathered from a dew-basin any different from ordinary fire and water? When a snake becomes a dragon, or when thatch is refined into grease, are they not the same as those born naturally? Yet the roots and causes of such transformations arise from the natural resonance of things. Without profound understanding and mastery of principle, one cannot grasp their essence; without tracing origins and perceiving endings, one cannot fathom their true nature.

Those of narrow vision and shallow knowledge, confined by their own limitations, dismiss the unfathomable as unfathomable and the divine as delusion. Because the Zhou and Confucius did not speak of such things, and the ancient texts do not record them, they dismiss them entirely—is this not ignorance? The common folk, seeing that Liu Xiang failed to make gold, conclude that the Way is nonexistent. Is this not like a farmer, after a flood or drought ruins his harvest, declaring that the five grains cannot be cultivated?

Wu Dawen, the Prefect of Chengdu, was learned and widely knowledgeable. He once told of studying under the Daoist Li Gen, who smelted lead and tin. Li added a medicine the size of a soybean to the crucible, stirred it with an iron spoon, and upon cooling, it became silver. Wu obtained the secret method but, intending to practice it himself, observed a hundred-day purification fast. Yet he was detained by official duties and never succeeded, often sighing that worldly affairs left no room for such pursuits.

Huan Junshan also spoke of Cheng Wei, a Yellow Gate Gentleman of the Han who was fond of the Yellow and White arts. He married the daughter of a family versed in such methods. Once, while accompanying the imperial procession, Cheng lacked proper attire and was deeply distressed. His wife said, "Let me prepare two bolts of fine silk."

The silk bolts then appeared before him without cause. Wei consulted the *Hongbao* in his pillow but failed to produce gold. His wife came to observe him, finding him fanning the coals to heat a tube filled with mercury. She said, "Let me test something before you." Drawing medicine from her pouch, she sprinkled a small amount into the tube. After the time it takes to eat a meal, she opened it, and it had turned to silver. Wei exclaimed in astonishment, "The Dao was near at hand in your possession, yet you never told me—why?" His wife replied, "One must be destined to receive it."

Thereafter, Wei day and night cajoled and enticed her, selling fields and houses to provide fine food and clothing, yet she still refused to reveal the method. Wei then conspired with his companions to beat and subdue her. His wife, sensing this, told him, "The Dao must be transmitted to the right person. If one meets such a person on the road, one teaches them at once. But if they are not the right person—outwardly compliant but inwardly false—then even if you cut them to pieces, the Dao will not emerge." Wei persisted in pressuring her, and his wife feigned madness, stripping naked and running off, smearing herself with mud before dying.

More recently, Hua Lingsi, the former Prefect of Lujiang, was a man of exceptional talent, broad learning, and extensive knowledge, yet he dismissed many unconventional matters as unbelievable. Later, a Daoist taught him the method of the Yellow and White. Hua tested it, melting lead in an iron vessel and adding powdered medicine, which turned it to silver. Melting this silver and adding another medicine transformed it into gold. He also learned the method of *cheshi* (penetrating vision) from the Daoist. Before a hundred days had passed, as he lay at night, he could clearly see the celestial patterns and his four neighbors, no longer perceiving the barriers of houses or fences. His deceased concubine, Yaohua, appeared to him, speaking as in life. When he offered sacrifices at a temple, he heard the temple deity respond to his bow, the altar seeming to shift with a sound. Hua then sighed, "Truly, the world contains all things. Though the Five Classics do not record them, one cannot dismiss them by mere conjecture. Yet those unversed in such arts, upon first hearing of them, how could they not be astonished?"

The arts of the Yellow and White are like compounding divine elixirs: both require a hundred days or more of purification and fasting, and one must have leisure to study the methods, with a mind attuned to their principles. They are not for the impure or the dull, nor for those who dabble superficially in the arts. Some methods require oral transmission, all of which should be received from a teacher. It is also fitting to practice in the depths of the mountains, in a place of purity, and not let common fools know of it. Yet Liu Xiang attempted this in the palace, having palace women attend to the work—certainly not those who had purified themselves, nor could he sever worldly affairs to prevent interruptions. How could he have succeeded?

The *New Discourses* of Huan Tan states: Shi Zixin, appointed as an aide to the Chancellor, was tasked with constructing a building. He mobilized officials, soldiers, and palace slaves for the project, but his attempts to make gold failed. The Chancellor, believing his own efforts insufficient, reported this to Empress Dowager Fu. The Empress, no longer desiring gold, had heard that its creation could produce an elixir for longevity and thus became eager for it. She appointed Shi as a Gentleman of the Palace and housed him in the Northern Palace, with attendants at his service. How could such divine methods be practiced in a palace, with ordinary people mingling and interfering? Even dyeing silk in the common world requires seclusion—if others see it, the process is ruined. How much more so for the transformations of the Yellow and White?

In all matters, great or small, one must grasp the essentials. If the method is not mastered, even making wine, sauce, vinegar, broth, or stew will fail—how much more so for great undertakings?

I once asked Master Zheng: "The Old Master said, 'Do not value rare and hard-to-obtain goods.' Yet in the age of perfect governance, gold was cast into mountains and jade discarded in valleys. Why did the ancients value gold and silver yet leave their methods behind?"

Master Zheng replied: "What the Old Master spoke of were those who sift through sand and split stones, drain mountains and dredge abysses, travel a thousand miles without regard for crushing or drowning, seeking rare treasures to disrupt the people’s seasons, never knowing contentment, adorning what is useless. Those who seek the Dao and aspire to longevity, yet still engage in commerce, lack integrity and deference, risking depths and dangers for profit, sparing neither body nor life, and failing to cultivate frugality—these are the ones he meant. As for true persons making gold, they do so to ingest it and attain immortality, not to amass wealth. Thus the scriptures say, 'Gold can be made, and the world can be transcended. Silver can also be ingested, though it is not as potent as gold.'"

I objected: "Why not ingest the gold and silver of the world instead of transmuting them? If one makes them, they are not genuine, and if not genuine, they are deceptive."

Master Zheng replied: "The gold and silver of the world are all good, yet Daoists are generally poor. As the saying goes, 'There are no fat immortals or wealthy Daoists.' If a master has ten or five disciples, how can they obtain enough gold and silver to provide for them? And they cannot travel far to gather them, so it is fitting to make them. Moreover, the gold created through transmutation is the essence of various medicines, superior to the natural kind. The *Scripture of Immortals* says, 'Cinnabar essence generates gold.' This is the explanation of making gold from elixirs. Thus, where cinnabar is found in the mountains, gold often lies beneath. When gold is made, it is a true substance, its interior and exterior as one, unchanging even after a hundred refinements. Hence the method states it can be used for nails, demonstrating its hardness and strength. This is attaining the Dao of nature. How can it be called deception?

Deception refers to coating iron with malachite to turn it red like copper, or transforming silver with egg white to make it yellow like gold—both altering only the exterior while the interior remains unchanged. The *lingzhi* mushroom grows naturally, yet the *Scriptures of Immortals* describe cultivating it with five stones and five woods. When harvested and ingested, it is no different from the natural kind, both granting longevity. This is akin to making gold."

The pheasant transforms into a clam, the sparrow into a mussel—both identical to those born naturally. Thus the *Scriptures of Immortals* state: "The flowing pearl, refined nine times, a father does not speak of it to his son; transformed into yellow and white, they naturally serve one another." It further says: "Cinnabar made into gold, ingested to ascend as an immortal—this is the path of the superior adept. Consuming *lingzhi*, guiding and pulling, swallowing *qi* to attain long life—this is the path of the middling adept. Eating grasses and trees to live a thousand years—this is the path of the lesser adept." It also states: "Gold and silver can be made, for it is the nature of the Dao. Long life can be learned and attained." The *Jade Tablet Records* say: "All under heaven may achieve long life; the hindrance lies only in hesitation, which prevents success."

To congeal mercury into gold makes it strong enough for nails. The *Bronze Pillar Scripture* states: "Cinnabar can be made into gold, *heche* (river chariot) into silver; standing, it can be accomplished, and once done, it is true. If you grasp the Dao, you may attain an immortal body." Huangshanzi said: "Heaven and earth possess gold, and I can make it—two yellows and one red, swiftly and without doubt." The *Tortoise Shell Script* declares: "My life is in my hands, not in heaven. When the returning elixir is complete, gold endures for ten thousand years." Did the ancients deceive us? The trouble is that those who know this Dao are often poor, and the medicines may be base yet grow in distant lands, unattainable in times of chaos.

Take *rong* salt and brine—both worthless things, in times of peace not even valued at a coin, yet now, though their price is unrestricted, they cannot be bought. Qiangli realgar, sought at a thousand times its weight, cannot be obtained. Merely knowing the method is no different from ignorance—how lamentable! Those who possess the method may suffer hunger and cold, unable to procure the ingredients, while the wealthy remain ignorant of the method. Even if they knew, none would believe. Suppose they did believe—having abundant gold and silver, who would spend their visible wealth to purchase medicines, fearing regret like casting aside a tether or chasing the wind? Thus none would attempt it.

Moreover, calculating the cost of the medicines against the value of the finished product reveals even greater profit, yet the required purification and toil deter them. Without the oral instructions of a clear master, one must not attempt this lightly.

"The medicines of physicians are exceedingly shallow and obvious, yet even their commonly used effective formulas are kept secret. Thus, formulas employ substances like *yougong* (roaming maidens of the rear palace), obscure adhesives, *fengjun* (sealed lord’s) clay pills, *mugui* (wooden ghost) seeds, *jinshangzhi* (gold merchant’s *lingzhi*), *feijungen* (flying lord’s root), *fulonggan* (crouching dragon’s liver), white horse sweat, floating cloud dregs, *longzidanyi* (dragon’s cinnabar robe), *yeguanggu* (night-shining bones), *baihuali* (hundred-flower nectar), and *dongzouzhai* (winter Zou’s fast)—all common things, yet without oral instructions, they remain incomprehensible. How much more so for the arts of yellow and white?

Those who can practice this do not merely keep it secret for its high cost. Once this Dao is achieved, one may attain long life. The Dao of long life is the ultimate Dao, and thus the ancients valued it highly.

The names of medicinal substances in formula books may coincide with common ones yet differ in reality. For example, *Heshang Chanü* (Riverbank Maiden) is not a woman; *Lingyang Ziming* (Lingyang’s Bright Son) is not a man; *Yu Yuliang* (Yu’s Remnant Grain) is not rice; *Yao Jiang* (Yao’s Brew) is not water. When the common folk see formulas using *longdan* (dragon’s gall) and *huzhang* (tiger’s paw), *jitou* (chicken’s head) and *yazhi* (duck’s foot), *mati* (horse’s hoof) and *quanxue* (dog’s blood), *shuwei* (rat’s tail) and *niuxi* (ox’s knees), they assume them to be blood-and-*qi* substances. Seeing *quepen* (broken basin), *fupen* (overturned basin), *fuwu* (cauldron shards), *daji* (great lance), *guijian* (ghost’s arrow), and *tiangou* (heaven’s hook), they take them for iron and pottery. Seeing *Huwang Shizhe* (Hu King’s Envoy), *Yigu Xinfu* (Leaning Maiden, New Wife), *Ye Zhangren* (Wild Elder), *Shoutian Gong* (Field Guardian Duke), *Daiwen Yu* (Daiwen Bathing), and *Xu Changqing* (Xu Longstalk), they assume them to be people’s names. Even familiar herbs may be unknown; how can one fully understand the profoundly secret formulas?

Liu Xiang failed to make gold—no wonder. Once the essentials are grasped, even ordinary people can succeed without needing the talents of sages or worthies. Was Liu Xiang a dullard? He simply lacked the oral instructions.

Now, I will record the concise and effective methods to bequeath to like-minded seekers of the future. First, take Wudu realgar, its cinnabar hue like a rooster’s comb, radiant and free of impurities—any quantity will do, but no less than five *jin*. Pound it to powder, mix with ox gall, and boil until dry. Take a red clay cauldron with a one-*dou* capacity, first line it with *rong* salt and realgar powder to a thickness of three *fen*, then add the realgar powder to a thickness of five *fen*, and again layer *rong* salt on top. Repeat this layering until complete. Add crushed charcoal the size of jujube pits to a thickness of two *cun*. Seal the cauldron’s exterior with a mixture of earthworm soil and *rong* salt, then cover it with another cauldron, sealing both with a three-*cun* thickness of the same mixture to prevent leakage. Let it dry in the shade for a month, then heat it with horse dung fire for three days and nights. When cool, open it and blow to separate the copper—it will flow like smelted copper or iron. Cast this copper into tubes to hold cinnabar water.

Again, heat it with horse dung fire for thirty days, then open the furnace and blow to obtain the gold. Use this gold to make tubes, which again hold cinnabar water. Heat with horse dung fire for another thirty days, then open, pound, and refine it. Take two parts raw cinnabar and one part mercury (quicksilver), and it will immediately congeal into yellow gold—lustrous and beautiful, strong enough for nails.

**Method for Making Cinnabar Water:**
Treat one *jin* of cinnabar, place it in a fresh bamboo tube, and add two *liang* each of realgar and nitre. Seal the tube’s top and bottom, close the opening, and seal it with lacquer-bone pills. Once dry, place it in strong bitter wine and bury it three *chi* deep in the earth. After thirty days, it will become water—red in color and bitter in taste.

**Method for Making Yellow Gold as Taught by Gold Tower Master from Green Grove Master**

First, forge tin into squares six *cun* wide and one *cun* two *fen* thick. Mix red salt with lye to a mud-like consistency and coat the tin, ensuring a uniform thickness of one *fen*. Layer these in a red clay crucible. For every ten *jin* of tin, use four *jin* of red salt. Seal the joints tightly and heat with horse dung fire for thirty days. Upon opening, the tin will appear ash-like, with bean-sized clusters within—these are yellow gold. Refine them in an earthen jar, using charcoal to fan the flames, and after ten smeltings, they will fully transform. From ten *jin* of tin, twenty *liang* of gold may be obtained. Only the red clay crucibles from Changsha, Guiyang, Yuzhang, and Nanhai are suitable, for the locals there commonly use such crucibles for cooking.

**Method for Making Red Salt**
Take one *jin* each of cold salt, cold selenite, cold alum, and white alum. Place them in an iron vessel and heat with charcoal until melted and turned red. Remove and use.

**Method for Transmuting Yellow Gold as Taught by Horned Master from Jiqiu Master**
First, place two parts alum water and selenite into an iron vessel and heat with charcoal until boiling. Add mercury in any desired quantity, stir until well combined, and after six or seven boils, pour onto the ground to form white silver. Then take one part each of cinnabar water and azurite water, and two parts realgar water. Place them in a bronze vessel over a gentle fire to boil, stirring frequently. Once combined, increase the heat until boiling again, then add the white silver in any quantity. After six or seven boils, pour onto the ground to congeal—this yields top-grade purple-polished gold.

**Method for Making Realgar Water**
