Place one *jin* of realgar into a fresh bamboo tube, adding two *liang* of nitre. Seal the top and bottom, close the opening, and seal with lacquer-bone pills. Immerse in strong vinegar and bury three *chi* deep in the earth. After twenty days, it will turn to water. The methods for making azurite water and alum water are the same, but each must be prepared in separate tubes.

**Method for Making Yellow Gold (Child’s Method)**
Fashion a large iron tube, twelve *cun* in diameter and twelve *cun* high. Make a small iron tube, six *cun* in diameter, and polish it smooth. Grind together one *jin* each of red halloysite, nitre, mica, hematite, and calcite, along with four *liang* of malachite and half a *jin* of sulfur. Sift finely, mix with vinegar, and coat the inside of the small tube to a thickness of two *fen*. Add one *jin* of mercury and half a *jin* of cinnabar, along with half a *jin* of *liangfei*.

**Method for Making *Liangfei***
Place ten *jin* of lead in an iron crucible over an open flame. Once melted, add three *liang* of mercury. Quickly skim off the early-formed substance with an iron ladle—this is *liangfei*. Stir until the mercury is no longer visible, then place it in the small tube. Cover with mica and secure with an iron lid. Set the large tube over a furnace, melt lead, and pour it into the large tube until it submerges the small tube, leaving half a *cun* exposed. Heat with a fierce fire for three days and nights to produce *zifen*. Melt ten *jin* of lead in an iron vessel for twenty days, then transfer to a bronze vessel. Once the lead melts, add a seven *cun*-square spoonful of *zifen* and stir—this will form yellow gold. To make white silver, place mercury in an iron vessel, add *zifen* to a depth of three *cun* or more, heat until combined, and pour into water to form silver.

**Method of Wuchengzi**
Make an iron tube nine *cun* long and five *cun* in diameter. Grind three *jin* of realgar with an equal part of earthworm castings to form a paste. Coat the tube to a thickness of three *cun*, leaving four *cun* of the opening unsealed. Add two *ge* of cinnabar water, then heat over horse dung fire until thoroughly dry. Place the tube inside a copper cylinder, seal tightly with a copper lid, and pack with yellow sand. Cover with earthworm castings and a thick layer of mud to prevent leaks. Place in a charcoal furnace with three *cun* of charcoal above the tube. When the tube’s opening glows red, cool and open it—all the realgar will have adhered to the copper cylinder. Repeat the process. From three *jin* of realgar essence, collect an equal amount of yellow sand to build a furnace of any size. To use, place the furnace in a charcoal fire until red-hot. Add mercury, and when the silver moves, introduce lead. Yellow gold will rise from the sides to the center—pour onto the ground to solidify.

Each furnace can produce one thousand five hundred *jin* of gold before its power is exhausted. Soak this gold in wine made from *Vitex negundo* and red millet for a hundred days to soften it. Form into pellets the size of small beans. Take one pellet three times daily; after consuming one *jin*, the three worms and corpse parasites will be expelled, and all illnesses cured. The blind will see, the deaf will hear, and the elderly will regain the vitality of a thirty-year-old. It renders one impervious to fire, and no evil, poison, cold wind, heat, or dampness can harm them. After consuming three *jin*, one may walk upon water, and the spirits of mountains and rivers will come to serve and protect. Lifespan will extend to match that of heaven and earth.

Boil one pellet in blood from a male pheasant and *zhu grass* juice, then rub it on the corners of the eyes to see ghosts and objects buried underground, enabling writing at night. Coat one pellet in white sheep’s blood and cast it into water—fish and dragons will immediately appear and may be captured. Coat one pellet in black sheep’s blood and rooster’s blood, then hang it above a city gate to prevent plague within a *li*. Apply it to the foreheads of cattle, sheep, and other livestock to protect them from disease and ward off tigers and leopards. Coat one pellet in tiger’s gall and snake fat, then throw it toward an enemy’s army from the direction of the moon’s current position—the army will descend into chaos, turning on one another and fleeing.

Coat one pellet in ox blood and cast it into a well—the water will immediately boil; cast it into flowing water, and the current will reverse for a hundred paces. Coat one pellet in white dog’s blood and place it in a shrine or temple chamber—its spirits will manifest and may be commanded. Coat one pellet in hare’s blood and set it in a place of sixfold yin—celestial kitchen maidens will promptly arrive, sufficient to serve sixty or seventy guests. Coat one pellet in carp’s gall and carry it into water—the water will part for a *zhang*, allowing one to breathe and move beneath the surface, and rain will not soak one’s garments. Boil one pellet in purple amaranth, hold the liquid in the mouth and swallow—one may go a hundred days without hunger. Boil one pellet with lodestone and place it in the hair—when striking an enemy, blades and arrows will not find their mark, and any who shoot will see their arrows turn back upon themselves. Combine soil from the six *ding* and six *ren* days with one pellet, and conceal it at the philtrum to become invisible; hold one pellet in the mouth, face north, and blow upon fire—it will be extinguished. On a *geng* or *xin* day at the *shen* or *you* hour, face west and hurl one pellet at a tree—the tree will wither that very day. Again, perform the Yu Step and cast one pellet at tigers, wolves, serpents, or vipers—they will die at once. Grind one pellet and write upon stone, and the characters will sink into the stone; write upon metal, and they will sink into the metal; write upon wood, and they will penetrate its grain, so deeply that no carving or scraping can remove them. For one who has died suddenly but not yet passed a night, place one pellet in water at the position of the moon’s current phase and insert it into the throat, then hold water in the mouth and spray it upon the dead person’s face—they will revive. Coat one pellet in fox blood and crane blood, place it in the claws, and point at anything—whatever is spoken will transform, so that when traveling through mountains, trees will seem to shift, and people will see it, though in truth nothing moves. In all cases of transmuting base metals into gold and silver, first establish an altar with Taiyi, the Mysterious Woman, and Laozi seated, and perform the offering rites as in the method for the Nine Elixirs, constantly burning the five incenses without cease. Once the gold is formed, first cast three *jin* into deep water and one *jin* into the marketplace, and only then may one freely use it as desired. Scroll Seventeen: "On Ascending and Crossing" Someone asked about the Dao of climbing mountains.

Bao Puzi said: "All who pursue the Dao, compound medicines, or seek refuge from chaos in reclusion must enter the mountains. Yet those who do not know the methods of entering mountains often encounter misfortune. Hence the proverb says: ‘Beneath Mount Taihua, white bones lie scattered.’ This refers to those who know but one thing and cannot prepare comprehensively—though they harbor the will to live, they instead hasten their deaths. Mountains, great or small, all possess numinous spirits; the larger the mountain, the greater its spirit, and the smaller the mountain, the lesser its spirit. To enter mountains without technique is to invite calamity: one may contract illness or suffer wounds and stabs, or be seized by terror and unease; one may see lights and shadows or hear strange sounds; or great trees may suddenly topple without wind, and boulders may fall without cause, striking and killing people; or one may be driven mad, wandering in confusion and tumbling into ravines; or one may be assailed by tigers, wolves, and venomous creatures. One must not enter mountains lightly.

The proper time is the third and ninth months, for these are the months when mountains open. One should also choose an auspicious day and hour within those months. If matters are urgent and one cannot wait, one may simply select an auspicious day and hour. All who enter mountains must first purify themselves for seven days, avoiding defilement, and carry the *Ascending Mountain Talisman* when departing. Perform the *Three-Five Rite* to encircle the body.

Moreover, the Five Peaks have years of receiving misfortune, just as the Nine Provinces experience cycles of decline and prosperity. When they receive the *Flying Talisman* of baleful *qi*, their lords and governors must not act. According to the *Zhou Duke’s Record of City Names*, the land is divided into celestial fields, and where calamity extends, one may avoid but not avert it. This applies to dwellings as well as mountains and peaks.

Furthermore, one must never enter the Eastern Peak in the first or second month of a *jia-yi* or *yin-mao* year; the Southern Peak in the fourth or fifth month of a *bing-ding* or *si-wu* year; the Western Peak in the seventh or eighth month of a *geng-xin* or *shen-you* year; the Central Peak in the four seasonal months of a *wu-ji* year; or the Northern Peak in the tenth or eleventh month of a *ren-gui* or *hai-zi* year. One need not enter Mount Taihua, Mount Huo, Mount Heng, Mount Tai, or Mount Songgao in these years, for the prohibitions extend to all directions of the peaks.

Moreover, the elders of all things—their essences can assume human form to delude and test people, though they cannot alter their true forms in mirrors. Thus, ancient Daoist adepts entering mountains always carried bright mirrors no less than nine *cun* in diameter, hanging them at their backs, so that old demons dared not approach. If something comes to test a person, one should glance at the mirror: if it is an immortal or a benevolent mountain spirit, its reflection will remain human. If it is a bird, beast, or malevolent spirit, its true form will appear in the mirror. When old demons depart, they always retreat backward; one may turn the mirror to face them and observe their backs. If they lack heels, they are old demons; if they have heels, they are mountain spirits.

Formerly, Zhang Gai and Ou Gaocheng, both absorbed in meditation within a stone chamber on Mount Yuntai in Shu, suddenly saw a man in yellow linen robes and a kudzu-fiber headscarf approach them, saying: ‘How toilsome, Daoists, to labor in such seclusion!’ The two glanced at their mirror and saw it was a deer. They asked: ‘You are an old mountain deer—how dare you feign human form?’ Before the words were finished, the figure turned into a deer and fled.

At the foot of Mount Linlü was an inn where ghosts dwelled. Every traveler who lodged there either died or fell ill. Nightly, dozens of figures appeared, clad in yellow, white, or black, some male, some female. Later, Zhi Boyi passed through and stayed the night. He sat reciting scriptures by bright candlelight. At midnight, over ten figures arrived and sat opposite him, playing *chupu* gambling games. Boyi secretly shone his mirror upon them and saw they were a pack of dogs. He grasped his candle, feigning clumsiness, and singed their robes with the flame, releasing the stench of scorched fur. Boyi carried a small knife, seized one, and stabbed it. It cried out in a human voice, then died and reverted to a dog. The rest fled, and the haunting ceased—all by the power of the mirror.

Superior adepts entering mountains carry the *Inner Writings of the Three Sovereigns* and the *True Forms of the Five Peaks*, summoning mountain spirits and consulting the *Register of Ghosts* to invoke the local earth deities and mountain officials. Thus, the specters of trees and stones and the spirits of mountains and rivers dare not test them. Lesser adepts establish the *Seventy-Two Spirit-Sealing Talismans* to control the *One Hundred Demons*, or use the *Vermilion Palace Seal* and the *Twelve Seals of Primordial Unity* to seal the four directions of their dwelling—so that the *One Hundred Demons* dare not approach. Still others wield the *Eight Might Staves* and wear the *Jade Tablet of Laozi*, commanding mountain spirits—how could they dare bring harm?

I have heard Master Zheng speak thus, though I cannot fully comprehend these matters. My teacher often told his disciples: ‘One who seeks the Dao as if dreading poverty or lamenting lowly status—how could they fail to attain it? The danger lies only in a lack of steadfast resolve, in pursuing the near and forgetting the far. One may delight in hearing the teachings, leaning forward eagerly, but before long, all is forgotten. The slightest gain is not secured, while the losses mount like mountains. How then can one penetrate the subtleties of the profound words and achieve the boundless heights of the Dao?’"

The *Baopuzi* states: "The greatest taboos for entering mountains are: the *wu* day of the first month, the *hai* day of the second month, the *shen* day of the third month, the *xu* day of the fourth month, the *wei* day of the fifth month, the *mao* day of the sixth month, the *jiazi* day of the seventh month, the *shenzi* day of the eighth month, the *yin* day of the ninth month, the *chen* and *wei* days of the tenth month, the *jichou* day of the eleventh month, and the *yin* day of the twelfth month.

Auspicious days for entering mountains are: *jiazi*, *jiayin*, *yihai*, *yisi*, *yimao*, *bingxu*, *bingwu*, and *bingchen*—all these days are most auspicious."

The *Baopuzi* further states: "According to the *Nine Heavens’ Secret Records* and the *Taiyi Dunjia*, the taboo days for entering mountains in long months are: the 3rd, 11th, 15th, 18th, 24th, 26th, and 30th; in short months, they are: the 1st, 5th, 13th, 16th, 26th, and 28th. Entering mountains on these days will surely invite trials from the mountain spirits. Moreover, one’s requests will go unfulfilled, and one’s endeavors will not succeed."

"Nor is this peril confined to Daoists—any person entering mountains on these days will meet with misfortune, encountering tigers, wolves, venomous creatures, and the like."

The *Baopuzi* states: "The dispositions of Heaven and Earth, the auspicious and inauspicious omens of *yin* and *yang*—vast and obscure, they are indeed difficult to fathom. I neither insist they exist nor dare affirm their absence. Yet the Yellow Emperor and the Grand Duke both trusted and relied upon them; in recent times, the accomplished men Yan Junping and Sima Qian both employed them. The classics and traditions record days for regulating calendars and discerning the seasons’ hardness and softness. As the ancient saying goes, *'The auspicious day is only* wu.'* This has been so since antiquity. The sovereign establishes the office of Grand Scribe, and for enfeoffments, appointments, ancestral rites, and suburban sacrifices to Heaven and Earth, all choose propitious times. Yet mediocre men of today, priding themselves on transcending convention, scorn the selection of favorable days in all their actions—are they not foolish and ignorant? Whenever I observe those entering mountains without regard for the proper times and days, I see the consequences below—one must not enter lightly.

According to the *Scripture of the Jade Lock*, those who wish to enter famous mountains must not be ignorant of the secret arts of *dunjia*, though it does not expound these matters in exhaustive detail. The *Lingbao Scripture* states: when entering mountains, one should use *bao* days and *yi* days; *zhuan* days are most auspicious, while *zhi* days and *fa* days will surely bring death—yet it does not elaborate further.

In my youth, I harbored the ambition to enter mountains, and thus I studied the books of *dunjia*, of which there are over sixty scrolls. The matters therein cannot be mastered hastily, so I excerpted their essentials to compile a ready reference for my satchel, though I do not transmit them in writing. Here, I discuss their general outlines. Those of discernment who wish to enter mountains and practice the Way should seek out those who know these arts—they are not entirely absent from the world.

The *Central Scripture of Dunjia* states: to seek the Dao, use *tiannei* days and *tiannei* hours to subdue ghosts and demons and deploy talismanic writ; to enter famous mountains on *tianqin* days and *tianqin* hours will ensure that the hundred evils—tigers, wolves, venomous creatures, and bandits—dare not approach. Exit through the *Heavenly Storehouse* and enter through the *Earthly Door*. All *guimao* days are the *Heavenly Storehouse*, and all *jisi* days are the *Earthly Door*.

It further states: to evade the chaos of the world and conceal one’s traces in famous mountains, free from care and calamity, use the *dingsi* day of the *shangyuan* period, called the *Time of Hidden Virtue* or *Heavenly Heart*—one may vanish utterly, as if the sun and moon have lost their light, unseen by humans or spirits.

It also states: those who enter famous mountains to seek immortality should use *guimao* days and *guimao* hours, also called *Heavenly Duke Days*—they will surely attain transcendence.

Moreover, when entering mountain forests, take grass from the *Blue Dragon* with the left hand, break it in half, and place it beneath the *Encounter Star*; pass through the *Bright Hall* into the *Great Yin*, proceeding with the *Yu Step*. Recite thrice: *'O Great Yin of the Marsh, General, hear alone: the grandson of the king, Wang Jia, shall not reveal this to outsiders. Let those who see Jia take him for a bundle of firewood; let those who do not see Jia take him for a non-human.'* Then place the held grass on the ground, take soil with the left hand to anoint the philtrum, hold the grass with the right hand to shield oneself, advance the left hand, and proceed with the *Yu Step* to the *guimao* position. Hold the breath and stand still—humans and spirits will be unable to see you. All *jia* days are the *Blue Dragon*, all *yi* days are the *Encounter Star*, all *bing* days are the *Bright Hall*, and all *ding* days are the *Great Yin*.

The *Bi* hexagram, having achieved the *Already Across* configuration, initially follows the first and second steps without adhering to the nine-step sequence, yet each step remains seven feet in length. It is also said that one foot corresponds to twenty-one feet, and one should look back to survey the nine steps.

The method of the *Yu Step* is as follows: stand upright with the right foot forward and the left foot behind. Next, advance the right foot again, then bring the left foot parallel to the right—this is the first step. Next, advance the right foot again, then the left foot, bringing the right foot parallel to the left—this is the second step. Next, advance the right foot once more, then bring the left foot parallel to the right—this is the third step. Thus, the way of the *Yu Step* is complete.

All arts under Heaven should employ the *Yu Step*—this is not limited to the present matter."

The *Baopuzi* states: "The *Lingbao Sutra* says that the so-called *Treasure Days* refer to days when the heavenly stems generate the earthly branches below, such as the days of *jia-wu* or *yi-si*. *Jia* is Wood, *wu* is Fire; *yi* is also Wood, *si* is also Fire—Fire is born of Wood. It also speaks of *Righteous Days* as days when the earthly branches generate the heavenly stems above, such as the days of *ren-shen* or *gui-you*. *Ren* is Water, *shen* is Metal; *gui* is also Water, *you* is also Metal—Water is born of Metal. The so-called *Restraining Days* are days when the heavenly stems overcome the earthly branches below, such as the days of *wu-zi* or *ji-hai*. *Wu* is Earth, *zi* is Water; *ji* is also Earth, *hai* is also Water—for in the principle of the Five Phases, Earth restrains Water. The so-called *Conquering Days* are days when the earthly branches overcome the heavenly stems above, such as the days of *jia-shen* or *yi-you*. *Jia* is Wood, *shen* is Metal; *yi* is also Wood, *you* is also Metal—Metal overcomes Wood. All other cases follow this pattern; by extension, all can be understood."

The *Baopuzi* states: "When entering famous mountains, choose an *opening* or *removal* day of the *jia-zi* cycle. Suspend five-inch squares of silk in the five colors from a large stone, and what you seek will surely be obtained.

It also says: When entering mountains, one should know the secret incantation of the Six *Jia*. The incantation goes: *'Before the arrayed ranks, all who face weapons and battle stand ready.'* These nine characters should always be secretly recited, and nothing will fail to be averted. This is what is meant by an essential method that is not cumbersome."

The *Baopuzi* states: "Among the mountain spirits, there is one shaped like a small child with a single foot, walking backward, which delights in assaulting people. If one enters the mountains and hears loud voices at night, it is called a *qi*; if you know its name and call it out, it will not dare to harm you. It is also called *renei*, and you may call it by either name.

There is also a mountain spirit, red as a drum and with a single foot, called *hui*. Another appears like a human, nine feet tall, clad in furs and wearing a bamboo hat, called *Jinlei*. Or it may resemble a dragon with five colors and red horns, called *Feifei*. In all cases, call them by their names, and they will not dare to cause harm."

The *Baopuzi* states: "In the mountains, if there is a large tree that can speak, it is not the tree itself that speaks but its spirit, called *Yunyang*; calling it brings good fortune. If you see firelight at night in the mountains, it is produced by long-dead trees—do not be alarmed. If you see Hu people at night, they are the spirits of bronze and iron. Seeing Qin people, they are the spirits of century-old trees. Do not be alarmed, for they cannot harm you.

If you encounter officials by mountain waters, they are called *Sijiao*; calling them by name brings good fortune. If you see a large snake wearing a cap and headscarf, it is called *Shengqing*; calling it brings good fortune. If you see officials in the mountains but only hear their voices without seeing their forms, and they ceaselessly call out to people, throw a white stone at them and they will fall silent. Another method is to fashion a spear from reeds and stab at them, which also brings good fortune.

If ghosts in the mountains call out to people, demanding food without cease, throw white cogon grass at them and they will die. If mountain ghosts constantly bewilder travelers, causing them to lose their way, throw a reed staff at them and they will die.

On *yin* days in the mountains, one who calls itself *Yu Official* is a tiger. One who calls itself *Lord of the Road* is a wolf. One who calls itself *Magistrate* is an old raccoon dog. On *mao* days, one who calls itself *Elder* is a hare. One who calls itself *Eastern King Father* is a elk. One who calls itself *Western Queen Mother* is a deer.

On *chen* days, one who calls itself *Rain Master* is a dragon. One who calls itself *River Earl* is a fish. One who calls itself *Gutless Duke* is a crab. On *si* days, one who calls itself *Lonely One* is a snake from the earth shrine. One who calls itself *Seasonal Lord* is a tortoise.

On *wu* days, one who calls itself *Three Dukes* is a horse. One who calls itself *Immortal* is an old tree. On *wei* days, one who calls itself *Host* is a sheep. One who calls itself *Official* is a river deer. On *shen* days, one who calls itself *Lord of Men* is a monkey. One who calls itself *Nine Ministers* is an ape.

On *you* days, one who calls itself *General* is an old chicken. One who calls itself *Bandit-Catcher* is a pheasant. On *xu* days, one who calls itself by a human name and surname is a dog. One who calls itself *Chengyang Duke* is a fox.

On *hai* days, one who calls itself *Divine Lord* is a pig. One who calls itself *Woman* is gold or jade. On *zi* days, one who calls itself *Shrine Lord* is a rat. One who calls itself *Divine Person* is a bat. On *chou* days, one who calls itself *Scholar* is an ox. Simply knowing the names of these creatures prevents them from causing harm."

Someone asked about the method of living in seclusion among mountains and marshes to avoid venomous snakes. *Baopuzi* replied: "In ancient times, Round Mound abounded with great serpents and also bore fine medicinal herbs. When the Yellow Emperor was about to ascend it, Guangchengzi taught him to wear realgar, and all the serpents departed. Now, if one carries five ounces or more of Wudu realgar—its color like a cock’s comb—into the mountains, forests, and grasses, one need not fear snakes. If a snake bites someone, applying a small amount of realgar powder into the wound will heal it immediately.

Though there are many kinds of snakes, only the viper and the green-gold snake bite with deadly urgency; if left untreated, they kill a person within a day. Those who do not know the methods to treat such bites but are bitten by these two snakes should cut away the wounded flesh with a knife and cast it to the ground—the flesh will boil as if scorched by fire, and soon char to ash, while the person survives. During the seventh and eighth months, when these snakes’ venom is at its peak, they cannot bite people, yet their poison remains unspent. Thus, they gnaw at large bamboo and small trees, which immediately wither and dry.

Nowadays, Daoist practitioners entering the mountains know only grand principles but not the way to avoid harm—this is no small matter. Before entering the mountains, one should first practice at home, learning the method of *jin* incantations. Visualize the sun, moon, Vermilion Bird, Dark Warrior, Azure Dragon, and White Tiger to guard one’s body. Upon reaching the mountains, forests, and grasses, draw three mouthfuls of *qi* from the left, hold it, and blow it into the mountain grasses, imagining this *qi* as a crimson mist filling dozens of *li*. If accompanied, have all followers stand in formation and blow *qi* upon them. Even if they tread upon snakes, the snakes will not dare move, and one will scarcely encounter any.

If a snake is seen, again draw three mouthfuls of *qi* from the left, hold it, press the tongue to the roof of the mouth, pinch the *duguan* point with the hand, close the Celestial Gate, and seal the Earthly Door. Then press down the snake’s head with an object, bind it with the hand, and draw a prison on the ground to contain it—one may even play with it. Though it coils around the neck, it will not dare bite. Without releasing the incantation, if one spits *qi* to blow upon it, the snake will never escape the prison.

If another is bitten by a snake, draw three mouthfuls of *qi* from the left and blow upon the wound—it will immediately heal and no longer pain. Even if the person is ten or more *li* away, one may project *qi* from afar, call their name, and for a man, direct the incantation to one’s left hand, or for a woman, to the right hand—they too will recover.

Master Jie’s method involves dwelling in the mountains, visualizing five-colored snakes, each one-headed. Then, holding the *qi*, pierce them with bent green bamboo or small wooden boards, pace the *Yu* step to the left, and imagine thousands of centipedes covering the body. Thus departing, one will never encounter snakes. Alternatively, carry dried ginger and aconite behind the elbow, or burn ox, sheep, or deer horn to fumigate the body, or wear Wang Fangping’s realgar pills, or place pig’s earwax and musk pills under the toenails—all are effective.

Moreover, musk deer and wild boars eat snakes, so their presence repels them. It is also said that the *ri* bird and the *geng* tortoise devour snakes. Thus, southerners entering the mountains carry the tail of the *geng* tortoise and the beak of the *ri* bird to ward off snakes. If bitten, scraping these two and applying them to the wound also heals it immediately. The *ri* bird is another name for the *zhen* bird.

Southerners entering the mountains also carry live centipedes in bamboo tubes. When the centipede senses snakes nearby, it stirs within the tube, prompting a careful search of the grasses—snakes will surely be found. When a great snake over a *zhang* long and a *wei* in girth is seen, the centipede can subdue it with *qi*, and the snake dies. If a centipede is seen on the bank, the great snake flees into deep water, but the centipede merely floats on the surface, projecting its *qi*. People see something perfectly green, as thick as a rope, descending straight into the water to the snake’s location. Soon, the snake floats up dead. Thus, southerners use powdered centipede to treat snakebites, and all recover immediately."

Someone asked: "In the mountains and valleys south of the Yangtze, many noxious creatures abound. Are there ways to avoid them?"

Bao Puzi replied: "The lofty plains of the Central States possess pure and harmonious *qi* of the earth, and the renowned mountains of the imperial domain are entirely free of such creatures. Yet in the wilds of Wu and Chu, where summer heat and dampness steam and smolder, even the sacred peaks of Heng and Huo teem with venomous pests.

There is also the short fox, known by many names—*yu*, archer, shadow-shooter—though in truth it is a water insect. Its form resembles a cicada, its body like a three-legged cup, with wings to fly. Though eyeless, its ears are keen, and in its mouth lies a transverse object like a crossbow’s horn. Upon hearing human voices, it aligns this mouth-part like a crossbow, using *qi* as its arrow, and shoots through the water at its target. Those struck in the body immediately develop sores; those struck in the shadow fall ill, though sores do not appear at once. Those who do not know the cure will die. The affliction resembles severe cold damage, and without treatment, death comes within ten days.

There are also sand lice, found both in water and on land. After fresh rain or before dawn and dusk, those who wade through such places will surely be infested. Only in the scorching sun or when grasses are dry do they become scarce. Tiny as the tip of a hair, they burrow into the skin upon contact, causing a prickling sensation like thorns. A slight touch brings great pain, but they may be plucked out with a needle—bright red as cinnabar, wriggling upon the fingernail. If left unremoved, the insect bores to the bone, then wanders throughout the body, much like the archer’s curse, and both are fatal.

When traveling through lands where these creatures dwell, upon returning to your lodging, scorch your body thoroughly with fire, and the lice will fall to the ground. Carrying the Eight-Ingredient Musk Pellet, the Transcendence Pellet, the Life-Protecting Pellet, the Jade Flask Pellet, the Rhinoceros Horn Pellet, the Seven-Star Pellet, or *ji’ni* will repel sand lice and short foxes. If these medicines cannot be obtained at once, even carrying fine musk alone is beneficial. Pound equal parts realgar and garlic into a pellet the size of a chicken’s egg and carry it—this, too, is effective."

If already afflicted, applying this medicine to the sore will also bring healing. Chewing red amaranth and drinking its juice, or applying it topically, will likewise heal. The roots of *wu qie*, *xuangou* grass, and *ge* vine—each of these three may be used alone; pound and consume their juice in one or two *sheng*. Furthermore, the archer’s curse insect hibernates in mountain valleys during winter. When heavy snow falls, seek it out: where the insect resides, snow will not accumulate, and qi rises as if from steaming heat. Dig there, and you will find it no deeper than a *chi* beneath the earth. Dry it in the shade, grind it to powder, and carry it—this will naturally repel the archer’s curse in summer. If a Daoist knows the *One Prohibition Formula* or the *Hundred Caverns Prohibitions*, and constantly maintains the prohibitions while guarding the True One, then no poisons will dare approach, and no other medicines need be used.”

Someone asked, “When a Daoist dwells in the mountains, sheltering among cliffs and peaks, there is no need for the warmth of layered robes—how can one become unafraid of wind and damp? May I inquire into the method?”

Master Baopu replied, “The *Golden Cake Powder*, *Three Yang Elixir*, *Changxin Pill*, *Xun Grass Enduring Winter Decoction*, *Solitary Swaying Ointment*, *Yinyu Dark Flower Powder*, and *Autumn Rehmannia Blood Pill*—none of these need be taken for more than fifty days to achieve a decade of freedom from wind and damp. If one ingests the great medicine of the Golden Elixir, though one may not yet ascend in emptiness or rise lightly, the body will remain untouched by illness. Even lying exposed to wind and damp cannot harm it.”

These seven medicines are meant only for those who have just begun to study the Dao. Master Yao merely ingested the *Three Yang Elixir* and could lie bare-chested upon ice without shivering. Both Master Jie and the Daoist Liang slept upon stone, even in autumn and winter amid wind and cold, and their methods have been tested and proven—these are secret techniques.”

Someone asked about the way to cross rivers and seas while warding off serpents and dragons. Master Baopu replied, “When a Daoist has no choice but to traverse great rivers, they should first, by the water’s edge, break a chicken egg and mix a small amount of powder with fragrant powder, stirring it into a vessel of water to bathe themselves. This will make them unafraid of wind, waves, and dragons. Additionally, wearing the *Talisman of the Eastern Sea Youth*, the *Water-Control Talisman*, or the *Penglai Charm* will repel all harm in the water. There are also the *Six Jia Three Metal Talismans* and the *Five Wood Prohibitions*. Another method: before entering the river, recite the incantation: *‘Roll up, roll up, River Earl, lead the way and ward off dragons; ten thousand calamities vanish, the heavens grow clear.’*

The *Golden Tablet Record* states: on the *bingwu* day of the fifth month at noon, grind the Five Stones and cast them into copper. The Five Stones are realgar, cinnabar, orpiment, alum, and azurite. Pulverize them, bathe them in the *Golden Flower Pool*, then place them in the *Six-One Divine Furnace* and smelt them with firewood from cassia trees. Once the copper is formed, refine it with hard charcoal, having boys and girls tend the fire. Take the male copper to forge the *yang* sword and the female copper to forge the *yin* sword, each five *cun* and five *fen* in length, corresponding to the number of earth to suppress water spirits. Carrying these while traveling on water will keep dragons, giant fish, and water deities from approaching. To distinguish male from female copper, have boys and girls pour water over the copper when it is red-hot from the fire. The copper will split into two pieces: the one with a raised portion is male copper, and the one with a depression is female copper. Engrave them with their names. When entering the water, wear the male sword on the left and the female sword on the right. If merely boarding a boat without wading into the water, wear the male sword on *yang* days and the female sword on *yin* days.

There are also the *Celestial Script of Great Characters* and the *Book of the Northern Emperor*; writing these on silk and carrying them will also ward off wind, waves, dragons, and water creatures.”

Someone asked about the method to repel the hundred ghosts of mountains, rivers, temples, and halls. Master Baopu replied, “If a Daoist constantly carries the *Heavenly Water Talisman*, the *Supreme Sovereign’s Bamboo Messenger Talisman*, the *Laozi’s Left Contract*, and invokes the *Three Generals of Guarding the True One and Contemplating the Three Departments*, ghosts will not dare approach. Alternatively, one may study the *Record of the Hundred Ghosts* to learn the names of all ghosts under heaven, as well as the *White Marsh Diagram* and the *Nine Cauldrons Record*—this will cause the multitude of ghosts to retreat. Another way is to ingest the *Quail Chick Red Stone Pill*, the *Azurite Night Radiance Powder*, the *Onion Seed Crow’s Eye Pill*, or swallow the *White Quartz and Mother-of-Pearl Powder*—all of which allow one to see ghosts, thereby making them fearful.”

Master Baopu said, “There are the *Forty-Nine Secret Talismans of the Central Womb of the Yellow Court* by Lord Lao. When entering mountain forests, on a *jiayin* day, write them in cinnabar on white silk, place them on a table at night, and make an offering to the Northern Dipper with a little wine and dried meat. State your name, bow twice, receive the talismans, and place them in the collar of your robe. This will ward off the hundred ghosts of mountains and rivers, ten thousand spirits, tigers, wolves, insects, and poisons. Even those who are not Daoists—when fleeing chaos and taking refuge in the mountains—would do well to know this method.” △*Mountain-Entering Talisman*

Master Baopu said, “The five talismans above are all *Mountain-Entering Talismans* of Lord Lao. Write them in cinnabar on peachwood boards, inscribing the characters large so they fill the entire surface. Place them on gates and doors, at the four directions and four corners, and along the sides of paths at critical points—within fifty paces of one’s dwelling—to ward off mountain spirits and demonic specters. They may also be affixed to beams and pillars within the household. Whether one dwells in the mountains or enters them briefly, all may employ these talismans, and no creature will dare harm them. Three talismans may be inscribed together on a single board. *This is not exclusive to the Ge lineage.*”

Master Baopu said, “This talisman is also one of Lord Lao’s *Mountain-Entering Talismans*; it may be affixed to beams and pillars within the household. Whether one dwells in the mountains or enters them briefly, all would do well to use it.”

Master Baopu said, “This is the *Mountain-Entering Talisman to Ward Off Tigers and Wolves*, transmitted by the immortal Chen Anshi. Write two talismans in cinnabar on silk, each distinct. Always keep four of each at one’s dwelling. When moving or traveling, retrieve and carry them away—this is profoundly sacred. The *Mountain-Opening Talisman* unlocks the gates of millennial *lei*-vines on renowned mountains, revealing hidden treasures, ancient scripts, gold, and jade. All methods to the right follow this principle, largely similar with minor variations.”

Baopu said, “This talisman is the *Divine Seal of the Hundred Ghosts, Serpents, Vipers, Tigers, and Wolves*, worn by Lord Lao. Carve it on a two-inch square of jujube heartwood, bow twice, and carry it—its divine efficacy is profound. This is the talisman of the immortal Chen Anshi.”

△ *Talismans for Wearing When Entering Mountains*
These three talismans may also be affixed to the left and right, front and back of oxen, horses, houses, and pigpens to ward off tigers and wolves.

Someone asked, “I have heard of Tan Chang, who could walk upon water or dwell within it for long periods. By what method did he achieve this?”

Baopu said, “By mixing scallion mucus with cassia and ingesting seven pills the size of *wutong* seeds, three times daily for three years, one may walk upon water. Master Zheng said that merely practicing breath-holding to a thousand breaths over time enables one to remain underwater for about a day. Obtaining a true *tongtian* rhinoceros horn over three inches long, carving it into the shape of a fish, and holding it in the mouth while entering water will cause the water to part three feet around, allowing one to breathe underwater. Moreover, the *tongtian* rhinoceros horn has a crimson vein like a thread running from base to tip. When a horn filled with rice is placed among a flock of chickens, they will not dare to peck at it, retreating in alarm before coming within inches. Thus, the people of the south sometimes call the *tongtian* rhinoceros horn the *hai ji* (‘frightening chicken’) rhinoceros horn. Placing this horn atop a grain pile prevents birds from gathering. On nights of thick fog and heavy dew, if placed in the central courtyard, it remains entirely dry. This rhinoceros dwells deep in the mountains, and on dark nights, its horn shines as brightly as a torch. When its horn is used as a stirrer to mix poisoned broth, white foam surges forth, and the poison’s potency vanishes. Stirring non-toxic substances produces no foam—this is how one discerns its power. When traveling through foreign lands in regions with *gu* poison, one should always stir food and drink with this rhinoceros horn beforehand. If a person is struck by a poisoned arrow and near death, piercing the wound with this rhinoceros horn stirrer will cause foam to emerge, and the wound will heal. The *tongtian* rhinoceros horn neutralizes poison because the beast feeds exclusively on toxic grasses and thorny plants, never consuming soft or smooth vegetation. Once a year, it sheds its horn among the mountain stones. If one finds it and carves a wooden replica matching its color, grain, and shape to replace it, the rhinoceros will not notice and will shed its horn in the same place the following year. Other rhinoceros horns also repel evil and neutralize poison, though none match the marvel of the *tongtian* horn. Alternatively, consuming the *Six Wu* talisman for a thousand days, or compounding the *Feng Yi Water Immortal Pill* with red-spotted spiders and seven-layered water striders, allows one to dwell underwater. Merely applying it to the soles of the feet enables walking upon water. Even head filth can make metal and iron float—how much more so these superior methods?”

Someone asked, “Those who cultivate the Dao often dwell in mountains and forests, where tigers and wolves pose great harm. How may one repel them?”

Baopu said, “Those of ancient times who entered mountains carried the *Yellow Spirit Yue Zhang Seal*, four inches wide with one hundred and twenty characters. Sealing mud with it and placing it a hundred paces in each of the four directions around one’s dwelling prevents tigers and wolves from approaching. Upon encountering fresh tiger tracks, impressing the seal in the direction of the tracks causes the tiger to flee; impressing it against the direction causes the tiger to return. Carrying this seal while traveling through mountains and forests also dispels fear of tigers and wolves. It does more than merely repel tigers and wolves—if there are malevolent spirits of mountains, rivers, earth altars, or temples that consume blood offerings and wield the power to bless or curse, sealing their paths with this mud severs their way, and they can no longer exercise their divinity. Formerly, in the waters of Shitou, there was a great soft-shelled turtle that dwelled in a deep pool, which people named *Yuan Pool*. This creature could manifest as a ghost and spread illness among humans. A Daoist of Wu named Dai Bing happened upon it and used the *Yue Zhang Seal* to make hundreds of mud seals. Boarding a boat, he cast them throughout the pool. After a long while, a great soft-shelled turtle over a *zhang* in length surfaced but dared not move. He then struck and killed it, and those afflicted by illness all recovered. Many smaller turtles emerged and died in rows upon the islet. If one suddenly encounters a tiger in the mountains, reciting the *Three-Five Prohibition* will cause it to retreat immediately. The method of the *Three-Five Prohibition* must be transmitted orally, for brush cannot fully convey its intricacies. Another method: simply visualize one’s body as a vermilion bird, three *zhang* in length, standing upon the tiger’s head, then hold the breath—the tiger will depart. If lodging in the mountains at night, secretly take a hairpin, hold the breath, and pierce the *White Tiger* with it—there will be nothing to fear. Another method: with the left hand, hold a knife, hold the breath, and trace a square on the ground while reciting the incantation: ‘North of Mount Heng, south of Mount Tai, thieves do not rise, tigers and wolves do not roam. The city walls are incomplete, sealed with a golden lock.’ Then lay the knife horizontally upon the *White Tiger* at midday—there will be nothing to fear. Alternatively, use the *Great Prohibition*, swallowing three hundred and sixty breaths, then take from the left and shout at the tiger—it will not dare to rise. Entering the mountains with this method also dispels fear of tigers. Other methods include the *Seven-Star Tiger Step*, the *Jade Spirit Talisman*, the *Eight Might Five Victory Talisman*, the *Li Er Taiping Talisman*, the *Central Yellow Canopy Seal*, and the *Sulfur Powder*. Burning ox and sheep horns or erecting the *Western Marchmount Lord’s Mountain-Prohibiting Talisman* also proves effective. These four talismans are omitted here.”

This talisman is Lord Lao’s *Mountain-Entering Talisman*, as described below. It may also be affixed to beams and pillars within the household. All who dwell in mountains and forests or enter them briefly may use it.

*Scroll Eighteen: Earthly Perfection*
Baopu said, “I have heard from my teacher that one who knows the One accomplishes all things.”

To know the One is to know all things; not to know the One is to know nothing at all. The Dao arises from the One, prized for its unparalleled singularity. Each dwells in its own place, mirroring Heaven, Earth, and Humanity—thus called the Three Ones. Heaven attains clarity through the One, Earth attains tranquility through the One, humanity attains life through the One, and spirits attain numinosity through the One. Metal sinks while feathers float, mountains stand firm and rivers flow—seen yet unseen, heard yet unheard. Preserve it, and it remains; neglect it, and it vanishes. Face it, and fortune follows; turn from it, and misfortune befalls. Safeguard it, and boundless blessings endure; lose it, and life withers as qi expires.

Lord Lao said: *"Vague and elusive, yet within it lies form; elusive and vague, yet within it lies substance."* This is the meaning of the One. Thus the immortal scriptures say: *"If you desire long life, guard the One with clarity. When hunger arises from contemplating the One, the One provides sustenance; when thirst arises from contemplating the One, the One offers drink."* The One has a name, a form, and a color. For men, it is nine *fen* in length; for women, six *fen*. It may reside in the lower cinnabar field, two *cun* and four *fen* below the navel, or in the middle cinnabar field within the Crimson Palace and Golden Gate beneath the heart, or between the brows—one *cun* back for the Hall of Light, two *cun* for the Grotto Chamber, and three *cun* for the upper cinnabar field. These are the treasures of the Daoist tradition, passed down through generations by blood-oath and whispered transmission of its name.

The One can generate yin and engender yang, reckon the cycles of cold and heat. Spring attains its vitality through the One, summer its growth, autumn its harvest, and winter its storage. Its vastness cannot be measured by the six directions, nor its minuteness compared to the tip of a hair. Of old, the Yellow Emperor journeyed east to Green Mound, passing Wind Mountain, where he met the Master of the Purple Court and received the *Inner Writ of the Three Sovereigns*, enabling him to summon the myriad spirits. To the south, he reached Round Ridge beneath the Yin Mulberry, observing where the hundred spirits ascend, gathering the blossoms of *ruo* and *qian*, and drinking from the waters of Cinnabar Peak. To the west, he met Master Central Yellow, receiving the *Ninefold Augmentation Formula*, then passed through Kongtong to study the *Scripture of Spontaneity* from Guangchengzi. To the north, he arrived at Vast Dyke, ascending Towering Thatch to meet the Great Hidden Lord and the Yellow Canopy Youth, receiving the *Divine Mushroom Chart*. Returning, he ascended Wangwu Mountain and obtained the *Divine Elixir Golden Formula Record*. At Mount Emei, he met the Heavenly Perfected Sovereign in the Jade Hall and inquired about the Way of the True One. The Sovereign said: *"You rule the four seas—do you still seek long life? Is this not greed?"* Their exchange cannot be fully recounted; I shall briefly touch upon one aspect.

The formula for long life and transcendence lies solely in the golden elixir; to preserve form and repel evil, only the True One suffices. Thus the ancients prized it above all. The immortal scriptures say: *"The nine-times-cycled elixir, the *Golden Fluid Scripture*, and the *Guard the One Formula* all reside within the Five Cities of Kunlun, stored in jade cases, inscribed on golden tablets, sealed with purple clay, and stamped with the central seal."*

I have heard from my teacher that the One abides in the Great Abyss of the North Pole. Before it lies the Hall of Light, behind it the Crimson Palace. Towering above is the resplendent canopy, golden towers soaring aloft. To the left, the *Gang* constellation; to the right, the *Kui*—waves surge and spray into the void. Dark mushrooms blanket the cliffs, vermilion grasses drape the peaks. White jade rises in jagged splendor, sun and moon cast their radiance. It traverses fire and water, passes through the mysterious and the yellow. Palaces and gates intertwine, curtains and jades gleam. Dragons and tigers stand guard, divine beings attend nearby. It neither acts nor bestows, yet the One rests in its place; neither slow nor swift, the One abides in its chamber. At ease and unhurried, the One does not depart. Guard the One and preserve the true, and you may commune with spirits. Curb desires and moderate food, and the One lingers. Even with blades at your neck, contemplating the One grants life. Knowing the One is not difficult—difficulty lies in persevering to the end. Guard it without fail, and you may endure forever. On land, it repels ferocious beasts; on water, it wards off flood dragons. You need not fear *wangliang* spirits or venomous creatures. Ghosts dare not approach, blades dare not strike. Such is the essence of the True One.

Bao Puzi said: “I have heard from my master that among the Daoist arts and various scriptures, the methods of visualization and contemplation that can repel evil and protect the body number in the thousands. Such as concealing one’s shadow and hiding one’s form, preserving form without birth, the Nine Transformations, Twelve Changes, and Twenty-Four Generations—methods to visualize the spirits within the body and perceive them through inner vision are beyond counting, and each has its efficacy. Yet some require visualizing thousands of things for self-defense, most of which are cumbersome and taxing to the mind. If one knows the Way of Guarding the One, all such practices may be discarded, for it is said: ‘He who knows the One completes all things.’

The oral transmission of the True One contains clear instructions. It is received on a day of royal vigor, with the blood of a white sacrificial animal, using white silk and white silver as tokens. The covenant is split like a golden contract, and reckless disclosure renders the spirits ineffectual. If a person guards the One, the One guards the person. Thus, blades find no edge to strike, and a hundred harms have no place for their malice. In ruin, one may yet succeed; in peril, one remains secure.

If in a haunted shrine, beneath mountain forests, in lands of great pestilence, among tombs, in the lairs of tigers and wolves, or the dens of serpents and vipers—guard the One without wavering, and all evils flee afar. But if one carelessly forgets to guard the One, the hundred ghosts may cause harm. If one lies down and is oppressed by nightmares, go to the central courtyard, gaze upon the Assistant Star, clench the fists, and guard the One—then the ghosts will depart. If it is overcast, simply remain indoors and face north to visualize the Assistant Star.

If besieged by armed bandits with no hope of escape, swiftly enter the hidden realm of the Six Jia, prostrate yourself, and guard the One—then the five weapons cannot harm you. One who guards the One may travel ten thousand *li*, join the army, or cross great rivers without needing to divine auspicious days or select propitious hours. When building, moving, or entering a new dwelling, one need not consult geomancy, star charts, or avoid the Tai Sui, Tai Yin Generals, monthly constructions, baleful stars, yearly taboos, or inauspicious omens—one will never encounter misfortune. This is a Way tested and proven by the sages of old.”

Master Baopuzi states: “The Way of the Mysterious One is likewise an essential method. It repels all harm and shares the same efficacy as the True One. My first Inner Chapter, titled *Unfolding the Mysterious*, refers precisely to this. Guarding the Mysterious One is even easier than guarding the True One. The True One has a name, surname, height, attire, and appearance, whereas the Mysterious One is simply perceived as such. First seek it at midday—this is what is meant by ‘knowing the white, guarding the black,’ such that one cannot die even if one wished to. Yet one must first maintain a hundred days of purity and fasting before one may await and attain it. It is obtained in no more than three or four days; once attained and guarded, it will never depart.

To guard the Mysterious One, one must also visualize one’s body dividing into three persons. Once these three appear, one may further multiply them to several dozen, all identical to oneself. Concealing or revealing them follows specific oral instructions—this is the Way of Divided Forms. Left Master, Jizi Xun, and Immortal Ge were thus able to appear in dozens of places in a single day. A host might converse with a guest in the hall while another host welcomed a guest at the gate, and yet another host fished by the water—guests could not discern which was the true host.

The Master said that guarding the One while cultivating the Bright Mirror allows one, upon perfecting the Mirror’s Way, to divide one’s form into dozens of persons, all identical in clothing and appearance.”

Master Baopuzi states: “The Master said that to attain long life, one must diligently ingest the Great Elixir; to achieve communion with spirits, one must divide the Golden and Watery Forms. Once the forms are divided, one may perceive the three *hun* and seven *po* within oneself, and the deities of heaven and earth may be encountered, while the spirits of mountains and rivers may be commanded.”

Master Baopuzi states: “Life is to be cherished, death is to be feared. Yet those who seek long life, nurture their nature, and avert death do not succeed without beginning in diligence and culminating in enduring vision. After the Way is perfected, there is little left to do. Before its completion, nothing is left undone. Gathering herbs and plants, toiling in mountains and marshes, refining and preparing medicines—all demand physical exertion. Climbing perilous heights, fording treacherous waters, laboring day and night without respite—without utmost resolve, one cannot endure.

To ascend to heaven upon the completion of the Golden Elixir, one must procure costly ingredients, which cannot be obtained hastily. One must rely on farming, herding, trade, or commerce to amass resources, accumulating years of toil before the elixir can be compounded. On the day of compounding, one must again maintain purity, fasting, and seclusion from worldly affairs. Amidst these difficulties, one must further focus the mind, guard the One, repel evil, and protect the body—ever as a sovereign governs a state or a general faces an enemy. Only thus may one attain the merit of long life. Those endowed with wisdom and great intellect, capable of ordering the world and aiding the common folk, may assuredly succeed in this endeavor. Mediocre and shallow people, though they may harbor aspirations, cannot persevere to the end.

Thus, the body of one person mirrors a nation. The chest and abdomen are like palaces, the four limbs like borderlands, the divisions of bones and joints like the hundred officials. The spirit is like the ruler, blood like ministers, and *qi* like the people. Therefore, one who knows how to govern the body can govern a state. Loving the people ensures the stability of the nation; nurturing *qi* ensures the wholeness of the body. When the people scatter, the nation perishes; when *qi* is exhausted, the body dies. The dead cannot be revived, the lost cannot be restored. Thus, the perfected person eliminates incipient calamities, treats illnesses before they arise, administers medicine before matters arise, and does not pursue what has already passed. The people are difficult to nurture yet easy to endanger; *qi* is difficult to purify yet easy to pollute. Therefore, one must carefully assess authority and virtue to preserve the altars of state, and curtail desires to fortify blood and *qi*. Only then does the True One abide, the Three and Seven are guarded, all harm is repelled, and life is prolonged.”

Master Baopuzi states: “The Master said that ingesting the Golden Elixir and Great Medicine, though one has not yet departed the world, keeps all evil at bay. If one merely ingests herbs, plants, or minor mineral elixirs, one may only cure illnesses and prolong life, but this is insufficient to avert external misfortunes. One may be assailed by ghosts, slighted by great mountain spirits, or invaded by spectral beings. Only guarding the True One can render one fearless of such entities. Next in efficacy is wearing divine talismans. To seek long life without knowing these two methods is perilous indeed. If three of four gates are closed, thieves may still enter—how much more so if all are open?”

One interlocutor said: “I, a humble man facing the wall, am shackled by Confucian teachings, knowing only the words of the Five Classics, Three Histories, and the hundred schools, along with florid poems and rhapsodies—useless, trifling compositions—upon which I have fixed my thoughts and guarded my mind for many years. Born into an age of manifold calamities, where chaos knows no end, halberds and battle-axes clash, and literary arts are scorned, I have squandered my efforts in vain. Though I have strained my mind to its utmost, delving into subtleties and plumbing hidden depths, I have found no official emolument therein, nor escaped the furrowed fields. Moreover, such labors have drained my vital essence and availed nothing for my years and life. Now, with gray hairs announcing twilight and my youthful aspirations in decline, I wish to turn back from delusion and seek the Way of life. Yet in this sudden, boundless confusion, I know not where to direct my steps—like fording a great river, I see no means of crossing. You, Master, have exhaustively studied the ancient canons and mastered the arcane and recondite. Might you tell me how many scrolls of Daoist texts there are in all, and deign to reveal their titles?”

Master Baopuzi replied: “I, too, once suffered from this same affliction. In former days, I was fortunate to meet my enlightened teacher, Master Zheng, though I regret that my dullness as a disciple left me unable to penetrate the most profound and lofty mysteries. At the time, though I served as one of his disciples, sweeping and cleaning, my talent and insight were shallow, and my years still young. My thoughts were not single-minded, and worldly attachments remained unsevered, so I gained little—this I count my greatest regret. Master Zheng was then over eighty years old, his temples already streaked with white, yet within a few years, his hair turned black again. His complexion was full and joyful, and he could draw a strong crossbow to shoot a hundred paces. He walked hundreds of *li* in a day and drank two *dou* of wine without becoming drunk. Whenever he climbed mountains, his body was light and nimble, scaling perilous heights and traversing treacherous paths—even young men pursuing him often could not keep up. His diet differed not from that of ordinary men, nor did I ever see him abstain from grain.

I once asked Huang Zhang, a senior disciple who had followed him earlier, and he told me that Master Zheng, on his return from Yuzhang, encountered a succession of fierce winds while crossing the Juegou Inlet. He had also heard that bandits lay ahead, so his companions urged him to wait for reinforcements. As everyone’s provisions were scant, Master Zheng shared his rice to relieve the others, himself eating nothing. Yet for fifty days, he did not hunger. Nor did I ever witness his practices—what arts he employed, I know not. Even by lamplight, he wrote in a hand finer than that of young men.”

Master Zheng was naturally attuned to music and skilled at playing the zither. When seated at leisure with several attendants, he would answer their questions without pause, his speech flowing uninterrupted, while simultaneously directing those tuning the strings to his left and right—adjusting their lengths with not the slightest error.

In my later years, I became Master Zheng’s disciple and requested to see his esoteric texts. He told me: *“The essential Dao fits on a foot of silk—enough to transcend the world. No more is needed.”* Yet he added: *“After broad study, one far surpasses those who have seen nothing. Once you grasp the intent, even simple techniques may serve to guard beginners against early pitfalls.”* He first presented me with nearly a hundred scrolls of Daoist admonitions and precepts, showing them to me gradually. As I had already read many of these works, I often questioned him about their ambiguities. Master Zheng said: *“You have the talent to discern matters—you may be taught. Yet what you know, though vast, lacks depth, and your mind strays to external learning, unable to focus. You have not yet delved deeply into the profound. Now I shall show you finer texts.”* He also promised that I would gradually receive short writings copied on silk, and over the years, the works I saw would amount to some two hundred scrolls—yet in the end, I never obtained them.

His other disciples were treated as servants, tasked with gathering firewood and tilling fields. Only I, frail and weak, was unfit for such labor. Yet with no way to prove myself, I often swept the floors, dusted the furniture, ground ink, and held the candle for Master Zheng, or helped transcribe old texts. Though he treated me as he did his senior disciples, he told me: *“Every scroll of miscellaneous Daoist texts contains valuable insights. You must only discern their essence and choose what to practice, not strive to memorize them all—lest you waste time and weary the mind. Once the Golden Elixir is achieved, all these become unnecessary.”* He sometimes considered teaching others, emphasizing the need for a proper sequence—beginning with the shallow and progressing to the profound—to guide learners without leaving them without a path to follow.

Master Zheng would not allow others to copy his books. One had to grasp their meaning, and though he might lend them for long periods, no one dared to steal even a single character. Originally a great Confucian scholar, he turned to the Dao in his later years yet continued teaching the *Book of Rites* and *Book of Documents* without interruption. His bearing was noble and upright, his demeanor dignified and proper—all who met him did so with reverence. When seeking his counsel, one always waited for his gentle countenance, never daring to be hasty.

The texts in my possession for over a month would have sufficed for extensive copying, yet I dared not secretly transcribe them, for Master Zheng was keen and perceptive. If he had discovered it, the minor loss would have outweighed the greater. Even at the outset of my studies, I dared not request too much, only making occasional, measured inquiries. Thus, I knew only to drink from the river but never filled my belly.

Among his fifty-odd disciples, only I was entrusted with the *Scripture of the Golden Elixir*, the *Three Sovereigns’ Inner Writings*, and the *Five Agents Record in the Pillow*. The rest did not even glimpse the titles of these works. Though I did not obtain all his texts, I recorded their names. Now I shall recount them for you, that later seekers of such writings may broaden their search:

**Daoist Scriptures:**
*The Three Sovereigns’ Inner Writings* (Heaven, Earth, Human, 3 scrolls),

*The Scripture of the Tortoise’s Pattern*,
*The Record of Shanyang*,
*The Record of the Jade Tally*,
*The Diagram of Eight Histories*,
*The Scripture of Entering the Chamber*,
*The Left and Right Tallies*,
*The Scripture of the Jade Calendar*,
*The Rites of Ascending to Heaven*,
*The Scripture of Nine Wonders*,
*The Scripture of Renewal*,
*The Scripture of Four Collars* (10 scrolls),
*The Scripture of Ingesting the Essence of the Sun and Moon*,
*The Scripture of Ingesting the Six Qi*,
*The Scripture of the Single Elixir*,
*The Scripture of Embryonic Breathing*,
*The Scripture of Circulating Qi to Cure Illness*,
*The Scripture of Victory Within* (10 scrolls),

All those without specified scroll numbers are single-scroll works. The following are various talismans: *The Talismans of Self-Arising*, *The Talismans of Golden Light*, *The Three Scrolls of the Great Mystery Talismans*, *The Talismans of Communing with Heaven*, *The Talismans of the Five Essences*, *The Talismans of the Stone Chamber*, *The Talismans of the Jade Stave*, *The Talismans of the Pillow*, *The Talismans of the Young Lad*, *The Talismans of the Nine Numinous*, *The Talismans of the Six Lords*, *The Talismans of the Mysterious Capital*, *The Talismans of the Yellow Emperor*, *The Talismans of the Thirty-Six Generals of Shao Qian*, *The Talismans of Prolonging Life*, *The Divine Talismans of Heavenly Water*, *The Forty-Nine True Talismans*, *The Talismans of Heavenly Water*, *The Talismans of the Green Dragon*, *The Talismans of the White Tiger*, *The Talismans of the Vermilion Bird*, *The Talismans of the Dark Warrior*, *The Talismans of the Vermilion Embryo*, *The Talismans of the Seven Mechanisms*, *The Talismans of the Nine Heavens’ Dispatch of Troops*, *The Talismans of the Nine Heavens*, *The Talismans of the Ancient Scripture*, *The Seven Talismans*, *The Great Talismans of Warding Off Calamity*, *The Talismans of Master Xuan*, *The Talismans of the Military Filial Classic and Yan Jun’s Three Pouches of Dragon-Tiger Avoidance of Arms*, *The Talismans of Embracing the Origin*, *The Talismans of Shen Xi*, *The Talismans of Yu Qiao*, *The Talismans of Dispelling Disasters*, *The Talismans of the Eight Trigrams*, *The Talismans of Overseeing Qian*, *The Talismans of Thunder and Lightning*, *The Talismans of Wan Bi*, *The Talismans of the Eight Might and Five Victories*, *The Talismans of Awesome Joy*, *The Talismans of Giant Victory*, *The Talismans of the Maiden Cai*, *The Talismans of Mysterious Essence*, *The Talismans of the Jade Calendar*, *The Talismans of the Northern Terrace*, *The Talismans of the Great Yin-Yang Warding*, *The Talismans of the Pillow*, *The Ten Scrolls of Talismans for Curing the Hundred Illnesses*, *The Ten Scrolls of Talismans for Suppressing the Strange*, *The Twenty Scrolls of Master Hu’s Talismans*, *The Nine Scrolls of the Nine Terraces Talismans*, *The Ten Scrolls of the Six Jia Communing with the Numinous Talismans*, *The Five Hundred Scrolls of the Six Yin Traveling Kitchen, Dragon Embryo, Stone Chamber, Three Metals, and Five Woods Preventing Death Talismans*, *The Talismans for Summoning and Curing Military Fires*, and *The Ten Scrolls of the Jade Axe Talismans*—these are all major talismans. The remaining minor ones are too numerous to record in full.

Bao Puzi said: "Master Zheng stated that talismans originate from the Lord Lao, and all are celestial patterns. The Lord Lao could commune with the numinous spirits, and the talismans were all bestowed by them. That people today find them seldom efficacious is due to their long transmission and the many errors in transcription. Moreover, their faith is not sincere, and thus their application also fails. It is like writing characters—if a talisman is erroneous, it not only brings no benefit but may even cause harm. Even with written characters, which people recognize, transcription errors still abound. Hence the proverb: 'After three transcriptions, a fish becomes a *lu*, a *yao* becomes a tiger.' This is what it means. The characters *qi* (七) and *shi* (士) differ only in the length and angle of a single stroke. Yet the characters on talismans today are unreadable, their errors undetectable, so none know their instability.

Among the world’s practitioners, there are those who receive the art of bodily empowerment, whose talismans alone prove effective. This is like a person who, when using musk, naturally emits fragrance—such ability cannot be transmitted. Even so, one must obtain an unerring talisman and apply it with a rectified mind. Though its effects may not be as swift as those of true bodily empowerment, it will still bring benefit.

Generally, for Daoist adepts seeking longevity, their focus lies in medicinal elixirs; talismans and swords serve only to repel ghosts and ward off evil. As for the major talismans that claim their use can lead to transcendence—these cannot be relied upon exclusively. In the Wu dynasty, there was a man named Jie Xiang who could read talismanic scripts and discern whether they were erroneous. Someone tested him by taking talismans for curing the hundred illnesses and various exorcistic talismans, removing their labels, and presenting them to him. He named each one accurately, and where errors existed, he corrected them for others. From that time onward, none have possessed such knowledge."

Someone asked, “Of the great elixirs of immortality, none surpasses the Golden Elixir. Having heard your instruction on this, may I dare ask whether talismanic writings possess the utmost spiritual efficacy?”

Master Baopu replied, “I have heard Master Zheng say that of all Daoist scriptures, none are weightier than the *Inner Writings of the Three Sovereigns* and the *True Forms of the Five Peaks*. The immortal officials and perfected beings of antiquity held this Way in the highest reverence, and it was not transmitted except to those bearing the name of immortals. It is passed down once every forty years, with blood-oaths sworn and pledges of fealty exchanged. The scriptures exist in all the famous mountains and Five Peaks, but they are concealed in secluded stone chambers. Only those who enter the mountains with sincere devotion and meditative focus will have the mountain spirits open the way, allowing them to behold the texts. There was one Bo Zhongli who obtained them in the mountains—he set up an altar, offered silk, and always made a copy before departing.

The scriptures must be kept in a pure and clean place. For every undertaking, one must first make obeisance to them as if attending a sovereign or father. The scriptures state: ‘Where the Three Sovereigns’ Writings reside, they repel evil spirits, pestilential qi, and all manner of calamities—whether sudden misfortune or flying disaster. If one lies gravely ill and on the verge of death, yet their faith in the Dao remains steadfast, holding these writings will ensure they do not perish. For a woman in difficult labor gasping for breath, holding them will bring forth the child at once. If a Daoist seeks long life and carries these writings into the mountains, they will ward off tigers, wolves, mountain spirits, the Five Poisons, and all manner of malevolent forces—none will dare approach. They allow one to cross rivers and seas, repel dragons, and still wind and waves. Mastering their methods, one may perform transformations and summon works of wonder.’”

Without need to select auspicious days or sites, households shall know no misfortune or calamity. Should one wish to build a new dwelling or tomb, copy the *Writings of the Earth Sovereign* dozens of times and scatter them upon the ground. On the morrow, observe where a yellow hue has settled—commence construction there, and the household shall surely prosper. Furthermore, when another is interred, inscribe the *Writings of the Human Sovereign*, along with one’s own name, upon paper and secretly place it within the tomb, ensuring no one knows. This will shield one from flying disasters and thieves. Should others plot against you, their schemes shall rebound to harm themselves.

These writings must first be purified through a hundred days of fasting and abstinence before one may summon the Celestial Deities, the Controller of Destiny, the Year-Star, the Day-Patrol, the spirits of the Five Peaks and Four Waterways, and the deities of earth and temple altars. All shall appear in human form, allowing one to inquire of fortune or misfortune, safety or peril, and the origins of afflictions plaguing the sick. Moreover, inscribing eighteen characters upon one’s garments ensures safe passage across rivers and seas, free from the peril of wind and waves.

Households possessing the *True Forms of the Five Peaks* shall repel armed conflict and treacherous rebellion. Those who seek to harm them shall instead suffer the calamity themselves. Daoists who come upon these texts must not fail in benevolence, compassion, and moral rectitude; should they lack purity and uprightness, disaster shall befall them, destroying their households. These writings must not be treated lightly.

Among the arts of transformation, the greatest is the *Five Phases Record of Master Mo*, originally comprising five scrolls. Before his ascension, Lord Liu An transcribed its essentials into a single scroll. Its methods employ elixirs and talismans to enable flight, ascent and descent, and boundless concealment. One may smile to become a woman, frown to become an old man, crouch to become a child, grasp a staff to become a forest, or sow seeds to grow edible melons and fruits. Drawing lines upon the ground creates rivers; heaping soil forms mountains. One may conjure traveling kitchens, summon clouds, and ignite flames—nothing lies beyond its power.

Next is the *Hidden Subtleties of the Jade Maiden*, a single scroll enabling transformations into flying birds, running beasts, gold, wood, jade, and stone. It can summon clouds and rain across a hundred *li*, or snow in equal measure. It allows crossing great waters without boats or bridges, dividing one’s form into a thousand, and soaring high upon the wind, entering and exiting without obstruction. One may exhale seven-colored qi, perceive the eight directions and the earth’s depths, emit light a thousand *zhang* to illuminate dark chambers—truly a great art. Yet its steps trace dozens of constellations in intricate patterns, difficult to master, and few can chart its course. The *Hongbao* and *Wanbi* of Huainan cannot compare.

There is also the *Seven Transformations of the White Tiger*: take the pelt of a white tiger slain on the third day of the third month, along with fresh blood from a sacrificial post, tiger blood, purple silk, woven sandals, and floating duckweed, and combine them on that same day. The first growth resembles sesame, bearing seeds; plant these, and each generation yields a new wonder. After seven plantings, use the seeds to achieve transformations—shifting form and visage at will, flying or sinking as one desires. Its effects resemble those of Master Mo’s *Five Phases* and the *Jade Maiden’s Hidden Subtleties*; beyond this, lesser arts are not worth discussing.

This survey aims to acquaint seekers of the Dao with the names of extraordinary texts. Master Zheng was not merely versed in the Five Classics and the Dao of immortality; he also mastered the Nine Palaces and Three Marvels, celestial reckoning, and the prophecies of the *River Chart* and *Luo Scripture*. Nothing escaped his meticulous study. In the first year of the *Taian* era, foreseeing the chaos of the age’s end and the coming turmoil in the south, he took up his satchel and immortal elixirs, leading his disciples into Mount Huo—vanishing without a trace.

**Chapter Twenty: Dispelling Delusion**
*Baopuzi* states: “To seek luminous pearls, one must plumb the depths of Hepu; only then may one obtain the night-shining pearl of the black dragon. To gather fine jade, one must quarry the cliffs of Jingshan; only then may one acquire the foot-long jade worth a city’s ransom. To seek a teacher and inquire of the Dao, if one fails to find the right master, departing in hope of eventual success is as futile as clinging in vain expectation of achievement. Time is squandered, efforts obstructed, and though one may later bitterly regret it, it will be too late.

Even in mundane matters, knowledge cannot be gained by mere sitting; how much more so for the affairs of immortals? Though one be sagely and wise, there is no self-enlightenment—no attainment through prolonged reflection or analogy. One must seek a teacher, one both profound and erudite, for the task is like drawing water from the vast sea or felling trees in an endless forest. The challenge lies not in scarcity but in one’s own inadequacy.

What tigers and leopards discard, raccoons and rats fight over. What Tao Zhu cast aside, Yuan Xian lacked. If one’s teacher lacks far-reaching insight and instead relies on a shallow, narrow-minded person—one whose knowledge is meager, whose source is shallow and flow stunted—then even if they grudgingly impart what little they know, their teachings will be trivial and unremarkable. Their own mastery is already flawed; if they further dilute their scant knowledge to instruct others, how can any success be expected? It is like begging grain at the gates of Boyi and Shuqi or seeking warmth in the home of Qian Lou—one may receive acorns and coarse cloth, but never a feast of fattened cattle or robes of brocade and fox fur.

Some cling to mediocre teachers, never awakening to their folly. Others chance upon a true master but fail to pursue them diligently. These are losses born of unawareness, irretrievable. Discerning a person’s depth is no easy task; the ancients’ difficulties were not without reason. White stone resembles jade, and the treacherous mimic the virtuous. The worthy conceal themselves further, possessing yet appearing as if they do not, while the deceitful flaunt their emptiness, feigning substance. Without the keenest discernment, how can one distinguish them?

Those who persist with mediocre teachers do not do so knowing their ignorance but truly believe them sufficient. Those who encounter a master of insight yet fail to serve them do not recognize their depth but truly see no difference.”

Those who grasp the essential Dao are free from desire for things and unmoved by worldly renown—how then would they parade themselves before the vulgar? Yet shallow and frivolous men, for the most part, boast and exaggerate, hardening their expressions and modulating their voices to adorn their falsehoods, dazzling and deluding latecomers to learning with their grand claims. They declare, for instance, that they have ascended famous mountains and met immortals. Those who hear such words in haste and fail to sift and examine them are rarely aware of the deception.
