I have often seen miscellaneous wandering Daoists frequenting the gates of the powerful, instructing their followers to fabricate empty reputations, claiming they are already four or five hundred years old. When someone happens to ask their age, they feign not to hear, smiling and nodding vaguely, saying they are eighty or ninety. Then, in a moment, they will say, “I once fasted in the mountains of Huayin for fifty years, then spent forty years in the Shaoshi range of Mount Song, followed by sixty years on Mount Tai, and another fifty years on Mount Ji with so-and-so.” They recount their experiences in detail, just so their listeners will tally the years and conclude they are already centuries old. Thereupon, those who fancy such things gather like smoke and mist, crowding at their doors.

Some practitioners of arts may happen to possess a natural constitution that allows them to see ghosts and spirits, and they are somewhat skilled in internal divination, knowing events of a person’s future and past—but in truth, they cannot alter fortune or misfortune in the slightest. They are no more than divining stalks and tortoise shells. When people see their minor successes, they hail them as divine beings, assuming they must know everything. If not, they may excel in talismanic water, incantations, and exorcisms, curing afflictions effectively, yet remain ignorant of the Way of immortality. Or they may practice miscellaneous arts, able to see ghosts and spirits, but these bring no benefit to their lifespan. Ask them about the Way of the Golden Elixir, and they generally know nothing. Upon closer examination, one finds they mostly deceive the world to amass wealth, stopping at nothing. Such men and common thieves follow different paths but arrive at the same end.

Relying on empty words is not as reliable as demonstrating verifiable deeds. To instruct later learners, I will recount comparable cases as evidence of such frauds.

There was once a man named Gu Qiang who took herbal remedies and practiced the methods of Rongcheng, Xuan, and Su. Though over eighty, he remained sharp and not greatly enfeebled by age. People of his time called him an immortal or the “Thousand-Year-Old Elder.” When Governor Ji of Yangzhou heard of him, he tested the claim by summoning him to Yidu. Upon arrival, Gu Qiang’s throat constricted and his words faltered, as if he knew far more than he was willing to reveal.

Thus, those eager for wonders gathered like echoes responding to sound, like shadows clinging to form—clouds converging, mists coalescing—all clamoring in praise, their gifts and offerings unceasing, leaving him with surplus gold and coin. Even the esteem once accorded Luan and Li in the former Han could not surpass this. Yet he never ceased taking asparagus root, proving his body had never known the elixir of cinnabar or the great medicine. Still, Gu Qiang had dabbled in books and records, possessing some knowledge of antiquity. He claimed to be four thousand years old, boldly spinning falsehoods without a blush. He insisted he had seen Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang, describing them all with vivid clarity as if it were fact.

The world says Yao’s brows had eight colors—this is untrue. His brows simply rose sharply at the temples, resembling the character for "eight." Yao was tall and grand, with a magnificent beard, drinking over two *hu* of wine in a day. Later generations exaggerated this to a thousand goblets, but he could not have managed such a feat—I myself witnessed him thoroughly intoxicated more than once. Though a sage, in his old age he handled governance less ably than in his prime. Even the banishment of the Four Fiends and the promotion of the Eight Virtues were owed to Shun’s merit.

Shun was but a lonely orphan from a humble family, yet possessed extraordinary talent. He farmed in obscurity on Mount Li, fished in Thunder Marsh, and made pottery by the sea—none of his contemporaries recognized his exceptional gifts. I saw how he transformed the people through virtue wherever he went. His eyes bore double pupils, a sign of great nobility. I often encouraged and consoled him: "Cultivate lofty ideals, aspire to the highest good, and fret not over poverty. The fire virtue has run its course; the yellow essence will rise. Who but you is destined to inherit the mandate?" Yet his father was utterly obstinate, his brother utterly wicked, ever scheming to kill Shun. I repeatedly admonished them: "This child will exalt your clan and bring blessings to the four seas—not just your family. Do not treat him lightly." Soon after, he received the abdication, and I recalled how my words had proven prophetic.

He further claimed: When Confucius’s mother was sixteen or seventeen, I divined that she would bear a noble son. When Zhongni was born, he was truly an extraordinary man—nine feet six inches tall, his forehead like Yao’s, his neck like Gaoyao’s, his shoulders like Zichan’s, though from the waist down he fell three inches short of Yu. Despite his poverty and lowly origins, even as a child he delighted in ritual vessels. I knew then he would achieve greatness.

When he grew to maturity, his lofty discourse astonished all who heard it, and disciples from near and far flocked to study under him, numbering several thousand in their registers. I delighted in listening to his words and often sought him out, though I regretted my own lack of learning, unable to engage him in deeper discussion. He often urged me to study the *Yi*, saying, "This is a fine text indeed. I have privately taken to it—my leather thongs have snapped thrice, my iron staff has bent thrice—and now I have attained great enlightenment."

In the fourteenth year of Duke Ai of Lu, a unicorn was captured during a western hunt and died. Confucius questioned me about it, and I told him this was no auspicious omen. Thereupon, he wept in sorrow. Later, he had an ill-omened dream and wished to see me again. At the time, it was the fourth month, and the heat was intense, so I could not go. Soon after, I heard he fell ill for seven days and passed away. To this day, I still vividly recall his countenance.

He also recounted: The First Emperor of Qin brought me to Pengcheng to retrieve the tripods of the Zhou. I told him, "These tripods are divine objects. They emerge of their own accord when virtue prevails and vanish when the Way is lost. Cultivate yourself, and they will come to you—you cannot obtain them by force." The First Emperor appeared deeply unsettled by my words, and indeed, the tripods could not be drawn out. He then apologized, saying, "You are truly a man of far-reaching insight."

He further spoke of Liu Bang and Xiang Yu, their fates as clear as if etched in stone—such matters are too numerous to record in full. Those who witnessed these events took them as jest, yet all who heard his words believed them.

Later, I grew increasingly senile, my memory fading and my affairs neglected. Magistrate Ji once gave me a jade cup, and I suddenly told him, "Long ago, Master Anqi bequeathed this to me." Later, I fell ill and died at the home of Huang Zheng in Shouchun. Zheng suspected I had transformed and departed. A year later, he had my coffin opened to check, and my body was still there, perfectly preserved.

These are all cases of fame without substance, causing the world to disbelieve in the existence of immortals—all due to such frauds who confuse the false with the true.

Wu Wen, the governor of Chengdu, spoke of a man from Wuyuan named Cai Dan, who was fond of the Dao but failed to find a worthy master or grasp its essential practices. He abandoned his family and livelihood, reciting day and night the *Huangting Jing*, the *Taiqing Zhong Jing*, the *Guantian Jiexiang*, and other texts of lesser importance. His mouth never ceased chanting, for he believed the Dao was exhausted in these works. Yet he never understood their practical application, merely admiring their florid rhetoric to deceive others. He taught that one need only recite them a thousand times to grasp their meaning. Over time, his family grew distressed, their resources depleted, while he showed no extraordinary abilities. Ashamed and resentful, unable to justify himself, he abandoned his home, claiming to have attained the Dao of immortality. He fled to remote mountains, yet knew nothing of gathering herbs or plants for avoiding grain. Instead, he sold firewood to exchange for food and clothing. After three years of hunger, cold, and hardship, someone recognized him, but he feigned ignorance. Unable to endure further, he returned home—emaciated, blackened, and skeletal, scarcely human. His family asked where he had been and whether he had truly become an immortal. He deceived them, saying, "I have not yet ascended to heaven, but I have become an earthbound immortal. My rank is still low, and I must serve the senior immortals. Gradually, I will be promoted. Recently, I was herding several dragons for Lord Lao. One of them, a five-colored dragon, was the finest—Lord Lao’s usual mount. I was tasked with guarding it, but I was negligent, playing dice with the junior immortals. Suddenly, the dragon vanished, and its whereabouts remain unknown. For this offense, I was punished and sent to the foot of Kunlun Mountain to weed three or four *qing* of land. The soil was fine, but the center was overgrown with brambles. The labor was indescribably arduous, and by law, I must serve ten years before being pardoned. Coincidentally, the immortals Po Quanzi and Wang Qiao came to inspect the work. I pleaded with them, and they helped me, then allowed me to return home temporarily to cultivate further and seek release. Thus, I have grown old and died."

At first, Dan claimed to have come from Kunlun. His relatives and friends eagerly asked what it was like. He replied, "No one asks how many *li* high the sky is—when you look up, it seems no more than ten or so *zhang* away. There grows the *muhe*, four *zhang* and nine *chi* tall, its ears as large as a cart. There are trees of pearl and jade, *shatang*, *langgan*, and *bigui*, as well as jade plums, jade melons, and jade peaches. Their forms resemble mundane peaches and plums, but they are luminous, translucent, and hard. They must be washed in jade well water to soften before eating. When the wind rises, the branches, flowers, and leaves of the pearl and jade trees strike one another, producing the five notes of music—clear and mournful, stirring the heart. Seeing my banishment and lost aspirations, I could not hear this without sorrow."

He also described Kunlun Mountain: "On one side, there are four hundred and forty gates, each four *li* wide. Within lie five cities and twelve towers. Below the towers are green dragons and white tigers, pythons over a hundred *li* long, their mouths and teeth like three-hundred-*hu* boats. Giant wasps, a *zhang* in size, possess venom deadly to elephants. There are also divine beasts—lions, *bixie*, three-horned deer, scorched sheep, copper-headed and iron-browed creatures with long fangs and chiseled teeth—thirty-six kinds in all. Knowing their names ensures that no evil spirits or beasts in the world dare harm you. Among the deities are the Headless One, Lord Daojing, Duke Xilu, Master Zhonghuang, and the Six Gate Lords. Zhang Yang, styled Ziyuan, was nearly admitted to the Jade Palace, but without Lord Lao’s bamboo tally or left-and-right seals, he could not enter. The Five Rivers flow from the mountain’s edges, and Weak Water encircles it. A goose feather does not float upon it, nor can flying birds cross—only immortals may traverse it. Above dwell divine birds and horses—*Youchang*, *Jiaoming*, *Teng Huang*, *Jiguang*—all capable of human speech and undying. Truly, it is a splendid immortal realm. I regret not being able to roam it freely."

At the time, many who heard Dan’s vivid account believed him.

In Puban of Hedong, there was a man named Xiang ●du who entered the mountains with his son to study immortality. After ten years, they returned home. When his family asked why, ●du said, "After three years of focused cultivation in the mountains, an immortal came to welcome me. We rode a dragon and ascended to heaven. For a long while, I looked down at the earth—dim and distant—already far below. The dragon moved swiftly, its head raised and tail lowered, making me feel perilously unsteady on its back. Upon reaching heaven, we first passed the Purple Palace, with its golden beds and jade tables, gleaming brilliantly—a truly noble place. The immortals gave me a cup of flowing rosy clouds, and after drinking it, I no longer felt hunger or thirst. Suddenly, I longed for home. When I appeared before the Heavenly Emperor, my bow was improper, and I was reprimanded and sent back, ordered to cultivate further before I might return. Once, the Prince of Huainan, Liu An, ascended to heaven and met the Heavenly Emperor. He sat cross-legged and boasted, calling himself 'the solitary one.' For this, he was punished and made to serve in the heavenly kitchens for three years. What am I compared to him?" In Hedong, ●du was thus nicknamed the "Reprimanded Immortal."

Such people abound, their kinds manifold—one cannot fail to examine them closely. These absurd tales are so blatant, yet some still do not recognize their falsity. How much less can one discern subtler deceptions, where frauds embellish semblances of truth? Without exceptional clarity, who could hastily distinguish them?

There are also those who falsely claim to be renowned Daoist masters of past ages. For instance, Bai He was said to be eight thousand seven hundred years old, appearing occasionally among the common folk before vanishing without a trace. No one knew his whereabouts.

In Luoyang there was a Daoist who had extensively studied various matters and was well-versed in alchemical arts and numerology. When people consulted Bai He on perplexing questions, he would respond to their inquiries with effortless elucidation, leaving no doubts unresolved—thus was he deemed a man of profound insight. Yet none knew his true age, though it was widely believed he must have neared a thousand years. Later, he suddenly departed, and none knew his whereabouts.

A man in Hebei then claimed to be Bai He, drawing throngs from far and near who flocked to serve and offer him gifts. When Bai He’s disciples heard of his reappearance, they rejoiced and went to meet him, only to confirm it was not him. The impostor then fled and vanished. The Five Classics and Four Divisions have become but withered straw dogs, the dregs of what once was. The so-called "traces" are but the marks left by feet—they are not the feet themselves. "Books" are the works of sages—they are not the sages themselves. Yet scholars travel a thousand *li* with satchels on their backs to seek their teachers. How much more should one diligently pursue and inquire into the Way of long life, which true immortals hold in such esteem? Yet one must carefully discern the true from the false!

I fear that men like Gu Qiang, Cai Dan, Xiang ●du, and Bai He will never cease to appear among the world’s people. Those who delight in such matters would do well to heed this account, that they may better sift the genuine from the spurious. The *Scriptures of the Immortals* also state that the pupils of an immortal’s eyes are square. In Luoyang, a man named Bai Zhongli told me that his own pupils were perfectly square—surely this marks him as an extraordinary person.

**Appendix**

**Fragmentary Passages from the *Baopuzi* Inner Chapters**

While collating the *Baopuzi* by hand, I examined various texts that cite it and found many passages absent from the current editions. I recorded them as I encountered them, eliminating duplicates and redundancies, yielding one hundred forty-five entries. Following the general principle of the original work, I have classified those concerning immortals, the elixir, and alchemy as *Fragmentary Passages from the Inner Chapters*, and the rest—refutations, explanations, and exegeses—as *Fragmentary Passages from the Outer Chapters*, each compiled into a single scroll.

**Ji Chang of Changbai**

*(Note: This compilation was edited by Yan Kejun, as recorded in *Tieqiao Man Gao*, Volume 6, and referenced in *Complete Jin Prose*, Volume 117.)*

When Master Ge the Immortal cast his talismanic writings and charms into the river, they drifted downstream. The next talisman he cast flowed upstream. The third talisman he cast neither sank nor floated but remained suspended, whereupon the two earlier talismans returned to join it.

*(From an old manuscript of the *Beitang Shuchao*, Volume 103.)*

Emperor Wu of Wei deemed Zuo Ci a deluded fraud and sought to execute him, ordering his soldiers to arrest him. Ci deliberately allowed himself to be seen but did not flee. When they attempted to torture him, seven identical Cis appeared in the prison, indistinguishable from the true one. The matter was reported to the emperor, who ordered all of them killed. In an instant, six of the Cis transformed into wooden tablets, while the seventh escaped and fled into a flock of sheep.

*(From an old manuscript of the *Beitang Shuchao*, Volume 104, "Zha Pian," and the *Taiping Imperial Encyclopedia*, Volume 606.)*

When Emperor Wu of Wei had Zuo Ci arrested, Ci fled into the marketplace. Officers reported that Ci had one blind eye and wore a hempen headscarf and unlined robes. Soon, every person in the market appeared the same.

*(From the *Taiping Imperial Encyclopedia*, Volume 740.)*

In his youth, Chengyang’s Xi Jian was out hunting when he fell into an empty tomb and grew famished. Inside, he saw a large tortoise that repeatedly turned in circles, facing no fixed direction, opening its mouth to inhale qi, now lowering its head, now raising it. Jian had long heard that tortoises could guide one in Daoist breathing exercises, so he tried following the tortoise’s movements and soon no longer felt hunger. After more than a hundred days, he endured great hardship. Later, someone happened to peer into the tomb, saw Jian, and helped him out. Thereafter, he mastered the art of swallowing qi and abstaining from grain.

The King of Wei summoned him and, dismissing objections, placed him in an earthen chamber to test him. After a year without food, his complexion remained radiant, and his strength was as before.

During the Wu dynasty, there was a man named Yao Guang who possessed the art of fire. The Lord of Wu personally came to test him. Thousands of bundles of reeds were piled up, and Guang sat atop them, with more thousands of bundles heaped upon him. A fierce wind was then used to set the reeds ablaze. When the fire burned out, it was thought that Guang would have been reduced to ashes. Yet he sat serenely amid the embers, shook out his robes, rose, and held a scroll of text. The Lord of Wu took it and examined it but could not comprehend it.

Li Nan was riding a red horse when he encountered another man on a white horse. The white horse neighed first, and the red horse responded. Nan said to his attendants, "That white horse says you will soon meet a yellow horse with a blind left eye—this is my son. Tell him to ride swiftly so we may catch up." His attendants did not believe him. After traveling two *li*, they indeed met the yellow horse with a blind left eye. Nan’s red horse neighed first, and the blind horse answered. When they questioned the rider, he turned out to be the son of the man on the white horse.

*Yiwen Leiju* 93, *Taiping Yulan* 897. The character *ye* in *Wangye Shu* is likely a scribal error for *zuo*. When the Governor of Jingzhou died, three white tigers with three heads prostrated themselves beneath the funeral carriage. *Beitang Shuchao* 35 (based on Yan Kejun’s collated and supplemented edition).

According to the report of the envoy Gan Zong in *Quan Jinwen* 117 (where *Zong* is written as *Chong*), matters of the Western Regions state: Foreign masters of the occult who possess divine incantations can, by performing the *Yu step* at the edge of a deep pool and blowing *qi*, summon a dragon to float to the surface. When it first emerges, it measures over ten *zhang* in length. The master then blows upon it again, and with each breath, the dragon shrinks. When it has diminished to a length of several *cun*, the master plucks it up and places it in a flask. The flask may contain four or five dragons, which are nourished with a little water, its mouth stoppered with a porous material. The kingdom often suffers from drought. When the masters hear of neighboring lands afflicted by scant rain and frequent drought, they carry the dragons to sell, each fetching tens of *jin* of gold. The entire kingdom gathers to raise the sum, and once paid, the master opens the flask, releases a dragon into a deep pool, and again performs the *Yu step* while blowing upon it. With each breath, the dragon lengthens by tens of *zhang*, and in an instant, clouds and rain gather on all sides.

*Yiwen Leiju* 96, *Taiping Yulan* 11, 736, 929.

The pines of Tianling, which lie like upturned canopies, and the cypresses of the great valleys, which grow inverted, all attain a height equal to heaven and endure as long as earth.

*Yiwen Leiju* 88 (based on Yan Kejun’s collated and supplemented edition).

Shepherd’s purse, wheat, and garlic wither by mid-summer.

*Yiwen Leiju* 82, *Taiping Yulan* 980.

The herbs that *ruoshi* consume must be of this kind. Moreover, among the creatures of the sea—clams, conches, and oysters—none can be eaten raw by ordinary people, much less by gentlemen or *ruoshi*.

*Yiwen Leiju* 97, *Taiping Yulan* 942.

The *qingyun zhi* grows on the northern slopes of famous mountains, between great blue stones. Its green cap has three layers, and above it, clouds and mist swirl. Its flavor is pungent and sweet. When dried in the shade and consumed, it grants a lifespan of a thousand years without aging, allowing one to ride the clouds, ascend to heaven, and behold spirits and gods.

*Yiwen Leiju* 98. (The following nineteen entries appear to be lost fragments from the *Xianyao* chapter.)

The *huanglong zhi* grows in divine mountains, resembling a yellow dragon. Its flavor is pungent and sweet. Harvested in all four seasons and dried in the shade, a daily dose of one *ge* grants a lifespan of ten thousand years and bestows radiance.

Ibid.

The *jinlan zhi* grows on the northern slopes of famous mountains, between gold and stone. Above it is a watery cap, from which its stem emerges.

(Note: *chi* is likely a scribal error for *cheng*.)

In the tenth day of autumn, seek it and drink its inner water—this grants a lifespan of a thousand years and sharpens hearing and sight. Ibid.

The *chiyun zhi* grows amid the towering stones of Mount Cang, resembling a standing figure, its form like linked drums. Its hue is lustrous. Harvested in summer and dried in the shade, when consumed, it enables one to ride the clouds, ascend to heaven to behold the eight extremities, commune with the divine, and extend life to ten thousand years. Ibid.

The *dan zhi* grows on the northern slopes of famous mountains, in the Kunlun range, at the headwaters of great valleys, amid gold and stone. Ibid.

The *huo zhi* is always harvested in summer. Its leaves are red above, its stem green below. The Red Pine Master consumed it and ever remained before the Queen Mother of the West, ascending and descending with the wind, journeying east and west. Ibid.

The *ren zhi* grows on the northern slopes of famous mountains, beneath a green cap with a white stem. Dried and consumed, a daily dose of half a *ge* grants longevity and allows one to remain underwater for long durations. Ibid.

The *yuejing zhi* grows in autumn on the southern slopes of mountains, upon stone, with a green stem and red top. Its flavor is pungent and bitter. Stored in copper vessels and consumed in the tenth month, it bestows a lifespan of ten thousand years. Ibid.

The *hei zhi* grows on the northern slopes of mountains, in great valleys, beneath a white cap with a red stem. Its flavor is sweet. Harvested in autumn and dried in the shade, a daily dose lightens the body, strengthens the teeth, and grants boundless existence with heaven and earth. Ibid.

*(Note: An entry for* huo zhi *appears above; this may be a duplication.)*
The *huo zhi* grows on the southern slopes of famous mountains. Its color is yellow and lustrous, as large as a carriage canopy. Ibid.

The *jin zhi* grows amid gold and stone, with a green cap and stem. Its flavor is sweet and pungent. Harvested in autumn and dried in the shade, when consumed, it grants a luminous body and a lifespan of ten thousand years. Ibid.

The *wan nian zhi* prevents aging and extends lifespan to nine thousand years. Ibid.

The *ye guang zhi* emerges from the northern slopes of famous mountains, in the great valleys’ headwaters amid gold and stone. Above it, floating clouds drift, its hues fivefold, with eyes like twin suns. Ibid.

The *bai yun zhi* grows on the northern slopes of famous mountains, upon white stone, veiled by white clouds, its white cap double-layered. Its flavor is pungent and sweet, with a slight bitterness. Harvested in autumn and dried in the shade, a daily dose of one *ge* averts wind and thunder, bestowing radiant complexion. Ibid.

The *yun mu zhi* grows on the northern slopes of famous mountains, its cap green and stem red. Its flavor is sweet. Gathered in late autumn with a bamboo knife and dried in the shade, when consumed, it grants a luminous body and a lifespan of a thousand years. Offered with beef jerky. Ibid.

The *ye zhi* grows on the southern slopes of famous mountains and in the headwaters of great valleys on Mount Yang. Its cap is red, its stem white, bearing two leaves and three fruits. Ibid.

*(Note: "Ye" should read "hua.")*

The *gui zhi* has a green cap and long stem. Dried in the shade and ground into powder, a daily dose of five *ge* reveals divine clarity and grants longevity. Ibid.

There are dragons of natural origin, and dragons transformed from serpents and caterpillars. *Chuxue Ji* 30, *Bai Kong Liu Tie* 95, *Taiping Yulan* 929.

Fields and lands possess natural eels, while *xing* stems, *qin* roots, and earth-dragons transform into eels. *Taiping Yulan* 937, and another passage in volume 1000 cites *qin* roots transforming into moss, likely referring to this. Ibid.

Tortoises and snakes, when dormant, absorb *qi*; in summer, they glut themselves yet grow emaciated, while in winter, burrowed in hibernation, they grow plump. *Chuxue Ji* 30.

According to the *Dui Su Pian*: Dragons, snakes, jiao, chi, ju, wei, *tuo*, and *■* can all endure winter without eating. During fasting, they grow fatter than when feeding. The meaning aligns, though the text differs entirely. This is likely a lost passage.

The *Yellow Emperor’s Medical Canon* contains a toad diagram, stating that on the second day of the waxing moon, toads begin to emerge, and one must not apply acupuncture or moxibustion to those areas. *Taiping Yulan* 4.

Liyang boasts the immortal chamber of Peng Zu, where prayers for rain are always answered. *Taiping Yulan* 11.

Zuo Yuanfang declared that Mount Jinhua is where the divine elixir may be compounded, shielding one from the perils of weapons and floods. *Taiping Yulan* 47.

On Kunlun and Penglai, the birds and beasts drink from jade wells, all attaining immortality and never dying. *Taiping Yulan* 20. (The character *quan* 泉, "spring," is omitted after "well.")

In high antiquity, there were no posthumous titles; they began only with the Zhou dynasty. The title of the Yellow Emperor was bestowed posthumously by later generations. Posthumous titles are derived from the deeds of one’s governance during life—these were not composed by the Yellow Emperor’s ministers. Common folk, who do not believe in immortals, would they ever grant the Yellow Emperor such a title? *Taiping Yulan* 562.

During the reign of Emperor Jing of Wu, a garrison commander at Jiangling, while constructing Guangling’s city walls, excavated tombs for timber. Later, they unearthed a grand tomb with double stone doors that pivoted open and shut, surrounded by a circular path wide enough for chariots—or perhaps horses. Inside, dozens of life-sized bronze figures, ten or so heads tall, wore tall crowns and robes, holding swords and standing in attendance. The spirit seat was carved from bronze, set against a stone wall behind. (The character *jun* 軍, "army," is omitted after "general.") Some said they were chamberlains, suggesting this was the tomb of a king or duke. When the coffin was broken open, a man lay within, his temples streaked with vivid gray, his face and body like that of the living. The coffin contained mica, nearly a foot thick, and thirty pairs of white jade discs cushioned the body. The bronze figures carried the corpse out and propped it against the tomb wall. A jade object, a foot long and shaped like a winter melon, fell from the corpse’s embrace. Both ears and nostrils contained gold pieces, each the size of a jujube. (The phrase *gu hai* 骨骸, "skeletal remains," should replace *ci deng* 此等.) These were artifacts that preserved the body without decay. *Taiping Yulan* 558, 805, 806, 808, 810, and 813.

Devote yourself wholeheartedly to your teacher, follow the Dao according to the method, deliver yourself and transcend the world, with lasting benefit. Serve your teacher with utmost reverence, and attaining the Dao will be your reward. Observe his moods and seize the moment. Failing to exert yourself in the teacher’s Way incurs an ineradicable sin. Study the Dao under a wise teacher, and no harm or disorder will arise. *Taiping Yulan* 659.

Dao Linzi possessed five types of *shijie* talismans; the present *Tai Xuan Yin Sheng* talisman, along with this one, enables liberation through illness. *Taiping Yulan* 664.

Xue Zhen, styled Jihe, was a native of Yandai. During the reign of King Wu of Zhou, he studied the Dao on the northern bank of the Zhongshan River. Having failed seven trials, his downfall stemmed from indulgence, dissipation, and the stagnation of his base desires. *Taiping Yulan* 666.

Guo Wenju, a native of Zhi County in Henan, entered Lushan Mountain to study the Dao. Alone, he attained a state of no emotion, where intent did not arise. Ibid.

Fan Lingzi, from youth, cherished the immortal’s Way, accumulating years in such devotion. Later, he encountered Sima Jizhu, who accompanied him into Changshan. After seven years, they entered a stone chamber where a jar inscribed with the *tuo* script was kept. Jizhu departed on a journey, earnestly cautioning him, "Beware—do not open it." Lingzi suddenly broke the seal and looked inside. Upon Jizhu’s return, he sent Lingzi away. Later, he summoned him again, entrusting him with the guardianship of a bronze coffer, once more warning him not to open it. Lingzi again broke the seal. Jizhu then dismissed him, and thus he failed to attain the Dao. Ibid.

Beneath the stone chamber, at the northeast corner, were five inscribed characters.

Feng Liang, a native of Nanyang, began his career as a county clerk in his youth. At thirty, he served as an assistant to the commandant, tasked with receiving the supervising inspector. Ashamed of his lack of ambition, he smashed his carriage, slaughtered his ox, and tore his tattered robes. Soon after, he left to study under a master, receiving instruction in the *Book of Songs*, the *Commentaries on the Classics*, the *Book of Rites*, and the *Book of Changes*, before turning to the study of Daoist arts and divination. After fifteen years of wandering, he returned home. Though the province and commandery extended honors and summons, he declined them all. By imperial edict, he was specially recommended as a worthy and virtuous candidate of the highest rank. [Text corrupt: "Ping Dao" likely should read "halfway."] He returned home. At sixty-seven, he abandoned the world, journeyed east into the mountains, and dwelled in the Deer Track Grotto.

An Qiu Wangzhi, styled Zhongdu, was a native of Changling in Jingzhao. He cultivated and revered the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. Emperor Cheng of Han [text corrupt: "from" may be an error; possible lacuna follows] held his virtue and power in high esteem, often treating him as a master. Wangzhi humbly diminished himself and withdrew. When Emperor Cheng visited him, if Wangzhi was engaged in a ritual or offering, the emperor would wait until the rites were complete before proceeding. The *Laozi Zhangju* contains the teachings of An Qiu. Suddenly stricken by grave illness, Wangzhi’s disciples, Gongsha Du [text corrupt: "with" should read "carried"] and others, placed him beneath a tree in the courtyard. Wangzhi, though gravely ill, sensed a chance of recovery. It was winter, yet he detected the fragrance of plums. Opening his eyes, he saw a pair of crimson plums hanging from a withered branch. Wangzhi raised his hand to catch them, and the plums fell into his palm. After eating them, his ailments vanished entirely, his body grew light, and his vision sharpened. He then departed, and none knew his whereabouts.

His family possessed a *Chart of the Three Sovereigns*. Before consulting it, one had to purify oneself through a hundred days of fasting. [Text corrupt: "Jun zhi" likely should read "to summon."] The chart could summon the spirits of Heaven and Earth, the Five Peaks, and the altars of soil and grain. Later, the Sage Lord commanded Qingxu Xiaoyou, the Perfected One, to compile the registers of the highest immortals, collectively titled the *Supreme Clarity* [alternate text: *Perfected and Orthodox*] *Dharma*, to subdue all demonic forces. The scripture was transmitted to others only once every hundred years, and even then, only after a period of fasting and purification. *Taiping Yulan*, 667.

Examining this passage, the subsequent quotation from the *Three Primordials’ Registers*, the *Numinous Treasure’s Red Writ of Primordial Simplicity*, and the *Jade Registers of the Great Plain* appears doubtful as a text from the *Baopuzi*, and thus it is not transcribed here.

Li A was practicing the Dao when he encountered a speeding carriage. The carriage ran over both his legs, breaking them. His disciple Gu Qiang (alternate text: Gu Bi, an error; the *Biographies of Divine Transcendents* renders it as Gu Qiang, and the *Dispelling Delusions* chapter also mentions this figure) saw this and was terrified. In an instant, Li A retrieved his severed legs and reattached them as before.

*Taiping Yulan*, 736.
This passage may be a lost fragment from the *Daoist Intentions* chapter.

Whenever Master Ge the Transcendent drank to intoxication, he would enter the pond before his gate and remain submerged the entire day before emerging. Once, while accompanying the Lord of Wu to Jingzhou, they encountered a great storm on their return, and Master Ge’s boat capsized. The Lord of Wu believed he had drowned. Shortly after, he appeared walking upon the water, his robes and shoes dry, his face flushed with wine. He explained that the day before, he had been summoned by Wu Zixu, who offered him wine, and thus he could not return immediately, causing the delay.

*Taiping Yulan*, 845.
The first three phrases differ from the *Dissolving Obstructions* chapter and are not identical to that text. The *Biographies of Divine Transcendents* also records the boat capsizing incident, though it differs from this account.

The *Wheel Star* pursues demons, the *Zhang Star* restrains souls, and the *Eastern Well* restores the spirit.

*Taiping Yulan*, 886.

The *Records of Wu Bei* state that the Eight Masters initially appeared to King An of Huainan as old men and were not granted an audience. In an instant, they all transformed into youths.

*Taiping Yulan*, 888.

Master Han Zhi treated a fifty-year-old mare with *rehmannia* and licorice, enabling it to bear three foals, and the mare lived to one hundred and thirty before dying.

*Taiping Yulan*, 897.

Southern water buffaloes lie in water year-round, regardless of winter or summer.

*Taiping Yulan*, 899.

Coat a pill of the Golden Elixir with stork’s blood, place it in one’s robe, and point at objects—whatever is spoken will transform accordingly.

*Taiping Yulan*, 925.

For the finest black sesame, steam it once in an evening as if cooking rice. Then dry it in the sun, steam it again, sift it finely, and mix with white honey to form pills the size of chicken eggs. Take two daily. After one year, the complexion becomes radiant and the body smooth; after two years, white hair turns black; after three years, fallen teeth regrow; after four years, one enters water without getting wet; after five years, one enters fire without burning; after six years, one’s running matches a galloping horse. Alternatively, mix with honey-water to make cakes resembling sugar, toast one cake, and consume it.

*Taiping Yulan*, 989.

The *Central Classic* states: *Gouwen* and *langdu* are the essence and qi of the Great Yin, governing death—thus, ingestion causes fatality.

*Taiping Yulan*, 990.

*Preface to the Inner Chapters of the Master Embracing Simplicity*

Though my physique lacks the transcendent grace—*Jin Shu* renders it "refined vigor"—and my talents lean toward the pursuit of effortless action, had I the power to spread my wings, I could soar through the ethereal clouds, or with swift strides outrun the wind and shadow. Yet I choose to fold my sturdy pinions among the flock of wrens—*Jin Shu* reads "sparrows"—and conceal my secluded path within the ranks of lame donkeys. How much less, then, when Nature endows me with wings of ordinary span and lends me feet as halting as a broken nag’s? A man who knows his limits will judge himself clearly; one who cannot will desist. How dare I, like a fly, aspire to the soaring flight of the heavens, or urge a limping turtle to chase the tracks of the flying hare? Should I adorn the ugliness of Mo Mu—*Jin Shu* has "profound ugliness"—and seek matchmakers to broadcast her praises, or push my worthless grains of sand into the goldsmith’s forge to demand a thousand taels? For a man of Jiao Yao’s stature to strive after Kuafu’s footsteps is why mediocre talent stumbles and falters—*Zang* edition reads "grieves," *Jin Shu* "hinders." For Yao Li’s frailty to force itself into the contest of lifting tripods is why the people of Qin sever their sinews. Thus, I renounce all hope of glory’s path and find my will at peace in the realm of poverty and adversity—*Jin Shu* reads "ruin." For me, coarse greens and beans hold the savor of the eight delicacies, and a thatched hut offers the joy of carved rafters. Therefore, I keep my distance from the doors of the powerful, though they lie but a footstep away, while those who know the Dao, though the road be long and arduous, will surely seek me out.

I have examined many rare books, yet most abound in cryptic language, difficult to decipher at a glance. Without the utmost precision, one cannot fathom their depths; without steadfast diligence, one cannot witness their full scope. Scholars of the Dao who are profound and broadly learned are few, while those who cut off inquiry with reckless assertions are many. Even when well-intentioned seekers wish to cultivate themselves, they are often at a loss in their haste, unsure where to begin. And when doubts arise, there is no one to consult—*Jin Shu* reads "no one sufficient to consult."

Thus, I compose this work, roughly outlining the principles of longevity. The most subtle and wondrous truths cannot be fully expressed in brush and ink. What follows is but a coarse and sketchy account, offered as a glimpse of one corner. I hope that those who ponder deeply may grasp more than half its meaning, though I do not expect the blind and unperceptive to plumb its depths or fathom its far-reaching implications. I merely discuss what must be addressed first—*original note*: "first" emended to "those who awaken first," *Jin Shu* reads "those who awaken first."

The scholars of this world cling only to the teachings of Zhou and Kong, shackled and bound until death, refusing to believe in the affairs of immortals, dismissing them as heretical and delusional. When they see this book of mine—*Jin Shu* omits "affairs" through "this" (ten characters)—they will not merely laugh uproariously—*Jin Shu* reads "not merely laugh loudly at it." They will also slander and defame what is true and correct. Therefore, I do not include it among the other works I have written—*original note*: "world" was erroneously repeated here; now corrected—and instead compile this separate volume, titled *Inner Chapters*, comprising twenty scrolls. These stand apart from the *Outer Chapters*, each with its own sequence—*Jin Shu* reads: "Thus, among my writings, those on the arts of alchemy are titled *Inner Chapters*, while the rest, comprising refutations and explications, are titled *Outer Chapters*, totaling one hundred and sixteen pieces in all." This is likely a later emendation by historians.

Though this work may not be worthy of concealment in the stone chambers of famous mountains—*Jin Shu* reads "conceal them in"—I shall at least seal it in a golden casket to show to those who recognize its value—*Jin Shu* ends here, omitting the following thirty-one characters. As for those with whom I cannot speak, I shall not let them see it. My aim is to provide future seekers of longevity with a means to dispel their confusion; I do not seek the belief of those who refuse to believe.

Respectfully inscribed.
*—Zang* edition attributes this to "Ge Hong of Zhichuan, respectfully inscribed." A later addition.

---

*Postscript to the Outer Chapters of the Master Embracing Simplicity*

The Master Embracing Simplicity is surnamed Ge, named Hong, styled Zhichuan, a native of Jurong in Danyang. His ancestors were the Ge Tian clan, rulers of antiquity who once held the realm. Later, they descended to become lords of various states and thus took Ge as their surname.

Hong’s great-grandfather served as Inspector of Jingzhou. When Wang Mang usurped the throne, my ancestor scorned to serve a traitorous regime, resigned his post, and returned home. Together with Zhai Yi, the Grand Administrator of Dongjun, he raised an army to punish Wang Mang but was defeated. Pardoned and spared from disaster, he feigned illness and withdrew from the world. Wang Mang, wary of the clan’s strength, feared future rebellion and exiled my ancestor to Langye.

My ancestor’s son, Ge Pu, raised troops to aid Emperor Guangwu and rendered great service. When Guangwu ascended the throne, he appointed Pu as General of Chariots and Cavalry, later promoting him to General of Agile Cavalry. He was enfeoffed as Marquis of Tong County in Xiapi, with a fief of five thousand households. In the early years of the dynasty, the marquis’s younger brother, Ge Wen, followed him on campaigns and achieved many victories. The marquis repeatedly memorialized the throne to plead Wen’s merits, but the court, citing Wen’s private service under his elder brother without official military rank, refused to acknowledge his contributions. The marquis declared: "My brother and I faced arrows and stones together; our bodies are covered in scars, and he lost his right eye. Yet he receives not even the slightest reward, while I am laden with gold and purple. How can I find peace in this?" He then submitted a personal petition, requesting that his own enfeoffment be transferred to his brother. The memorial reached the emperor, who, wishing to honor the marquis’s noble integrity, granted the request. Wen, unable to decline, accepted the title. He immediately established a residence for the General of Agile Cavalry in Bowang Ward, where the foundations and stone pillars remain to this day. He also divided his income to provide for the officers and soldiers, granting them the same provisions as the two lords. The General of Agile Cavalry urged him to stop, but he refused.

The General of Agile Cavalry said, "This only burdens the people of the state further—how can it be called yielding?" He then feigned other business and crossed the Yangtze to the south, making his home in Jurong. His sons and younger brothers tilled the land themselves, finding joy in the classics. Wen repeatedly sent envoys to welcome the general back, but he never returned. He also ordered men to guard the residence in Bowang Ward, hoping the general might one day return, yet for generations no one lived there.

Hong’s grandfather was learned in all fields, delving deeply into the subtle and profound. His literary and artistic attainments were unmatched in his time, possessing the talent to govern the state. He served Wu, holding office as magistrate of the three counties of Haiyan, Lin’an, and Shanyin, then as Gentleman of the Palace, Palace Attendant, Censor-in-Chief, Governor of Luling, Minister of Personnel, Junior Tutor to the Crown Prince, President of the Secretariat, Grand Master of Ceremonies, Palace Attendant, Chamberlain for Attendants, and General Who Assists Wu, enfeoffed as Marquis of Wushou County.

Hong’s father was known for his filial piety and fraternal love, serving as a model for scholars. He exhaustively studied all that was recorded in the annals. He served Wu as Gentleman of the Five Offices and Chief Examiner, then as magistrate of the two counties of Jiancheng and Nanchang, Gentleman of the Secretariat, Chief Judge of the Court of Judicial Review, Central Protector, and was appointed Governor of Kuaiji but had not yet assumed office when the Jin armies descended the river and the western frontier fell.

Possessing both civil and martial talent, he was universally acclaimed by court and commoner alike. He was thus transferred to command the five commanderies in response to the emergency. The Grand Commander provided him with five thousand personal troops to lead the expeditionary forces and defend the border. Yet what Heaven destroys, man cannot sustain. Thus the lord submitted in reverence, and the Nine Provinces became as one in allegiance.

Hong, by virtue of his former office, was appointed Gentleman, then gradually promoted to Grand Master of Splendid Happiness, serving as Chief Examiner and magistrate of Feixiang County. The county had twenty thousand households and was the most well-governed in the province. His virtuous transformation was extraordinary: kindness pervaded, punishments were just, the countryside resounded with praise, the roads were free of wrongdoing. He did not cultivate public fields, yet trade across borders flourished like a market. Gifts as small as autumn hairs never entered his gate; even paper and brushes were supplied from his own wealth. Punishments were set aside, yet order prevailed; he spoke not, yet transformation spread. Due to illness, he resigned his post. Later, an imperial edict appointed him Chamberlain to the King of Wu. He maintained a stern demeanor, correcting errors, promoting the worthy, impeaching the crooked, and bringing solemn harmony to military and state affairs. He was transferred to serve as Governor of Shaoling and died in office.

Hong was his third son. Born late, he was indulged by his parents and not early disciplined in books and history. At thirteen, he lost his kind father, deprived prematurely of paternal instruction. Stricken by hunger and hardship, he personally undertook farming, working under the stars and treading the grass, toiling diligently in the fields. He repeatedly suffered the ravages of war and fire, and his family’s books were utterly destroyed. In the intervals between farming, he had nothing to read. He carried his satchel and walked to borrow books, yet often obtained them from only one household, rarely acquiring complete works. To afford paper and brushes, he daily cut and sold firewood. Wherever he cultivated fields or gardens, he wrote by firelight. For these reasons, he could not early engage with the arts and literature. Often lacking paper, he wrote on both sides of the sheets, making his texts scarcely readable.

At sixteen, he began studying the *Classic of Filial Piety*, the *Analects*, the *Book of Songs*, and the *Book of Changes*. Poverty prevented him from seeking distant teachers and friends; isolated and poorly informed, his understanding was shallow, his thoughts limited, and he grasped little of the great principles. Yet he greedily sought to broaden his reading. Of the many books he perused—from the orthodox classics and various histories to the words of the Hundred Schools, down to short and miscellaneous writings—nearly ten thousand scrolls, there were none he did not memorize and master. However, his nature was dull and forgetful, and with little literary training, his will was not focused. What he retained was meager, and he was not free from confusion. Yet when writing, he could still draw upon these works, though he never became a pure Confucian or a master fit to transmit teachings.

He glanced at the *River Chart* and *Luo Writing* apocrypha but did not linger over them. He disliked books on astrology, calculation, the Nine Palaces, the three board games, *Taiyi*, and flying tally divination, never engaging with them, finding them burdensome and uninteresting. Later, he studied methods of wind angles, cloud-gazing, the Three Origins, *Dunjia*, *Liuren*, and *Taiyi*, gaining a rough understanding of their principles but never delving deeply. He considered such arts generally employed for others’ purposes, arising from human desires, and saw no urgent need to labor over them. He deemed it better to focus on the beneficial study of the masters’ books, and so abandoned them again.

According to the *Separate Record of Arts and Letters*, there were 13,299 scrolls in total. Since the Wei era, the corpus of writings had doubled compared to earlier times, making him realize how much he had not seen. Books from the Jiangnan region were incomplete and scattered. Once, he had intended to journey to the capital to seek rare and extraordinary texts, but just as he set out, great chaos erupted. He turned back halfway, often lamenting this.

Now nearing forty, his long-held aspirations have waned. He practices only reduction upon reduction, striving for non-action, dwelling in marshes and wilds merely to preserve his life. His broad learning has thus daily declined.

As for Hong’s character, (text missing) he was dull and rustic, slow of speech and inarticulate. His appearance was ugly, yet he never sought to embellish or boast. His cap and shoes were filthy and tattered, his clothes often ragged, yet he was not ashamed. The fashions and customs of the world changed swiftly and repeatedly.

At times, his robes would hang loosely with a broad sash, or his sleeves would be long and flowing; at others, his garments would be so short they barely covered his feet, or his skirts would drag upon the ground. Yet Hong remained steadfast in constancy, refusing to follow the shifting tides of the world. His words were always plain and truthful, free of jest or mockery. When he found no kindred spirit, he would sit in silence all day. Thus, the people of his homeland all called him a man who embraced simplicity, and so he took this as his sobriquet when writing his books.

By nature, Hong was frail and sickly, afflicted with many illnesses. Poor and without carriage or horse, he could not bear to walk, nor did he enjoy it. Moreover, he despised the corrupt customs of the age—its abandonment of fundamentals for trivialities, its excessive socializing. Thus, he took up his brush in seclusion, guarding stillness behind his wattle gate, with no place to rush or follow. Even those in power and privilege, though they lived nearby, scarcely knew him.

His clothes could not ward off the cold, his roof could not keep out the rain, his food could not fill his stomach, and his name never left his door—yet he felt no distress. Without servants, his fences collapsed, brambles choked his courtyard, and weeds overran his eaves and steps. To go out, he had to push through thorns; to enter, he had to part the grass. Some critics thought his mind soared far while neglecting what was near, and that he bore his lack of help with resentment. But he did not understand the ways of seeking favor...

Thus, he never paid formal visits to officials or superiors. When it came to attending grand funerals or visiting the sick and distressed, he would steel himself to go, determined to ensure no duty went unfulfilled. Yet when illness struck or his strength waned, he often fell short, and critics would reproach him for it. He would acknowledge his fault without distress. If his intentions held no excess but his body betrayed his will, he felt no shame before himself—why then should he expect others to understand?

Only those of discerning judgment could forgive his steadfast embrace of simplicity, not as a means to cultivate loftiness. Most people admired the warmth of close ties and prized the intimacy of hidden chambers, but he believed knowing others was no easy task—even sages found it difficult. Superficial bonds, where words aligned but minds diverged, brought no benefit, only harm. Though he could not sever all ties as Zhu Gongshu had, he insisted on clarity and thorough understanding before investing his trust.

For this, he was widely despised yet never altered his ways. Those who chased advancement and bowed at the gates of power despised his difference from themselves, slandering him as a hollow man who scorned the world. But his conduct was rooted in conviction; praise and blame alike were as if unheard.

Some, when afflicted by those near to them, would flaunt their strengths while belittling others’ weaknesses. Though he stood at the humble end of the scholar’s path, when speaking with others, he always gauged their understanding and discussed only what they knew, never forcing them toward what lay beyond their grasp. When debating with learned men, he would lay out the essentials. If they struggled to grasp the meaning, he would merely outline the general intent to prompt their awakening, avoiding exhaustive reasoning that might leave them unable to respond. Those who pondered deeply would often realize the truth on their own.

When he deemed a matter unworthy of discussion, even if pressed, he would plead ignorance to avoid the burden of explanation. By nature, he deeply disliked imposing on officials or superiors. From youth to old age, he had quietly aided several friends in distress, speaking to those in power only when unavoidable. Yet those he helped never knew of his efforts—he could not bear to see them wronged and acted in secret. For others, even close acquaintances who held office or power but shared no bond with him, he never burdened them with so much as a word or half a character.

When his own supplies ran low and medicine was urgently needed, he would turn to friends and accept their help without hesitation. Any assistance he received, he repaid gradually over time, ensuring the giver never noticed. He accepted gifts only from those of like mind, never indiscriminately.

If his food stores lasted ten days, he would share them to relieve others’ want. But if his own needs were barely met, he would not deprive himself. He did not cultivate ostentatious minor virtues nor pursue meticulous petty integrity. When villagers—ordinary, decent folk—occasionally brought wine and food to visit, he would not refuse, even if they were not his equals. Later, if he repaid them, it was never immediately.

He once remarked that the historian’s claim that one should not eat with brothers, or Huasheng’s insistence on purity with close friends, were but pretenses to seek fame—far from the magnanimity of true statesmen. He particularly despised those without principle who neglected the fundamentals of farming and sericulture to covet ill-gotten gains. Those who controlled local opinion sold offices for bribes. Those with power exploited their positions to extort wealth. Some took bribes from criminals, others wronged innocent households, or harbored fugitives and sheltered outlaws. Some conscripted laborers to obstruct public service, or hoarded goods to inflate prices. Some monopolized markets to seize profits from common folk, or seized others’ fields to plunder the weak. They deceived officials to reap illicit gains, flaunting their spoils before wives and concubines while seeking fame and rank. With such people, he refused all association. Thus, the vulgar despised him for despising them, and naturally kept their distance.

His lane saw no carriage tracks, his hall no guests of differing views. His courtyard could have been set with bird snares, his tables and mats thick with dust. From the time he gained understanding until old age, he never spoke of others’ faults or pried into their private affairs—it came naturally. Even with servants, he never mocked their shortcomings or shameful acts. He never judged or ranked people’s merits, nor did he delight in criticizing others’ likes and dislikes. If pressed by superiors, he would speak only of the finest qualities in those he discussed. When commenting on writing, he would highlight its strengths without dwelling on its flaws. Thus, he incurred no resentment from praise or blame.

When powerful men occasionally asked about officials or commoners, he would praise the capable and virtuous for their admirable traits. For the greedy, violent, or incompetent, he would claim ignorance. For this, he was often reproached for being overly cautious, unable to clearly distinguish right from wrong. Yet he never dared change his ways.

Seeing how people in the world delight in discussing others, comparing them to this or that figure, their judgments are not necessarily fair, yet their praise or blame, their approval or disapproval, often miss the proper standard. Those who receive praise take it as their due, though their virtue may not truly warrant it; those who suffer criticism come to hate it to the bone, more bitterly than blood feuds. This only deepened Hong’s caution, and so he no longer spoke of scholars or gentlemen at all. Even when it came to his own clan and disciples, he would leave their appraisal to the community, refusing to weigh or judge their worth. Some mocked him for this. Hong replied: “I am a person—one who belongs to myself. By right, I should know myself best. If someone were to ask me to compare myself to the ancients or to my contemporaries, to place myself among them, I truly would not know with whom I could be matched. How, then, could I presume to assess those who are not myself?

In the corrupt customs of the late Han, cliques divided into factions. Men like Xu Zijiang used their tongues to gain influence, turning debates and disputes into feuds between clans. Thus, the scholars of Runan had no fixed reputations, only the monthly critiques of ‘moonlit judgments.’ Even Emperor Wu of Wei deeply despised this, seeking to take their heads—so they scattered and fled, nearly to the point of annihilation. The lesson is not far off; one may well take it as a teacher.

Moreover, people are not easily known. Even fathers and elder brothers may not fully understand their sons and younger brothers. If someone agrees with me, does that immediately make them right? If they differ from me, does that immediately make them wrong?”

Some begin but do not finish. Even Yao, the Duke of Zhou, Confucius, and Jizha all harbored regrets over what they could not fully attain. How could one, being but a common man, trust in the flickering light of his own dim insights to lightly judge the affairs of others? Are these not the very heights of sagely wisdom and great virtue?

During the Tai’an era, when Shi Bing rebelled, six provinces fell into chaos, their governance overturned and their people turned to treachery. The Grand Marshal of the Righteous Army, Yao Hong, was repeatedly pressed into service as a military commander. Fearing for his homeland and dreading the depth of the calamity, he recalled the ancient principle of acting swiftly in times of crisis. Yet he also feared military discipline and dared not follow his own inclinations. Thus, he recruited several hundred men and advanced with the allied forces. When they attacked a separate rebel commander and routed him, treasures piled like mountains and jewels covered the ground. The other armies all abandoned their ranks to plunder, their carts laden with spoils. Hong alone ordered his troops not to stray from their formations. Any soldier who gathered loot was immediately executed as an example. Thus, none dared lay down their weapons. Soon, hundreds of hidden rebels ambushed the other armies. Those forces, now disordered, their men and horses burdened with plunder and devoid of fighting spirit, fell into panic. The dead and wounded lay scattered, nearly beyond recovery. Only Hong’s army remained in strict formation, their carts aligned, suffering no losses. They rescued the other armies from total collapse, and Hong’s role was decisive. Later, in another battle, he slew a minor rebel chieftain, capturing many armored troops and heads, which he presented to the command tent. The Grand Marshal then appointed Hong as General of the Vanquishing Waves, granting him a hundred bolts of cloth. Many generals hoarded such rewards or sent them home, but Hong distributed his among his officers and soldiers, as well as to impoverished acquaintances. With the remaining ten bolts, he bought meat and wine to feast his officers and clerks. For a time, this became the talk of the day.

When the rebellion was quelled, Hong cast aside his weapons and armor and journeyed directly to Luoyang, seeking rare books. He made no mention of his military achievements, secretly admiring the principle of Lu Zhonglian, who refused the gold of Liaocheng, and Wu Zixu, who declined the rewards for restoring Chu—those who succeed yet do not remain. Just then, the capital fell into great turmoil, the northern roads were blocked, and Chen Min rebelled in Jiangdong, cutting off the route home. Coincidentally, an old friend, Ji Jundao of Qiao Commandery (originally written as *Judao*, amended following the *Yilin* and *Book of Jin*), was appointed Inspector of Guangzhou. He memorialized to have Hong serve as his aide. Though this was not Hong’s preference, the south offered a refuge, so he reluctantly accepted. Sent ahead to muster troops, he waited in Guangzhou when Jundao was later killed en route. There, he was repeatedly invited by regional commanders to serve, but declined each time.

He reflected that wealth and rank could be gradually attained but not suddenly grasped, and even their minor details were enough to weary a man. Moreover, glory, position, and profit were like lodgings for a traveler—neither permanent nor something one could cling to. What rises must fall, what blazes must fade, like spring blossoms that wither in an instant. Why rejoice in gaining them or grieve in losing them? Regrets and anxieties, fears and struggles—endless and unspeakable—were not worth pursuing. Assessing his own nature as deeply indolent and his talents severely limited, he knew that even with bowed shoulders and hurried steps, he could not achieve fame or avoid trouble, let alone without such efforts. Better to cultivate the Dao of Master Red Pine and Wang Qiao, which lay within himself and did not depend on others.

He resolved to ascend famous mountains, practice dietary regimens, and nurture his nature—no neglect was involved. If one could not simultaneously aid the world, how could one cultivate tranquility without abandoning worldly affairs? And true understanding was indeed difficult; one could not begrudge inquiry or discussion with others. Thus, his carriage and horse never passed through the domains of the powerful. Not a single word of his writings was exchanged with those in office. Though he could not entirely withdraw from the scholar’s world, he could not refuse those who sought him out, and their visits disrupted his focus. He sighed: even in the mountains and forests, the Dao was absent. The ancients who cultivated the Dao entered the mountains and forests precisely to distance themselves from clamor and prevent their minds from being disturbed. Now, he would fulfill his original aspiration: leaving his homeland, he would journey to Mount Song to follow the path of Fangping and Liang Gong.

He had already devoted effort to compiling his philosophical works, the *Inner* and *Outer Chapters*, and now arranged them to present to posterity. At fifteen or sixteen, he had written poems, rhapsodies, and miscellaneous essays, believing they might circulate in his time (the words *in his time* were originally omitted, supplied from the *Yilin*). By his twentieth year, upon further reflection, he found much of it unsatisfactory. His talent had not necessarily improved; rather, his reading had broadened, and he now discerned the difference between beauty and ugliness. Thus, he discarded much of his early work, keeping less than one in ten. Apart from his philosophical writings, he still had over a hundred scrolls of miscellaneous works, though they had not yet been fully revised. Many were written in sorrow and indignation, and he lacked the leisure to review them carefully. Others, upon finishing a piece, would immediately declare it satisfying. But Hong’s talent was dull and his thoughts slow; he could not do the same. Each time he revised a character in his writing, it improved, but his laziness and the volume of his work prevented frequent review.

In his twenties, he had calculated that writing minor, trivial pieces wasted time better spent establishing a school of thought. Thus, he began drafting his philosophical works. Amidst the chaos of war, he wandered and was displaced, losing some writings. For over a decade on the road, he did not take up his brush. Only in the Jianwu era did he settle, completing twenty scrolls of *Inner Chapters*, fifty scrolls of *Outer Chapters*, a hundred scrolls of stele inscriptions, eulogies, poems, and rhapsodies, and thirty scrolls of military dispatches, proclamations, memorials, letters, and notes.

He also compiled *Biographies of Divine Transmigrants* in ten scrolls, drawing from sources not included in common records—here following the old manuscript, where "藏本" originally had "上." He further composed *Biographies of Recluses* in ten scrolls for those who never entered official service. Additionally, he excerpted essential passages from the Five Classics, the Seven Histories, and the works of the Hundred Schools, along with military affairs, technical arts, and miscellaneous curiosities, totaling three hundred and ten scrolls, each with its own catalog.

The *Inner Chapters* discuss matters of divine immortals, alchemical recipes, ghosts and transformations, techniques for nourishing life and prolonging years, and methods for warding off misfortune and averting calamity, belonging to the Daoist tradition. The *Outer Chapters* address the gains and losses of ordinary life, the right and wrong of worldly affairs, and pertain to the Confucian school.

When Hong read Emperor Wen of Wei’s *Dianlun* (here corrected from "典目" in the *Zang* edition to follow the old manuscript) and its self-narrative (here corrected from "未" in the *Zang* edition), which included accounts of *danqi* (a board game), swordplay, and other pursuits, he considered briefly recounting his own skills. Yet in truth, he had few talents and could not boast idly. Now, he would fully disclose what he did not excel in.

Hong’s nature was dull and his temperament sluggish, with few hobbies. From childhood (here, "总发垂髫" refers to youth), he lagged behind his peers in tossing tiles or wrestling. He never engaged in cockfighting, goose-racing, or horse-chasing. When others played board games, he paid no attention; even if dragged to watch, he remained indifferent, as if dozing in daylight. To this day, he does not know how many lines are on a *weiqi* board or the names of *chupu* pieces. He deemed such trivial pastimes distractions that wasted time and energy—harming governance for officials, disrupting study for scholars, distracting farmers from their labor, and causing merchants to lose their wealth. Before a game’s outcome was decided, players quarreled in the streets, hearts burning with agitation, faces twisted in distress. What was called "amusement" was in fact torment. It eroded integrity, sparked conflict, led to the exchange of bribes, and sowed hidden grudges. In ancient times, Duke Min of Song and the Wu crown prince brought about disasters of shattered skulls, rebellions, and the annihilation of seven states, nearly toppling the imperial court—a warning clear for a hundred generations. Whenever Hong observed such games, he witnessed shame and fury intertwined, fists flying, insults hurled, and friendships severed. Grievances, whether great or small, often invited regret; such pursuits were not worth the trouble.

Confucius once cautioned against "daytime napping," but compared to Hong, even that was preferable. Napping, at least, brought no harm, nor did it risk resentment or legal disputes. The sage still wore out bamboo slips thrice over in his devotion to study—how could an ordinary man of modest talent hope to master everything? All such games paled beside the value of even a foot of written text. Thus, Hong, who had no fondness for them, never partook, though this set him apart from common folk.

In his youth, he once learned archery, but his strength was insufficient to draw a powerful bow—only something like Yan Gao’s. He believed archery was among the Six Arts and could be used to repel bandits, resist raids, and hunt birds and beasts, so he practiced it. While in military service, he once shot pursuing cavalry, felling them with his arrows, killing two enemies and one horse, thereby escaping death. He also trained in the saber and shield, as well as single and double halberds, mastering oral formulas and essential techniques for subduing opponents. There were even secret methods, their skill bordering on the divine. When facing an untrained opponent, he could prevail decisively, unstoppable in any direction.

Later, he learned the seven-foot staff technique, which could be used to penetrate blades and seize halberds. Yet these were merely peripheral skills of no urgent necessity—knowing them was like possessing the horn of a qilin or the spur of a phoenix: what use were they? (Here, the *Zang* edition omits "过," which has been restored from the old manuscript.) Such abilities, once acquired, were rarely needed.

From a young age, Hong had a steadfast resolve never to enter officialdom. Whenever he read the biographies of Chao Fu, Xu You, the recluse of Beizishan, the two Jiangs of Shihu, Yuan Fa, and Longzi, he would set down his book and lean forward in admiration, emulating their character. He aspired to refine his mastery of the Five Classics and compose a philosophical work to ensure posterity would recognize him as a scholar of letters. Later, when the provincial authorities and the General of Chariots and Cavalry summoned him, he declined all offers.

His name was recommended to the Prime Minister’s office of the Prince of Langye. When the righteous troops first rose, after the rebels were pacified, he made no effort to claim merit. He went to the office to discuss his contributions, yet those in charge held no hope of reward. The King of Jin, responding to Heaven and aligning with the people, restored order from chaos, revived the nearly severed imperial lineage, and renewed the abandoned sacrifices of the ancestral temples. Mindful of the unfulfilled rewards of the former dynasty, he sought to encourage future service by bestowing recognition. Hong followed the general precedent. In the *gengyin* year, an edict granted him the title of Marquis of Guanzhong, with a fief of two hundred households in Jurong. (Here, the *Zang* edition reads "edict" as "imperial decree," which has been emended following the old manuscript.)

Though he had joined the campaign to save his homeland, his efforts were deemed unworthy of record. The conferral of gold and purple seals was not his original aspiration. He had hoped to emulate Lu Lian from afar and follow Tian Chou’s example nearby, submitting a memorial to firmly decline the honor and thus fulfill his modest ambition. However, a general decree allowed no exceptions, and his request was denied. In ancient times, Zhong You declined a reward he was due to receive, yet this discouraged others from doing good. With the barbarians not yet subdued and the realm still troubled, the state sought to clarify rewards and punishments to uphold its laws. How could a humble man like himself cling to his petty, timid resolve and resist the grand, all-encompassing system? Thus, he set aside his objections and respectfully accepted the imperial command.

Hong later composed an autobiographical essay. Some questioned him, saying: "In the past, Wang Chong, in his sixties, saw his path blocked and his hopes dashed. Fearing his name would perish with his body, he wrote an account of his life’s end. Yet you, in the prime of your years, live in an era of the Dao’s flourishing. Soon you will be summoned with the gifts of *jiebai* silk, welcomed in a carriage of rushes, your brilliance illuminating the Nine Five, your renown surpassing that of Kunwu. Why lament that your fragrance remains unspread, and instead busy yourself with the concerns of an old scholar?"

Hong replied: "Heaven and Earth stretch boundlessly, yet man dwells as a transient guest. The morning mushroom’s radiance fades before the day is done; the spring blossom’s glory withers within ten days. Even the swiftest winds or fleeting lightning may not outpace their end. A lifespan of a hundred years is but a shooting star’s trail of smoke; gray hairs pass like an arrow through a gap. How much more so for those who perish before their time, like leaves falling before autumn? Thus, Xiangzi lamented the unharvested grain, and Yang Wu grieved his early death. Surveying the worthies of antiquity, some soared like dragons through literary arts, others crouched like tigers through martial deeds. Their great merits were recorded in the archives of alliances, their virtuous reputations echoed in pipes and strings. Though their bodies may have crumbled into dust, their praises drift on the wind, enduring through the ages. Even after a thousand generations, their memory remains revered.

I, however, am mediocre and obscure, lingering in obscurity. My talents do not suit the times, my conduct runs counter to the world. My voice rings discordant with the common crowd, my steps tread paths at odds with others. Within, I lack the patronage of the Jin and Zhang clans; without, I have no friends to brush off their caps in anticipation. Though the road ahead is smooth, my feet are not those of a *qilin*; though the six directions stretch wide, my wings are not those of the great Peng. Above, I cannot soar like an eagle to aid the state; below, I have no means to glorify my parents or leave a name behind. My virtues will not be entrusted to a worthy historian, nor will my fame be inscribed on bells and tripods. Thus, in the margins of my writings, I composed this autobiographical essay. Though it may do nothing to alter my fate, I rely on future generations to preserve my words."

---

**Postscript to the Collated and Published *Baopuzi Inner Chapters***

The宗旨 of the Daoist school lies in purity, stillness, and emptiness. Its distortions may lead to scheming or licentiousness, but it originally had nothing to do with alchemical elixirs, transmutation of metals, *qi*-absorption, breath circulation, incantations, talismans, or other such arts. The fangshi of the Qin and Han dynasties never associated themselves with Laozi. Even when they invoked the Yellow Emperor, it was not in the sense of Daoist teachings. The *Yiwen Zhi* of the *Hanshu* classifies the Yellow Emperor’s writings under Daoism and immortality, following the *Qilüe*, which in turn derived from the *Bielu*. Liu Xin, though well-versed in the *Hongbao* and a firm believer in immortality, still separated the techniques of the fangshi from the various masters when cataloging the imperial archives—they were not conflated.

By the late Eastern Han, Emperor Huan favored immortality and worshipped Laozi. Zhang Heng, son of Zhang Ling, appointed men as *jijiu* to oversee the study of the *Laozi’s Five Thousand Characters*. The attachment of immortality practices to Daoism truly began here. The *Inner Chapters* of the *Baopuzi* represent the teachings of the ancient immortality school. Though Ge Hong classified the *Inner Chapters* as Daoist, the scriptures and talismans he cites number 282, with no mention of the Daoist masters. He even states that Laozi’s discussions are too broad and vague, while Zhuangzi, Wenzi, Guan Yin, and their followers, though followers of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, never reached the profound truths of immortality, being distant by a thousand *li*. Examining its purport, it is clearly distinct from Daoism.

Moreover, later generations of immortality seekers revered Wei Boyang as their orthodox founder. This work merely mentions the title of Boyang’s *Inner Chapters* without a single word of praise, noting only in the *Shenxian Zhuan* that the *Cantong Qi* uses hexagrams to explain the principles of elixir-making. Thus, Ge Hong’s teachings differ not only from Daoism but also share scarcely any common ground with later immortality schools.

I have long held that the immortality arts of the Han originally diverged from the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. By the Wei and Jin dynasties, metaphysical discourse flourished, and classical studies branched in many directions. Daoism claimed kinship with Confucianism, while immortality practices became entangled with Daoism. Yet they merely borrowed its name without altering its substance. By the Song and Yuan dynasties, the *Cantong Qi*’s furnace and fire were reinterpreted as internal alchemy, refining and nurturing yin and yang, merging primordial *qi*. External alchemy, breath cultivation, and embryonic breathing were dismissed as lesser paths; metal transmutation, talismans, and incantations as heterodox practices; and the *huangbai xuansu* arts as perverse techniques. Only the dual cultivation of nature and life was deemed the secret to preserving the Valley Spirit, attaining immortality, and ascending as a transcendent. This doctrine drew from Chan Buddhism, incorporated *Yijing* principles, and pursued subtleties and wonders, even seeking to unify Confucianism, Buddhism, and Daoism.

From this point onward, the Han and Jin transmissions of the immortals’ teachings were utterly transformed, their names and realities thoroughly confounded. Yet the works of Master Ge clung to the transmitted teachings, making no claim to wondrous insight—much as Confucians expound the classics, so too did the immortals’ school preserve its Han learning.

Sun Boyuan, the Salt Commissioner, was deeply devoted to ancient learning and synthesized the Nine Schools to produce a corrected edition of the *Baopuzi*. Finding the Ming block-print and Tianyi Pavilion manuscript rife with errors and omissions, he collated them by hand and further entrusted me and Gu Jian● to compare our private copies with other texts, revising the work through multiple rounds of proofreading. Boyuan compiled the prefaces and catalog, intending to publish it.
