In response, I have here set forth the historical divergence and convergence of the immortals’ teachings and the Daoist school, arranging my account as a preface for the reader’s consideration.

Composed on the *jiayin* day of the seventh month, the seventeenth year of the Jiaqing reign [1812], by Fang Weidian of Tongcheng.

**Note:** The Ming block-print of the *Baopuzi* appends a separate treatise, *Biezhi*, after the Inner Chapters, exclusively discussing breath circulation and physical exercises. This neither accords with the original intent of the Inner Chapters nor matches the style of Jin-era writing. Consulting Ge Zhichuan’s own preface, no such work is mentioned. Neither the Sui nor Tang bibliographies record it. Only the *Song History: Arts and Letters* lists *Baopuzi Biezhi* in two fascicles under Daoism, with a note stating the author is unknown—it does not attribute it to Zhichuan. This late-appearing text is wholly unreliable. Moreover, the current version, at over five hundred and sixty characters, does not even fill a single fascicle and is not the Song or Yuan original. Thus, it has been excised and will not be appended to the end of the work.

**Postscript by Weidian.**

---

### *Baopuzi* Inner Chapters Catalog
1. *Changxuan* (Expounding the Mystery)
2. *Lunxian* (On Immortals)
3. *Duisu* (Answering the Common)
4. *Jindan* (The Golden Elixir)
5. *Zhili* (Ultimate Principles)
6. *Weizhi* (Subtle Pointers)
7. *Sainan* (Resolving Difficulties)
8. *Shizhi* (Dispelling Doubts)
9. *Daoyi* (The Meaning of the Dao)
10. *Mingben* (Illuminating the Root)
11. *Xianyao* (Immortal Medicines)
12. *Bianwen* (Discerning Questions)
13. *Jiyan* (Utmost Words)
14. *Qinqiu* (Diligent Seeking)
15. *Zaying* (Miscellaneous Responses)
16. *Huangbai* (The Yellow and White)
17. *Dengshe* (Ascending and Crossing)
18. *Dizhen* (Earthly Perfection)
19. *Xialan* (Distant Perusals)
20. *Quhuo* (Dispelling Delusions)

The above catalog follows the Daoist Canon edition.

**Note:** The *Baopuzi* Inner Chapters preface states, “This separate work is titled *Inner Chapters*, comprising twenty fascicles, with its own sequence distinct from the Outer Chapters.” The Outer Chapters’ preface further clarifies, “The Inner Chapters total twenty fascicles, the Outer Chapters fifty fascicles,” and specifies, “The Inner Chapters discuss immortals, elixirs, ghosts, transformations, life-nurturing, longevity, warding off misfortune, and exorcising calamities—all pertaining to Daoism. The Outer Chapters address worldly gains and losses, the right and wrong of affairs—pertaining to Confucianism.” The *Sui History: Bibliography* likewise classifies the Inner Chapters under Daoism, separate from the Outer Chapters. Though the Daoist Canon includes both, they were never originally combined. This is evident from the insertion of *Baopuzi Biezhi* between the Inner and Outer Chapters. Ming scholars, extracting the text from the Daoist Canon without recognizing its three distinct works, erroneously titled the whole *Baopuzi*. This edition corrects that error by publishing only the twenty-fascicle Inner Chapters, restoring Ge’s original arrangement.

The *Old Tang History: Bibliography* and other catalogs all list twenty fascicles. The *Sui History* records twenty-one fascicles plus one of phonetic glosses, likely including the preface and catalog as twenty-two. The phonetic glosses are long lost. The Daoist Canon places the preface before the first fascicle, hence its omission from the fascicle count.

**Query:** If *Biezhi* was a separate work, why does it not appear in Ge’s preface? The Daoist Canon also includes *Baopuzi Yangsheng Lun*, *Zhichuan Zhenren Jiaozheng Shu* (one fascicle), *Baopuzi Shenxian Jinju Jing* (three fascicles), and *Ge Zhichuan Jinmu Wanling Lun*—none of which appear in Ge’s preface. Thus, *Biezhi* follows the same pattern and is clearly not by Zhichuan.

**Preface to the Collated *Baopuzi* Inner Chapters by Sun Xingyan, the Five Pines Recluse, tenth month of the sixteenth year of the Jiaqing reign [1811].**

The essence of Daoism lies in purity, tranquility, and emptiness. Its corruptions may devolve into scheming or licentiousness, but it originally had no truck with golden elixirs, immortal medicines, the Yellow and White arts, breath circulation, physical exercises, incantations, or talismans. The fangshi of Qin and Han never invoked Laozi. Even when they invoked the Yellow Emperor, it was not Daoist doctrine. The *Han History: Arts and Letters* divides the Yellow Emperor’s works between Daoism and the immortals, following the *Seven Summaries*, which in turn derived from the *Separate Records*. Liu Xin, though versed in the *Hongbao* scriptures and a devout believer in immortals, still classified esoteric arts separately from the philosophers when overseeing the imperial library—they were not conflated.

By the late Eastern Han, Emperor Huan favored immortals and sacrificed to Laozi. Zhang Heng, son of Zhang Ling, appointed men as *jijiu* to master the *Laozi’s Five Thousand Characters*. The immortals’ teachings only then began to attach themselves to Daoism. The *Baopuzi* Inner Chapters preserve the ancient immortals’ doctrines. Though Ge claimed them for Daoism, the 282 immortal scriptures and divine talismans he cited include no Daoist masters. He dismissed Laozi’s teachings as too general, and Zhuangzi, Wenzi, Guan Yin, and Xi as mere followers of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, “millions of leagues” from the immortals’ truths. His aims diverge entirely from Daoism. Later immortality seekers revered Wei Boyang as their orthodox lineage, yet this work only mentions the *Cantong Qi* by name without a single citation, merely noting in the *Shenxian Zhuan* that the *Cantong Qi* used hexagrams to explain elixir-making.

The learning of Zichuan (Ge Hong) not only differs from Daoism but also shares scarcely any common ground with the later immortals’ tradition. I have often observed that the immortality arts of the Han originally diverged from the Yellow Emperor and Laozi’s teachings. By the Wei-Jin period, abstruse discourse flourished, and classical scholarship branched into many paths. Daoism falsely claimed kinship with Confucianism, while the immortals’ tradition became entangled with Daoism—yet this was merely a borrowing of its name, not a transformation of its essence. By the Song and Yuan dynasties, the *Cantong Qi*’s furnace and fire were reinterpreted to expound inner alchemy, refining yin and yang, harmonizing primordial *qi*, and dismissing medicinal ingestion and embryonic breathing as lesser paths, alchemical gold and silver as heterodox arts, and talismans and incantations as peripheral practices. The sole focus became the dual cultivation of nature and life, the secret to "the Valley Spirit’s immortality" and the ascent to transcendence. This doctrine drew from Chan Buddhism, incorporated *Yijing* principles, and pursued subtlety and profundity, even seeking to unify the Three Teachings. Thus, the immortals’ tradition handed down from the Han and Jin was entirely transformed, its name and substance thoroughly conflated.

Ge Hong’s work, however, adheres rigidly to his master’s transmission, eschewing mystical insight. It may be likened to Confucian exegetes interpreting the classics—is it not the Han scholarship of the immortals’ tradition? Sun Boyuan, the Salt and Iron Transport Commissioner, deeply cherished ancient learning and synthesized the Nine Schools. Observing that the Ming-era *Baopuzi* and the Tianyi Pavilion manuscript were riddled with errors and omissions, he personally collated them and entrusted me and Gu Jian● to compare our private copies with other texts, revising them through multiple rounds of verification. Boyuan compiled a bibliographic preface and intended to publish the work. Thus, I have here set forth the historical divergence and convergence of the immortals’ tradition and Daoism, arranging it as a preface for the reader’s reference.

Composed on the *jiayin* day of the seventh month, the seventeenth year of the Jiaqing reign (1812), by Fang Weidian of Tongcheng.

**Note:** The Ming edition of the *Baopuzi* appends a separate chapter, *Biezhi*, after the Inner Chapters, exclusively discussing breath circulation and guiding exercises. This contradicts the Inner Chapters’ original intent, and its language is crude, unworthy of a Jin-dynasty hand. Consulting Zichuan’s own preface reveals no such work, and neither the Sui nor Tang bibliographies record it. Only the *Song History*’s "Arts and Letters" section lists *Baopuzi Biezhi* in two volumes, with a note stating the author is unknown—it does not attribute it to Zichuan. This latecomer text is utterly unreliable. Moreover, the current version, at just over 560 characters, does not even fill a single fascicle and is not the Song or Yuan original. It has therefore been excised and will not be appended to the end.

**Fang Weidian’s postscript.**

---

**Catalogue of Ge Hong’s Writings**

- *Baopuzi Inner Chapters*: 20 fascicles. Includes preface. Recorded in the Sui bibliography as 21 fascicles. Extant.
- *Baopuzi Outer Chapters*: 50 fascicles. Includes preface. The Sui bibliography records 30 fascicles, noting the Liang dynasty had 51. Extant.
- *Stele Eulogies, Poems, and Rhapsodies*: 100 fascicles. Includes preface. The *Jin History*’s biography records "eulogies" as "dirges." The *Xianyuan Bianzhu* and Chen Masu’s *Daoist Learning Biographies* concur.
- *Military Documents, Memorials, Letters, and Records*: 30 fascicles. Includes preface. The *Jin History* biography records "military documents and memorials."
- *Biographies of Immortals*: 10 fascicles. Includes preface. Recorded in the *Jin History* biography. Extant.
- *Biographies of Recluses*: 10 fascicles. Includes preface. Recorded in the *Jin History* biography.
- *Military Affairs, Techniques, Miscellaneous Matters, and Essential Oddities*: 310 fascicles. Includes preface. The *Jin History* biography records "techniques and miscellaneous matters."
- *Jinkui Prescriptions*: 100 fascicles. Recorded in the *Jin History* biography.
- *Yuhan Prescriptions*: 100 fascicles. Mentioned in the *Baopuzi*’s *Zaying* chapter. Likely identical to the *Jinkui Prescriptions* above.
- *Emergency Prescriptions for the Elbow*: 4 fascicles. Recorded in the *Jin History* biography. The *Zaying* chapter records *Rescue Prescriptions for Sudden Illness* in 3 fascicles. The Sui bibliography records *Elbow Prescriptions* in 6 fascicles. The Old *Tang History* records *Elbow Rescue Prescriptions for Sudden Illness* in 4 fascicles. The *Siku Quanshu* catalogue records *Elbow Emergency Prescriptions* in 8 fascicles. Extant. The *Daoist Canon*’s Orthodox Section, Part One, records *Elbow Emergency Prescriptions* in 8 fascicles, mistakenly attributed to "Immortal Ge."
- *Divine Immortals’ Dietary Prescriptions*: 10 fascicles. Recorded in the Sui bibliography.
- *Taiqing Divine Immortals’ Dietary Classic*: 5 fascicles. Recorded in the New *Tang History*.
- *Dietary Prescriptions*: 4 fascicles. Recorded in fascicle 9 of the Tang monk Falin’s *Bianzheng Lun*.
- *Yuhan Decoction Prescriptions*: 5 fascicles. Recorded in the Sui bibliography.
- *Black Hair Wine Prescriptions*: 1 fascicle. Recorded in the *Chongwen General Catalogue* and the *Tongzhi Lue*.
- *Discourse on the Armillary Sphere*: Recorded in the *Jin History*’s "Astronomical Treatise." Supplemented in Wen Tingzhen’s *Supplement to the Jin History’s Arts and Letters*.
- *Record of Mount Mofu*: 1 fascicle. Supplemented in the *Supplement to the Jin History’s Arts and Letters*.
- *Tidal Theory*: Supplemented in the *Supplement to the Jin History’s Arts and Letters*. Briefly mentioned in the Outer Chapters’ lost fragments.
- *Military Method: Secluded Void, Lunar Timing, and Secret Essentials*: 1 fascicle. Recorded in the New *Tang History*.
- *Yinfu Ten Virtues Classic*: 1 fascicle. Recorded in the New *Tang History*.
- *Baopuzi Military Techniques*: Supplemented in the *Supplement to the Jin History’s Arts and Letters*. Yan Kejun’s *Complete Jin Prose* identifies this as a lost chapter of the Outer Chapters, collecting 42 fragments. Here, its title is separately recorded.
- *Golden and Wooden Myriad Spirits Formula*: 1 fascicle. Recorded in the Song bibliography and the *Tongzhi Lue*. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongshen* section, *Zhongshu* category. Likely an abridged version of the *Golden Elixir* chapter.
- *Taiqing Jade Stele*: 1 fascicle. Recorded in the Song bibliography. Contains Ge Hong’s dialogues with Zheng Siyuan. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongshen* section, *Zhongshu* category.
- *Great Elixir Dialogues*: 1 fascicle. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongshen* section, *Zhongshu* category.
- *Elbow Secrets of the Returned Elixir*: 3 fascicles. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongshen* section, *Zhongshu* category.
- *Four Masters’ Essential Secrets*: 1 fascicle. Recorded in the *Tongzhi Lue*. Compiles alchemical records by Liu Xiang, Lingyangzi, Baopuzi, and Hu Gangzi.
- *Baopuzi’s Discourse on Nourishing Life*: 1 fascicle. Recorded in the Song bibliography. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongshen* section, *Fangfa* category.
- *Master Zichuan’s Verified Techniques*: 1 fascicle. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongshen* section, *Zhongshu* category.
- *Divine Immortals’ Golden Liquefaction Classic*: 3 fascicles. Recorded in the *Tongzhi Lue* without an author. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongshen* section, *Zhongshu* category.

Yan Kejun notes: The *Baopuzi’s Discourse on Nourishing Life*’s first half corresponds to the *Dizhen* chapter, while its latter half complements the *Jiyan* chapter. *Master Zichuan’s Verified Techniques* is a later elaboration.

*The Divine Immortals’ Golden Liquefaction Classic* in three fascicles, of which the latter two constitute the *Golden Elixir* section. Cited in *Tieqiao Miscellaneous Manuscripts*, fascicle six, under "Dai Jilian’s Preface to the *Baopuzi*." Sun Yirang’s *Notes from Yizhai*, in the entry on *Baopuzi’s Subtle Principles*, states: *The Golden Liquefaction Classic* was forged by Jin-Song dynasty figures who falsely attributed it to Ge Hong. *Essential Characters from the Garden of Scripts* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Old Tang Bibliography*; *Yan’s Family Instructions* in the *Evidential Research* chapter renders it as *Garden of Scripts*. Ma Guohan produced a reconstructed edition. *Excerpts from the Records of the Grand Historian* in fourteen fascicles, recorded in the *New Tang Bibliography*. *Excerpts from the Book of Han* in thirty fascicles, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*. *Excerpts from the Book of Later Han* in thirty fascicles, recorded in the *Old Tang Bibliography*. *Biographies of Good Officials* in ten fascicles, as per his biography. *Accounts of Anomalies* in ten fascicles, as per his biography. *Miscellaneous Records of the Western Capital* in six fascicles, recorded in the *Old Tang Bibliography*. The *Concise Catalogue of the Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*, in the *Masters* section (fascicle twelve), notes: Early editions variously attribute it to Liu Xin of the Han or Ge Hong of the Jin, but it was in fact composed by Wu Jun of the Liang. However, Yu Jiaxi’s *Annotated Catalogue of the Four Treasuries*, in the seventh examination of the *Masters* section, definitively attributes it to Ge Hong. Extant. *The Esoteric Inner Transmission of Emperor Wu of Han* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography* without an author. Wen Tingzhen’s *Supplement to the Jin Bibliography* states: The *Japanese Extant Books Catalogue* attributes it to Ge Hong, and this attribution is followed here. Yu Jiaxi’s *Annotated Catalogue of the Four Treasuries*, in the seventh examination of the *Masters* section, definitively attributes it to Ge Hong of the Jin. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongzhen* section, *Jizhuan* category. *Preface and Exegesis to the Laozi’s Daodejing* in two fascicles, recorded in the *Old Tang Bibliography*. *Collated and Annotated Zhuangzi* in seventeen fascicles, recorded in Fazhen’s *Disquisitions on Rectifying Errors*; supplemented in the *Jin Bibliography*. *On Changes to Mourning Attire* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*. *Instant-Use Secret Manual of the Hidden Stem Method from the Elbow-Bag*, in one fascicle, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*; the *Dengshe* chapter renders it as *Instant-Use Manual from the Bag*. *Hidden Stem Reversal Diagrams* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*. *Essential Uses of the Hidden Stem Method* in four fascicles, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*. *Secret Essentials of the Hidden Stem Method* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*. *Essentials of the Hidden Stem Method* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*. *Three Primordials’ Hidden Stem Diagrams* in three fascicles, recorded in the *Old Tang Bibliography*. *Tortoise Divination* in two fascicles, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*. *Miscellaneous Divinations from the Zhouyi* in ten fascicles, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*. *The Writings of Master Baopu*, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography*’s *Collections* section. *Preface to the Esoteric Arts of the Bedchamber* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Sui Bibliography* as "by Master Ge"; also in the *New Tang Bibliography*. Possibly by Ge Hong. *The Perfected Lord of the Great One’s Song of Securing Destiny* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Song Bibliography*. *Baopuzi’s Separate Principles* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Tongzhi Lue*. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Taiqing* section. Likely compiled by later figures from oral instructions on exhalation, inhalation, and guiding *qi*, resembling *Embryonic Breathing* techniques. *Essential Instructions on Embryonic Breathing* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Tongzhi Lue*. *Embryonic Breathing Techniques* in one fascicle, supplemented in the *Jin Bibliography*. *Supplement to the Biography of Guo Wen*, supplemented in the *Jin Bibliography*.

Note: Guo Wen, styled Wenju, was a native of Zhi in Henei. A contemporary of Ge Hong, he likewise withdrew from the world amid the chaos of the age.

Predeceased Hong. See the *Biographies of Recluses* in the *Book of Jin*. *Song of the Five Metals and Dragon-Tiger* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Comprehensive Catalog of Chongwen*. *Textual Illustrations of the True Forms of the Five Marchmounts* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Comprehensive Catalog of Chongwen*. *Precepts Sutra of Laozi* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Tongzhi Lue*. *Record of the Guanzhong Region* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Song Bibliography*. Cited in the *Jade Sea*, referencing the *Book Catalog of the Restoration*. *Inner Biography of the Two Lords of Mayin* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Song Bibliography*. *Miscellaneous Instructions on Reclusion* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Song Bibliography*. Note: The *Baopuzi*’s *Miscellaneous Arts* chapter responds to inquiries on the Dao of reclusion, which may be the source for these *Miscellaneous Instructions*. *Records of the Perfected Immortals of Primordial Commencement* in one fascicle, recorded in the *Song Bibliography*. Extant in the *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongzhen* section, *Registers* category. Liu Shipei’s *Reading Notes on the Daoist Canon* states: “This text’s secondary title bears the five characters *Ge Hong’s Pillow Records*; its contents list the ranks, titles, and administrative seats of various immortals—identical to the currently transmitted *Pillow Records*.” Note: The *Daoist Canon*’s *Dongshen* section, *Methods* category, contains another *Pillow Records* in one fascicle, detailing techniques for nourishing life and extending destiny, entirely distinct from the *Records of the Perfected Immortals*. The *Tongzhi Lue* records *Sun Simiao’s Pillow Records* in one fascicle, leaving the attribution uncertain. The *Complete Library of the Four Treasuries* *General Catalog* states: *Pillow Book* in one fascicle, traditionally attributed to Ge Hong of the Jin. Examination of the *Bibliographies of the Sui, Tang, and Song* reveals only *Mozi’s Pillow Records* and *Pillow Essentials*, with no mention of Ge Hong’s *Pillow Book*. This text (*Pillow Book*) is also titled *Records of the Perfected Immortals of Primordial Commencement*. Yu Jiaxi’s *Disquisitions on the Four Treasuries General Catalog*, fascicle nineteen, argues that the *Pillow Book* mentions Xu Mu and Xu Yuqian, while Hong was Xu Mu’s senior, and Xu Yuqian a later generation—Hong could not have witnessed their deaths, let alone included them in his writings. Thus, the *Pillow Book* (*Records of the Perfected Immortals*) “clearly did not originate with (Ge) Hong.” Mr. Yu’s argument is sound. *Baopuzi’s Records of the Jade Tablets*, supplemented in the *Jin Bibliography*. Note: This likely refers to the *Baopuzi*’s citation of the *Records of the Jade Tablets*, not a text authored by Ge Hong. Xu Jian’s *Primary Learning Record*, fascicle twenty-nine, *Fox* section, thirteenth entry, quotes the *Baopuzi’s Records of the Jade Tablets*: “Foxes, wildcats, and wolves all live eight hundred years; after three hundred years, they briefly assume human form.” This precisely matches the *Baopuzi*’s *Popular Customs* chapter’s citation of the *Records of the Jade Tablets* (cf. Dunhuang manuscript fragments). The *Extensive Perusal* chapter already lists the *Records of the Jade Tablets* in one fascicle, confirming Ge Hong’s access to the text. Yet the *Primary Learning Record* initially misattributes the *Records of the Jade Tablets* to the *Baopuzi*. Yan Kejun’s *Iron Bridge Miscellany*, fascicle six, suggests this record may be a later forgery—an inaccurate claim. Rather, it was likely a misattribution by Tang scholars such as Xu Jian.
